Chapter 1: Why Coaching?  We’ve had people ask us why we put so much emphasis on professional coaching skills for Agile coaches. In this book, we attempt to give you insights into both the why and the how. We are passionate about this topic because we have experienced the positive impact that utilizing these skills has had on our clients. We recognize that Agile coaching utilizes a broad skill set and incorporates expertise and experience.  We will not place a heavy focus on technical, mentoring, training, facilitating, or consulting skills in this book because these topics are well covered by other authors. We recognize and validate their importance and for the purpose of this book, we wanted to highlight the one that we believe needs the most attention in the industry overall, coaching.  All these disciplines, experience, and expertise are required to coach Agile organizations in the adoption of Agile ways of being, thinking, and working. We also recognize that underneath the Agile expertise and guidance lies the heart of the issue: cultural transformation. In our experience, to make sustainable change in organizations you must work in partnership with your clients to help them create the change they want. Consulting is an aspect of Agile coaching. Training, mentoring, and facilitating are also aspects of Agile coaching. In this book, we attempt to place the emphasis on professional coaching because our experience has proven that this area is the most misunderstood. In fact, it is the area that many don’t know exists. It is our belief that if you are going to function as an Agile coach, you should fully understand the competency area that is prominently displayed on your title: Coach. Cherie, “Let me start with a first-hand account of how embodying a coaching mindset and behaviors has made a more sustainable impact. I began working with a client that already had one Agile coach on site. He was leading the engagement and was making good progress. He had the favor of the top leaders in the organization, and they trusted him. The client decided to add a few more coaches, so things could move along faster. As is my custom, when I arrived on the scene, I spent a few weeks getting to know people and just observing how things were done in the organization. I noticed some interesting patterns across teams. There were several things that showed up as mirror images across teams. Of course, this in and of itself wasn’t too big of a deal. People share best practices all the time, so it’s not unusual for organizations to have multiple teams doing some of the same things. After seeing the pattern repeat itself multiple times and asking a few questions, I was able to put the pieces together and identify which teams were working with the original Agile coach and which teams were not. It became obvious to me that the coach must have a heavy hand in deciding how teams would do things. Then, I noticed the coach would often say things like, “(x person) is uncoachable.” He also mentioned that when he deemed someone uncoachable, he would go to their manager and tell 

---

## Page 5

 
5 
them that a person wasn’t complying with coaching. Thus, the manager would discipline the employee and instruct them they had better listen to the coach. This was interesting and seemed contrary to my normal way of working. At about the three-weeks into the engagement, I quietly slipped into a team’s Daily Scrum being conducted in a conference room. I noticed the team was standing up around the conference table looking at their electronic Scrum board displayed on a large monitor. Several of those who saw me walk in began to look at me a bit nervously. I just smiled and encouraged them with a nod to ignore me and keep going. After they finished with the Daily Scrum, they looked to me for input. Since it was their daily Scrum and I was still new, I didn’t really see much to add. They were collaborating and seemed to have a plan for the day. I wondered why they were all standing around the table, but I let it pass, since many people have picked up the industry’s “stand-up” terminology and feel a need to stand up even when it isn’t adding value. Then, it happened. The moment when the world I had stepped into began to make sense. Someone said to me, ‘Wow, I can’t believe you didn’t say anything.’ I shrugged and said, ‘There didn’t seem to be any major issues with what you were doing, so there really wasn’t anything to say.’ Their response was, ‘If x coach had walked in and saw us looking at our board with this filter we would have gotten in trouble. He would have scolded us and then went and told our manager that we weren’t listening to him.’ I remained non-reactive and asked, ‘Well, what makes you not use the board with that filter when he isn’t in the room?’ Their response, ‘It doesn’t give us what we need to plan properly. This filter is how we need to see the information.’ After hearing this, I simply responded, ‘Well, it seems to me that you should use this filter if it’s the one that adds value.’ I worked with this client for a year alongside the other coach. We approached things very differently. At our core, we had the same beliefs about Agile and how things could be done more effectively. We simply had different ways of approaching our work. He tended to be more consultative and dictative in his approach. He identified things that were wrong and gave instructions to make corrections. He took on the responsibility for the change the client wanted to make and, as a result, his success was based on forcing fast compliance with the rules. I chose to take a coaching approach to my work. I let the responsibility for making changes remain with the client. I tended to ask more questions and give fewer dictates. I certainly shared information about things that might be more effective. Whenever I did, my standard way of doing so was to add my thoughts to the ideas they already had and allow them to utilize those ideas as they saw fit. If I thought a group was making a decision that had risks or impacts that concerned me, I would ask questions to help them uncover how they would deal with those risks. Occasionally I would even say, ‘In my experience, I haven’t seen this solution work out well for others. I don’t think it’s the best option for you.’ If they decided to move forward with the option, I supported the experiment and got curious whether it would work out for them. Sometimes it did. I also spent some time training new team members and new team startups. I started working with the individuals and teams who had been labeled ‘uncoachable’ because I knew these were the people who were not interested in simply doing what they were told. These people were ready for change and wanted to make changes that added value and made sense. I wanted them to stop being seen as ‘uncoachable’ because I didn’t think it was a fair assessment. I mentored employee Scrum Masters to be coaches and challenged them to allow their teams to be both self-accountable and responsible. Rather than trying to take control, I 

---

## Page 6

 
6 
challenged the Scrum Masters to release control and ask questions that allowed the teams to think outside the box rather than simply following orders. This way of working really resonated with team members. Soon, Scrum Masters and the teams began taking chances, experimenting, and getting noticeable results. I’ll be honest and say that I was not revered by the other two coaches working with that client. In fact, I became so disliked by them that one of them gave me a verbal thrashing and stopped associating with me. The other simply refused to even look at me when we passed one another in the hall and didn’t speak to me the last three months I was there. Why? Because I wasn’t forcing compliance. From their perspective, I was allowing things to roll backwards rather than pushing it forward. From my perspective, I was moving slower and creating a more sustainable impact by allowing the client to decide for themselves how they would improve. Shortly after I left the engagement, something interesting happened. The leader of that organization decided they were doing so well, they called the ‘transformation’ a success and released the coaches. They also determined that since they were doing so well, they no longer needed Scrum Masters. Those teams who had been allowed to make their own decisions and grow organically started being forced to do things they saw as not valuable and, in their words, ‘not Agile.’ Within a year, all the most talented people I had worked with left that organization after long careers, stating, ‘They brought Agile to us, and it was the right thing to do. Now, they are taking it away and expecting us to go back to the way things were before. We simply don’t believe in that way of working anymore and can’t do it.’ These people had changed their mindset. They each said that they had changed the way they think about how work should be approached, and it had changed who they were from the inside out. They had adopted Agile values and principles into their core value system. They believed that a collaborative, cross functional, self-accountable work environment was the best way to work. These people had become Agile thinkers and refused to think any other way. That’s what sustainable change looks like. Others in the organization who had been under a more dictative approach had only changed behaviors. So, going back to the way things were relieved some pressure.”  This is just one of many examples where we were able to see the impacts of how we work with client’s side by side how others work with clients. It is true that working from a coaching stance does not create fast change. However, it does have a stronger likelihood of creating lasting change. Forcing behavior change can be a fast way to gain compliance and can gain favor with organizational leaders. It also creates a vanity metric around how fast things are changing. What is important is not how quickly things can start changing, but how sustainable the changes are. If a company hires an Agile coach for six months and that coach gains fast traction through strong force and threats, compliance may come quickly. This creates a scenario where the coach becomes the forcing mechanism that is holding all the change in place. However, when the coach leaves, the forcing mechanism, the coach in this case, is removed and much of the change generally rolls back. If the change rolls back, that company has just invested large sums of time and money with no sustainable results. They may bring in another coach and repeat the process or they may simply say Agile doesn’t work and give up. 

---

## Page 7

 
7 
It has been our experience that a much more valuable investment for an organization is to invest a year of time and resources to work with someone who takes a coaching approach. Improvement will likely happen in smaller increments. Experiments will be done to help find the right solutions. People will make changes and then go back to what they are used to until they see the value they were getting with new methods. Momentum will build and what will start to slowly build up in force will keep moving after the coach leaves because the organization itself is the forcing mechanism for change. Michael, “In the fifth week of an engagement, after watching another Agile coach provide consulting advice, I asked the management team, ‘What job has your organization hired Agile coaches to do?’ The other coach had some success initially. He had been telling the management team what they should be doing and how they should be adopting change. Eventually, he ran into trouble because the management team was not aligned on its business goals. He had simply not asked enough questions to fully understand the client’s needs and culture to give the cookie cutter advice he offered. When that coach used the same approach working with teams, they found the consulting advice overwhelming. On one occasion, a Scrum Master cried for 20 minutes while listening to the coach tell her how she should be doing her job.  I asked management what they wanted Agile to do for them. They replied they wanted to run more product experiments that would lead to quick successes. They believed this would help them to get additional funding from their strategic partners. I suggested and helped them put together a workshop based on Lean Startup ideas. The VP of Engineering took ownership and ran the workshop, and I happily let the client drive. Team members found the ideas shared in the workshop riveting, and they quickly collaborated on developing small experiments they could immediately execute. By running these experiments, they learned more about what their strategic customers wanted. And no one shed a tear.” When a coach leaves an organization, the changes should not roll backward. Compliance is easier to install and often takes hold quickly. Sustainable change takes time and effort to gain momentum. We wrote this book because it is our belief that Agile coaches should be working with clients to bring sustainable change. We believe that a coaching mindset is critical to sustainability. Many Agile coaches work from a consulting mindset and don’t necessarily have a full understanding about how a coaching mindset can be critical to making change at the organizational level. In this book, we hope to bring that knowledge to the industry. Why Your Company Need Agile Coaching, Not Management Consulting   Perhaps you are the person at your company who is on a mission to make improvements, so you are reading this book to understand what an Agile coach can do for you. As your company thinks about who it wants to partner with on its agile journey, it has probably considered hiring a management consultant. Common wisdom dictates they’ll help you move faster, work better, and find the perfect solution to streamline your process. But all their long hours and sleek 

---

## Page 8

 
8 
PowerPoints won’t solve your problem long-term. Because much of what management consultants do is for show.  In a very revealing article for Harvard Business Review1, Professors Alaric Bourgoin and Jean-Francois Harvey points out all the ways consultants essentially “fake it ‘till they make it.” They rely on shared industry knowledge to sound like experts. They stay late and arrive early so you think they’re working harder than you. And those “million-dollar PowerPoints” with clean lines and actionable solutions are most likely upcycled from previous clients.  Although Bourgoin and Harvey note this behavior as a hazard of the consulting profession, that still hasn’t changed the fact that it’s a $256 billion industry focused on treating the symptoms of a business issue as opposed to addressing the source of the problem. The invitational approach to Agile coaching that we describe in this book, on the other hand, seeks to increase the capability of an organization to solve its own problems. Where consultants enter a situation and immediately begin dishing out solutions, commands, and advice, an agile coach works with the company to identify the root of workflow problems and improve flexibility. Most importantly, agile coaches want to put your people at the helm of change — not an outsider.  Michael, “As an agile coach for more than 10 years, and only 1 of about 150 Scrum Alliance Certified Enterprise Coaches in the world, I’ve seen first-hand the dramatic difference our approach can make.”   By valuing your people as your greatest asset, you’ll create long-term, actionable change that promotes exponential growth for your business. But for this to work, businesses need to be willing to function in tandem with an agile coach and not expect the consultant experience. Otherwise, both sides will experience a catastrophe.  So, before you leap into a new Agile coaching session, here are a few things you should consider: First, Agile coaches work with pre-existing needs instead of creating new ones. As a business leader, you don’t need to be told how important change is — you already know. And yet, a management consultant often strives to stir up a sense of urgency around change. This old-school line of thought was led by John Kotter, a big name in business theory and management consulting. Kotter always said creating urgency was the first thing you should do when working with a company. But in reality, “creating urgency” is based on fear. For example, maybe your team would benefit from improved workplace dynamics, tightened workflows, or simply finding better ways to communicate.  No amount of standing over people’s shoulders and telling them to change (“with urgency”) is going to solve the root of any of those problems. Instead, good Agile coaches tap into pre-existing desires. They don’t step in and say, “This is what’s urgent.” They listen and ask, “What 
 1   https://hbr.org/2018/07/how-consultants-project-expertise-and-learn-at-the-same-time 

---

## Page 9

 
9 
do you want to improve?” And more importantly, “How do you want to improve it?” Then, and only then, can you and your team find the most efficient way forward together. 
Second, Agile coaching removes the middleman, helping you foster strong internal relationships. Few people will volunteer themselves for conflict, so it’s tempting to have a consultant step in and do all the talking for you. Michael says, “I was recently working with a Senior Vice President (SVP) who was going through this exact scenario. He hired someone who would talk with his teams, report back what was said, and then make recommendations. I couldn’t believe he refused to speak with his team directly.”   
What happens in a communication funnel like this is it creates a parasitic relationship. An outside influence is all of sudden needed to communicate with your team — and without it things may fall apart. You lose your power as a leader. Instead, the management consultant runs the show. And that’s not the way to win big. 
The best wins come from the people in the company. Remember: your people are your experts. They already know best; you just have to get them to talk to you. A2 good Agile coach can help push the right buttons and open the right channels to start the necessary conversations without getting in the middle. 
Third, Agile coaching puts people over profits by any means. For the greatest success, your company needs to value people as humans. 
We’ve all seen how massively a company can fail if they don’t. Valeant Pharmaceuticals3, for example, was under fire for gouging drug prices — they upped the cost of a vital prescription to 5500% of its original cost in a single night. They’ve since endured major rebranding to shake off all the scandal. 
 2  https://www.ft.com/content/0bb37fd2-ef63-11e5-aff5-19b4e253664a 3  https://fortune.com/2015/10/31/valeant-scandal/ 

---

## Page 10

 
10 
They brought on McKinsey management consulting. The problem with agencies like McKinsey is they’re most interested in companies that make money at whatever cost. They aren’t in tune with the way the world is moving — valuing human agency over treating people as disposable. 
Conversely, a human-centric approach inspires and values true innovation. 
Agile coaches can help companies understand where their ethical boundaries are, and then operate in a way that honors them. By feeling valued and needed, teams will feel more capable of disrupting the world around them. Without that sense, they may feel like just another cog in a machine. And cogs rarely lead to innovation. 
By listening to your people, needs, goals and challenges, Agile coaches help you to create the best solutions internally. We facilitate but don’t dictate, allowing you to innovate the way you’re meant to. This keeps companies growing and functioning, without the need for a consultant six months down the road. In fact, our goal as coaches is to never see you again — we have nothing to gain from your failure. And that may be the biggest difference between a consultant and a coach — true investment in your success. 
Chapter 2: The Coaching Mindset The magic of a mixed drink comes from the variety of different ingredients. You get a little of this, a little of that, and a bit of another thing, all blended to create the perfect cocktail. While this concept may be great for crafting amazing drinks, it’s not the way to conduct an amazing professional coaching session. The magic of professional coaching comes from sticking with a single, solitary ingredient: coaching. You don’t mix in a little consulting, a little advising, and a batch of rules and regulations you insist clients follow. You need to stick to straight-up coaching without watering it down if you want your clients to reap the greatest rewards and success. A quote from one of the founding members of the International Coach Federation (ICF), Marcia Reynolds that Cherie and Alex captured during a podcast4 with Tandem Coaching sums it up neatly: “The notion of Hybrid Coaching dilutes the value of coaching. When you mix mentoring, advice giving and leading people to what is best for them into what you call coaching, people come to expect the easy way out. They look forward to you telling them what to do. This might be 
 4  https://tandemcoaching.academy/coaching-the-person-not-the-problem-with-marcia-reynolds/ 

---

## Page 11

 
11 
helpful, but if coaching is what they really want or need, they're mis-experiencing this powerful technology for creating breakthroughs and growth.” Agile coaching, on the other hand, is intended to be a mix of disciplines. Each discipline has power to bring to the engagement. The question is: Can the Agile coach exercise enough self-management to be in the discipline the client needs and, when shifting disciplines, do so effectively and purposefully? It is our opinion that to effectively switch between disciplines, an Agile coach must understand and embody a coaching mindset. The Coaching Mindset The coaching mindset is not only a way of behaving when you are conducting a 1-1 or team coaching session. Many people can “act” like a coach, utilize coaching skills, and even conduct a good coaching session that checks all the boxes. Coaching skills can be taught. A coaching mindset must be developed purposefully. Many people have coaching skills. However, knowing how to coach doesn’t make you a coach. The thing that’s critical to have a coaching mindset is the ability to see the client as competent, resourceful, creative, and not in need of fixing by the coach. When you look at people as fully able to exercise their talents, skills, experience, and expertise to make decisions, you treat them differently. What we have witnessed in our experience working with clients among other coaches is that those who have a coaching mindset treat the client as an equal. Those who don’t have a coaching mindset often treat the client as if they are subject to the coach.  Much like the coaching mindset, and Agile mindset is a way of thinking, believing, and behaving.  What we have noticed in our experience is that Agile coaches who have an Agile mindset are more likely to have a practical application of Agile rather than a dictative application where everything in the book must exist. An Agile mindset is a mindset that embodies the Agile values and principles and thinks in a way that is collaborative, focused on results rather than processes, and is empowering. When you put this way of thinking differently about how to approach work together with a coaching mindset that sees people differently, everything about the way you engage changes. Think about it this way. When you work with an organization, and they adopt Scrum, Kanban, or some other discipline the first thing they, and many Agile, coaches think about is following “the rules”. They make sure the teams are having the right meetings. They make sure the teams are creating backlog items a certain way. They make sure the teams are right sizing the work. None of these things are incorrect. However, they are all external actions and can be done with no real change occurring in the organization.  Cherie, “In my experience focusing only on behaviors often results in people not changing the way they think about how they approach work. If they don’t know the why, or the behaviors aren’t solving a problem then they go through the motions. If people believe their jobs or performance reviews may be negatively impacted, they will comply if they can keep moving forward. I’ve often witnessed managers who will say, “Sure, fine, we’ll do it this way if it makes you happy.” Then, when an emergent challenge comes, they default to their habitual way of 

---

## Page 12

 
12 
thinking and do what they’ve always done. Because they haven’t changed the way they think about work and decisions, they just go back to the same way of making decisions that they know works.   Now, let's bring this back to coaching skills. When a coaching session is going well, it’s easy to focus on following the coaching arc, asking questions, and giving the client space to think through their challenges. However, when the session isn’t perfect it’s the coaching mindset that keeps the coach in the proper stance. If you don’t have a coaching mindset, coaching behaviors are a fine idea, but they are not convenient when you have seen the client’s problem before, and you “know” what they can do to solve it. Coaching behaviors don’t seem important when you believe that the client should take certain actions if they want to be successful. Moving them forward, so you can add value to the engagement is what will seem most important at that moment. However, it’s the coaching mindset that enables you to self-manage. It enables you to carefully consider if stepping into mentoring or consulting is what’s best for the client long term. Sometimes it is important to make a shift and help the client understand something they genuinely don’t know that is vital for them to move forward. However, what we’ve seen more often is that it is not what the client needs, but what the coach needs that drives the coach to step into consulting or mentoring. The coach needs to feel like they are adding value. Sometimes they need to prove their expertise. Often, they just want to escalate the timeline to seeing changes happen in the organization. However, the question of success isn’t how quickly an organization can install new behaviors. The question of success is how sustainable are the changes? Agile coaches often talk about “individuals and interactions over process and tools” then focus on process and tools. Perhaps they focus on interactions because those are easily seen through behaviors. But how often do we really focus on the individuals? Professional coaching has a concept: coach the person, not the problem. At first glance, this concept doesn’t fit into Agile coaching. Let's be honest, companies hire us to install Agile and fix the problems they are having, so they can get business results. To do this sustainably, it is important to help the client focus on the problem, so they can take ownership of the changes they want to make. However, the Agile coach needs to focus on the client. Most decisions and actions clients take are temporal experiments. The decisions and actions are not as important as how they went about determining that the decisions and actions were the most appropriate. If you focus on the client's mindset you will focus more on the values and principles of Agile than the best practices of Agile. This will enable them to make good decisions that keep them in alignment with the way they want to see their organization work. By learning to think like an agilist, their behaviors will display Agileness. Forcing them to do Agile behaviors will not create Agile thinking. To develop a coaching mindset, there are a few key shifts in thinking that must occur. Competent — Not broken or in need of being fixed 

---

## Page 13

 
13 
This may very well be the most important concept in this book. It underlies the coaching mindset, determines how you see people, and reinforces the decisions you make about how to work with clients at that moment. “Seeing people as competent was a core aspect of the mindset that enabled me to move to a place of mastery and effectiveness in coaching,” says Cherie. “A coaching mindset moves beyond the coaching conversation and becomes a part of who you are. You see people differently. And, because you see them differently, you interact and react differently. The coaching mindset enables you to be curious instead of arrogant.” Cherie, “Whenever I do any coach training, there is a powerful exercise I do. I ask people to take a moment to reflect on and discuss these two questions, 1) What will be different about my interactions if I genuinely and authentically believe that every person in the organization I am coaching is completely competent and don’t need me to fix them? 2) In what ways have I been judging the people in the organizations I’ve coached to be incompetent and need me to do things properly?” Our challenge to you is to reflect on these questions and do some soul-searching. Are you seeing the individuals in the organizations you coach as fully competent? Or have you judged that some of them are broken and need you to tell them how to change? If you are up for a challenge, we invite you to look around at other parts of your life. Who are you seeing as not competent to make their own decisions and be responsible for their actions? Perhaps it’s a partner who approaches life differently than you do. Could it be a parent who doesn’t understand technology and trusts you enough to ask for help? Maybe a teenager or a younger child that you feel compelled to protect, so you won’t allow them to make mistakes and learn valuable lessons they will need later in life.  Your ability to trust in the competence of other people is at the pinnacle of allowing them to take ownership and responsibility for what they want to change and what they don’t. When you can trust that they are competent to take control of their own life, profession, and company you will be able to trust them to decide what changes they will undertake now, and which will be deprioritized. It is only when you trust in the competence of the teams and organizations you coach that you will model self-organization. Until then, you will insist that people self-organize within the boundaries you give them. When you believe your client is competent things shift. When you notice something happening that seems completely off base, you will react differently. Have you ever rolled your eyes when seeing a process, the client is following that just doesn’t make sense? Have you gotten frustrated because even though you’ve talked about it at length, the client keeps saying they will do one thing and keep doing something else? Have you gotten a bit angry when seeing how a manager or team member is acting? Perhaps they are blocking progress or not allowing the others to function properly in their roles. Perhaps their command-and-control behavior is triggering you to want to march into their office and tell them how hard they are making things for everyone else. We all have these moments, and these are all symptoms of not seeing the client as competent and of you being triggered to “fix” them. 

---

## Page 14

 
14 
What happens differently if you see your client differently? When you see these same things happening, instead of being triggered to react, you will get curious. When you know the client is competent, you also know that there is a perfectly reasonable explanation for what’s happening. If this process was put in place, there must have been a problem it was instituted to solve. You will start to ask questions to understand the problem. You will ask how the current process is working for them and if there’s anything about it that isn’t working. Instead of deciding for them that they must change the process, you will help them examine the process and decide for themselves if it needs to change. Creative and resourceful — not in need of rescuing Supporting clients to reach the outcomes they seek begins with a true partnership. You not only need to believe that your clients are competent. You also need to believe that they are creative and resourceful and can figure out the best next steps for themselves, their team, and their company. If we believe this, we will leave our superhero cape at home and trust the client. The challenge we face as Agile coaches is the thing that also sets us apart and makes companies hire us — our experience and expertise. Masterful coaching in an Agile environment doesn’t mean that we leave what we know at the door, that’s not partnership. There is more to Agile coaching than just showing up and asking questions. And there’s more to Agile coaching than showing up teaching everyone how to do their jobs. You must know more than Agile to be an effective Agile coach. You have to experience working with broken Agile repeatedly before you can understand the challenges clients face when trying to improve.   That experience enables you to empathize with what the client is experiencing. No one comes to work every day hoping to fail. People want improvement. What they need from their Agile coach is to be able to understand and empathize with the client and partner with them to figure out how to remove the barriers to success. Keep in mind that clients need empathy not sympathy. Empathy connects with what the client may be experiencing and gives validation and acceptance. Sympathy feels pity for the client and expresses a belief in the client’s brokenness. If we think the client is broken, it will impact our ability to work with them to create sustainable change. If they are broken, and you fix them, the client will grow dependent on you to solve their problems. You might get lots of recognition and appreciation for helping them, but this is not in the client’s best interest. For them to create sustainable changes the client needs to take ownership of making the change for themselves. This is how they learn to think through the challenges they face and make educated decisions about how to overcome them. Your goal as a coach should always be to ensure that the client continues the work after you leave. Showing concern for the client is not the same as worrying about the client. Concern communicates that you care about the client and want the best success for them. It communicates that you are all in and a full partner in doing the work. It builds trust with your clients when they know you support them. 

---

## Page 15

 
15 
Worry, on the other hand, expresses that you want to somehow fix the client’s problems if given a chance. It expresses that you can fix the situation when you believe your client can’t. Worry will cause you to push your own ideas, solutions, and agendas on the client. Worry drives you to put on the superhero cape and tell the client to step aside because you have the answer to their problems.  Safeguard against falling into this type of codependent relationship with the client. Codependency from an Agile coaching standpoint means you think the client needs you to be successful, and the client thinks they need you to be successful. Neither of these beliefs are helpful, and they certainly are not true. “A simple way to tell that you are falling into the ‘worry’ trap is to monitor how you communicate with other Agile coaches,” says Michael. “If you find yourself saying ‘If they just did x all their problems would be solved’ or ‘They need to do x to be successful’ that probably means that you’ve put on your superhero cape.” Please also take note that what we are recommending is not merely an external facade. You must intentionally cultivate this belief into your way of being. It goes beyond a single coaching conversation or encounter. Look around the Agile community. Who are you putting on pedestals? Whom are you dismissing? Instead of seeing people as stack ranked, see them as human. All deserving to be seen as creative, resourceful, and unbroken. If you can do this successfully, you will find brilliant people in the community that you can mentor to become even better. You will also find that people you thought you would mentor, actually mentor you. You will experience that those you turn to for mentoring will learn from you too. When you can cultivate the belief that humans are humans and should all be treated as valuable, you will be able to support any client. Clients need to trust that your support is genuine before they can express the truth of their situation without worrying what you might think. Cultivate the belief that your client is creative, resourceful, and unbroken. Become fascinated with what others might find shocking. Allow yourself to accept the client for who they are with all the things that are part of that package. Don’t dismiss things the client says and assume they are non-relevant. Instead, become curious about why the client is sharing this information with you. Consider how it is all part of the bigger picture. When clients trust you, they will tell you what you never expected to hear. Learn to control your responses. Learn to take it in without the judgment reflex taking over. See information shared as data for the client to work with rather than data to be sorted, analyzed, and judged by you. Cultivate a habitual reaction of curiosity rather than shock, defensiveness, or judgment. Cherie, “I learned to control the shock, defensiveness, and judgment reactions by becoming acquainted with my internal physical happenings when these responses are triggered. Each time I felt the trigger, I immediately checked my facial expression to ensure neutrality. I checked to make sure I was breathing normally. I relaxed my face, cleared my mind, and just listened. I reminded myself to be curious and to suspend judgment. Doing this repeatedly outside of coaching conversations helped condition my mind and emotions to detect and respond to the physical trigger. Focusing on building this ability makes it an instinct now during coaching 

---

## Page 16

 
16 
conversations. Remember, you can’t control triggers. You can control how you react and respond.” Equal — not better than or less than If you really want to be a masterful coach, cultivate the belief that you and your client are equal partners. You might have more experience and expertise in some areas and that’s what you bring to the table. They have different experience and expertise and that’s what they bring to the table. Trust that the client is the expert of their own business. They know the intricate details about the operations, the personalities, and the company culture. Therefore they should be making the decisions about what will work and what will not. Your salary, job title, reporting structure, etc., do not place you above staff and below executives. All are to be seen as equal partners. If you can’t see your clients as equals it will cause you to look at some people as not as competent as you are. Clients can feel this, and they also see it in your behaviors when you treat people with differing levels of respect, care, and patience. If you see executives as more important than you, it will hinder your ability to be straightforward with them. You will hold back because you don’t want to upset them. This is not helpful for the client. If they hired, you as their coach they see you as equal already. Treat staff with the same level of respect you give executives and give executives the same level of challenge you give to staff. Everyone will benefit.   
Chapter 3: Developing a Solid Foundation  Developing a Solid Personal Foundation You wouldn’t build a house on a foundation of sand, would you? You’d want bedrock beneath the structure to keep it standing strong, stable, and balanced. A solid foundation also helps stop the house from shifting, the floors from buckling, and the walls from eventually cracking or even collapsing. Building a home on a foundation of bedrock is a common understanding, yet some of us may overlook our foundation as we’re zipping through life. You may run from one client to the next, putting their needs above your own to keep your business booming. You may put self-care on the back burner, thinking it’s a nice option — or a luxury — not a necessity. Nothing could be further from the truth.  If you keep up such a frenetic pace, what eventually happens? You crack. Collapse. Have a breakdown. This can be especially true in the enterprise coaching realm, where you are regularly meeting with high-stakes clients with equally high demands. 

---

## Page 17

 
17 
When you give it your all — without the benefit of a solid personal foundation — you are going to run out of things to give. You’ve heard it on airplanes all the time. Don’t try to help anyone else with their oxygen mask until you’ve put on your mask first. That oxygen mask is the personal foundation that keeps you going. Building one is more than just a good idea. It’s the only way to live if you want to be able to give your best every single day.  How do you begin to create a foundation that keeps you strong, stable, and balanced? That's what this chapter is all about. Daily Practices Build Strong Foundation Establishing daily practices that contribute to a strong personal foundation requires a combination of self-awareness and courage. The self-awareness part is covered by knowing what makes you tick. What nourishes you? What’s good for you? What do you need to do for yourself regularly?  Figuring this out entails a deep dive into you. You need to determine what is needed for you to thrive. Not what society says you need. Not what your partner, parents, or neighbors say you need. You are the only person who can fully understand what you need to have a stable foundation. You are the one who determines what creates just the right amount of balance in your life. Once you pinpoint habits and lifestyle practices that keep you going, the courage portion of the equation kicks in. Knowing what you need is not enough. You must regularly implement those things and not let life disrupt your self-balance. This can take a lot of courage, especially if what you need is not aligned with the typical way of doing things. Just ask Cherie. She says, “Society tells us we should be working from 8 a.m. to 5 p.m., with a few short breaks and maybe lunch in between. I don’t work well in the morning. My brain just can’t seem to focus. I also don’t like going to sleep at what most people would consider a reasonable hour. This means that waking up early in the morning to start working at 8 am leaves me tired and sleep deprived. However, I do work well from 8 p.m. to 2 a.m. I excel at detailed work at night when there are no distractions, and my brain is firing on all cylinders. To keep a sustainable pace, I need to do what works for me.  So, I keep the hours that work for me. I schedule my client calls to start later in the day, take a break 5-7 pm then often work until the early morning hours. I sleep in until I naturally wake up in the mornings and take a nap when needed. This works well for me, so I ignore what society demands and care for myself in the way that is best for me.”  Knowing yourself and breaking free of society’s rules that don’t suit you. That’s the epitome of courage when it comes to building a solid personal foundation. It’s crucial to make your own rules, based on what you need to maintain a sustainable pace. Michael says, “Meditation and breathwork sessions are essential parts of my daily routine. I do 20 minutes in the morning and another 20 minutes in the afternoon. I schedule extra sessions when I start to see warning signs that I’m losing my sustainable pace. Signs like insomnia, increased stress levels, and talking to myself. I immediately turn to meditation and breath work to get me back on track.” 

---

## Page 18

 
18 
Other daily practices include journaling, exercising, and eating right. You need to model a sustainable pace if you’re going to recommend it. So, integrate what you preach into who you are. You can’t support others if you’re not supporting yourself, and this involves constantly nourishing your body, mind, and soul with what you need to thrive. Large client bases and high-stakes clients require enterprise coaches to spend a lot of time being at their best. Since we can only give from our abundance, it’s imperative to keep our abundance flowing. Cherie says, “I am very intentional about my calendar. I block out mornings, no work until at least 10 am, so I can wake without an alarm clock. I block out time off and when I’m busy I block my calendar to receive any more appointments for 1-2 months at a time. I also schedule doctor, dental, hair, nail, and massage appointments for the entire year. This prevents me from having to struggle trying to find appointments at the last minute. It reduces stress and prevents me from taking on too much work. I also block off weekends as no appointment days and often spend most of Saturday and Sunday relaxing and resting, reading fiction novels, and letting my body heal itself. This sabbath keeps me in good mental and physical balance.” What Happens When We Falter? Regardless of how self-aware, courageous, and disciplined we may be, we’re still human. That means we’re bound to fall out of our stable, balanced, and healthy zone from time to time. What happens then? We all have the signals that let us know when we’re not at our best. We have to recognize when it’s happening. Cherie says, “When I get tired, I tend to be less filtered. I get more easily frustrated and have less patience for other humans. As a result, I end up blurting out things that can be harmful. I know that means I need to invest in some self-care practices and to sleep more.” Michael says, “I know I’m off-balance when my ego gets involved, or I become afraid or angry and then lose neutrality. I may become defensive, start lashing out at others and draw attention to myself. That’s exactly what the ego wants. I know right there I need more self-care, more daily practices keeping me at my best.” When we’re in balance, it’s easy to be generous, compassionate, and kind. When we’re not, we revert to less healthy behaviors and habits. The quicker we can realize we’re off-kilter, the quicker we can remedy it. This helps keep damage, to us and others, to a minimum. That is exactly why self-care comes first. This is the opposite of what most of us have been taught. In fact, the messages we often get are that self-care is a weakness, although because of the courage it takes to run counter to cultural norms, it’s a sign of both wisdom and strength.  Once we begin to more easily recognize when we’re off-balance, we can look at how we got that way. What have we done, or not done, leading up to the imbalance? What behaviors are you doing that you don’t like, and what circumstances led up to this? Knowing what road led to the situation can help us recognize it even earlier, to change course before we’re more deeply entrenched in behaviors we’d rather not exhibit. 

---

## Page 19

 
19 
Cherie says, “I have often found myself getting a bit overwhelmed in a situation, then shifting into blaming mode, getting frustrated, and being very abrupt in the way I communicate. One day, after such an episode, I apologized to my son, a teenager at the time, for my behavior. In his wisdom, he was able to see a pattern that I was so entrenched in that I couldn’t see it for myself.  He explained a part of my behavior that I would never have understood without him. He expressed total acceptance of my poor behavior and said, ‘It’s okay mom. I know that you get frustrated when you are confused. You don’t mean any harm.’ Because of his insight into my reactions, I can now recognize when I’m starting to feel overwhelmed, and stressed, and it reminds me to stop and ask myself what I am confused about. This helps me to not act in a way I’ll have to apologize for later.” The key here is to watch yourself operate, to note when you’re slipping out of balance, and why. Run your mind and emotions in “debug” mode. When you’re deeply engrossed in a situation, it can be challenging to witness your behaviors as a neutral observer. That’s where you can ask for help from a friend or fellow coach. They can step in to point out the behavior, and even help redirect the situation. Michael says, “I tend to get upset when clients don’t understand what I’m saying. I can end up raising my voice and blaming the client for not getting it. Once I was co-coaching with a friend. When this happened during the engagement, my friend would step in and take over the conversation. This was perfect. It gave me time to step back and regroup before I made a challenging situation worse.” Consider getting a coach, working with a coaching supervisor, or asking a friend to help. Like self-care, asking for help is another sign of strength that’s pegged by society as a weakness. It takes a strong and courageous person to recognize the need for assistance. And when it comes to mental health, there is no shame in taking care of your mental health the same way you should take care of your physical health.  Once you recognize you’re off-balance, you may want to pause for an appropriate amount of time. This timeframe could be quite long, as our brain chemicals need to calm down. After we’re back in balance, it’s time to follow-up with additional actions. Apologize if needed. If you hurt someone or acted in a disruptive way, an apology can help correct the situation. An apology also acts as a sign of acknowledgement that you were out of line, which is much more connecting than simply ignoring the incident as if it never happened. If you find yourself apologizing often, it may mean that you need to become more aware of your thoughts and triggers, so you can build different responses and habits. This is great work to do with a professional coach. It is important to also be aware that it is possible that this is an indicator that there is a deeper issue that may be more suitable for counselling than coaching. The aftermath is also a good time to journal. In addition to helping, you manage anxiety and reduce stress, The University of Rochester Medical Center notes that journaling allows you to track day-to-day signs, so you can more easily recognize triggers and create ways to more effectively control them. 

---

## Page 20

 
20 
Journaling can also help you notice patterns of behavior that crop up again and again. If you notice you keep apologizing to the same people for the same thing, the goal is not to keep apologizing for what you continue to do. That’s using an apology to cover up a dysfunctional behavior instead of changing. The goal is to stop doing it. Tracking your habits and behaviors can shed light on why a particular behavior keeps occurring, and what you can do to stop the behavior from repeating itself. Another trap that you can fall into as a coping mechanism when you’re out of balance is to stop caring, to practice stonewalling. You might use it to anesthetize. You will step out of the situation, true. But if you don’t step out to regroup and return, you will step out of the conflict to avoid it. Then, you may continue to go through the motions and collect your coaching fees, but because you are in the self-protective mode, you will no longer provide the level of support your client needs and deserves. Perhaps even worse, you will no longer be true to your own values. Strengthening Emotional Intelligence “Emotional intelligence is the ability you have to be smart about your emotions,” writes Professor of Psychology Leslie Riopel in an article published at Positive Psychology. Another Psychology Today article adds that it can be viewed as the ability to identify and manage your own emotions, as well as the emotions of others. The framework for emotional intelligence, or EQ, is divided into four areas. They are: ● Self-awareness ● Self-management ● Social awareness ● Relationship management 
 From: https://positivepsychology.com/emotional-intelligence-frameworks/  


---

## Page 21

 
21 
People tend to naturally have different levels of emotional intelligence, but the good news is that it can be developed and strengthened.  EQ is a set of skills that can be built upon over time. The first step is to determine how much emotional intelligence you currently have. Start by learning more about it. Take an assessment. See where your strengths and weaknesses lie, so you can maintain the former and build on the latter. Working with a friend or fellow coach can also come in handy here, as they’re more likely to notice when you are behaving in detrimental ways concerning your own emotions and the emotions of others. You can find EQ assessments across the web, such as the Emotional Intelligence 2.0 assessment. In our opinion, assessments that have a 180 or 360 component are more effective because when a person lacks emotional intelligence it’s hard for them to assess. Comparing their own assessment of themselves to what others say can often provide the outside perspective needed to see the impacts we have on others. Cherie uses the Genos Intelligence 180 and 360 assessments for clients who wish to receive coaching to improve their level of emotional intelligence.  Michael says, “When I began learning more about emotional intelligence, I had a blinding insight. It was that you can’t control other people with your emotions. That means yelling at someone doesn’t help you. Once I realized this at the cognitive level, I had to let the lesson sink into my heart. That was followed by trying out the new behavior and getting used to it — and being okay with feeling uncomfortable and failing. Strengthening my emotional intelligence made me feel more solid, more comfortable, more grounded. It makes other people feel safer.” You’re not going to snap to a high level of emotional intelligence just because you want to or feel you need to. You have to work at it consistently. It can be hard work. It can be uncomfortable work. But the results are worth it. When your emotions are out of control, you become unpredictable, behave poorly, and end up feeling scared and judged. Other people are also scared, as they don’t know how you’re going to react.  Any issues with emotional intelligence in the enterprise coaching realm can be especially damaging. A coach that is out of control emotionally will learn, rather quickly, that it can sever the client relationship in an instant. After all, if you cannot control your own emotions, how are you going to help teams of senior executives? People need to be able to trust you if they are to be open to coaching because the position of the client brings a lot of vulnerability to people who may not be used to being vulnerable. The higher up the corporate ladder your clients are, the higher the stakes are for them and the more emotional intelligence you must exercise when working with them. Self-Discovery Builds the Foundation One final and extremely important pillar in building a solid personal foundation is embarking on a journey of self-discovery. Knowing what you need, and desire is the only way you can be sure to fulfill it. There are many ways to engage in self-discovery. Cherie often tells people, “My journey to becoming a professional coach was life altering. During coach training I received so 

---

## Page 22

 
22 
much coaching and had so many mindset shifts that by the time I was finished I was totally different. This is the power of coaching. I still utilize coaching and find it very useful to keep self-discovering and becoming the best version of myself possible.” Cherie, Michael, and Alex have all found the well-known book “The 5 Love Languages5” and its partner book focused on the business world “The 5 Language of Appreciation in the Workplace6” by Gary Smalley helpful in uncovering and discovering emotional communication preferences. These books outline five different ways people express and receive love, acceptance, and appreciation. Not only can you determine your own appreciation language, but you can also learn how to use it to connect with others to create the most meaningful and fulfilling relationships. Journaling is once again a useful tool for self-discovery, as is ensuring you listen to your heart. You need to discern what needs and wants are coming from your heart, and not from a head that’s been fed with societal messages its entire life. As noted earlier, what you need and what society says you should need are often two very different things. Don’t be ashamed of what you want — or who you are. Don’t judge yourself or your desires, labeling things as “good” or “bad.” Be easy on yourself. Be kind to yourself. Treat yourself the same way you would treat a friend since you are not likely to judge or belittle them for wanting or needing something that’s different or unique. A question that often arises here is how do you know if your desire is a heartfelt desire and not a vice? Let’s say you want to blow off steam gambling in Las Vegas on your next vacation, betting your entire paycheck for the thrill of it. How do you know if this is something you need to do to maintain a firm personal foundation or a vice you’re drawn into? One way to determine between a vice and a desire is to do a spot check by thinking long-term. For the long term, is betting your entire paycheck in Las Vegas something that is likely to end up helping or hurting you? Is this something you feel in control of and can plan without bringing harm to yourself, your profession, and your family or is it something that controls you that you will later regret? We are responsible for all our actions and the results they bring about, both externally and internally. If your actions are likely to cause hardship and grief for yourself or those around you, it may be wise to think twice about proceeding. Self-discovery also means living in alignment with your values, something you can only do if you know what they are. Self-discovery may sound like hard work, and it is. But it is nowhere near as difficult as living day after day, month after month, not knowing what you want and need, or even why you’re doing what you’re doing. It’s challenging to consistently grow and succeed if you’re not sure of your higher purpose. Once again, the benefits of all that hard work will be worth it. Michael says, “It unleashes an enormous amount of energy and flow when I’m in tune and in harmony with my higher purpose. There’s no internal resistance. I am swimming through  5  https://www.amazon.com/Love-Languages-Secret-that-Lasts-ebook/dp/B00OICLVBI/ref=sr_1_1?dchild=1&keywords=5+love+languages&qid=1633324878&sr=8-1  6  https://www.amazon.com/Languages-Appreciation-Workplace-Organizations-Encouraging-ebook/dp/B07F7NYY9P/ref=sr_1_2?dchild=1&keywords=appreciation+languages&qid=1633324934&sr=8-2 

---

## Page 23

 
23 
life, no longer running through rocks. I am passionate about what I do, and it’s a passion others can see and feel. It makes them excited, too.” Michael uses a technique called his personal reflection worksheet to monitor his state over time. He also uses it when building new relationships to give people an overview of who he is and how he operates.  His personal worksheet contains the following categories:  ● Style: Use this section to jot down your known personality traits and behaviors ● Values: Write down just a few things that you find the most important ● Growing edge: Try to describe the main behavior or emotional trait you would like to work on right now ● Kryptonite: Even Superman had a vulnerability ● Common emotional state: Simply take note of the way you usually feel ● Preferences for receiving assistance: Similar to knowing your appreciation language, figure out which kinds of assistance have proven most beneficial to you. ● Personality tests: We find it worthwhile to explore personality through a variety of personality tests Michael’s Personal Worksheet Example
