## Page 24

 
24 
In summary, to support organizational transformation, an Agile coach benefits from having a deep ‘root’ that keeps them always grounded. One of the greatest gifts that a coach can give to others is to not operate out of fear or frustration. Doing so requires an exquisite sense of self awareness and deep self-understanding as well as a profound ability to continually do one’s own work. We hope that some tools and approaches in this chapter will be helpful to you in becoming more connected to yourself. 
 Chapter 4: Developing Coaching Values  Developing and living your core values as an Agile coach is more important than certifications. Certificates generally indicate that you took a class and received some learning. Learning is important. What is more important is that you can integrate what you learned into the work you do. We are not discouraging certifications and continuous learning. We are discouraging taking class after class and never implementing the learning received. What we are talking about is building competency. Agile, as an industry and an approach to work, is much more of a “state” than it is a destination. It’s not just a way of improving efficiencies within an organization or streamlining communication between team members. Agile is, in fact, a way of being—and a dedication to the process of always looking for ways to improve yourself and those around you for the greater good. This means Agile coaches must be just as committed to that same learning process. Part of the competency of being a masterful coach knows the values you live by. We expect our clients to know and understand the values and principles of Agile and believe that if they are adopted the client will be successful. It is our opinion that Agile coaches should also have a set of values they live by that describe the belief system their coaching derives from. It sets an expectation for what the client will experience when engaging with the coach. And it gives the coach a foundation to work from and always return to when they get off base. The Importance of the Coach’s Value System One of the most under-discussed aspects of Agile coaching is the importance of personal values. This is the difference between “being” and “doing.”  Some coaches only focus on what they do in a client engagement: their value proposition, project goals, timeline, costs, etc. These include their competencies, skills, degrees, books they’ve read, courses they’ve taken, masterminds they’ve attended, and so on. And all these things define and “speak for” our skill level. However, they do not give very much insight into who we are as human beings.  

---

## Page 25

 
25 
The underlying qualities that do speak about our humanity are our core values—many of which are arguably just as important, if not more important than our core competencies. These are the fundamental habits, perspectives, traits, personality quirks, and soft skills we bring to every engagement. Cherie is sharing and describing her values here as a jumping off point to inspire you to reflect on your own value system around how you engage with the world as a coach. However, your values may look very different. Your coaching values are specific to you and not something anyone else can give you. Developing a set of core values isn’t meant to be an exercise to create a pretty pie chart that you put in a file and never see again. It is intended to be a reflective activity that grounds you in who you really are and who you want to be. Determining and committing to your coaching values can be a career impacting experience. Committing to these values that declare who you are as a coach can be a defining moment in developing your coaching mindset. For Cherie, this became a declaration to her clients that told them what to expect from a coaching engagement with her. The values became the mantra for how she embodied coaching. Cherie says, “I use my values to stay grounded and to have the courage to do the hard work. When I’m tempted to take over the change for the client, I reflect on my values of leaving no trace and empowering. When I get frustrated, I reflect on my values of doing no harm and seeing the client as competent and whole. When I have a challenging conversation, I reflect on truth telling and being the mirror. When I’m tired, and I want to give up, I reflect on my values of engaged neutrality and letting passion guide. These values always remind me of who I am and how I do this work.”    
 


---

## Page 26

 
26 
 Leave No Trace: This means that when I work with an organization, though the impacts of my presence can be seen in the outcomes they achieve there isn’t a visible imprint of my presence that someone can come behind and say, “Cherie Silas must have been the coach here because she always …” My goal is to honor the client and partner with them to design the right solutions for them. Not to implement some framework that might one day make me famous.  One way that I prevent leaving a trace is to refrain from implementing “best practices” or “common practices” that are part of a checklist for what each client should adopt if they want to “do Agile.” Instead, I honor each client by finding out what their specific problems are and by working with them to discover how they can best change and improve. I believe that we cannot apply simple solutions like “best practices” to complex problems. When we have the type of complex problems that show up in large systems, the best way to approach them is through experimentation and learning through trial and error. Only the client understands their situation well enough to truly design the best experiments and decide which new ways of working they want to adopt. If I engage in honoring this value, the client’s imprint and not mine that remains in the organization long after I have faded away. My role as the coach is always to facilitate discovery towards success rather than implement prescriptive ways of working.  Do No Harm This value means that when I am finished working with a client, they should be better off because of their experience working with me. I strive to ensure that people are better when I leave than they were when I first encountered them. This means that I must be invested in their success and not focused on my own. If I push my agenda on people, I will also push them to make decisions that may not be right for them. To force my agenda would be to do harm.  My goal is to honor the client’s expertise in their organization and circumstances and be a partner that provokes them to flourish. Another way that I do no harm is to always remember the power I hold in my professional opinions. Yes, there are times I give my professional opinion, but I always communicate that these are just that — my opinions. These opinions may not perfectly fit within the context of their organizational culture, so the client must take control of their destiny. There are no generic right answers to every problem because the truth is, it depends on all the details I can’t possibly know. Because of this, I am careful to not throw around opinions that make me look like an expert. Instead, I ask questions to help both me and the client to understand more about the situation and the outcomes they hope to achieve. I ask questions that will help them understand the impacts of the decisions they make. And when I do give a professional opinion, I do it in a way that keeps the client in charge of their destiny. I can’t change their company for them. Nor do I want that responsibility. If I were to start 

---

## Page 27

 
27 
taking ownership of their changes, I would surely harm the organization because I don’t have to live with the consequences of those decisions. My preference is to give information that helps inform the client’s decision and then support them in the decisions they make. If they decide against my recommendation, I must trust that this is the right decision for them at this point in history. At some point, if it becomes apparent to them that they have made a wrong decision, I partner with them to shift direction. Competent and Whole Seeing my clients as competent and whole is what enables me to be in full partnership for their well-being and success and neutral when it comes to how they accomplish their goals. During every moment I work with any client I must be able to see them as competent and think, “They can knock it out of the ballpark! They have the capacity for greatness.” They may not have the same skills set as me and there may be some knowledge or experience, I have that is different from theirs; but, this doesn’t mean they are not competent. Rather, it means that we make a great partnership because we both bring different strengths to the relationship to work with.  I must always consciously believe that my clients are not broken so I am not tempted to fix them with my own solutions. Even if the systems they set up in their company seem strange to me, it doesn’t mean I need to fix them. My role is to partner with them. I’m not above them, and I’m not beneath them. I walk alongside them in partnership, and we figure out together what they would like to change or improve and design experiments to find the right answers. On occasion, when I find myself wanting to fix or save a client from their challenges, this is a big red flag for me that I am not holding neutrality in our partnership. When this happens, I must self-reflect and make the proper adjustments to see my client from the right perspective. If I cannot see my client as whole, complete, competent, and unbroken then I have an ethical obligation to recuse myself from being their coach and to recommend a more suitable partner. Be The Mirror As a coach, it is not my role to assess and determine what’s broken in my client’s world. It is also not my place to decide what they need to fix or how. My role is to understand the goals they want to achieve and share my observations, intuitions, concerns, and what I notice from a systemic perspective. I am not able to make judgments on these things or to tell them if or how they should change them. I am there to reflect to them what may be obvious but also needs to be made transparent. This allows them to determine if they want a change so we can work together to determine how they want to bring that change into reality.  One example of how I become a mirror is to reflect to my client how it tells me it sees itself. When I start work with an organization, and at periodic intervals during the relationship, I survey the organization to understand how it views itself. I meet with people from all roles, all levels, and functional areas to hear what themes arise from them about what they are doing well and about things they can focus on for the change to be more successful. This is not an assessment where I am determining if they are “doing Agile” correctly. This is an exercise on hearing how 

---

## Page 28

 
28 
the system sees itself. I do this by listening to hear the recurring themes that arise. Afterward, I reflect on these themes back to the organization to see if what I heard resonates with them. If it does not, we talk about those gaps and identify if there is a misalignment in parts of the organization or if I understood incorrectly. We use the output of this exercise for the organization to select improvement items and to create a plan for how it wants to move forward as we partner to achieve the success, they design for themselves. TruthTeller This is my commitment to myself and to my clients that reminds me to courageously say what needs to be said when no one else will. When I first engage with a client, we have a conversation about how the client, and I will work together. I generally ask questions like, “When I have an observation that might be a bit hard to hear, what is the best way to approach you with that information?” and “How challenging would you like me to be during our work together?” My experience has shown me that no one wants to tell the leader a truth that might upset them. However, not having the person in your life who will speak frankly and who cares enough to risk the relationship for your best interest can stagnate your ability to be successful and to grow.  People need this information to understand what’s holding them back. As their partner, I commit to always speak the truth, even when it’s not the popular thing to say. When people hire me as a coach, they can be sure that they will hear the truth spoken with professionalism and kindness and I won’t back down from hard conversations. The point here is not to win or to have an argument about how I think the client needs to change. The point is that I am in partnership with them to help them achieve success the way they define it. If I am in partnership, I will not withhold observations that bring transparency to process, behaviors, actions, inactions, conflicts, etc., happening in the system. It is only with transparency that a system can inspect and adapt properly. Without true transparency they can’t inspect what needs to change, so the adaptations made will never truly solve the problems manifesting in the system. By modeling courageous conversations that make truth visible, the organization can learn the benefits of transparency, inspection, and adaptation. Engaged Neutrality As a coach, I must help my clients focus on their definition of success. I must believe in them, be in their corner fighting for them, celebrate with them, and be sincerely invested in their future. At the same time, I must let them be in control of the path to get there. I will walk with them on the path and offer challenging support to the decisions they make. I will design experiments with them and give them my perspective to consider. But what I will not do is step out of a neutral position when it comes to where they want to go and how they will get there. These choices are theirs. As a partner, I respect their ability to be the expert in their own life and company, so I will not try to force my ideas or agendas on them. My commitment to my clients is to be fully engaged in their success while maintaining a neutral stance. I uphold their goals, dreams, values, and reflect to them what I see and hear. But I don’t try to influence them to follow my ideas or vision of what success could be for them. When they 

---

## Page 29

 
29 
are not acting on the decisions they make or improvements they commit to making I help them get perspective on the why. I don’t “hold them accountable” for not doing what they said they would do. Instead, I probe into how they are getting in their own way. I help them to re-envision where they want to go and how they want to get there. I partner with them to design a more suitable accountability structure where they can be responsible to enforce their behaviors. It is not my role to hold them accountable. It is my role to hold accountability up to them for them to examine and determine the next steps.  Empower This value causes me to remember that I will not allow a client to become dependent on me. I refuse to be in a situation where people think they need my permission to act, or they need me to decide for them. I don’t want that kind of power in people’s lives. And that’s not a sustainable model. I want to leave behind a sustainable culture and way of working so that people can continue moving forward in the direction we started long after we stop working together. Coaching should leave people empowered with a new way of thinking, a new way of sorting through decisions, and a capacity to see things from new perspectives. If I am working at my best with my clients, they will learn how to work independently of me, so their accomplishments do not roll back when I’m no longer in the picture. My goal from the beginning must always be how they will operate when we have finished our engagement. I want to leave a legacy of independent success that they own and can take credit for creating. Let Passion Guide This value focuses on me and how I make decisions about what work I will say yes to and what work I will say no to. It is important to me that I only do work that brings me personal fulfillment, passion, and energy. If I take an assignment that I am not passionate about I will not be fully invested in the work. My heart will not be in it, and I’ll be miserable the whole time I’m working with the organization. When my full passion is not engaged, I am not the best coach I can be for my clients and that’s not the coach I want to be. This means that I will not accept work just because it is paying work. I must resist the temptation to allow worry or fear of not making it financially drive me to accept work that isn’t the right fit. Instead, I must trust that if this is the work I am supposed to be doing, and I’m making an impact then the right work will come, and I will be financially stable. If I take a client because I need money, but it’s not the right fit, this violates my ethical standard because if I do this, I will enter the relationship for my own needs and not serve the client’s needs. Benefits of Defining Coaching Values These are Cherie’s core values, and we fully recognize that each of these will not resonate with everyone. Rather than adopting her core values, we encourage all coaches to think about their own core values, write them down, and refine their list over time. Understanding who you are as a coach and how you want to show up is more valuable than getting another certification, reading another Agile coaching book, taking another training course, or going to another 

---

## Page 30

 
30 
conference. It is great to add to your knowledge and gain new skills, but not at the expense of avoiding the most important part: discovering and defining what makes you, you. After all, clients don’t hire your certifications, tools, and knowledge. They hire you.  Here are some of the benefits that come from the hard work of defining your core values as an Agile coach. They will: ● Help guide client engagements ● Help you to be intentional about who you are when you interact with clients ● They serve as a grounding point for when you get off-center ● Allow you to make more self-aware decisions ● Reveal who you should work with—and who you enjoy working with the most ● Encourage and foster more meaningful collaboration ● Create consistency in how you interact with clients and increases trust ● Increase your credibility as a coach because you live what you speak ● Improve your coaching presence and the impact of your presence on your clients  Take some time to think about your values concerning how you serve clients. Make them conscious and explicit. We all inherently have our own set of values. We often aren’t aware of the values we adhere to because we haven’t reflected enough on them to put them into words. Take time to reflect, write them down and say them out loud. It may take a few weeks or even months to fully solidify the values you hold about coaching. Some values will change and emerge over time. It’s important to capture them and consciously bring them to the forefront. Once you understand the value system that drives your work, you can intentionally make choices that don’t violate them. This will give you a more fulfilled career and make you a better coach for your clients. There’s a quote with unsure origin that says, “The way you do one thing is the way you do everything.” If you can’t take the time to pinpoint the underlying values that make you a trustworthy and credible coach, then what does that say about your ability to help your clients do the same? You will have clients that need to understand and clarify their personal values, team values, organizational values. If you have done the work for yourself, you will be better able to partner with them. Here’s a simple method to discover your core values: First, imagine a time when you felt fulfilled and accomplished. Second, imagine a time when you were unfulfilled and unproductive. Without overcomplicating things, these two scenarios can become the variables you always want to move toward and against. What circumstances make you feel fulfilled? How are you when you feel accomplished? What do you want? What environment brings you the most joy, and why? Similarly, what are the circumstances when you feel unfulfilled? What is usually happening when you feel unproductive? What things frustrate you? What will you not tolerate from yourself and others? What situations do you not want to relive? 

---

## Page 31

 
31 
The answers to these questions and others become a blueprint you can follow to align yourself with your most fulfilling and honest work. This investigation can help you define what makes you feel alive and whole—and what causes you stress, sparks conflict, and ultimately leads to an unfulfilling situation. This personal discovery will enable you to say to your potential clients and employers, “This is who I am and how I work with clients, and I want us to both be clear on what a relationship will be like between us.”  Personal Accountability  Now, once you’ve settled on your core values, here’s how to keep yourself accountable: Share your core values with your clients, so they know who to expect to show up as their coach. Michael likes to share his values in proposals, so they are clear up front. Periodically throughout an engagement, take some time to think about how well your core values are being displayed in the work you are doing. Cherie says, “If I’m feeling particularly grumpy or frustrated by an engagement, I’ll ask myself specifically whether I am honoring my core values or not. ‘Am I censoring myself? Am I not being a truth-teller? Do I feel like I have to be a different version of myself in this environment?’ In general, when I’m not happy working with a client, after reflecting a bit, I realize that there is some violation of my core values happening. This is a time when seeking coaching supervision can be extremely helpful.” At the same time, when a client asks, “Do you want to extend our contract and continue working together,” this inner-questioning process is just as important. “Have I been able to be my true self? Have I been able to be true to my values? Am I enjoying the work? Do I believe there is still more impact I can make?”  Cherie shares, “At times, I have chosen not to continue working with a client simply because the arrangement did not align with my core values and what, and I knew I would not be fulfilled doing the work.” Don’t wait until later in your career to begin taking your core values into account. When you are first starting in the business, there is a lot of focus on just landing clients. You are focused on generating new business and proving that you are competent at the work you’ve been hired to do. If we aren’t intentional, we will lose ourselves in the shuffle. We’ll take on engagements only for the money or experience, and we’ll be miserable in the process. Our mental health will suffer, our relationships outside the office will suffer, and our physical health may be impacted. It’s worth it to consider the contracts you take closely. Make sure they work for you and not just for the client. To be clear, we are not saying that hard skills and core competencies should not be a priority. What we are saying is that your competencies and values should be pursued on parallel paths. You are not just on a path of career advancement, but also one of self-discovery. “I can say, for 

---

## Page 32

 
32 
myself, my values have changed over the course of my career as an Agile coach. I value different things today than I did five years ago, or a decade ago. And so, as time goes on, it’s my responsibility to continue checking in with myself and redefining my core values as a professional. This allows me to make more conscious decisions about whom I want to work with, and why,” says Cherie. If you don’t understand your values, you may very well spend years and years at odds with yourself.  For example, in many corporate environments, leaders understand that the way to success is “pushing” people—pushing, pushing, pushing. Cherie reflects, “I don’t enjoy doing that, nor do I believe it leads to the most effective result. So even though there are a lot of clients who would be willing to pay me lots of money to push, push, push, those engagements would force me to abandon one of my core values. I would be at war with myself. And that internal friction would end up causing problems for me, and the client. In every new client relationship, my primary goal is to ensure the work I am doing is being done alongside these core values. Otherwise, I am letting the client, and myself down. 
Chapter 5: Engaged Neutrality This chapter dives into the concept of neutrality, exploring its nuances and its importance as part of the underlying foundation for any successful coaching relationship. Neutrality refers to the ability to remain objective, without promoting your own viewpoints, or pushing your opinions on the client. Neutrality in coaching does not mean communicating in robotic, emotionless, or monotone methods. Neutrality isn’t about being disconnected from the client. It’s about being unattached to your own opinions about how the client determines they want to move forward. When you slip out of neutrality, you are likely slipping out of coaching mode. Sometimes that means you are taking on the role of a mentor or consultant. You might even be stepping into the role of the Agile police. As an Agile coach, it’s fine to intentionally step over into these other roles, however when working from a coaching stance, you should hold your opinions lightly. Give the client your perspective and then let them decide what’s best. If a coach remains neutral, does that mean they don’t care? No. There is a big difference between neutrality and apathy. A state of neutrality allows you to be okay with whatever direction the client decides to go. You care about a client’s success, but you don’t force them into a specific direction for achieving it. Neutrality is not about being lax or indifferent, either. We need to be as fully invested as our client, but we don’t take ownership for their change. We hold the process — the client owns the content. They will live with the outcome of their decisions, we won’t. Whatever course they want to take or the outcome they desire is what matters, not the course we think they should take or the outcome we would desire. It’s imperative for coaches to take a neutral stance on the methods your client uses to achieve success on their goals. You want to acknowledge the work they are doing to move forward, and you want to do so without approving or disapproving of the steps they decide to take. Working in 

---

## Page 33

 
33 
neutrality means that you refrain from judgment and instead use curiosity. Judgment isn’t only in a negative sense. We can judge something to be a good idea or a good next step. The problem with judging things as good (or bad) is that it exerts influence over the client. It puts the coach in a ranked position over the client. This might cause the client to respond to our reaction as advice to take a specific action. If we influence the client to take specific actions or decisions, we are taking responsibility for their change. For the client to make a sustainable move forward, it must be on their own terms. When working with teams/groups of people neutrality means that we must not give an impression of taking sides or picking one idea over another. We can’t react to one idea saying, “That’s a great idea!” and not acknowledge others the same way, or we will send the message that we have selected one idea to be more viable than the others. What we can do instead is to acknowledge that the idea has been given to the group and ask the group for more ideas or for their reaction to the idea. When working with organizations it is sometimes particularly hard to maintain neutrality and not take sides. Keep in mind that when we hear one person tell their side of the story, we can become biased. We can also be triangulated in the middle of someone else’s conflict. It’s important for us to show empathy for what people are experiencing. However, showing sympathy by joining sides with them can create more harm than good. Cherie recalls, “I can remember working in an organization where staff members were telling me stories about how a particular manager was acting. I got really frustrated with the manager’s described behavior and started getting angry. I can remember wanting to yell at them and say, ‘What are you doing?’ Then, I realized that I had moved far from neutrality. I had chosen sides instead of remaining in a neutral position that would have been more helpful for the staff and manager to resolve the conflict. I moved into the savior role and wanted to tell the manager off. I also judged the manager based upon third party information without even speaking to him to understand his perspective. And I moved into a superior position thinking that I had authority to force the manager to change the behavior. It was this incident that helped me to realize that if I truly saw my clients as competent, creative, resourceful, and unbroken I would get curious instead of angry in situations like this.” If neutrality sounds like a challenging thing to achieve, that’s because it can be. The human brain is wired to make automatic judgments about other people’s behaviors. That’s how we quickly assess a situation without spending exorbitant amounts of time or energy trying to understand everything we encounter. But when you’re in the role of the coach, you want to use self-management to short-circuit that automatic judgment process. Instead of automatically labeling something the client says or decides as “good” or “bad,” take a neutral stance. It is not the coach’s role to decide if what the client says is good or bad. From the coach’s perspective, everything should be simply data for the client to work with to gain more awareness. 

---

## Page 34

 
34 
Maintaining neutrality gets easier the more you pay conscious attention to your thought process. Yet it’s not always something that can be achieved. If you’re not able to maintain neutrality regarding your client, it may be a good time to work with a coaching supervisor to discuss the work you are doing to figure out why. A supervisor can help you to reflect on specific client cases that are challenging, so you can get new perspectives on how to interact more effectively. Michael recalls, “I had an individual client who reached out for coaching because he wanted to lose an extraordinary amount of weight in a rapid-fire amount of time, something like 30 pounds in a month. We spent about an hour exploring the possible actions the client could take to achieve this goal. At the end of our in-depth exploration, I was not able to be neutral with respect to what the client was planning to do to lose that amount of weight so quickly. So, I had to recommend that he work with a different coach.” Not all cases of lost neutrality necessarily require terminating the relationship. It depends on the specific situation and if you’re able to regain and maintain neutrality going forward. In the weight-loss case, where Michael knew deep in his soul, he’d be unable to move forward comfortably with the client’s way of approaching success, a break was required. But in another case, when Michael found himself slipping out of neutrality, he was able to amend the situation, bounce back to neutrality, and continue the coaching relationship. “I recall one situation where a manager was not supporting the change work that was being done in the organization she led. She was not participating and taking ownership of the changes the organization wanted to see, and she was not offering support the teams needed to be successful. In my emotionally charged state, I remember saying: ‘The manager has abandoned her team.’ This way of thinking and judging the client is not aligned with a coaching mindset. The judgmental statement was an outward sign that I was not maintaining my neutrality. I was picking sides. I decided that the team was a victim, and the manager was a villain. This is not neutrality, and it is not helpful to the progress of the organization. While I was not able to maintain neutrality at that moment, I was able to get it back. I recognized the state I was in, and I reached out for help. Getting support from other coaches helped me regain a state of neutrality,” recalls Michael. Engaged Neutrality Being neutral doesn’t mean that you should be idle. Contrarily, your role is specifically to help the client to gain more awareness about multiple perspectives, options, and even risks associated with the ideas they bring forward. If a coach has opinions, does being neutral mean they can’t express them? While we certainly don’t want to force our own opinions or agenda on the client, we may share them if it feels right. We might have opinions, expertise, or experience that we add to the conversation in a brainstorming style, but it’s up to the client to decide the actions they take. Opinions can be expressed in a way that presents them as information, without attempting to sway the client’s own opinion. 

---

## Page 35

 
35 
We like to think of neutrality in terms of “engaged neutrality.” When working with clients, we should be fully in partnership with them and invested in their success. Being in full partnership means that we bring our whole selves to the relationship. We aren’t required to interact with our clients in monotone voices or ask only open-ended questions. There’s much more to coaching than that. As a partner in the relationship our full engagement shows up in multiple ways. Our personality comes through the conversation which helps the client build a personal trusted relationship with us. When the client shows emotion, we shouldn’t “maintain neutral tones.” We should match that energy and experience it with the client. Then, if that state is not helpful for the client, we can shift our energy, so the client can follow us to move into a more useful state. Being in engaged neutrality means that we are fully listening to the client, and we are mentally stepping back, so we can see the bigger picture and take note of what it looks like from a higher viewpoint. Are you noticing trends or behaviors in the client that might be useful for them to explore? If so, share that information with the client and let them decide what to do with it. Being engaged is noticing and sharing — neutrality is allowing the client the power to decide what to do with the information. Engaged neutrality means taking the risk of challenging the client’s thinking. Do you get the sense that the client is holding back? Tell them what you are sensing. Does it sound like the client is playing it safe? Tell them — challenge them to think outside the box. Is the client expressing limiting beliefs that seem to be holding them back? Challenge the beliefs. Ask them how they can look at the situation from a different perspective. Does it sound like they are making excuses for not doing what they know needs to be done? Tell them and ask them what’s happening. Engaged neutrality doesn’t mean standing by and watching while your client walks blindly off a cliff. Engaged means shouting, “Hey! That’s a cliff!” Especially in Agile coaching, we have a responsibility to our clients to bring our experience and expertise to the table to give them more information to make educated decisions. This doesn’t mean telling them what they must do. However, it does sometimes mean that you will tell them what you know from experience and ask them how they will ensure that the same issues don’t go unaddressed in their organization. When they say they are going to make decision X, and you get an uncomfortable feeling because you’ve seen that end up in disaster in the past, you have a responsibility to use your expertise. Let your expertise inform you on how to ask probing questions that cause the client to think about their situation in ways they didn’t know to think. This is being engaged. Then, after exploring the decision thoroughly with the client, neutrality is not trying to force them to change their mind because of your experiences. Trust the client to know their domain much better than you. Just because it hasn’t worked in your experience doesn’t mean it won’t work in this experience. Every environment is different and functions differently. What’s perfect in one place may be a complete failure somewhere else. Can coaches remain neutral while still suggesting methods to use to achieve an outcome? As with everything in Agile, the answer is, “It depends.” If you find yourself “suggesting” anything when the client has not specifically asked you for suggestions, you have already lost your 

---

## Page 36

 
36 
neutrality. That doesn’t mean that at times you can’t provide examples of methods as information for the client to consider. Especially if they ask for them. Remember, it can be extremely frustrating to a client when they say, “How would you do this?” for you to answer, “How would you do this?” In fact, when leading a coaching organization at a large Fortune 500 company, this was the number one complaint about Agile coaches Cherie received from business units. “They never answer me! They just ask me what I would do!” Well folks, that’s not skillful coaching. There’s a skillful balance between making contributions to the pool of information and being afraid to interact with the client’s request for help. Cherie shares, “Generally I handle these requests for my experiences or expertise in a few different ways depending on the presenting circumstances. First, I remember that most of the time when people say, “What do you think?” it’s an unconscious stall tactic to give themselves time to think. So, I watch them. If they are thinking, then I remain silent while they think and start talking again. It wasn’t a real request for input. Second, if they are asking me something like, “What would you do?” I generally don’t have enough information to tell them what I would do. So, I think for a moment, then I say something like, “Well, tell me about this…” (notice I didn’t ask them, “Well what would you do?”) This way, my question is seen as genuine engagement. If they truly need advice/mentoring, then more information is needed. Most of the time, getting them to think in a new direction results in them being able to keep moving forward, and we don’t come back to hearing what I would do. Third, when the client really does need some ideas, I give them very brief thoughts with no detail and follow with a question like, “How would something like this work in your organization?” or I might ask them, “What ideas do you get from this?” Remember, even if the client asks for your expertise or experience, and you give it, hold it loosely. Clients don’t need to execute on your examples. Agile coaches will often be called upon to give consulting advice. This is part of the job and not off limits. However, when giving your professional opinion to the client it's important to remember that the client knows their domain much better than you. This is their company — not yours. Tell them what your opinion is. Ask them for their thoughts on how this might work in their company. Every professional opinion the Agile coach has will not be adopted by the client. And that’s okay. Partner with the client to adopt the things they are ready to move forward with and to inspect and adapt in areas where the client is not yet ready or able to move forward. Don’t forget to take into consideration that every client system has its own sets of complications. Just because something needs to change for ultimate success, it doesn’t mean that the client has the power to change it at this moment. To the coach, it might seem like one simple change. However, to the client that one simple change might represent hundreds of changes with multiple impediments to address. Clients need to be strategic. Agile for Agile’s sake is an unacceptable reason for a coach to suggest change. There must be a viable business reason why change is being adopted.  Coaching Presence and Neutrality Coaching presence refers to the ability to be fully conscious and create a spontaneous relationship with the client. Coaching presence is an interesting competency. It’s what you bring to the conversation and the relationship. When people interact with you, they need to feel 

---

## Page 37

 
37 
honored and respected. Engaged neutrality is key to developing a safe, trusted relationship with the client that enables them to be vulnerable enough to discuss challenges and ideas freely. Part of coaching presence is being observant which means you’re going to notice things like a little smile or a light in the client’s eye when they mention something. Or you may note they are a little tense, stressed or repeating phrases like “I have to.” Neutrality allows you to curiously ask what their reaction is all about instead of automatically coming to a subjective conclusion that may or may not be correct. Coaching presence also requires being empathetic. This means you can relate to mentally take on the client’s perspective and understand how their experience is valid for them. This gives them permission to feel what they’re feeling without judgment. Neutrality allows you to relate to what they’re experiencing without tainting it with your opinion. You don’t have to agree with their viewpoint. Simply being able to understand and acknowledge their viewpoint is critical to the relationship. Asking reflective and responsive questions is dependent upon engaged neutrality because it allows you to maintain the client’s rhythm without trying to direct or manipulate the conversation. You and the client can dance with the moment, with the conversation flowing in a natural rhythm with the client driving. Neutrality allows you to remain curious about what you and the client are going to discover in your conversation. You’re not asking questions that are colored by your judgment or leading in a specific direction you feel the discussion should take. The coach is free to make contributions to the conversation, but they also need to invite the client to respond any way the client wants. This means not being offended if a client rejects methods, ideas or even observations you bring up. You can mention an observation you have, such as a client reacting to something with what appears to be fear. But you want to ask the client if that observation is accurate. “What’s your perspective on that? What comes to mind when you hear me say that?” You may be totally off-base. Even if your observation is on target, and fear is part of the situation, it’s not something you want to focus on unless the client chooses that focus. If a client doesn’t want to discuss a certain issue, a seasoned coach will know to leave it alone, let it go. Not return to it again and again just because the coach thinks it needs to be discussed. It only needs to be discussed if the client decides it needs to be discussed. Neutrality allows you to accept the client’s response, whatever it may be, without trying to prove to the client that you are correct. Coaching involves listening, paying attention, and asking questions — all while partnering with the client in the situation. You can help the client brainstorm possibilities about ways they might solve the problem and move forward, but you also want to draw some of those possibilities from the client. Part of the way a coach helps the client is to playback possibilities the client expressed. “I heard you mention three areas you want to focus on. Which one would you like to focus on first? How would you like to focus on it?”  Neutrality again allows you to offer the client power, choice, and ownership of the agenda. 

---

## Page 38

 
38 
Neutrality allows you to create space for the client to generate their own learning. You’re putting the information in the hands of the client. The client is the one who gets to decide what to do with that information. Remember that the client is completely competent. They can and need to do the learning in order to create sustainable results. What you’re learning about the client and what they’re learning about themselves may be different things. You don’t want to assume you are both learning the same things. Instead of saying something like, “I see that you’re learning XYZ,” you can say, “What are you learning about this?” Instead of saying something like, “Can you tell that to the manager?” you can say, “What can you do with this information? How can you use this information to make an impact?” Conclusion Hopefully by this point it is crystal clear why engaged neutrality is vital for a successful coaching relationship and what it looks like from a practical standpoint. The client is the one choosing the path they want to take and how they want to take it. They have honored you with the gift of partnering with them to witness their journey. Your job is to help the client think through choices and take ownership. You are there to support and partner with the client in a non-judgmental and helpful way, and that can only be done when you retain a state of neutrality. Chapter 6: Challenge and Support “I’m getting a bit frustrated with a trend I’m seeing in the Scrum Master and Agile community, and I think we need to do something about it. People seem to think that if they are coaching, they can only ask questions. Coaches feel helpless when their teams are heading off a cliff and think they can’t do anything about it. There’s been so much focus on asking powerful questions that coaching has been relegated to helplessly asking questions and leaves no room for challenge.” And with those words Allison Pollard7 summed up what many in the Agile community have experienced and frustrated with for years. These beliefs are a symptom of an oversimplification of what coaching with competency really means. It is also a confusion between coaching skills and coaching. There are many reasons this oversimplification has run rampant in the Agile community. Coaching programs that teach skills in a series of classes but require paying thousands of dollars for a tag-on program to learn how to competently use those skills. The fact that coaching is an unregulated industry, and anyone can call themselves a coach, with or without proper training. Even books like this one have contributed to the oversimplification because there is a huge gap between knowing about coaching and being able to apply that knowledge competently. We have seen repeatedly that people who have done some self-study can describe coaching perfectly. Then, when we have a coaching conversation what they do as a coach doesn’t mirror how they explain coaching. Why? Because coaching is a competency. To build competency, you need feedback from people with 
 7  https://www.linkedin.com/in/allisonpollard/  

---

## Page 39

 
39 
tested and verified competency in that area who can guide you on how to utilize the skills appropriately. Part of competency as a coach is understanding that coaching is much more than asking what people often call “powerful questions.” The term “powerful questions” is very misleading. It gives the impression that a question, in and of itself, is powerful. That’s simply not the case. What makes a question powerful is dependent upon many factors. Was the question constructed well? Short questions that are open-ended and not leading are well constructed questions. Was the question transactional or transformational? Transactional questions seek data transfer from the client to the coach. They look at what the client already knows or has experienced and asks the client to transfer that knowledge to the coach. These questions are rarely powerful. Transformational questions cause the client to think and discover. They look at what the client has not already experienced and may not have given thought to. They create learning for the client and have the potential to be very powerful. However, there’s more needed for a question to be powerful than just the question. Otherwise, lists of powerful questions would enable everyone to have consistently powerful coaching sessions. Was the question responsive? Questions that coaches ask should be in response to what the client is communicating. If the coach is listening actively, the questions asked should resonate with the client because they focus on what the client is focusing on. And lastly, did the coach get lucky? Yep — did the coach get lucky? “What does that mean?” you may be asking yourself. “Coaching powerfully isn’t about luck!” Well, to some extent, yes, it is. You can ask well-constructed, transformational, non-leading, questions that help the client think and still don’t land so powerfully that the client discovers the huge realization they needed to move forward. That powerful landing is a matter of asking a good question, that happened to catch the client at the exact moment in time when they were able to make a connection that changed everything. The coach never knows when or if that will happen and neither does the client. That’s where luck comes in. This doesn’t mean that competency isn’t important, contrarily, competency is of major importance. Without competency everything is just luck. Competency creates the conditions needed for the transformational work to happen. Beyond questions, there are many other competencies that make coaching masterful. For example, being courageous enough to make challenging observations that help the client see things they are refusing to see. Using your instincts and intuition to challenge the client to say what they are not saying. Sometimes, even confronting the client with the lies they are telling themselves. Skillful Agile coaches will often find the need to challenge Agile teams in the ways they are thinking and decisions they make. These types of challenges can only emerge in a relationship where there is a high level of vulnerability and trust in both the coach and the client. Building that type of rapport with a client quickly takes a high level of competence. Strong coaching relationships have “healthy tension” between the coach and client. The question is whether the coach competently can create an environment of healthy tension where the client feels both supported and challenged. Clients may operate from deeply embedded 

---

## Page 40

 
40 
assumptions that they believe to be true — even when there is no proof. Offering both challenge and support can enable the client to dig in deeper to validate or invalidate those assumptions. Michael once had a client who was a business owner. The business owner consistently complained, “My clients always pay me late.” This was frustrating him and causing him to be discontent with running his own company. Michael challenged the client’s discontent with questions about the financial impact of the late payments. When they sat down and looked at the books together the client realized that his assumption that clients were paying him late had no basis. With more coaching, the client realized that he had been basing the belief on events from previous years when he had challenges getting timely payments from his clients. He was assuming that the current clients were also not prompt with payment. This misinformation in his mind was subconsciously affecting his interactions, performance, and overall attitude. Challenging questions helped him break free of the misinformation. It got him to think outside his rigid beliefs. Once he got a new perspective, he was able to be more flexible in his thinking. The best way to think about this is through the Healthy Tension Dynamic Model© created by Cherie Silas of Tandem Coaching.   The model is created by placing different scenarios in four quadrants (see below for image). On the Y-axis is “challenge,” and on the X-axis is “support.”  
  


---

## Page 41

 
41 
As you look at this model you must take into consideration that every client will experience high challenge at a different level. You can’t use the same amount of high challenge with everyone — some need more challenge and some need less. What is key is that you balance the level of challenge and support. So, if you are going to be very supportive of a client, you must balance that high support with an appropriate level of challenge. Otherwise, your client will experience care and comfort but won’t necessarily feel a need to make changes that will bring them closer to their goal. If you offer a high level of challenge, you need to balance it with a high level of support, or the client will feel beat up and unmotivated.  To get the balance right you must learn your client. Utilizing other bodies of knowledge and assessments like Gary Chapman’s Appreciation Languages can help you identify the level of support vs challenge the client needs. Tools like this are also helpful when coaching teams to understand how the system interacts with itself. For example, a client that is primarily motivated by “words of affirmation” may experience a challenge very differently than someone who is primarily motivated by “quality time.” This doesn’t mean that the “words of affirmation” client can’t handle being challenged. It does mean that they get more from a friendly, caring challenge than an “in your face” direct challenge. What this means for the coach is that they must develop competency in how to challenge clients effectively. This model doesn't describe the intensity of a challenge — your client’s needs should determine intensity. What it does describe is how to balance challenge with support and what happens when there is an imbalance present. Understanding this model can help you diagnose your interactions with the client and make the shifts necessary to provide the best service to your clients. Low Challenge / Low Support  In the model, this quadrant is described as “not coaching.” Technically, it could be coaching but at a very low skill and competency level. When people coach from this quadrant you will likely see low support manifest as not listening to the client. The questions the coach asks may be way off base and show a clear lack of connection to what the client is saying. The coach may be using a formula or technique and walking the client through without paying attention to what the client needs. The coach may even be using a list of questions and following a checklist, so they may be asking well-formed questions that seem to be disconnected from what the client is saying.  Low challenge might manifest as asking closed and leading questions. Providing solutions for the client. Not making observations. Taking what the client says at face value and not digging in deeper. Focusing on getting to a list of next steps instead of focusing on the client. Not questioning assumptions or asking how the client will carry out their next steps. It could mean not pressing on the client’s limiting beliefs or assumptions. In short, low challenge coupled with low support isn’t very helpful for the client. 

---

## Page 42

 
42 
If you are just learning how to coach, this is a great place to start, but not a great place to stay. Focus on growing your competency and get feedback from a qualified mentor coach.  High Challenge / Low Support This dynamic is extremely common in corporate environments. A boss or a manager is telling an employee to perform a task (high challenge) but being unwilling to provide additional resources to aid in their request. For example, a manager may ask a team of developers to accelerate a project timeline, work longer hours, etc. But when the developers ask for assistance in offloading other work or being excused from time consuming meetings, those requests are not approved. This same dynamic can be in the Agile coaching relationship — especially when the coach is operating more from a consulting mindset than a coaching mindset. In this scenario, the coach might be assessing the actions of the individuals, teams, and organizations and giving opinions on whether those actions are appropriate or inappropriate.  For example, they might be saying things like, “Managers don’t belong at a daily Scrum.” If the coach does not offer the appropriate support of asking the manager what he/she is getting from the daily Scrum the coach creates resistance. The manager will not have their needs met and will push back against the coach who is offering a high level of challenge with low support. Telling a manager, “You need to back up and let the team decide what to do,” offers a high level of challenge and requires the balanced support of asking the manager what problem they are trying to solve and what concerns them. Without the balancing support, the coach destroys the trust needed to create the right level of empowerment and responsibility in the organization. The coach is dominating the organization and is not partnering with them to bring the change the organization desires. Instead, they are enforcing their own agenda. Another example of high challenge/ low support is with a client Cherie had. In her initial conversations with the client, he told her that he needed a strong level of challenge to get him moving. He told her not to hold back and to push him hard. He said he had been procrastinating for two years and wasn't moving forward to meeting his dream of getting his company to the next level. In one of their coaching sessions, the client appeared to be procrastinating and making excuses for why he wasn’t moving forward. He said that he only needed one more client to be self-supporting. He said that all he needed to do to land that one client was to send a few emails to people who were waiting for help. Towards the end of the conversation, Cherie pulled back and looked at the bigger picture, she made a very strong observation. “So, what you’re telling me is that there’s one copy-paste email that you need to send out to ten people. It will take less than 15 minutes. That’s all that’s standing between you and the life you want to live and yet, you won’t send the email. What’s the real reason you won’t take the next step?” Because of the client’s request to be extremely challenging, Cherie confronted him in a more forceful vocal pattern than she uses with other clients. The mistake Cherie made in this situation wasn’t the way she challenged the client. It was that she focused on his request for a high level of challenge and didn’t leverage enough support on that call to bring balance. The client waited 3 months before scheduling another call because he said he wanted to make progress before 

---

## Page 43

 
43 
they met again. While the story ended well and the client went on to make strong progress, Cherie still learned a lesson. It’s the coach’s responsibility to hold the balance.  Low Challenge / High Support  On the other side of the spectrum is the dynamic where the coach is offering sympathy for the client’s plight but does not exercise the courage to challenge them to take the next step. We call this “colluding.” It’s what friends do when one friend is venting, and the other says, “You’re right. Your boss is a moron,” but won’t challenge the individual to take any responsibility for their own behavior that contributed to the situation. As a result, one party receives a significant amount of support for their situation, but because nothing is challenging them to move forward, the cycle repeats itself endlessly. This same cycle happens in coaching when the coach steps out of the neutral position and takes sides with one team member over another. A team member complains about another team member who they conflict with, and the coach agrees that the other team member should have behaved differently. By doing this, the coach reinforces the behavior of the team member to pull the coach in rather than have a direct conversation with the other person. This creates a higher level of conflict on the team because the team member feels validated. While the coach is being highly supportive, they are not challenging the client to step up and have a healthy conflict to resolve the issue. The client is better served if the coach offers support by saying, “I’m sorry that you are having this conflict. It must be frustrating for you.” Then, offer a balanced challenge to the client by saying, “What can you change to get closer to what you want?” Another example of high support and low challenge that Cherie witnessed was at a company where there had been several acquisitions and people were holding alliances to their original companies. Team members were pulled from their original company’s product and put on a new product team for the acquisition company. The team reacted negatively to the change and were miserable with a new platform, a new manager, a new product, and a whole new work environment they didn’t want. Their performance was suffering. They had delivered very little over the past few months and really seemed to be in victim mode. Cherie sat in on a retrospective where the team focused on problems, they had no control over and talked a lot about how everything external to the team was holding them back. When it came time for them to decide on an improvement plan, the team selected one item: Keep being awesome. The Scrum Master congratulated them for all their hard work and told them how awesome they were and ended the session. This was a situation where the Scrum master was a very nice guy. He had been working with the team members for a few years and was also in a position where he was unhappy with the acquisition. Instead of challenging the team to look at things they had power to change or challenging them to take responsibility for their own actions and decisions, he was colluding with them to remain in victim mode. His high level of support and low level of challenge enabled the team to keep spiraling instead of picking up the pieces and moving forward. The team is better 

---

## Page 44

 
44 
served if the Scrum Master challenges them to describe what they are doing that makes them so awesome. He could challenge them by asking what value the customers and stakeholders are receiving from the current level of awesomeness.  He could even challenge them by “calling BS” on their “keep being awesome” plan and asking how that is helping them. High Challenge / High Support This quadrant is where the most effective coaching happens. It has support to make the client feel safe, listened to, seen, and encouraged. And it has an equal amount of challenge to push the client to boundaries, disrupt their thinking, and open new perspectives that will keep them moving forward. This balance of challenge and support will show up in different ways depending on the coach and the client. Cherie worked for a team once that wasn’t delivering. When she was assigned to the team, twelve weeks had gone by, and they had only delivered a few stories and no complete features. Cherie observed for an entire sprint before deciding what to do. She noticed that work was backing up at the point where it went into testing. The person doing QA reported each stand-up that he had 1-2 hours left on a certain task, and the task stayed in that state for the entire sprint even though he said he was working on it every day and almost done. There was one newer developer on the team who was very focused on making improvements and getting the team moving forward. He asked if the QA person needed help and was told that he didn’t because he was almost done. During the second sprint Cherie asked if she could do a visual radar with data that might help the team start to see patterns and make more educated decisions about how much work to bring into the sprint. She also wanted the team and product owner to have information that told them if they should inform stakeholders that the work, they promised in the sprint was not going to be delivered. The team agreed to the radar. Cherie made a radar on a flip chart that showed the two weeks of the sprint and for each day there was a red/yellow/green line with a scale of 1-10. Every day at stand-up Cherie asked how confident the team was that they would finish all the work they took into the sprint. Each day they said they were confident. Cherie was able to see from the progress that the work wasn’t likely to get completed and couldn’t understand what was making the team keep declaring confidence. Each day around 3pm Cherie would do a pulse check with the team and ask them to go to the radar and put a dot by the number that represented their level of confidence that all the work committed to in the sprint would be completed.  Around the 5th day, shortly before 3pm the newer developer walked over to the QA person’s desk and said, “Look, in a few minutes she (pointing to Cherie) is going to ask us how confident we are that the work of the sprint is going to be complete. I’m going to lie and say I’m fully confident, and I have no idea where you are or what you are doing, but I’m sick of lying.” The two team members argued while Cherie stayed in her seat trying to hide her joy that they were arguing. After a few minutes they identified what was happening that was causing the QA person to fall behind and worked out a solution to resolve it and got a commitment for how the 

---

## Page 45

 
45 
work would be reflected on the task board, so everyone could see the progress. They also made an agreement that if anyone on the team was stuck on a task for more than an hour, they would ask for a second pair of eyes on the work. In this scenario Cherie supported the team by taking time to understand their problem, withholding judgment, and giving the team the power to make decisions. She focused on the problem they wanted to solve and utilized tools and techniques to give them the information they needed to become more aware of what was really happening. She challenged the team by not interfering with the long overdue argument. She also challenged them by uncovering the problem and making it highly visible. She applied pressure by asking the daily question, "How confident are you?" consistently and making it more and more uncomfortable to keep up the facade that all was fine. And she challenged them by not solving the problem for them. Ultimately, the core problem on the team was that they were not collaborating and not being transparent when it came to how much work they could handle. It went way beyond the obvious symptom of the QA person not finishing work. The team was outwardly friendly, inwardly boiling, and refusing to go through the storming period that would enable them to combine ideas and become higher performing. By creating an environment where the team felt safe to have conflict, then applying challenging pressure, Cherie was able to help the team break the barriers and move forward. Eventually they became one of the most respected teams in the organization. 
    

---

Part 2: Getting Started

## Page 47

Chapter 7: Business Development is for Everyone  We suspect that many people who are reading this book to grow their skills as an enterprise Agile coach fall into a few categories: Internal FTE coaches, Sub-Contract Internal Coaches, and Independent Direct Contract Internal Coaches. Regardless of how you come to work with an organization, one thing remains constant. What you do before jumping in matters!  Discussions with Agile coaches through our years of mentoring others tells us that Agile coaches have a few common recurring challenges when it comes to engaging with new clients. First, taking contract or FTE engagements that sound like a great opportunity only to get in and find out that the client and the coach are very far out of alignment on what needs to be done. Second, taking sub-contracting roles with a staffing agency and finding out after getting on site that the coach’s hands are tied because what the agency sold the client is far from what the Agile coach believes should happen. And the coach doesn’t have access to the decision makers or the power to influence change at the levels where the impediments reside. Third, figuring out how to find new clients as an independent Agile coach.  We also suspect that many who are reading this book will aspire developing their career through a higher-level certification in Agile coaching such as the Scrum Alliance, Certified Enterprise, or Certified Team Coach designations. Many of the baseline aspects of what Scrum Alliance is looking for in candidates of these guide level certifications is what coaches need to really develop their coaching competency and business. As you’ll see in this chapter, getting exposure to potential clients takes a lot of up-front work and our goal is to share some of our experiences and techniques to help you.   Exposure isn’t the only important aspect of business. It is also important to find the right clients and to make sure that you are clear on what you’re getting into. That’s where having a pre-engagement approach comes into the picture. Whether you’re new to the Agile coaching scene and working as a full-time employee or looking to grow your existing practice, a pre-engagement approach can help ensure all new engagements are as successful and satisfying as possible. This chapter fills you in on some ways to find and take on new Agile coaching engagements, complete with a rundown on:  ● Business development activities ● Determining a correct fit  ● Finding out what the client really wants and needs ● Getting clear on the terms of the engagement  Business Development  Perhaps you’ve decided that you are going to go independent, and you’ve created a great website to sell your services and training products to potential customers. It’s possible that you’ve realized that while setting up a website is a great idea, it doesn’t create an unending 

---

## Page 48

 
48 
stream of clients. A website is simply a landing place where your business development activities drive potential customers to get a better understanding of who you are and what you have to offer.   Michael says, “Your website is not just a business card on the internet. You need to pay attention to it and refresh it on a regular basis. You need to update it with your speaking engagements, new services and other new content that shows you’re an active participant in the industry.”  The challenge is driving traffic to your website and to you as a provider of coaching services. That’s only going to happen if you engage in the art of business development. Business development involves all the activities, ideas and initiatives required for creating or improving a business. These activities take time, effort, and money. There are experts who can help you with a business development plan for your company and they know more about driving sales than we do. And there are lots of things you can begin doing today to get you started.  You have a generous selection of ideas and helpful activities to choose from when it comes to establishing or growing your Agile coaching practice. Regardless of which activities you choose, the key is consistency. For clients to engage you, they have to know who you are. Becoming consistently visible is incredibly important for getting business. Here are a few areas that successful Agile coaches, trainers, and consultants engage to create strong networks and visibility in the Agile community.  Agile Community Involvement, Participation, and Thought Leadership  Some Agile coaches get a lot of business from other Agile coaches, particularly when existing clients of those coaches need a specialty that another coach can provide. Michael says, “Let’s say a coach comes to me and tells me they specialize in Upstream Kanban. I will keep them in mind and pass along their name to clients who ask, as I’m not an expert in that area.” Getting involved in the Agile community enables you to learn from the Agile community and to connect to others.   Beyond learning, conferences are great opportunities to meet new people and engage with community leaders in meaningful ways. Events in the Agile community range from free local or virtual events to in person major conferences around the world. There’s always something available in your time zone and budget if you know what to look for. Some examples of events that are great for learning and building strong networks of people with a wide range of interests and experiences are below.   ● Major conferences held by organizations like Scrum Alliance, Agile Alliance, ICF, EMCC. These major conferences often involve domestic and international travel and can be costly. They are also the major events where agilists and coaches around the world come to network and share ideas.  

---

## Page 49

 
49 
● Local conferences are often sponsored by Agile and coaching communities or Agile focused companies and are generally an affordable option without the expense of travel. These events generally have a reasonably low-ticket price. ● User groups and meetups are often a great entry point for engaging with the Agile community. Many of these events are virtual so you can join communities around the world, build networks, and learn new creative ways of solving problems. These events are usually free. ● Coaching camps are often sponsored by local Agile communities and by larger organizations. These are often a mix of open space and targeted learning with lots of time for practices and networking. These are generally low-cost events and might include travel costs. ● Open space and lean coffee events are often held as part of major conferences or sponsored by local user groups to collaborate and share thoughts and ideas about current issues in the Agile world. ● Public training classes are a great way to have access to experienced thought leaders who can answer questions and give guidance while teaching on a topic that you need to grow your career.  Training is moderately costly and often results in a certification or formal continuous education credits.  Agile community participation can open the door for great relationships with other agilists around the world. What it doesn’t necessarily do is get you exposure to people who want to buy your products and services. To get exposure, you need to also focus on making contributions that will impact the growth of the Agile community through sharing your expertise and experience. Thought leadership and helping others in the Agile community grow into stronger careers and become more successful with clients is a great way to gain exposure to prospective clients without doing lots of “selling.”  An added benefit is that most conferences extend registration to the event as speaker compensation. Some of the major conferences will also pay hotel or travel expenses. In general, unless you are a Keynote speaker conferences do not compensate in cash, although there are exceptions.   A great way to get started speaking in the Agile community is through meetups and user groups. Agile communities around the world meet monthly to provide a learning space for members. This is a great way to get comfortable presenting topics you are passionate about. Most speakers develop the content for two or three presentations a year and present them at multiple conferences and user groups. Different people attend the events so the topics can be reused, improved, and modified for each group as necessary to keep them relevant. A simple search on meetup or search engine for Agile meetups or Agile user groups can yield lots of options. Searching the term “Agile call for papers” or something similar can present options around the world for submitting your talks to both major and minor conferences. Don’t let your worries about not being known keep you from submitting. The only way to get known is to get out there and do it. Conference reviewers generally focus on the topic and abstract, not the person presenting the topic to select the conference lineup, so everyone has a fair shot.   

---

## Page 50

 
50 
Michael’s perspective is that more experienced speakers may have half a dozen talks that can be given on a moment’s notice. Newer coaches may have one or two. Every so often, coaches refresh their talks to present new ones. When reaching out to organizations who are potential clients, say, “Here’s a set of five talks I have. I think this one will resonate with your organization.”  While it might seem like a great opportunity to make some cash to present a topic at a company, think longer term. It is more effective if you give these small talks for free. It creates more opportunities to get your name out there and establish authority in front of lots of people. Once you’ve already made a connection with the company and they see the quality of your work there’s a stronger chance they’ll call you for a paid coaching or training event. Speaking is a primary business development tool. We challenge you to find a way to speak to a group at least once a month, sometimes several times a week if you’re doing a big launch.”  Once you get selected as a speaker at an event, don’t forget to leverage the power of social media to get your face and name associated with having something to say in the Agile community. Many people don’t post announcements of events they are speaking at because they feel awkward self-promoting. One way to think about it is that you are building a brand in the industry and the only way you can build that brand is to get it visible. Let others know what you are speaking about - it may create an opportunity for them to ask you to come present at their company community of practice or to do a lunch and learn event that can be the open door for you to get new clients.  Writing is another highly effective business development tool, especially in the form of content marketing. Content marketing involves consistently sharing valuable, compelling content that delivers information readers want to know while positioning you as an expert in your field.  It also helps potential clients understand how you coach.   Another way to make contributions to the Agile community is to get involved in writing and publishing content that is useful and easily accessible. Content can include things like blogs, videos, white papers, or even getting involved with community-based volunteer projects that get learning materials into the hands of others.   Keep in mind that simply writing a blog and publishing it might feel like a huge amount of work and be an exciting accomplishment, but it won’t bring you visibility if no one reads it. Promoting your content via social media either through posts or digital marking is an important step to getting your work visible. Getting your work visible is even more important than creating it. The more you engage the world with your content, the more helpful you will be and the more visibility you will have. When people see your name and associate it with a particular topic. When they get stuck on that topic, they will remember you and it increases the chances of getting a call for help.  Michael says, “I personally focus on posting something on LinkedIn at least once a day, and something on Medium several times a week. I also co-author at least one book every year.  

---

## Page 51

 
51 
Contributing to trusted publications, like InfoQ and Forbes, is another good way to get your name and content out there.” The key is consistent engagement with the community you want to impact.
