Cherie shares, “One example of community contributions I’ve made that has had a big impact on the growth of the Agile community is the Scrum Alliance Labs Path to Coaching Program. This program contains over 80 videos and lays the foundation for understanding the competencies needed to coach Agile organizations via an invitational coaching approach. This series has over 45,000 users as of mid 2021 with new registrations happening daily. This project has injected lots of learning into the Agile space, has created curiosity for professional coaching, and has created a connection between me and the Agile community that is amazing. It is not uncommon for me to speak to people for the first time and hear them say that they feel like they know me because they have spent so much time with me in the videos I created. This project took me roughly 40 hours to complete and we published unedited rough videos. It didn’t have to be perfect - it just had to be valuable.”   Another example of contributing to and leadership in the Agile community is the Best Agile Articles project. Originally Michael’s idea, this project curates the Best Agile Articles published by agilists around the world and packages them into a single book each year. The idea behind this project is that there is plenty of wisdom in the Agile community and it is often hard to find. So, having a single place where great ideas can reside and be distributed is a considerably valuable concept. The first BAA book was published in 2017 through the work of a group of volunteers from the Agile community. The PDF version is given away free as a community resource and hard copies and Kindle versions can be purchased via Amazon. The articles that make it to the book are selected via double blind voting where volunteers read the nominated articles and vote on the articles with no knowledge of who the author of the articles is. Many new authors are represented in the publication and get exposure that they would not otherwise have. The BAA project also hosts the BAA website where access to all year's articles can be found. The point of the book isn’t for us to make money, it’s to make an impact and to increase the visibility of people in the community who are using writing to contribute value to the Agile world.  Community Leadership  Another way to invest in the Agile community while creating visibility for your talent is to take an active role in community leadership activities. This might include volunteering for existing events and organizations to create avenues for people to learn and to share their experience with others. Connect with local Agile communities and volunteer to assist with conferences, meetups, and other events. If there’s no local community, start one. Certification organizations that are unique to Agile, such as the Women in Agile, Scrum Alliance, Scrum.org, and Agile Alliance are all visible organizations with credibility who are making an impact on the Agile world. Working with them in a volunteer capacity helps you establish authority and increase visibility. Volunteering to run an event or serve on a volunteer committee can be a great way to build a professional network with agilists from across the globe.  

---

## Page 52

 
52 
 Another great way to promote your company is to start an annual event. Michael hosts a local conference, Agile Games West in San Francisco, with about 100 people in attendance every year.   Creating Sustainable Change and Scaling Agile Coaching with Business Development  Maybe you are in an FTE role and thinking that this business development chapter doesn’t really apply to me. Well, it does. While a lot of the business development activities are designed to help independent Agile coaches, Agile coaches who work full-time in organizations also need to keep business development at the forefront of their minds. Rob Rubin says, “You need to build your personal brand as well as your company’s brand.”  Unlike more established job roles and fields, Agile coaching is relatively new. It doesn’t have a career ladder to follow, or any set path to greater and greater success. That means Agile coaches really must advocate for themselves, even within an organization. One way to do that is to become an industry leader and gain competency and experienced certifications such as the Scrum Alliance, Certified Team or Enterprise Coach. These designations require community involvement, contribution, and leadership as part of the path. So, even if you don’t intend to be an independent coach these activities serve a purpose to help you develop in your career.  At some point, you are likely to be looking for a full-time coaching job. One of the easiest ways for employers to get to know you is to look at your online content.  Getting to know you through your resume is typically not ideal. Resumes tend to deliver a rather dull list of been-there’s, done-that’s. High-quality online content does a much better job of showcasing your knowledge, skills, attitudes, and overall personality.   Another way you can use business development is to leverage it to create sustainable change within your current organization by utilizing the same activities and content to build a content library. This gives you visibility in the company both now and when you are gone. It keeps you front of mind so when they are looking for help in the future, they are likely to reach out to you. They also have you in mind when someone asks for a referral for a position.   Cherie discovered the formula for getting the most value out of intellectual capital while working with clients doing enterprise Agile adoption work. She was in a full-time employee role as the only enterprise coach in a huge company. She quickly found that she needed ways to scale her coaching impact without overextending herself because there were simply too many people wanting to learn from her. She had previously worked in large enterprises and knew the value of scaling her work to make a greater impact and to work at a sustainable pace, so she began looking for a similar opportunity with this employer. She was getting pulled on to answer some questions over and over, so she began utilizing the company’s intranet system to build an Agile information center. As she answered questions for people, she began to build a backlog of common questions and challenges happening in the organization. Rather than answering the same questions over and over, Cherie began writing blogs and creating short videos to answer 

---

## Page 53

 
53 
these questions. This allowed her to answer the questions thoughtfully and more fully. It also created a content library where she could send people to get them in the habit of looking there for information rather than asking Cherie. When someone asked a question by email that she already had content on, she would send them the link and tell them that they could find the answer to this question and many others in the content library. This trained people to go there first and created a way to develop sustainability for the organization so that long after she left, they still had this content to utilize.   A great side benefit of creating the content on the company’s intranet was that when Cherie visited new offices people recognized her from her videos and immediately had trust and rapport. She also had credibility because people had already utilized her content to learn and solve problems on their own.   Cherie further used this method to develop other Agile coaches in the organization. She worked with Scrum Masters, Product Owners, and managers to create content describing problems they solved, techniques and experiences they tried, and to share things they were learning with one another. By doing this she was not only helping individuals grow but was creating a self-developing organization that curated its own expertise to continue learning in a sustainable way long after she was gone. Sustainable change is a powerful way to leave a mark on an organization.   In later years, after she left that organization to start her own company, Cherie found out that people were still using the content she created to learn and grow. Her impact remained and she continued to get exposure and gain credibility in the company. This enabled her to return occasionally to do consultations and coaching as an external consultant and coach. Because people knew her through her work, they trusted her to return to help them.  Another valuable thing Cherie did was reuse the content she shared with the company for business development externally. She made sure that her writings and videos were free from any references to the company and utilized them to post on her website to share with the Agile community. Each time she created a piece of content to share internally she shared it externally also. In the same way, she reused content she created for the external world to share internally. Whenever she developed a conference presentation, she also used it to do sessions internally. When she created content for her website to share in the Agile community, she reused it to post on the company intranet. So, in essence, she was using her content to make an impact on her clients, the Agile community, and the future of the company she would one day open.   Cherie’s advice about business development is to “waste nothing and reuse everything.” None of these business development activities are fast fixes and they are often investments into your personal brand or a company you haven’t even decided to open yet. These approaches typically require a long period of time to develop momentum. You must engage in the activities repeatedly without a lot of feedback. It typically takes years to start getting inbound queries so it's never too early to get started to build your personal brand and simply make an impact on the 

---

## Page 54

 
54 
Agile community. Even if you never decide to go out on your own, you will be making a valuable contribution to the world.  Michael says, “In my case, it took about 10 years to get established as an independent Agile coach and only work with clients who reach out to me. You just need to find one thing and do it repeatedly. I found that writing best suited my skills and interests, so I write mini posts every day, longer articles every week, and a book every year. This is not to discourage you. It’s just to let you know what to expect—and to underscore the importance of persistence. Keep your writing, speaking, and digital marketing going daily. People will begin to look forward to it. Stop your daily outreach, and people will quickly forget about you. One of my favorite sayings is, ‘Get going and then get good.’ It's a reminder not to fall into the trap of perfectionism. One of the biggest enemies of Agile coaches is perfectionism. They think they must be perfect—to hit on that perfect pearl of wisdom with everything they do or create. This type of mindset blocks them from doing anything at all, never mind doing things repeatedly.”  To Niche of not to Niche - That is the Question  The question of niche is an interesting one. In most business development advice, you’ll hear that you must pick a niche to focus on. Cherie shares, “I am not certain that picking a niche is how it really works. In my experience at least, I didn’t go out looking for a niche and find one to label myself with. What happened in my case was that I fell in love with a particular type of work, became good at it, and became known for it. So, I guess you could say my niche found me. The reason I bring this up is because people often stress over picking a niche when they are in the beginning of their career development. Before they have had the opportunity to experience working with a variety of clients doing a variety of things, they are trying to pick one thing to be known for. To me that’s like deciding as a young child what you are going to be when you grow up. You can only speak from the perspective of what you think might be interesting but without the experience of doing that you don’t know if it’s what you are going to want to spend your life doing. My point here is that you may have a sense of what you want to focus on, but you don’t have to decide that up front and then miss the opportunity to discover what you really love and what kind of clients you want to work with.”   That said, it can be a powerful business development strategy to position or brand yourself in a very specific niche. Rather than being a generic Agile coach, having a market differentiator can open the right doors you really want to walk into. Being known as a specific type of Agile coach, one with a focused specialization can set you apart from the crowd. When people have a specific need, they seek you out based upon your specialization which can mean that you get more of the work you love to do.  Lyssa Adkins is known for her contributions defining the work of Agile coaching as a mix of consulting, mentoring, training, facilitating, and professional coaching. Cherie Silas is known for her contributions strengthening the competency of professional coaching in the Agile world. Alex Kudinov is known for applying his in-depth technical and business knowledge to help technology executives solve complex business problems. Jeff Paton is known for bringing Story Mapping to 

---

## Page 55

 
55 
the Agile world. Roman Pichler is known for his work in the product management space. Michael Sahota is known for his culture and leadership work. Michael de la Maza is known for contributing to the Agile games community and helped start Agile Games New England, Agile Games West, and Agile Games Midwest conferences.  Having a specialized niche can make you more valuable, as does being good at what you do. There is a certain stratum of clients who want the best in the business, and they will pay for it. That’s the goal of your branding - connecting you to those people. In a growing field, performance is extremely valuable. Being excellent at what you do makes business development becomes a lot easier. You share your passion and deliver what you promise, and people start to talk. In today's technology and social media driven world, word-of-mouth marketing is a powerful business driver, with the ability to generate ongoing business from satisfied clients.  Michael says, “In 2010 I worked with Rob Rubin when he was VP of Engineering at Carbonite, a successful startup that went public. He hired me again, five years later, when he was working at edX, an educational non-profit in Massachusetts.” Determining a Good Fit    While your ultimate goal as an Agile coach may be to have a steady stream of dream companies knocking at your door, either working with you as a consultant or hiring you as an employee, you may have to adjust your expectations a bit at the start. You may want to open the door for varying opportunities that may not be ideal until you become more established. Working in these companies can be a great learning experience. As you get experience and build competency to make an impact with clients you will want to start thinking about making sure you select the right clients to work with.   
Chapter 8 - Selecting the Right Clients Many Agile coaches are most inclined—and overjoyed—to enter an engagement in which an Agile mindset already exists. But it’s too much to ask a client to be Agile before the Agile transformation begins. Which brings us to a big question. What kind of coach do you really want to be? A second question is also important to ask yourself is, “At what point do I want to enter an engagement?” These two questions need to be answered before you start finding your next client if you want to take on work that you truly enjoy doing.  The first question, “What kind of coach do I really want to be,” is about understanding if the work you enjoy doing is Organizational Coaching, Agile Adoption and Process Improvement Coaching, Culture Transition Coaching, Product Management Development Coaching, or Business Agility Coaching, etc. You may be thinking that you want to do it all! Perhaps you label yourself an Agile Transformation Coach. Whatever you decide, it’s important to understand what 

---

## Page 56

 
56 
that means because it defines the scope of the work you enjoy and the intention of the clients you want to work with.   If you view yourself as an Agile Transformation coach and you go into an organization where the person who brought you in wants to push Agile on the company. Then, when you get there, you realize that what the leaders of the company want is to improve their existing waterfall practices. They are okay with a few isolated Scrum teams and maybe Kanban teams but have no intention to do a wide sweeping transformation work. You are going to be miserable trying to force your client to do something they aren’t interested in doing. However, if you view yourself as an organizational coach who is there to help the organization improve. It won’t matter if Agile is the answer or if simply mapping out their existing processes and helping them find ways to make them work better is what they need.   It may take you a while to figure out what kind of work you really enjoy doing so experiment and take note of what work motivates you and what doesn’t. Over time you can isolate what you love and focus on that. The second question is also important to find the answer to if you want to do work you really enjoy. “At what point do I want to enter an engagement?”  Maybe you want to work in companies that already have an Agile mindset, they are relatively successful with their implementation of Agile, and need someone to help them maintain and improve that momentum. That’s fine and those are wonderfully satisfying companies to work in. You get to enjoy the benefits of working in the environment you truly love. The goal of many Agile coaches is to work in an Agile company. Here’s the catch. If that’s the environment you want to work in, you need to refer to question one and make sure you’ve answered it according to this.   Agile Transformation Coaches and Organizational Coaches rarely get to work in these environments. They enter environments where the company has done no work at all towards becoming more Agile or they’ve done a few home-grown isolated efforts that haven’t gained much traction. They face tons of resistance and a big uphill climb to get to an Agile environment. It takes years and often the coach is gone before it’s fully realized if it is realized at all.  There’s also the question of whether you want to be an Agile coach in a multi-team environment or an enterprise coach working with entire large enterprises and whole company structures. Either way, unless it’s a very small company, chances are you can’t do it alone. Scrum Alliance defines a Certified Team Coach (CTC) as a coach who works with organizations that have multiple teams. They work directly with teams and the first and second layer of management. The name “Certified Team Coach” may give the wrong message about the work. A CTC is a coach of an organization and often focuses mostly on engineering and product management areas. If it has more than 10 teams or so, there is probably more than one coach who will be working there.   Scrum Alliance defines a Certified Enterprise Coach (CEC) as a coach who works with the top layers of management in an enterprise down to the first and second layers of management. 

---

## Page 57

 
57 
They may work with teams also in smaller organizations but in larger enterprises they generally work with leadership. They also focus far beyond engineering and product management. Their work stretches out into all areas of the company such as Human Resources where they might help them to define new job roles and role definitions or develop new merit and compensation practices that focus on team-based work rather than individual contributor work. They might help them develop new performance management structures that support team environments. A CEC might work with the finance and PMO departments to help them develop new ways to map cost to capitalization and change funding models to team or project based rather than project or FTE count.   They might work with Audit to help them understand how to map the current waterfall-based audit questions to where those controls can be found in an Agile environment. They might work with sales, marketing, and operations to help them find better ways to communicate to the customers in collaboration with product management so what they sell matches what’s being built and can communicate delivery dates in a more skillful manner. They may work with legal to develop Agile contracts to replace waterfall focused contracts. They are also working with leaders on strategy and looking at business objectives that aren’t being met to understand what changes can be made in the organization that will achieve the desired business results.   These are just a few examples of things an Enterprise Coach might be doing, and depending on where they wish to specialize, the focus will likely narrow a bit. Doing all this work in a small company with less than 10 teams is very different from doing it in a company with thousands of employees with multiple divisions and engineering groups. In these very large companies, it is likely that there will be multiple Enterprise Coaches each working with separate divisions. Coordination and collaboration become important in these situations so each coach can leverage the collective wisdom and momentum of the others to bring the entire company forward in a more cohesive way.   Understanding the kind of work, you want to do and where you want to enter the process is important to help you narrow down and find the right clients. As you can see from the examples given previously there are lots of facets to this work. It can rarely be done alone since such an extensive skill set and background are needed to understand how to help the client get the results they desire. No one can know everything so understanding what you do know and what you want to focus on will help you to thrive.   Michael shares, “When I started off as an independent Agile coach, any client who was willing to pay me was a good fit. I needed the money. Once things got rolling, I created a detailed checklist that helped me decide if an engagement was one, I wanted to take. That checklist included things like the size and level of the organization, driving distance and compensation.   Today I have a lot of inbound requests for my services, and I can afford to be even more selective. I use my intuition and examine my feelings when I’m speaking with prospective clients to determine if they seem like a match.   

---

## Page 58

 
58 
Charting a Correct Fit   Determining a correct fit can also be done using a three-by-three chart, one that cross-references the team, group and organization with practices, structure, and culture. New coaches typically start off at the team level focusing on practices.  To use the chart, simply indicate with a check mark what are your areas of focus. If you focus on team-level practices, then you would put a checkmark in the upper left-hand box. If you focus on organizational culture, you will put a checkmark in the lower right-hand box. Most coaches focus on several of the nine areas. Very few focus on all nine. This will help you attract suitable clients and repel the ones who are not a good fit.   Team Organization Enterprise Practices    Structure    Culture     Financials also have to be reasonable, although what defines reasonable will vary for everyone. Just like any other profession, when first getting started rates are on the lower end and once you get established, they will likely go up.   Michael notices, “Many Agile coaches grossly underprice their services. You need to get to a point that you’re comfortable asking for what you’re worth. Compensation is a signal of quality. When you price your services like a junior project coordinator, the client will expect to utilize you as one.”  Pre- Engagement Conversations  Conversations with prospective clients need to be highly transparent, with both parties having a clear idea of how the engagement is designed to unfold. Pre-engagement discussions are your opportunity to make sure that you and the client are fully aligned and that you both understand the what and the why of the engagement. Even if you are working towards getting a full-time employee role in an organization as a coach, it is important to do the proper pre-engagement work before accepting an offer. This can mean the difference between doing the work you love and feeling trapped in an impossible situation that you hate.   If you are an independent coach, pre-engagement conversations are likely called negotiations, proposal reviews, or pitches. However, if you are searching for FTE contracting or subcontracting positions these are more likely called interviews. Looking at pre-engagement conversations as a series of job interviews can put you in a mindset that won’t be particularly helpful to set up a strong start point as an enterprise coach.  

---

## Page 59

 
59 
If your goal is to be an Agile coach focused on helping already Agile teams and organizations improve, you will probably treat these conversations more as interviews. You’ll be looking for answers to questions like: ● What is the culture of the organization? ● How connected is leadership to ensuring that agility is valued and promoted? ● How are teams organized and what roles exist in the organization? ● What is the role of the Scrum master? ● How are Product Owners utilized in the organization? ● What do you expect the role of the Agile coach to be responsible for? ● How many hours per week are most people working? ● How available will the managers be to me? ● What are your biggest organizational and technical impediments and challenges?   These questions are all about the current state of the organization and the environment the coach will work in. These are great questions if your interest is to join an organization that is already well on its way in its adoption of Agile. These engagements can be fun, interesting, and somewhat challenging. It’s a great place for someone who is just getting into Agile coaching to start and can be a very comfortable place to work for years.   For people who are looking into enterprise coaching and are possibly coming in at the very beginning of an engagement or into a failed engagement the questions you want to ask are very different. These conversations are more about setting expectations and uncovering what’s really happening in the organization. These are the conversations where you bring alignment and ensure that the client understands what they are getting ready to undertake. This is also your opportunity to make sure that what you want to do is in alignment with what the client wants and will tolerate.  As you’re shaping proposals for prospective clients, it’s best to collaborate and co-create the proposal together. Make the proposal a living document that you both are editing and contributing to. Schedule multiple conversations about the proposal as it is being developed.   One of the practices that can take up lots of your personal time and not return results is to have a 10-minute conversation about what the client wants then creating a proposal on your own and sending it over for review. You’re likely to hear, “OK, we’ll evaluate it and get back to you.” And that may be the last thing you hear. Ever.   Their evaluation may take extensive time.  They may change their minds a dozen times.  They may hire someone else while you’re still waiting for a response. They may never get back to you at all.  They may not have the budget for what’s in the proposal. They may be price shopping and trying to find the best deal.  

---

## Page 60

 
60 
Remember, Agile coaching is a partnership. This partnership begins in the early stages. A more effective move is to tell the organization you’ll create a draft of a proposal that you can make edits on together. This allows them to make the exact changes they want directly on the proposal while you’re both in on the conversation. It creates partnership and shows the client that the work you do with them will be collaborative. They’ll know from the very start that you aren’t going to go off and do the work and make decisions while they focus on more important things. If the work they want you to do isn’t important enough to warrant their time and energy defining it, this may be an indicator of what you’ll experience once you get in and start working.  Be Direct: ‘Unsell’ Yourself  Rather than trying to persuade clients to hire you, ask pointed questions that get to the heart of why they are turning to Agile at this point in their journey. An important question to ask them is: What happens if you don’t do this change initiative?  This question builds a business case for what they need. It also creates a sense of urgency when you ask if the work needs to happen today. You want to hear them say yes, it needs to happen now. This directly relates back to the value of what needs to be done, the value of what it’s worth.   Another important question is: Why did you call me specifically?   You want to find out why they picked you and not one of the thousands of other Agile coaches in the market. The response you get can be reflected to them in the proposal. If they say they picked you for your specific experience with XYZ, your proposal should mention how you have specific experience with XYZ.  Another question you don’t want to fail to ask is: Why do you need to do this now?   This question further highlights the urgency (or lack thereof) while also increasing a prospective client’s trust in you. Maybe they’re not sure why the work needs to be done now, or perhaps they say that it doesn’t. If that’s the case, you can say something like, “Then let’s not do it right now. Circle back to me in three months.”    At this level, the pre-engagement work is more about conversations you want to have with the client, not just questions you want answered to make sure that there is a good fit for you to enjoy working there. Before jumping into an engagement, it is important to understand the “whys” behind the client’s desire to bring in an Agile coach so you can understand what they really need. We recommend that at a minimum you have discussions with the client about these topics before proposing what you might offer.   Business challenges and expected results: It’s important to understand what problems the client is having that they are hoping Agile will help them resolve. It is not uncommon for a client to give the answer to this question as they want to adopt Agile. The problem they are attempting to 

---

## Page 61

 
61 
solve is that they don’t know how. As previously mentioned, Agile is not the goal. Agile is a set of solutions implemented to solve business problems. It is important to get clear on the business problems the client has that they are hoping Agile will help them resolve. If the person you are speaking to can’t tell you the business problems either they are not the right person to be speaking to or they are not ready to undertake the work of adopting Agile.   Definition of success: It’s important to understand how the client will measure success of the engagement. This should tie back to business results and there will also be some other things included in the definition of success. It’s important for you and them to get clear on what will tell them that the business results have been achieved. You’ll have time once in the engagement to get into specific detailed measures of success for each objective they have so in these conversations you are looking to understand what success looks like at the business level.  This conversation will also give you the information you need to determine what kind of engagement the client wants. [See the chapter on Engagement Types for a deeper description of the engagement types.]   The investment level: This is a defining topic that will make it clear what type of engagement the client is looking for, how much change they really want, and what they are willing to invest to get to the end they desire. Therefore, it is important to understand the business results they are trying to achieve. Understanding the challenges, they have, and business results they want will give insight into some of what might need to happen to get to those results. For example, suppose their business challenge is that they want to improve time to market because they are losing customers who complain that they aren’t getting the features they want. It is important to help them understand that this might mean that there will need to be technology changes which will come at a high cost. Depending on their current state, adding automation to speed up delivery time might mean the client will have to invest in training their current workforce or hiring people who know how to do the automation work. It might mean that they need to make major investments and changes in how code is managed and restricted to move faster. This may mean that you will want to talk to technology leaders prior to taking the engagement to make sure there is alignment. It also may mean that the way they are doing product development today will need to change. This means that there will be impacts to product management or a need to shift from projects to products which will impact funding structure, job roles, and will cost money to train and hire people to focus on this work.  It is important to help the client understand what they are asking for and how deeply they want to invest in making change happen. The level of investment the client is willing to make will determine the scope of the work. It will also tell you if you are the right coach to lead the effort.  Michael recommends, “Get all of the stakeholders and decision makers in the room during the proposal process. When everyone’s together in a group, you may find the people at the company themselves are not fully aligned. The PMO of a fast-growing startup reached out to me, saying they were aligned with the VP of Engineering and the VP of Product about increasing agility throughout the organization. Once we got everyone in a room, we collectively 

---

## Page 62

 
62 
discovered that the two VPs had a very different perspective—one that was not aligned with the PMO.”   Budget: The budget conversation encompasses more than just your hourly rate. It includes the total amount of money the company has earmarked for the changes they want to make. You need to consider how many coaches it will take to get the results the client wants. Unless it is a small company or the client wants to only do surface level work, chances are you can’t do it alone. Does the client understand the level of financial cost involved in the work and do they have the budget to dedicate to the changes they need to make? Do they have funding for training, hiring, organizational restructure, technical restructure, or do they just want one individual to come and help them improve what they are already doing with no major changes?   These initial conversations can help you to have a discussion with the client about the scope of the work and what it might take to get there. Until you know this information it’s going to be hard to put together a proposal and discuss the cost of the engagement.   Cherie says, “A core difference I see in doing enterprise adoption and transformation work and being an Agile coach is that someone who wants to just join an organization and be an Agile coach is more interested in culture and discusses the working conditions and tactical aspects of the work with the client. Someone who understands enterprise-wide adoption is going to focus more on the bigger strategic picture and what it’s going to take to turn the whole ship around. There is a different level of focus, talent, and experience needed for enterprise work. I see lots of people labeling themselves as enterprise coaches on LinkedIn, but I’m not fully convinced that they all understand what that means. I would rather lose a client who isn’t ready to take on all the changes needed to get the business results they want than to take an engagement and be miserable because the client wants magic to happen without them making the required investment. I don’t have a magic wand.”  Michael shares, “One prospective client requested a proposal with very specific details. They said they had long-term objectives in mind and wanted one-on-one coaching, executive coaching, and management coaching. I submitted a proposal covering all they asked for. I later found out they switched to wanting someone for team coaching with short-term objectives. It’s important to know the client will very often not have the vaguest understanding of what they really want or will not express it using standard Agile terminology. They will often change their mind during the proposal process without being explicit. It’s up to you to help the client be explicit. Think of them as having a big, messy ball of yarn in their heads. The proposal process helps them unwind it.”    Aligning on what the client wants and what you offer   After this initial set of conversations, if the client still seems viable for you it’s important to align on some high-level strategy before jumping in. Leaving this step out of the process can mean that you end up in a situation where the client expects you to make the changes to their company while they sit by idly watching you work. It can also mean that you end up in a 

---

## Page 63

 
63 
situation where the client expects you to tell them what to do so they don’t have to make decisions. If you get into one of these situations it also means that the client has someone to blame when things don’t go as planned – you.   Cherie shares her process: “Before accepting an engagement, I have a conversation about who I am, the work I do, how I operate, and what I expect from them as a client. They need to understand that I am a coach. Yes, I also do some consulting, but I will not take ownership for the changes they want to make. That’s their job.   Some of the discussions I have around this are:   What to look for in an Agile coach: The client needs to understand what an Agile coach is and what they do. There are many different types of Agile coaches who have different focuses. Depending on what the client wants they will need different coaches to do the work. I make sure that clients understand the level of talent and experience they should be looking for in an enterprise coach. Even if I’m not the right coach for them, I want them to understand what to look for as they continue their search.   How I work and what I expect: I want to make sure that before hiring me my clients understand what I do and how I expect to work with them. I describe the different ways I might be engaging with them including consulting, mentoring, training, facilitating, and coaching. I also make it extremely clear that they own the changes they wish to make. I am their partner in the endeavor. I don’t have (nor want) the power to make changes in their organization and what I want for them is to develop sustainable change. If I own the change, that won’t happen. I also expect direct access and 1-1 coaching relationships to the leaders whose organizations will be working to adopt change. If that can’t happen, they aren’t ready for enterprise coaching.   I also require that the client provide an individual or preferably a team of people who are at a level that has budget and decision-making power with the influence to implement widespread change. These are the people who I will be partnering with who will be responsible for facilitating the changes in the organization.   Disengagement plan: Before starting an engagement, it is important to be clear on how and under what terms the engagement will end. How long should the client expect the coach to work with them? Cherie says, “For smaller efforts that aren’t looking for widespread enterprise change, I personally cap my time investment at one year. For large enterprises wanting to do major work, we discuss a three-year horizon. I am also very clear with the client that this timeline doesn’t mean the work will be complete – it means this is when I plan to disengage. For longer engagements we discuss the strategy for how I fade out over time.”    Signing the deal  

---

## Page 64

 
64 
Depending on the size of the company and the scope of work, this initial pre-engagement work might take many months (sometimes years for large enterprises). This is one reason why most of the coaches I see in the industry do more contract or subcontract work after another company has gone through this engagement process. Doing that is a viable way to earn a living. It also may put you in a position where you take on an engagement blindly without this pre-work and just dive in to do what you can. This is yet another reason why you need to be clear on what kind of coach you are and what work you want to do. Dealing with the specifics of winning engagements can be a long arduous process that not everyone wants to undertake. It is certainly easier and faster to sub-contract for someone else who has already done the deeper pre-engagement work.   If you are taking on an FTE role, the final negotiations are likely to focus on total compensation. If you are taking on a sub-contract with an agency the final negotiations are likely about hourly rate or corp-to-corp requirements. However, if you are contracting directly with the client, you will be discussing Master Services Agreements (MSA) and Statements of Work (SOW).  Master Services Agreement is the document that outlines the terms and conditions of being a vendor who can do business with their company. It is not specific to the work you will be doing. Rather, it outlines the corp-to-corp rules and agreements. These agreements may outline things like requirements to carry liability and/or workers compensation insurance. It also covers things like intellectual property ownership, non-disclosure, non-solicitation, arbitration, etc. It is important to read these documents carefully because this is the overarching agreement with the company that will potentially enable you to do work there for years to come. Once you can get an MSA signed with a company it generally makes the process much quicker in the future.   After you get an MSA signed, the actual work outlined in your proposal will be described in the Statement of Work. Each piece of work you do for the company under separate proposals over time will be covered by a SOW. Generally, the person with ownership of the budget has authority to sign off on SOW’s up to a given dollar amount. It’s important to understand what that dollar amount is when you prepare the proposal. If the work, they want to do is larger than that dollar amount the SOW will have to go through extended reviews and approvals and can get stuck in the process for months.   Often, when the work is too large, it can be split into multiple proposals and SOWs to keep them under the maximum signature authority limits. For example, training might be placed into one proposal/SOW and coaching placed in another. Together the total cost of these may be significantly above the limits of the budget holder. However, when separated they both fall within the limits to get signed quickly. The quicker the SOW gets signed, the quicker you can start the engagement.   The SOW will contain the terms described in the proposal. It’s a good idea to make sure that the SOW is Agile in nature and contains terms that allow for iterative discovery and updates to expected outcomes and focus. Michael says, “You’re never sure how things are going to progress as you start the coaching, and you don’t want to paint yourself into a corner you can’t 

---

## Page 65

 
65 
get out of. Never make any drop-dead promises. You can only do so much. The rest is up to the organization. If the client doesn’t have cross-functional teams, don’t promise you can create cross-functional teams. You can’t change the organization they have. You can only work with them to help them change themselves. Don’t be in the business of selling miracles.”   Summing It Up  Good intentions are not good enough when it comes to helping to ensure a coaching engagement will succeed. To paraphrase Deming, it’s not enough to have good intentions, you need to know what to do and have good intentions. You need a set of pre-engagement actions that make sure all the pieces are in place to map out a successful path.  Business development activities help draw clients your way, at which time you can determine if they’re a good fit. Talking to clients about the engagement is a must, a discussion that can be bolstered by co-creating the proposal and asking pointed questions. Your final step is to create detailed terms for the engagement that include details on payment, delivery, and outcomes.    While there is never a 100% guarantee that every Agile coaching engagement you take will work out perfectly, a pre-engagement system will go a long way to ensure that you engage with the right clients most of the time. And if you keep up your side of the agreement, there’s an excellent chance you’ll keep advancing and finish strong.  
Chapter 9: The Relationship Agreement: STORMMES(c) Model Congratulations! You’ve landed that role as an Agile coach, and you’re ready to help an organization bring about sweeping changes that will impact the way they think about developing products and services. Coaching an organization through this change means that you’ll likely be working with leaders, teams, and individuals.   There’s going to be lots of relationships to manage. How will your clients know what to expect from you? How does the client know what you expect from them? Where does your responsibility stop and theirs begin? What outcomes do they expect to get from your engagement? How will you measure success? There are so many important questions you likely have about how you and the client will know the engagement is successful and that you have added value to their organization. Guess what? The client has those same questions, and it's part of the coaching to work with them to make those answers clear.  If you’ve ever attended a professional coach training program, it's likely that you had lots of practice with creating a coaching agreement for the coaching session. In Cherie and Alex’s coach training program at Tandem Coaching Academy, they have found that this is the number one competency that professional coaches struggle with getting right. Why? Because it’s hard. One reason it's so hard is that while a coaching conversation is supposed to sound like a conversation happening between two people in a strong relationship, it’s a different kind of conversation. In day-to-day conversations, you generally don’t start with questions like, “What is 

---

## Page 66

 
66 
the purpose of our conversation today?” and “What would you like to accomplish in the time we have together?” and “How will you know that we have had a successful conversation?” That’s just weird.  Add to that the fact that a coaching relationship isn’t intended to be a relationship like you have with your friends or loved ones. It’s a professional relationship that maintains a level of vulnerability and trust that often exceeds the borders of relationships with friends and families. However, these relationships are not intended to extend beyond the borders of professionalism. Coaching relationships are confidential in nature and the coach and client come together for a specific purpose to achieve specific goals.   Cherie shares, “I’ve seen many coaches jump into coaching relationships in the same way they handled practice sessions with other coaching students. Every conversation is a one-off conversation and there’s no overarching goal for the work they do together. It always surprises me when I work with a coach who doesn’t start the engagement with coming to an understanding on what the overarching goals for our work will be. Yet, it’s happened to me over and over even with skilled coaches. This same surprise happens every time I work with an Agile coach, and they do not have an overarching agreement for the engagement. These are generally the engagements where the leaders are saying things like, “I don’t know what the coach is doing, and I don’t know what value they are adding.”  “Don't let your eagerness to show value as quickly as possible lead you to ignore this critical step,” says Michael. "Without a solid foundation, you will be guessing about what your clients want and how to work with them."  It is important that coaches understand how to manage the coaching relationship and not just one-off coaching conversations. In fact, we advise clients that they should not hire a coach who doesn’t start the relationship with a clear understanding of the focus, indicators of success, and the plan for getting there together. Coaches who understand how to hold the entire relationship are more likely to get you to the intended goal than coaches who just view each session or day at the office as a disconnected event. The relationship agreement gives structure and clarity to the engagement and ensures alignment between the parties.  With that in mind, Cherie developed a model for creating the relationship agreement. The model has been widely accepted by coaches in the Agile community who give feedback that they are using the model in their day-to-day work both as internal and external coaches. It is a model that can be used successfully with both individual and team clients and even when the client is a large organization doing Agile transformation work.  Cherie needed a consistent way to create a strong relationship agreement with her clients at the onset of an engagement. She wanted to ensure that all the important things for a successful start get covered. She needed to ensure that there was alignment on the outcomes desired by the end of the engagement. Agreeing on measurements of success and cadence of check-ins to reflect on progress and adjust overarching goals was also important to her. Through her work 

---

## Page 67

 
67 
she developed the STORMMES© model that provided the structure needed to ensure there was clear alignment on expectations for coaching engagement.  STORMMES© is an acronym that outlines a list of topics a coach and client should cover when co-creating their relationship. As with using any model, the only way to explain it is linearly, but it is unlikely that you will use this model in the order presented below. Instead, focus on your client and have the right conversation that covers all these topics in the order that feels the most natural. The best coaches are always responsive to client’s needs above holding tight to using any model as presented for learning purposes.   The contents in this model might be covered in one conversation or through several conversations. Some parts of this will be in your written agreement with the client and other parts will be verbal. When coaching an organization, you should have an overarching relationship agreement with the organizational client and separate relationship agreements with each team or individual you work with.  The topics covered by the acronym STORMMES© are:  S — SUBJECT: Discuss the overall focus of the coaching engagement with your client, so you can both understand what the general focus of your work will be. For example, your individual client may want to focus on their overall way of communicating with others and improve how they are perceived by higher-ranking leaders in their organization. Clients will often label this something like confidence or executive presence. A team client might want to focus on improving their delivery time or their predictability. The organizational client might have a larger general focus like, improving our ability to meet our target release dates and improve customer sentiment. This discussion will usually transition seamlessly into the M-MOTIVATION or O-OUTCOMES topics. It’s not important to follow the model in presentation order. Just ensure that your conversations cover all the topics at some point.  T — TIMEFRAMES: If you are working with organizations, some timeframes conversation will take place early on and will be included in the written legal contract. Once you get into the organization and clarify the specific goals, you will want to dig deeper into timeframes expectations. Then, with each team and individual client you will need to discuss the timeframes for your engagements.  Some of the timeframes that need to be discussed, understood, and agreed to are: ●  Engagement length — will this engagement be several weeks or months; or will it be a stated number of sessions? ● Frequency — how often will you meet with the client? Will sessions be set on a pre-defined cadence and be placed into the calendar as standing appointments or will the client book sessions as needed? What happens if the client needs to reschedule or cancel a session? What is the cancellation window, and what happens if the client misses the window? 

---

## Page 68

 
68 
● Length — how long are the coaching sessions? If the client is going to be late for the session, will the session be extended to fill the time lost, or will it be deducted from the session time? How long will the coach wait for the client before they are considered a no-show? What happens if the client is a no-show? ● Progress Check-ins — how often will the coach and client take time to reflect on progress and adjust the goals or relationship agreement? What if the client wants to make a change prior to this outlined time? What if there are concerns about the progress being made? How will conflicts between the coach and client be handled?  O — OUTCOMES: These outcomes often answer the question: What will be different at the end of our coaching engagement if we have been successful in our work together? These overarching goals are the north star for the coaching engagement.  The coach is responsible for ensuring that as the engagement progresses, the client is doing work that moves them in the direction of these intended outcomes. If not, the coach should make that transparent to the client and find out if the agreed upon goals need to change. [See chapter on Well Formed Outcomes]   R — ROLES: The conversation about roles is a critical part of co-creating the coaching relationship with the client. Agile coaches often say that they get into client engagements as a coach to find out that what the client really wants is a consultant. This is an indication that the "Roles" conversation needs strengthening.   ICF is clear that it is the coach’s responsibility to explain to their clients the difference between coaching and other disciplines and ensure that the client is clear on if they need a coach and not some other helping profession. The coach is responsible for educating the client on what the experience of coaching will be like, so the client understands what they are contracting for. When this isn’t done properly both the coach and the client are likely to be frustrated and disappointed. It is a good idea to create a checklist of sorts to ensure that you don’t forget to cover important items in the initial conversations with the client. Some things the coach should discuss with the client are listed below; however, this is not an exhaustive list.  ● Confidentiality ● Mandatory reporting ● Adherence to the ICF ethics (if the coach is an ICF credential holder) ● Client ownership of their own change and success ● Role of coach ● Role of client ● How the client can best prepare for sessions ● The importance of reflection after sessions ● Most of the work must happen between sessions ● Legal terms (often in the written contract) ● Discussing the most effective ways to communicate with one another for a successful relationship ● Any other details specific to your coaching methods and arrangements  

---

## Page 69

 
69 
 M — MEASURES: The "Measures" conversation often happens during or after the conversation about outcomes/goals. For each outcome, the client wants to achieve through coaching it is important to understand how they will know they have achieved it. These can sometimes be quantifiable measurements like getting the promotion they were working towards or improving their GPA by 20%.  Sometimes the client will state measures that are more quantitative in nature such as I will feel more confident. These squishy measures should be investigated further to understand things like what will be different if you are more confident? How confident are you now? Where do you want to be after our engagement? What is different about your after state from your current state?  This conversation might also outline milestones along the way to the destination which will help the coach and client see progress along the way and not only at the end of the engagement. This can help them to identify when to make shifts and adjustments along the way as necessary. [See chapters on Impact Mapping and Well-Formed Outcomes]  M — MOTIVATION: The motivation conversation is closely related to both the “SUBJECT” and “OUTCOMES” conversations. This is the “why” behind the client’s reason for engaging a coach. It’s the thing that will keep the client moving forward when things seem stalled or when it gets tough to make progress. The discussion around motivation can take the form of understanding why the goals are important to the client. What value achieving these goals will bring to the client’s life.  What will happen if the changes the client wishes to make are not achieved vs what will happen if they are achieved. What is at stake for the client or what opportunity makes the investment in coaching worth making?  Understanding the motivation for the change is important for the client to talk through because it helps them to solidify the reason they are investing in coaching and get really clear on why it is important to be committed to making the desired changes. The motivation is important for the coach to understand because it can be used to continue to point the client in the direction  E — ENVIRONMENT: The conversation about the environment can give the coach and the client valuable information and insights into the systemic factors that promote and prevent success for the client. What is happening in their environment (professional and personal) that is contributing to the ability to achieve these goals? What things in their environment (people, processes, etc.) are available to support them in moving forward? Who are the personal and professional stakeholders that need to be considered or consulted during the engagement? What about their lifestyle or environment might be a hindrance to their success? What or who can be leveraged to move the client forward?   S — START The “how to get started” conversation is one of prioritization and value assignment. What goals are most important for the client to focus on during the engagement? What is the priority order of the different goals they want to achieve? Which goals can be worked on concurrently and which might need to happen consecutively? How does the client want to 

---

## Page 70

 
70 
approach tackling these goals in the work together? And very importantly, where does the client want to begin the work?  Coaching engagements are more than just having a series of one-hour conversations with clients who want to talk. Engagements are about getting things accomplished and meeting goals. The client has very real reasons for seeking the assistance of a coach in meeting their goals. This model can be a helpful framework to ensure that your work with the client is set up for the client’s best success. We encourage you to utilize this model in a flexible manner to strengthen the way you co-create your coaching engagements.     Chapter 10: Goal Definition via Impact Mapping  Before digging into the nuts and bolts of the impact mapping, something needs to be said of the impact that you have when bringing a tool or a technique to an organization. This tool, impact mapping, is a square peg, and hopefully after reading this book, and maybe digging into some adjacent literature you will be able to carry this square peg in your toolbox. The fact that it’s in your toolbox will not mean that any round hole you will see is going to be a right fit for this peg to be inserted into.  Firstly, remember, you are a coach, and you are inviting your clients, enterprises, teams, and individuals to experience greater awareness. It’s they who possess greater knowledge, skills, and experiences about the inner workings of their systems. It’s they, who have the background and understanding to assess the hole, look at the peg you are holding and decide whether the two are a good fit.  This is not to suggest that you can’t introduce a tool like impact mapping if you are coaching. Of course, you can! And your clients will rightfully expect you to introduce tools, in the right context, at the right time. Part of the job is to know your tool belt and how and when to appropriately use one tool or another.  Now, the big question is, how do you know if the impact mapping tool is appropriate and when to introduce it? And the answer is, you don't. To find out, you will have to be comfortable operating in ambiguity, suggesting and holding it lightly. You will have to regard your client as knowledgeable and not in need of being fixed. Exercise the courage to run small experiments, fail and run another one. These are the skills you bring to the table that serve the client so well in the end. The impact mapping technique is both powerful and easy to use. Facilitating it through a coaching approach multiplies its power. Most impact maps you see will contain 4 parts: goals (the why), actors (the who), impacts (the how), and actions (the what). While perusing the 

---

## Page 71

 
71 
internet you might find some different approaches to constructing impact maps. For simplicity and consistency, we stick with this structure, described by Gojko Adžić in his book Impact Mapping: Making a big impact with software products and projects8.  Part 1: Goals - The Why Usually, the process starts with identifying the goals. Well, mostly. Sometimes it does not start with the goals and that is addressed in the section of this chapter “There and Back Again.”  But for now, let’s start with goals. Let's assume this is a greenfield initiative, not littered by artifacts, outputs, or projects.  Alex says, “I won’t be wasting any electronic or physical paper for that matter to tell you how important goals are. With coaching you start with goals. Without goals coaching will lack direction, have reduced impact, and may not get the client where they want to go. Goals are the north star that will guide organizations, teams, and individuals.” David Clutterbuck in his Coaching the Team at Workbook says that, “all steps in the chain of purpose, mission, strategy, goals, and tactics require continuous attention to maintain the essential alignment.” And surely enough, impact mapping is a technique that aligns our organizations and teams around the goals with a deeper dive into the tactics of bringing those goals about. While we are starting with the goals here in the impact mapping, it is of crucial importance to understand that the purpose, mission, and strategy of organizations or teams you coach are essential inputs into the goals that you will outline in your impact map. As a matter of fact, while coaching through the goal discovery process you may find yourself asking questions like, ● What makes this goal important? ● Who says it is important? ● What will happen if we do not achieve this goal? ● When this goal is achieved what will be different for us? ● How will we know that we will have achieved the goal? As with any other questions suggested in this book, these are not intended to be a checklist of questions you need to go through to get to a clear understanding of the goals. Listen to your client, get curious, ask questions that make sense in context to help them understand their goals more fully.  Asking these types of questions will help your client to get a clearer understanding of what they want to achieve. However, no goal is worth the time spent on its creation or the paper it’s written on without a clear understanding of how to measure its achievement.  SMART goals became a staple of the goal setting process, whether in business settings, or for personal development purposes. You can find a few variations on the SMART acronym. For the purposes of this chapter, use the variation of Specific, Measurable, Achievable, Relevant and Time-bound. As a coach, you can introduce this model to your client and ask relevant questions for them to decide how to smarten their goals.  In Alex’s experience the measurable part is where most teams and organizations start to experience problems in their goal setting process. There might be a few reasons for that. Until  8  https://www.amazon.com/Impact-Mapping-software-products-projects-ebook/dp/B009KWDKVA 

---

## Page 72

 
72 
their goals are measurable, they are just wishful thinking, squishy feel-good statements that can be bent depending on the circumstances. In organizations where people’s well-being depends on achieving the stated goals people might be reluctant to put specific numbers on them. Numbers feel real and produce pressure and make people feel like they win if they reach them and lose if they don’t. If you notice people's reluctance around putting specific numbers to the stated goals, you might get curious about the reasons for the hesitation. Coaching can enable them to identify, discuss and potentially solve those problems. It may be a worthwhile detour to take for teams to continue their goal-setting journey. Impact mapping takes a very rigorous approach to goal measurement. According to Gojko Adžić For a goal to be meaningful, it should contain the following 5 parts, 1. What is being measured. In this example, Alex’s client, an oil company, wanted to use deferred production of oil (i.e., oil they could not extract now due to downtime, or other reasons) as their measurement. Decreasing what they called deferred BOE (barrels of oil equivalent) would increase current production, and consequently, increase profitability.  2. How it will be measured. Get specific about the source of the numbers. Identify systems, sources, reports, people, where the values are coming from. For this specific example the company used an existing “Spotfire” report that was generated based on the data in the “ProCount” database.  3. The current value of the measurement (benchmark) – you will be surprised how many times your clients will have a hard time identifying the current measurement. This step cannot be skipped so taking a break to gather the benchmark measurement might be useful.  4. Minimally acceptable target value – this is the measure you will use to identify if further investment in the goal is profitable. For the minimally acceptable target value measure to be meaningful, your clients must pair it with a specific date this measure should be achieved. Such a pairing makes the pivot or persevere decision, coupled with accompanying financial decisions much easier.   In his book The Lean Startup: How Today’s Entrepreneurs Use Continuous Innovation to Create Radically Successful Businesses9, Eric Ries introduces the concept of the pivot or persevere point. This is a point in time, or in the development of a startup, project, or enterprise where people inspect the results to date and decide whether to invest more time, money, effort, and other resources in the current development or to do something else.   Alex’s oil company example used 90% as the minimally acceptable value for the deferred BOE and 3 months as our time horizon.  9   https://www.amazon.com/Lean-Startup-Entrepreneurs-Continuous-Innovation-ebook/dp/B004J4XGN6/ref=sr_1_1?dchild=1&keywords=lean+startup&qid=1633379412&sr=8-1 


---

## Page 73

 
73 
 5. Optimal value – this is the outcome strived for. It might also be a value that, when achieved, will make further investments in the goals decline in value such that a shift to invest in different organizational and team goals is warranted. By coaching your clients through this process of not only making their goals SMART, but also building significant rigor into the measuring part of it, you can help them achieve more clarity around their goals. Some clients might want to build a few measurements for a single goal. For example, achieving revenue targets might be measured in a variety of ways, depending on who measures. At this point I would have put my mentor hat on and recommended against such a practice. First, it muddies the waters and creates a potential conflict not only from the pure measurement perspective but also at the pivot/persevere decision point. Second, as a coach, when clients say, “we need more metrics,” what I actually hear is, “we don’t have a clear goal, yet.” In my practice, having a single way of measuring a goal provides clarity, focus, and simplicity. You can also choose to take this part to a different level with well-formed outcomes. This, by itself, is a much deeper dive into organizational goals, capabilities, an exploration of available and needed resources. Well-formed outcomes chapter gives you a primer on the coaching approach to facilitate your client’s creation of such outcomes. Part 2: Actors - The Who Now that you have your goals clear and have a good understanding of what it will look like to achieve them, it is time to move on to defining the actors, i.e., people, groups, teams, organizations, and other entities that either help you achieve the goal or stand in your way. Yep, you read it right, not only does Alex recommend identifying your “collaborators,” i.e., those who help you achieve the goals, but also detractors.  The go-to technique for this is brainstorming. And since you likely prefer a coaching stance to all others, after explaining this impact map step, you will usually invite your clients to brainstorm.  Brainstorming is a wonderful technique often used in coaching. Brainstorming allows for a free flow of ideas. Provides a collaborative, supportive, and safe environment, where every voice is heard and no idea is dismissed as silly, outlandish, impractical, etc. The rules of brainstorming are simple and powerful. They are, ● Suspend judgment – any idea is a good idea ● Let your imagination fly – everything is possible, and nothing is impossible, ● Build off each other's ideas – we are doing it in a group setting, right? So why not to use the collective imagination to build even better ideas, ● Stay on topic – this one is a bit hairy and requires self-discipline. If someone gets off-topic in their ideas we should not be dismissing it outright as per the rules above, and it won’t be helpful for a coach to chastise the group, “stay on topic,” every time or ever the coach perceives that the ideas veer off the topic. These rules should be laid out before the exercise starts and coach should trust the client to be self-disciplined and resourceful to stay on track without coach intervention, ● One idea and conversation at a time – There are a few problems with multiple conversations at a time. First, if more than one person is talking then not enough people are listening. And brainstorming is not only about idea generation, but also listening to 

---

## Page 74

 
74 
the collective wisdom. Second, if people start talking at the same time, it is most likely they are not suggesting new ideas and building on others’ ideas, but discussing, arguing, or dismissing others’ ideas. That is not the point of brainstorming. As a coach (and a facilitator for this exercise) you may want to remind the team that they will have plenty time for discussions and debates and this is the time for generating new ideas, ● Be visual – write, draw, or sketch your idea to make it visible for everyone to see right away. That will help the rule #2 – building of each other ideas, ● The more, the merrier – encourage people to generate as many ideas as possible, don’t rush. After the first burst people need more time to get into something that they have never thought of. This is the time for the proverbial AWE (And What Else) question.  And there your clients go to the races – piling up some great ideas and uncovering hopefully a multitude of actors who help or detract from achieving the goals. This is the awareness step of the exercise, this is what coaching is all about – discovering something new or looking at something already known from a different angle to get a new perspective. Can you contribute to the brainstorming session other than facilitating the process move forward? You can, carefully. One of the worst things the coach can do when asked for their opinion is to say, “well, I am a coach, and it’s not my place to have an opinion,” or something along those lines. Yes, you do have an opinion, and if you are in a familiar environment, you probably have some insights to share with your clients, whether these insights come from observing this very client, or from your previous engagements and work you have done. However, there are two things you want to be mindful of when giving insights as a coach. Be wary and aware of your power position – whether real or perceptual. Remember that coaching is an equal partnership where no one is above any other party involved. If your clients perceive you as superior to them in knowledge, skills, competencies, and influence they might start giving your inputs much more deference than those inputs deserve. They may also diminish the value of their own ideas. “I have seen this happen many times during coaching engagements, particularly when the Agile coach is a former manager,” says Michael. “This is a trap that causes client disempowerment.” A power token attached to an idea, regardless of how good it is, makes that idea unfairly influential on the one hand, and comes with the “not my idea” pushback on the other. As Sara Smith, an ICF Board Chair, said during a podcast with Cherie and Alex, “tell them what to do, and you engage their left, rational brain. They will write it down, execute on it with much less thought they otherwise would give an idea, and come back for more.” That is not our goal in coaching. We want them to come up with the ideas, and own the execution without external selling, pushing, and threatening with consequences. Hence, there are two rules around your contribution as a coach to the brainstorming content. First, never volunteer your ideas first or succumb to being pushed to go first. People are never so stuck that they cannot come up with a single idea. What they might be having are thoughts that their ideas are not worthy of consideration, so they shut themselves down and defer to you. You probably see that the problem here is not that they can’t have any ideas, but that more safety, openness, and respect are needed. That’s the job for a coach. That’s where you should put most of your effort to enable people to speak freely. Work on that and you might never need to volunteer your ideas to start with. Second, if you do give your ideas and contribute to the common pile of thoughts, make sure these ideas are treated the same as others. The coaching term for this is to hold your ideas 

---

## Page 75

 
75 
lightly, contribute them with no attachment. While holding your ideas lightly is a nice concept, it’s hard not to be invested in your own ideas. If you are an expert in a specific area and this idea revolves around the area of your expertise, it’s even harder. And that’s where your coaching training and competencies should come into play. It is not about you; it is never about you. It is about them and their outcomes. So please contribute your ideas, while helping create an open, collaborative, and safe environment, and do so with no attachment to your contributions. Lastly, why should you waste any time with identifying detractors? The idea here is not to put detractors on the map and figure out how to work around them but to create more awareness about them. Identifying detractors can help you think about supporters who counterweigh the detractors' problems, concerns, and hesitations. These supporters are the people who help push ideas through and get things done. Those counterbalancing individuals should also be on your map. The idea is to focus on the interests of the counterbalancing supporters as they are working towards the same impact rather than against it. These supporters are the people whose interests you want to explore, amplify, and protect. This will help remove and diminish the effects of the detractors. Thus, identifying detractors is an intermediary step that you might take to get even deeper into identifying the real actors behind the goals.       Part 3: Impacts - The How  Now that you sorted through your actors and picked those you wanted to put on the map, you dig into the actions they take, regularly or one-off, to achieve the goals identified in the first step. You can use the same brainstorming technique described above. In my experience, this part does not always require a deep dive, however, you want to make sure that the discovery process doesn’t shift to assigning blame and making judgments. The purpose of designing the impact map is to help clients move forward towards achieving the goals, rather than telling everyone how poor of a job someone else does.  As you are working through the impact discovery process, you might want to pay particular attention to which impacts are real and which are desirable. Identify which impacts are performed today, and which are those the client would like to have, but don’t have now. If some impacts are those that your clients want to implement, but never 


---

## Page 76

 
76 
had time, funding, or people, encourage them to put those impacts on the map and mark them as future or desired impacts. This will provoke more insightful, aspirational, and hard conversations. It will give your client a fuller picture of how they might approach achieving their goals. Here we come to one of the most curious outcomes of the impact mapping. A lot of these conversations in the Agile world start around a piece of software, or a piece of a product, which has software at its core. However, what I found many times is that by the time we get to the impacts, some non-software pieces start sprouting up here and there. I have seen a spectrum of reactions from people when they see these effects coming around, surprise, excitement, annoyance, and even anxiety. As a coach you should pay particular attention to these reactions as they might make or break the outcomes of the exercise. Some might be thinking (or saying), “we never thought about it that way,” which is a fertile ground for an exploration towards the possibilities these new ways of thinking open. Others might say, “That will never work, it’s outside of our area of control, let’s get real.” Resist the urge to heed these voices. Having a comprehensive impact map with visibility into inner workings of the organization that are usually hidden or perceived out of reach is indispensable in gaining a better understanding of what the next level of performance might be.
