Part 4: Actions - The What And lastly, we arrive at a point where we can start asking the question, “What do we do to support the actions identified in the previous step for actors to achieve the goals?” In this step coaching will depend on your client readiness to make decisions and the stage of the process they are in. In many cases, when Alex ran this exercise, the client already had a few ideas of what they wanted to do with the product. They wanted a framework that would help them to put those ideas into a bigger picture. If this is the case, you will have quite a few good ideas and you will start seeing them sorted out to help with specific actions your actors take. If you run the exercise from scratch, and the organization does not have any notion of what they would like the product or a set of actions be, your coaching process through this step might resemble those prior steps, i.e., another brainstorming exercise with a focus on supporting specific actions.  As you transition to coaching this step, it is a good idea to take a step back and take in the whole picture that the map presents so far. You might want to ask the following types of questions to deepen the conversation, ● What do you see?  ● What stands out? ● What surprises you? ● What about the process has prompted most thinking and reflection? ● Where can the most impact be achieved in the least amount of time? These are just some sample questions. Of course, you will be coaching your client considering their context, process, and content, so make sure your questions are customized for their environment. There and Back Again 

---

## Page 77

 
77 
In many cases you will have an opportunity to work on a greenfield initiative, or with newly formed teams, or with organizations that are willing to start from scratch. But what if you must work with teams that have existing initiatives underway? What if the organization tasks you with helping them make sense of the existing portfolio landscape?  You can still start, as described above, from goal definition and follow the impact mapping process. As we stated before, identifying purpose, mission, strategy, goals and tactics for organizations and teams has its own inherent value and people will benefit from the clarity these activities bring. You can also start from the bottom (or depending on the orientation of your map, from the right), trying to fit in the existing solutions and identify which actions they support. The types of questions you might ask are, ● When we implement this, who will it support? ● What difference will it make in their lives? ● How impactful is it going to be? ● What will it enable them to do they cannot do now? You might want to ask questions about actors, their actions, efficiency and effectiveness of their actions, their daily routines and changes these solutions will introduce (hopefully for the best). As you are having this conversation with your clients you will start noticing that branches of the map start taking shape. Note down actors and their actions and start attaching solutions to those branches. Do not forget the goals those actors pursue – as this will give your client important measures and metrics as to which direction to grow the map further.  Hold all the ideas and map structures lightly. The impact map is not supposed to be a plan that your client rigidly executes. It is a living and breathing document that prompts a conversation and helps with important decision-making processes. A comprehensive impact map can help to answer the following questions, ● I have this great idea of a new feature – should we implement it? ● Are we focusing our limited resources on the right goal? ● Do our focus and efforts bring results? Bring enough results? Bring timely results? ● How do we know which goals are important and which ones should we focus on next? ● How do we align people and resources around organizational goals so that they have a better understanding of why we do what we do and what is coming next? A comprehensive and well thought out impact map will help you ask and answer these and many other questions that are integral to the success of your client’s enterprise. What Can You Use It For? The beauty of impact mapping is its simplicity and versatility. With limited effort you can help clients get new clarity around a variety of issues.  Of course, since most of the Agile practice revolves around creating complex products, that is the area where impact mapping is arguably applied most. As described below it can be used for greenfield initiatives, where your clients might only have vague ideas of what goals they are trying to achieve with the proposed products and solutions. At the same time, those undertakings that are well underway can benefit from impact mapping as well.  

---

## Page 78

 
78 
Alex has successfully applied impact mapping to bring clarity to product development in information technology, financial industry, oil and gas, and education. While it is convenient and expedient to only involve product owners in this exercise, there are immense benefits in including the whole product development team. Remember the saying, “no one resisted the change they helped co-design.” Getting input and involving your developers at this level will help the organization to design better products and benefit from the creativity and ingenuity of smart and highly paid people. Alex also ran a workshop for an executive team that was during transforming its development organization which included product, development, and operations. They were looking for better ways of supporting their teams on this complex journey. They started with a goal of successful transformation and defined a few metrics that would allow them to make important financial and organizational decisions as they were progressing with the initiative.  Then, they mapped out all the involved actors. In this case not only those were the teams directly involved in the transformation, but also executives from other functions, such as finance, marketing, and talent management, that were crucial to the success of the transformation. Identifying actors whose actions potentially would make or break the undertaking gave this executive team a lot of clarity and focus into actions they should be implementing to ensure the success of the whole initiative. Having identified all these external actors helped the executive team to empathize better with those individuals. They were able to better understand their involvement in the process and design their own actions. Some of those actions were more involvement, regular communications and updates, and better transparency to help move this complex initiative forward. The last example is probably the most interesting. A team Alex worked with used impact mapping to understand what it takes to be a high performing team. Their goal was defined as, “to become a high performing team,” and they identified a few metrics that would tell them they met their goal. These were delivery, learning, and relationship targets that would validate the team to be high performing according to its members. What the team decided to do next was different from a standard impact mapping approach. They identified each team member as an actor, as well as broad groups of members, such as designers, ops, and developers. Working diligently through actions of these actors towards the goal of becoming a high performing team revealed a lot to the team. It revealed the way its individual members were thinking and yielded several very fruitful conversations about how the team operated. Ultimately, it helped all the team members understand their present state and what it would take to get where they all wanted to be. In the last step the team co-designed together actions that would help individuals and functional groups within the team be more effective and focused on their way of becoming a high-performing team. Alex shares, “My sincere hope is that after reading this detailed description of the impact mapping technique and getting some ideas from the use cases about how you can coach teams through it, this tool will take its rightful place in your Agile coaching tool belt.”  
Chapter 11: Designing Well Formed Outcomes   

---

## Page 79

 
79 
The Problem – Remedy – Outcome Coaching Sequence   Individuals, teams, and organizations alike struggle to work towards clear outcomes. This is not for a reason that those clear outcomes are hard to imagine or come by. It is primarily for the reason of the hectic and ever-changing environment they operate in. Sometimes in the hustle and bustle of the day we start mistaking efficiency for effectiveness.  Alex shares, “I cannot tell you how many times I heard from my clients, ‘I need to use every minute of my day with purpose, I need to get more efficient with using my time.’  As I am listening to another client saying that I become immensely curious, what this efficiency will give them and what difference it will make in their lives? At the same time, the most effective individuals, teams, and organizations I met, worked with, and coached were laser focused on being effective in everything they do, whether mundane day-to-day tasks, or striving in achieving long-term goals. Hustle and bustle were their sworn enemies, and they did everything in their power to keep those enemies at bay. Only after succeeding at that, through a variety of ways, like automation for development teams, effective delegation for executives, clear understanding of objectives to be achieved through well-defined key results, could they effectively focus on what matters.” The most effective teams are always able to verbalize and, most importantly, visualize their goals and outcomes. Sensory acuity, which will be discussed a bit later, is one of the key factors to creating and maintaining well-formed outcomes. By hearing, seeing, and feeling the results of their labor teams capable of realizing the importance of the tasks at hand and goals ahead of them.  So where do you start as coaches? You start where your clients are, of course. Go to their bus stop and invite them to take a ride! And what kind of a ride is that? It depends on what you hear in their communication. This chapter introduces a simple technique that you can use with your clients to help them clearly understand their goals and the outcomes these goals will bring about. Active listening is an integral part of any coaching engagement, session, and a core competency of any professional coach. By using focused and global listening you hear not only what is said, but also what is communicated. You hear the details that words cannot portray fairly and accurately. Luckily, this technique does not require you to listen on those higher levels, all you need to be doing is listening intently to what is said.  PRO - Problem, Remedy, Outcomes Method Imagine your client comes to a session and vents, “They never listen! Or they only listen to themselves. They talk, talk, talk non-stop, and never get to doing anything. That is totally pathetic.” Think for a moment about what you hear. Venting – of course, frustration, moaning, and a few other words come to mind. You hear a lot of displeasure and things they don’t like. Things are pathetic! Notice what is missing – a desire or wish to change anything, something. Of course, you hear that unspoken desire, “I want them to just stop talking!” however it would be a stretch to assume that those are the desires of your client. 

---

## Page 80

 
80 
 Everything that is happening there is happening now, and, probably, happening constantly, hence so much frustration and almost exasperation! What you hear there is the manifestation and description of a problem. The problem that your client identified (and supposedly wants to get rid of).  Here’s another similar example, “I don’t need their attitude! I am sick and tired of going round and round! They never listen, and when they do, they do not do what I want. Ever!” Sounds like a problem. However, at this point you don’t know what your client wants, if anything, besides to vent. You can help your client start untangling this problem by asking a simple and a very powerful question that utilizes a variation of a Clean Language10 question and technique.  Client: I don’t need their attitude! I am sick and tired of their antics! Coach: And when that happens, what would you like to happen instead? (Well, to be fair, if you are coaching that client, you probably would start with acknowledging their frustration, but that’s a different topic.) Notice what this question does. It shifts the client's focus from the complaints to the direction of her desires. A pure Clean Language question would have sounded like, “And when, attitude, and their antics, what would you like to have happened?” Weird, isn’t it? Let’s not do it. A simple plain language variation works better in an organizational setting. What you are asking here, in a polite and non-dismissive way, is for the client to describe their desires and wishes. If the client is at the problem level, they are not ready to be thinking about outcomes, so you are asking for a solution to their problem, for a remedy. Client: I just want them to shut up for a change and listen for once! Great, this is progress. The client is verbalizing the remedy they want. They are describing what the alternative might be like. In that world, their colleagues, family members, friends, or some other people would stop doing all the talking and start listening instead.  The client doesn’t always start a conversation with the problem. Sometimes they might start a conversation with a remedy. They might start like this, “I want to talk about my colleagues who are always talking and never listen, and I want them to shut up and listen for a change!” When you hear a remedy, you will instantly recognize a desire, need, or want to have more or less of something – more of being listened to in this case, or more of their colleagues keeping quiet, or less of their colleagues incessant talking! Also notice that the client manages to squeeze the problem and a solution in the same sentence. And while doing that, they imagine some future in which their desired state will be achieved. What is missing here though are the results and deeper understanding of that future state. In the desired future, the colleagues will be talking less and listening more. The big coaching question still waiting for an answer is, “So... what?”  The client is still a step away from stating the outcomes they desire clearly. And your job is to help them take that last step. Here is another modified Clean Language question to use to move them forward,  10 If you want to learn more about Clean Language, its roots, uses, and structures, we recommend Clean Language: Revealing Metaphors and Opening Minds by Wendy Sullivan and Judy Rees.   

---

## Page 81

 
81 
Client: I want them to stop talking and talking and start listening! Coach: And when they stop talking and start listening, then what happens? Another version of this question would ask, “And when they stop talking and start listening, what happens next?” You can use whichever version sounds better to you. Notice what this question does. It assumes that the desired state is reached. Now what? What do you experience there? What is different for you? What is it like for you to be in your desired state? Now the client can start thinking about what is possible when the desired state is achieved, and what might their next steps be.  Coach: And when they stop talking and start listening, then what happens? Client: Well, hm, then those on the teams who have opinions but had no chance to voice them will be heard and because of that we might be able to make better decisions! Now your client starts talking! Now they are realizing what is there, beyond their wish for those people to stop talking and how that will benefit them and those around them. This is the outcome they want. Not perfect, and not well-formed, but still an outcome that provides much more clarity and awareness to your client than an emotionally charged problem or a wish lacking a clear understanding of the consequences. This outcome contains a desire for something beneficial for your client, their environment, and those in it. This outcome projects in the future and, most importantly, it lacks any reference to the problem.  Having helped your client to arrive at this outcome you can help them to gain more clarity around it, and that we will discuss in a bit. What is also possible here is to create a loop with solutions and guide your client through a decision-making routine. Having a clear outcome enables your client to step back, put aside the remedy they came up with, and think about other solutions, actions, and decisions they can take to achieve the desired outcome. The coach can help them flesh out the desired outcomes using another set of Clean Language questions, such as, And when you are making better decisions, what kind of better is that better? Is there anything else about those better decisions? Those better decisions are like what? Again, these are modified Clean Language questions and do not follow the original questions word for word or observe the prescribed structure. However, these are more suited for working with your enterprise client in a professional environment without weirding them out.  And while these questions are powerful and will serve their purpose of bringing more clarity and understanding of the outcome to your client, there’s a better and a more powerful way to coach around outcomes. That way is to make them well formed.  Developing Well-Formed Outcomes  A well-formed outcome is defined as a desired future state that is specific, measurable, and achievable. A well-formed outcome can be incorporated into a business plan to create a timeline and guide day-to-

---

## Page 82

 
82 
day work. Well-formed outcomes can also be used for personal goals such as setting up new habits or changing long standing behaviors. Coaches in their natural state of engaged neutrality are uniquely equipped to help their clients, whether individual or groups, to define, shape, and achieve these outcomes. To put it plainly, a well-formed outcome is forward looking, defined clearly and plainly, and free of “muddy thinking.” You can use well-formed outcomes as a tool to help your client create positive change in their lives. If they have a goal that they want to achieve, it's important to clearly define what it is they want to accomplish and how they are going to do it. A well-formed outcome creates the necessary clarity by helping them focus on what needs to happen for the goal to be achieved. Well-formed outcomes are SMART and PURE11. You probably have heard about SMART goals before. It is an acronym that specifies the qualities a goal should have. In a variety of sources these components are referred to as Specific, Measurable, Achievable, Relevant, and Time-Bound. PURE is an additional concept. By bringing it about and working through it we can help turn SMART goals into well-formed outcomes. PURE includes the following components, Positive terms, Under Control, Right sized and doable, and Ecological. As you might be noticing, the techniques being described in this chapter will call upon many core coaching competencies outlined by the International Coaching Federation since these are Professional Coaching methods that can be utilized in your work as an Agile Coach. By bringing professional coaching competencies like active listening, direct communication, and coaching presence into your work as an Agile Coach you are better positioned to help your client have the strongest outcomes possible from the coaching engagement.  As you build out your outcomes with your client keep in mind that it’s more of a circular than linear process. You might circle back and make updates to a part you’ve already covered, and you might cover multiple parts in one step.  
   11  https://www.peterlangebrands.com/blog/smart-is-not-pure 


---

## Page 83

 
83 
 To ensure that your outcomes are well-formed there are nine conditions that the outcomes must meet: 1. Stated in positive terms (focuses on what you want -not what you don’t want) 2. As specific as possible 3. Measurable through sensory and evidence-based conditions 4. Within your control (Something you can do. Not what you want someone else to do) 5. Considers the context in which the outcome should be true (has appropriate scope) 6. Must have access to the resources required to reach the outcome (time, budget, people, supplies, tools, etc.) 7. Preserves existing benefits  8. Ecologically sound 9. Defined first steps  Conditions #1: Well-formed outcomes are stated in positive terms  Do not mistake the “positive terms'' condition for dragging the clients down the warm and fuzzy road of thinking positively. This is different. Stating an outcome in positive terms provides clarity of thought around the desire our clients have. Language has a great power over our ways of thinking. Do a mental experiment. Concentrate and tell yourself, “Do not think of a pink elephant.” If you are brutally honest with yourself, have you had a fleeting image of a weird pink elephant going through your mind? If not, you are in the minority. What we do by stating our wishes in negative terms, we make our minds and ourselves to think about the things that we don’t want. We inadvertently keep them front and center, thus making it so much harder to get them out of sight, out of mind. Suppose you want to forget the pink elephant and focus on thinking about a purple gorilla. Instead of telling you, “Do not think about a pink elephant.” (Negative term) It is more effective to tell you, “Think about a purple gorilla.” (Positive term) The same rule applies to parenting. Oftentimes parents tell their children what they don’t want them to do (and inadvertently put ideas in their minds), rather than making their wishes clear through verbalizing what the desired behaviors and outcomes are. In NLP12, this concept is referred to as towards and away from language patterns. Let’s get back to coaching though. By now, you hopefully realize that stating outcomes in unequivocally positive terms will help your clients move forward without unneeded distractions. An interesting parallel here can be drawn with an exercise a lot of Agile coaches (and trainers) are doing – Stop/Start/More of/Less of. Alex says, “I would argue that each and every entry on the stop list should have a matching entry on the Start list, otherwise the request will have a problem and no remedy or outcome.” PRO13 coaching is also highly applicable in a situation where your client states the problem, or outcomes in a negative, or “away from” terms. The questions like, “When you don't have [x] what would you like to have happen?” or “What would you like to have instead?” will help to provide a much needed reframe to the outcome your client desires and focus their mind on the goal ahead of them rather than something undesirable they are trying to get away from. Here are some examples of positive reframes,  12 Neuro-Linguistic Programming 13 https://cleanlanguage.co.uk/articles/blogs/104/The-Evolution-of-the-Problem-Remedy-Outcome-PRO-Model.html 

---

## Page 84

 
84 
Instead of: “We need to do a round of layoffs” Try: “We need to increase our margins” Instead of: “We need to stop missing the deadlines” Try: “We need to deliver when our customers expect” Instead of: “Our retrospectives are a snooze fest of what went well, what went wrong, what needs to be changed” Try: “Let’s get engagement back into retrospectives” The process of helping a client develop well-formed outcomes is a coaching process. We suggest that you explain the concept of well-formed outcomes to the client and make it transparent rather than trying to pick their outcomes from the conversation. You want to have them take ownership of developing the outcomes while you coach them through the process of thinking about the outcomes they want to achieve. The outcomes the client wants to achieve must be outcomes within their own control. For example, if the client wants “to make sure my sister can be a doctor” this is not in the control of the client. The sister is the one who must want to be a doctor and do the work it takes to get there. When you dig in further the client likely wants to provide her sister with the opportunities and support, she needs to go to medical school. This is something the client can control. We’ll go deeper into Condition #2 a little further down the road. We’ll follow three examples through the process so you can see how they might be formulated. We will assume that these clients are walking through the PRO process and having a coaching conversation that’s not being recorded in the example. Keep in mind that the examples shown here are simplistic and your client’s world will be very different and likely more complex. It may take a few sessions to work through developing outcomes; especially if they are very large and end up having several sub-outcomes that they will need to accomplish along the way to their ultimate end state. Notice how the outcomes morph as we walk through the relevant conditions and refine them.  *********Grey Box***********  Conditions #1: Stated in positive terms  Examples:  Individual Client:  Problem - College is expensive and I don't want my children to go into debt to get an education. Remedy - Make sure there is money available to pay for their education, so they don’t need loans Outcome - I want to be able to fully fund up to three college educations by the time my children reach college age.  Organizational Leadership Team Client:  

---

## Page 85

 
85 
Problem - Our budget for consulting is way overspent. We’re bleeding money. We will not survive another 3 years if we don’t do something soon. Remedy - Bring the consulting talent in-house and stop paying external consultants. Outcome - We want to increase our free cash flow (FCF) via budget controls  Organizational Team-level Client: Problem - Our budget for consultant coaches is being cut. We need coaches and our funding will be cut over the next three years by ¼ of our current allocation. Remedy - We need to build an in-house coaching program and bring the coaching talent in house so we won’t be so dependent on consultants. Outcome - We want to develop in-house coaching competency that can support the company’s coaching needs. **********Grey Box***********     Conditions 2 & 3: Specific and are measurable by Sensory Acuity   Specificity of the desired outcome is a key to be able to start on a path of bringing about those outcomes. When the desired outcome is defined in clear terms, our clients will better know what actions they need to be taking to achieve them. A favorite quote from the Cheshire Cat fits well here, “If you don’t know where you are going, any road will take you there.” It does not matter which actions you are taking, or which decisions you are making, if you don’t have a clear outcome in mind.  One method of getting the outcomes more specific is to use NLP techniques to deal with 3 chief reasons for Meta Model Violations – Generalizations, Distortions, and Deletions14. We won't go into detail about all the techniques that are applicable here, you can find much-detailed discussions in fantastic works by NLP creators Bandler and Grinder and others. Another way to achieve required specificity and measurability of the outcomes is to help the clients achieve a requisite level of sensory acuity. A person's level of sensory acuity is equal to the capacity they must be acutely aware through their senses.  Human beings have five well-defined and studied representational systems, ways that experiences are represented in our minds, that comprise an acronym VAKOG. That stands for Visual, Auditory, Kinesthetic, Olfactory, and Gustatory. In the corporate world people tend to communicate through the first three. However olfactory and gustatory systems might come into play with well-formed outcomes if the client perceives the world best through them. They may also come into play if smell and taste are an  14   https://www.nlpworld.co.uk/nlp-training-meta-model/ 

---

## Page 86

 
86 
important part of the environment in which the client functions. For example, you might coach a restaurant owner, pastry chef, perfume maker, medical professional, police officer, cleaning professional, etc.  Every human being has a preferred representational system (in rarer case two of them). The core idea of NLP – neuro-linguistic programming – is that our words, and language as a whole, is a great window into our inner world, and specifically, the way we perceive it. Our communication betrays the preferred representational system we use in our daily lives. You, as a coach, will serve your client well if you recognize their preferred representational system and form your interventions, questions, and thoughts through the lens of that system. Developing a well-formed outcome, in other words involves helping your clients realize how to identify measurable progress through their representational systems – VACOG – what the world around them will, correspondingly, look, sound, and feel like when they arrive at their intended destination. This process is called achieving sensory acuity of the outcome. The questions that you might explore with your clients here will be, “What will you see differently when you are done?” “When this is achieved, what will you hear others saying?” “What will it feel like to finally arrive at this outcome?” Depending on the person and the nature of the work being done with the client, you might also ask, “What will you smell in the air when you achieve this?” “What will you taste when this is complete?”   Do not skimp on this important work, however awkward it might feel for you and your client. Having a strong sensory acuity of the desired outcome not only provides a clear understanding of what it might look like when implemented, but also serves as an additional motivator (or in rare cases demotivator) for your client to move forward. Creating these mental images of success helps them to continuously connect to what it will be like when they have achieved the outcomes they want.   *********Grey Box***********  Conditions: #2 Specific - #3 Measurable by Sensory Acuity  Individual Client:  Positive Outcome - I want my children to have a fully funded college education  Specific - I want to have enough money saved in my children’s college fund for them to pay all their tuition, books, and boarding for up to 6 years at a state college.  Measurable -  ● I will feel at peace when it is time for them to start shopping for colleges ● I won’t worry about how my children will pay for college while they are in high school ● I won’t fear that if my children don’t get scholarships or grants, they won’t be able to go to school ● I will see all three of my children graduate from college ● I will hear their names called at graduation ceremonies ● I will see that they have no student loans to pay off 

---

## Page 87

 
87 
● I will feel proud when they are able to start their adult lives with no education debt  Organizational Leadership Team Client:  Positive Outcome - We want to increase our free cash flow (FCF)  Specific - We want to increase our free cash flow by 12% over the next three years by redirecting the current consulting budget to FCF.  Measurable -  ● We will observe the annual budget and expenditure for consulting reduce yearly for a period of three years ● We will see our FTE cost rise for roles formerly held by consultants increase at a rate less than our current consulting costs ● We will hear that the competency of our staff is comparable to that of the current consultants ● We will feel relieved when we don’t have huge consulting bills to pay  Organizational Team-level Client: Positive Outcome - We need in house coaching competency that can support the company’s coaching needs. Specific - We will have a highly competent centralized coaching function that has the responsibility for the hiring, development, and alignment for coaching needs throughout the company without dependence on external consultants. Measurable -  ● We will hear the departments with coaches say that the quality of our internal coaches is comparable to that of the consultant coaches ● We will see that we have proper job descriptions, hiring practices, and compensation in place to hire competent coaches ● We will feel confident that we can serve the company’s coaching needs well when we have experienced coaches on staff ● We will experience the training and program being in operation and will see newer coaches building competency over time  **********Grey Box*********** Well-formed outcomes should be attractive and motivational   There’s nothing new or groundbreaking about the concept that goals should be attractive and motivating. And motivation is hard. Time and time again we see clients setting lofty goals that sound great in theory. However, those goals are simply attractive words without a clear destination and solid 

---

## Page 88

 
88 
steps towards implementing them. Accomplishing any worthwhile goal requires effort via the expenditure of mental, physical, and psychological energies. Human beings are programmed by nature to conserve all those energies, if possible, for those critical moments where such expenditure is highly warranted, i.e., running away from a saber tooth tiger or a bear. Hopefully none of the goals you will encounter with your clients include those animals unless you are coaching zoo or national park personnel. That leaves you and your client with the fundamental question of where they should go to find that motivation; what might compel their pre-programmed nature to expend precious energy and move towards achieving the goal. There is no clear-cut answer to this important question. However, there are a few techniques you can deploy to help the client find their real motivation.  Perceptual Positions Coaching Since the well-formed outcomes concept comes from the field of neuro-linguistic programming, let’s first look at what that field has to offer us in terms of motivation.   We have already talked about the sensory acuity that allows the client to “experience” their desired outcomes by utilizing their representational system modalities. You can also help them to experience their desired end state by utilizing perceptual positions to understand what it will be like when they have success from multiple perspectives, theirs, others involved, neutral third parties, and the larger system. ● First perceptual position is the client and deals with how the client sees, feels, and acts in this specific situation.  ● Second perceptual position is that of the client’s opponent. The word opponent is used quite loosely here to signify any other party to the situation, conversation, conflict, or other association. The second perceptual position deals with the perception of the other party in the same situation, including the way they perceive the client’s behaviors and contributions to it.  As you coach the client through the second perceptual position, encourage them to disassociate themselves from their own position in the situation and associate with their opponent. This will enable them to engage in more empathy and awareness of the situation.  ● Third perceptual position is that of an independent observer. We ask our client to disassociate themselves from all the parties of the situation, take the stance of a fly on the wall, and see it through the eyes of those who have no stake in the game. What does the situation look like if you were to look at it from a side and had no interest in resolving it one way or another?  ● Fourth perceptual position deals with the larger system as it views all the positions from afar at the same time and can observe things like the micro and macro impacts to the wider system. Thus, asking the client to take various positions not only allows them to understand the situation, in this case their goal more fully, but also find those points they like and dislike about them. As the client's awareness grows, they might come across some very attractive qualities of the situation and discover the real motivation they want to employ to achieve these goals.  As you and the client talk through these representational systems and perceptual positions, it is important to take time to have the client reflect on what new insights they have. They will want to take some of these insights forward to use them and others they want to dispose of.  Values Coaching 

---

## Page 89

 
89 
Values coaching is another tool you can deploy to help the client fully understand or even find their motivation. Connecting the goals to their values, or, even better, core values – those that are core to their being, is a powerful way to help the client see whether the goal is worth their time and energy to pursue. It would be a shame for the client to set their site on the goal, invest considerable resources and then realize that the goal and the state that results from its implementation contradict their values. In the context of Agile Coaching, the client can be an individual or a system such as a group of people, a team, an organization, or the whole company. When we work with systems, they are considered a single entity. Cherie refers to these as multi-person clients. Values work goes directly to the top of the Dilts Pyramid15 of Logical Levels, not only touching upon the values and beliefs, but, most likely, to the very sense of self, identity, and purpose of the human being (or organization) in front of you. Working at this level is both impactful and fulfilling, as the changes at the top of the pyramid – to the purpose, identity, values, beliefs – will almost inevitably result in a chain reaction and many changes at the bottom levels of the pyramid – environment, behaviors, and competencies and capabilities.  As we are working with organizations, the question of motivation becomes more pronounced and acute, since it is the system – the team, the department, and the organization itself that must align around a set of motivational goals. That system has many variables and parts that do not all think, feel, or behave alike. While coaching the system, the coach needs to zoom out and observe the system a bit out of focus. That allows the coach to step back from hearing many different voices and get a sense of the agreement or disagreement that emerges in the system.  As an Agile coach, the values you coach around might be corporate values and how the outcomes the client desires are out of alignment with those values. When working with sub-systems, you might coach around organizational values or Agile values and principles.   Conditions # 4, 5 & 6: Under client’s control, content and scope, resources To be well-formed, the outcomes must be completely under client’s control. The client should have the power, authority, and control to be able to implement the actions and realize the benefits of the outcomes. The desired outcomes cannot be dependent on someone else’s performance that is outside of the client’s control.  At the same time the client should be the one who initiated the outcome and is fully invested in benefits it will provide.  Earlier we mentioned this example of an individual’s outcome being under their own control: If the client wants “to make sure my children graduate from the best colleges in the country” this is not in the control of the client. The children are the ones who must do the work it takes to get there. When you dig in further the client likely wants to provide her children with the opportunities and support, they need to go to the best colleges in the country. This is something the client can control.  Generating under control and self-initiated outcomes is probably one of the hairiest parts of the well-formed outcomes when used at the organizational or team level. As you might see this approach can present a few challenges when working with organizations and teams within those organizations. When  15   https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z87fGbtSJFY  

---

## Page 90

 
90 
you are working at the enterprise level, self-initiation is less of an issue. At the very top level the initiatives are mostly self-initiated. Strategic initiatives are developed at the enterprise level and flow down to the organizations & teams who must decide what they will do to contribute to the execution of the strategy.  Even at the enterprise level, the problem to solve, improvement to make, or outcomes they decide to pursue will not likely be completely self-initiated. The desired outcomes are more likely to be responses to market forces, external factors, or may be internal initiatives that seem to be useful for moving the client in the right direction. There can be an infinite number of sources that serve as a motivation and inspiration for the client to embark on this journey.  What self-initiation means is that the client employs their own agency and willingness to design and put their energy and resources behind the goals and outcomes they desire. Well-formed outcomes can’t be designed and handed over to someone to achieve. They must be generated by the person wanting the outcomes and who will be the one doing the work. This goes to the heart of the motivation discussed previously. If outcomes can’t be handed down for someone to do, the conflict between organizational goals and internal teams trying to create their own well-formed outcomes must be resolved. When the goals and outcomes are created by someone else in the C-suite, it seems to be a non-starter for the teams to adopt the intended outcomes as their own well-formed ones.  This is really a question of considering the context of the outcomes. The C-Suite in this case must decide if the outcomes (and likely sub outcomes since enterprise initiatives are generally very large) are to be fully under C-Suite control and how far the scope of the work they want to do will extend. They get to decide the boundaries. If this was an individual and their goal was to be more courageous and outspoken, they would get to decide the scope. Who do they want to be more courageous and outspoken with? In what context is the outcome to be true. Do they want to be more courageous and outspoken every day and with everyone they encounter, or just with certain people and in certain circumstances? For the C-Suite they decide how much control they want and how far that control reaches.  There are two possible scenarios in play: Let's say the C-Suite develops a set of well-formed outcomes they want to achieve. They outline sub-outcomes that need to be met if the ultimate outcomes are to be achieved.  In Scenario #1 The C-Suite decides that they want to have full control of the entire set of activities and sub-outcomes that need to be met to achieve the ultimate outcome. While this is a very expensive and very slow way to work, it puts them in control, and they own the outcomes. In this case, the team is handed a well-formed outcome (sub-outcome) and told to execute it. The team didn’t design the outcome; they are simply a necessary resource needed to execute the tasks needed to arrive at the intended outcome. In this case, because the team does not own the outcome and doesn’t have control, they become just a tool to complete the work. There likely won’t be tons of motivation to see the ultimate outcome achieved - they may not even fully understand the larger picture of the outcome. There is likely to be lots of rework and change controls. There will probably be a lot of process and reporting required. [Sounds a bit like a waterfall project, doesn’t it?] The team will be motivated to do their own part so they can be successful but aren’t very invested in the higher goals. In this scenario the team does not have well-formed outcomes. They have orders from above that they are following so 

---

## Page 91

 
91 
someone else can achieve their own well-formed outcomes. In this scenario the context (scope) is under the C-Suite’s purview. They have decided that the scope of the outcomes they want to achieve are all subject to the control of the C-Suite and they have not limited their involvement.  Scenario 2: The C-Suite determines the outcomes and identifies the sub-outcomes that need to be true to get the outcomes they desire. They decide that they will present the sub-outcomes to the organizations/teams in their purview and problems for the organizations/teams to solve. In this case, the team will be handed the sub-outcomes and given control to figure out how they will solve the challenge. In this case, the teams will be well served by creating their own version of outcomes that they can stand behind fully, that have all the attributes of well-formed outcomes and that drive the organization towards the bigger goals. As each team creates their own outcomes that will contribute to the ultimate outcome of the company, they each have autonomy to solve the problems they take ownership of. The alignment of results, expectations, and ways of working while keeping the bigger enterprise outcome in mind and checking their results against it will keep various work groups from veering off course.  Which brings us to another challenge that we see in our work with organizations throughout industries – work dependencies. The well-formed outcomes approach is clear – in order for the outcome to be well-formed those who desire and work towards such an outcome should not be dependent on anyone else but themselves to get to the outcome.  Dependencies The moment they identify any dependency on an outside intervention they don’t have control over, they need to reconsider the scope, approach, methods of achieving the outcome or the combination thereof. A lot of best and brightest Agile minds are fighting with the ideas of identifying and managing dependencies and a few major approaches are emerging. Examples of these approaches include managing the dependencies (SAFe), eliminating the dependencies (Scrum@Scale), and restructuring (LeSS). A masterful Agile coach can be indispensable during the process of realizing, surfacing, identifying, and shedding light on such dependencies. Identifying dependencies requires a concerted effort from teams, stakeholders, and organizations. Some dependencies exist on the organizational level and working within their parameters or mitigating their impact sometimes requires involvement of the management up to the C-suite.  An example of such dependency might be an outdated or excessively rigid funding process for the initiatives whereas the approval process might involve multiple levels of stakeholders. Such a process does not lend itself to any well-formed outcome to be built around it.  Sometimes dependencies come from the way the work is structured and sequenced and the way the teams are organized. For example, along the application tiers rather than around the functionality of the product. A few scaling frameworks exist that suggest ways of dealing with those dependencies. Be wary of frameworks that suggest managing dependencies. What they offer is to keep the status quo, regardless of its origin and meaning intact   

---

## Page 92

 
92 
*********Grey Box***********  Conditions # 4, 5 & 6: Under client’s control, content and scope, resources Individual Client:  Specific Outcome - I want to have enough money saved in my children’s college fund for them to pay all their tuition, books, and boarding for up to 6 years at a state college.  [Updated] Controlled, scoped, resourced specific outcome: have a fully funded education reserve that will be available to pay any remaining tuition, books, room, and board at a state college after scholarships and grants are applied for any of my children who decide to attend college. [Updated] Measurable and Evidence Based -  ● I will feel at peace when it is time for them to start shopping for colleges ● I won’t worry about how my children will pay for college while they are in high school ● I won’t fear that if my children don’t get scholarships or grants, they won’t be able to go to school ● I will see all three of my children graduate from college ● I will hear their names called at graduation ceremonies ● I will see that they have no student loans to pay off ● I will feel proud when they are able to start their adult lives with no education debt ● The account has a minimum of 80k available for each child by their Senior year of High School. ● The account is held in an interest-bearing low risk investment account that is accessible for education expenses. Organizational Leadership Team Client:  Specific Outcome - We want to increase our free cash flow by 12% over the next three years by redirecting the current consulting budget to FCF.  [Updated] Controlled, scoped, resourced specific outcome: have a fully developed financial strategy and sufficient economic stability to ensure company viability in fluctuating market conditions.  [Updated] Measurable and Evidence Based -  ● We will observe that the annual budget is in alignment with our business strategy ● We will observe an increase in internal talent and staffing budget that coincides with a decrease in consulting services ● We will hear that the competency of our staff is comparable to that of the current consultants ● We will feel relieved when we have a budget focuses funds on the most valuable work reduces cost to non-performing  ● Company capital will be at a minimum of “_____” ● Company FCF will be at a minimum of “_____”  ● We will hear and observe that the quality of our products is not reduced as efficiencies are developed.  

---

## Page 93

 
93 
Organizational Team-level Client: Specific Outcome - We will have a highly competent centralized coaching function that has the responsibility for the hiring, development, and alignment for coaching needs throughout the company without dependence on external consultants. [Updated] Controlled, scoped, resourced specific outcome: Measurable ● We will hear the departments with coaches say that the quality of our internal coaches is comparable to that of the consultant coaches ● We will see that we have proper job descriptions, hiring practices, and compensation in place to hire competent coaches ● We will feel confident that we can serve the company’s coaching needs well when we have experienced coaches on staff ● We will experience the training and program being in operation and will see newer coaches building competency over time **********Grey Box***********  Conditions #3 & 7 - Measurable and Preserves Existing Benefits   While measures are essential to achieving any goal, be it personal, professional, or organizational, sometimes it is exceedingly hard to pinpoint those relevant metrics and measurements the client needs to be monitoring and taking measure of. Partially that is because the client lacks the clear understanding of the goal (sensory acuity), partly because the argument “you cannot measure what you do not have” (which is a fallacy), and partly, because other conditions of the well-formedness of the outcome are not met.  For coaching, an easy way to begin figuring out the complicated question of measurements might be to start with what the client measures right now. However, the bigger question is how current measurements, or the system of measurements is serving the client. It always behooves you to keep in mind the Goodhart’s law,16 “When a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure.”  Imagine the organization wants to increase its profit margins, which can be achieved in a multitude of ways. The company, for example, might consider raising prices targeting the top line, or cut expenses, affecting the bottom line. They need to be mindful of unintended consequences that will almost inevitably raise their collective ugly head.  Raising the prices without consideration of the market conditions might lead to customer dissatisfaction and market share loss. Cutting cost is often fraught with either cutting too much or cutting where it hurts which might shift people's attention away from the goal of increasing margin and lead to a cycle of perverse incentives and motivations.  
 16    https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goodhart%27s_law#cite_note-4  

---

## Page 94

 
94 
Focusing your measurements narrowly and depriving people of the ways to see the bigger picture in which this motivation and its consequences play out, might result in effects that are opposite to those the company is seeking in the first place.  Well-Formed outcomes address this challenge through its condition of “Preserving Existing Benefits.” While developing outcomes, discuss with the client the things that are working well now that they don’t want to lose by achieving the new outcomes. The client needs to measure the aspects that will tell them they are achieving the desired results and they need to ensure that they are not losing the existing benefits they have when starting. If the outcome is stated in a way that, if achieved, endangers existing benefits, another revision is needed. They may also need to include preserving certain benefits into the measures of success so they can be monitored alongside other data.  In the case presented above, where the company had a very narrowly focused outcome of increasing its profit margins. If this narrow profit margin creates conditions that will endanger existing conditions the client wants to preserve, they may want to broaden the outcome.  It is likely that the real outcome the client is trying to achieve is ensuring company viability during the stormy market times. Increasing profit margin is just one solution to this broader outcome. By making the outcome broader, it provides space for multiple viable solutions to arise which can be evaluated for preserving existing benefits before committing to them. Measuring Outcomes There are a few comprehensive approaches to measuring the outcomes. While these approaches have books dedicated to describing their purposes and uses, two are worth mentioning here. The first approach is OKRs – Objectives and Key Results. This method is attributed to Andy Grove of Intel as the creator and was popularized by John Doerr in his book “Measure What Matters17”. This method is often used poorly and even abused when not clearly understood so we recommend that you read more about them and clearly understand them before simply jumping in with the client. When used well, this method can be very effective. Another approach pioneered by Scrum.org is Evidence Based Management (EBM) that was fashioned by Ken Schwaber after the evidence-based medicine that is used widely by healthcare professionals.  Regardless of which approach your client chooses to employ, you will serve them well by encouraging them to continuously inspect and adapt the measurement system they decide to put in place. Measures should not be set in stone, and they should not be so fluid as to render any measurable observations and resulting data meaningless. The client will be served well by striking the balance between having a few well-defined measures they want to keep an eye on, and the flexibility of their mindset with an understanding that as they progress towards the set goal, they should be willing (and able) to inspect the existing measures and maybe adopt new ones as they become available.  Condition #8 Ecologically Sound  To relieve your mind, let's start with clarity that ecologically sound is not referring to things like climate change.  In well-formed outcomes, ecologically sound means that you help the client to zoom out and take the 30,000 feet view to inspect how it will be affected by the desired outcome. In doing this you  17   https://www.amazon.com/Measure-What-Matters-Google-Foundation/dp/0525536221  

---

## Page 95

 
95 
help them take a wider system view through the lens of success to understand the ripple effects achieving the desired outcome will have in the full ecology of the system. (Or in the case of an individual, their lives.)  Think about it as checking for the unintended consequences. Unintended consequences can behave much like a body of water does when a boat passes through. Sometimes the boat glides over the water and creates small ripples that wander up to the shoreline. Other times the boat creates a wake that can overturn other boats and flood the shoreline. Unintended consequences aren’t inherently bad. Sometimes they are good and positive and reinforce the determination to implement the outcome. Sometimes they are just consequences where the client stays neutral to their existence and just notices them and moves on. And sometimes these consequences might be negative. Identifying these negative consequences when creating outcomes enables you to adjust and avoid them.  Alex shares, “I heard a fascinating story from the old days of Hewlett Packard, when that great company still bore the names of its founders and cared more about its innovation and world impact than its shareholders and the bottom line. Every day, mid-morning a coffee cart was driven through the office space. All the engineers stopped their work and came to grab some coffee. Then one of the costs cutting initiatives hit and the coffee carts were eliminated. The spirit of innovation in the company has never been the same again. Of course, it is a stretch to claim that Hewlett Packard became less innovative and more boring a corporation just because the coffee carts disappeared. However, what the decision makers did not take into the account was that the coffee breaks were opportunities for the engineers to get together, discuss, share, and debate the innovative ideas that propelled the company forward. With coffee carts these important informal opportunities that spread innovation were gone and the spirit was lost.”  When implementing a major (or minor) change you will frequently be hyper focused on the change itself and on the future state desired. What is usually missing is a different point of view that can be brought about by a single question, “How does the current state serve us?” There is almost a knee jerk reaction to answer that it does not. Are you crazy? It is horrible, that is why we are implementing these changes. But if you help your clients overcome this first knee jerk reaction, slow down and think, they will realize that there are positive sides to the current state, policy, process, or way of doing business. Remember that people always do their best with the resources and knowledge they have available. Those who designed the current state were not evil or ignorant. They likely had noble goals and outcomes in mind. What will not serve your clients best is throwing out all the present state and replacing it with something completely new. Waterfall is not evil, it worked for the companies and organizations for decades, maybe not as optimally and effectively as it could have, but it worked. What you can do is help your clients see all the facets of the current state – good and bad and choose what they want to leave behind, what they want to change, and what they want to preserve.  By ensuring that you have checked all nooks and crannies and haven’t left any stone unturned in search of the positive benefits of the current state you will improve the stickiness of the change to be implemented and take another step to make it effective, well-serving, and sustainable. Having a well-formed outcome that is clear, specific, and understood by the client is a key to their success. They should have a clear understanding of what they want to achieve before they start doing anything about it. This will help them stay focused and prevent any distractions that may lead to poor outcomes; it will also provide them with an overarching goal that can be used to validate tactical 

---

## Page 96

 
96 
decisions they need to be making along the way. Now, you are equipped with tools and understanding to help your client to define these well-formed outcomes and thus help them move forward towards their better future. 
Chapter 12: Coaching Organizational Systems  Cherie and Alex’s work at Tandem Coaching Academy has incorporated a learning path for Coaching Human Systems that focuses on organizational and team coaching because we know that coaching teams and organizations can be tricky work. This chapter introduces seven concepts we teach regarding how to shift from individual coaching to coaching a system using professional coaching competencies for multi-person clients and utilizing systems thinking as a foundational skill when coaching teams and organizations.    Scaling the coaching agreement  When working with organizations and teams, you will need to have multi-level coaching agreements. The most familiar agreement is the team agreement, where the team determines how they will work together. In the Agile space this is generally referred to as the team’s working agreement. You will likely use coaching to facilitate the development of this agreement for the team, however it is not actually a coaching agreement. The coaching agreement comes in when you shift from how the team will work with one another to how they will work with you. The coaching agreement focuses on how you and the client, team in this case, will work together and the outcomes you will focus on achieving.   You may have multiple coaching relationships in an organizational setting, so it is important that you have a coaching agreement with each client, whether individual, group, or team. Though you may have separate coaching relationships with individuals, when coaching the team, the team itself as an intact entity (system), not the individuals, is the client. The level at which you are working in the organization will determine the highest-level system for the purposes of your coaching agreements. You might consider this top-level coaching agreement an engagement agreement or engagement plan where you highlight the organizational business outcomes you will be working with the client to achieve.   Think of it this way, if you are working with a large company that has multiple divisions, departments, and teams of managers and staff you are working with a large system (the company) and its varying subsystems (each grouping below). Some of the systems are persistent, such as departments, management teams, etc. and others are transient such as a group of people in a meeting or training class.   If you are an enterprise coach, you should have a coaching agreement with the highest level of the organization you are contracted to work with. For example, if you are engaged to work with a portfolio, and are working with the leaders there you should have a coaching agreement that outlines the business outcomes you are working towards as an organization which will guide the 

---

## Page 97

 
97 
work done at the descending levels. The leaders at that level will be the ones you make the agreement with, and the agreement really covers the work you will be doing with the portfolio as one entire system. The everyday work you do will be with subsystems like programs, teams, groups, and individuals who work in that portfolio.  Being clear on the coaching agreement up front including what you are focusing on, how you will measure success at the portfolio level will set the scope and focus of your engagement. It will also make expectations clear for what is expected of you and of the client. Having this agreement in place will set the stage for a stronger relationship and increase the likelihood of a successful engagement from the perspective that if you don’t know where you’re going, you’ll never know when you get there. Alex always says, “Strong contracts are the basis for strong working relationships. How we work together is clearly defined and we know how we will handle things when problems arise. With that out of the way, we can trust each other and put all our energy in collaborating and focusing on the work at hand.”    We’ve spent a lot of time in this book focusing on defining goals and outcomes because this is quite possibly the most important part of the work you will do as an agile coach. Clients, whether individual or organizational, will have an idea of why they want to engage a coach but generally haven’t thought through the outcomes they want in enough detail to clearly define them. Coaching your client through this is the beginning of the engagement process. Without the top-level engagement agreement in place, the value you are adding as a coach will not be clear to you or to the client.   What we’ve given in the last few chapters are multiple tools and models you can use to develop strong coaching relationships with your clients. They aren’t intended to be a checkbox process or method that you implement one after the other. You will more likely be utilizing the STORMMES © model to make sure that you have covered the essential elements while you are working with the client to define the engagement before and after you sign the contract.   You will likely use impact mapping once you get started to get a clear picture of what the client wants to happen once you figure out what the goals are. Well-formed outcomes help you define how you will know you’ve gotten where you want to be. The output of the coaching agreements will look different at every level you work with. You will have to develop your own methods of working with clients and the tools and models we’ve provided here can help you with that.   Cherie explains, “There was a point in my journey as an agile coach when I noticed that coaches and clients were pretty far out of sync. Coaches basically had the perspective that ‘we are agile, we are not going to document a plan for clients.’ I’m not certain if the thinking was that they were holding the Agile value of responding to change over following a plan, or if they really didn’t understand that the client saying they have no idea what the Agile coach is doing is an indicator that their engagement is in jeopardy. I think that this perspective of ‘we can’t give you a coaching plan’ is shortsighted and, quite frankly, wrong. If you don’t know where you are going, how will you know you’re going in the right direction? Leaders of organizations are right to be concerned if they are paying astronomical rates to have you in their company and they can’t 

---

## Page 98

 
98 
identify what it is that you are doing. When I ran coaching organizations, I taught (and required) the coaches to work with their organizations to develop engagement plans. Once these plans were in place, the client was clearer on what and why the coach was there and what outcomes they were both working towards. This immediately reduced the tension between the client and their budget concerns. It also gave the coach and client something to have an ongoing conversation about to review progress over time and to inspect and adapt as needed.   Cherie has a process that she uses which fits nicely after when she would be utilizing the STORMMES © model to have a high-level overview of what the engagement is intended to produce. Before mapping out goals using the impact mapping technique, the goals need to be defined. Cherie uses a process of “system entry” that serves several purposes. First, it enables her to make a personal connection with many individuals and listen to what they believe the challenges in the organization are. This is the first step in building healthy client relationships and developing trust and safety between her and the members of the organization. Second, it gives her the ability to hear how the system views itself so she can reflect it back to the system and find out if there’s alignment. Third, it enables her to hear how the client believes it should best solve its own challenges. All this information is utilized to build the engagement plan. Here is a high-level overview of her process.   Step 1: Get alignment with the top leaders (she includes this in her SOW) that you will start the engagement by conducting an engagement survey and that they are responsible for communicating this to the organization.  Step 2: Set up time to have conversations with people from every role and every level of the organization and organizations that directly impact the work they are doing. Cherie meets with the highest leaders one on one. Senior leaders one on one or in sets of 2-3. Line managers in groups of 2-3. She meets with a sampling of whole teams and small groups of 4-5 of each role. She generally sets aside the first week or two to conduct these conversations. She gets the list of who she will be speaking to from the organizational leaders, or the individual assigned as her change partner. The people she will be talking with get an introduction email from the leader explaining who she is and why she will be talking with them.   Step 3: Have the conversations. Keep in mind, this survey is not an assessment of the organization. The intent is not for the coach to figure out what’s broken and tell the client how to fix it. The intent is to hear the system describe what is working and not working and what they view as the most important things to improve. After talking to just a few people, themes start to arise. Most organizations have roughly 3-7 prominent themes that come from the conversations. To get the most valid information, everyone should understand that discussions are confidential and only themes will be disclosed. No actual comments or things individuals or teams say will be included in the output of the survey.  Step 4: Document the most prominent themes that arise from the conversations. Cherie uses a process of identifying each of them with a high level “current state” and “desired state” description. The desired state is a combination of what the client has said they want and what 

---

## Page 99

 
99 
your expertise tells you the desired state should be. These are not detailed and are generally 3-5 sentences long and fit on one PowerPoint slide for each theme. It’s enough to have a conversation about where they are and where they would like to be.   Step 5: Review themes with the top-level leaders to confirm that they are in alignment with what the rest of the system has described. Since you are simply reflecting what they told you back to them, there is rarely a case where the leaders do not agree with the themes that have risen to the surface. At this stage the leaders need to decide if they want to focus on all the themes that have arisen or if there are some that they want to make the business decision to set aside for now. There should be a discussion around how these themes tie back to business outcomes and what impact to business outcomes they expect if these themes are resolved. In essence, what will be the return on investment if they adopt these themes into the engagement plan that the organization will be focusing on for the next year (or other specified timebox)? There should also be a discussion about the priority order of the goals. Which goals will be focused on first and which ones fall to the bottom of the list of priorities if we can’t make progress on all of them?  Step 6: Once the desired state, return on investment, and priority of the goals is determined, the next step is to identify how success will be measured for each theme. How will you and the organization identify that the desired state has been achieved? (The well-formed outcomes process can help with this.)   Step 7: Identify potential coaching activities that will be done to help the organization meet the goals. These potential coaching activities are a blend of what the client said they needed during the survey. This often includes things like training and role definition. It is important for you and the client to understand that this is not a step-by-step list of all the things you will be doing. It’s more of a high-level view of what some of the work might be. You won’t know exactly what to do until you start working more closely with the organization. However, this part helps the leaders to have insight into what the coach is working on while they are getting paid to “walk around talking to people.”   This simple, high-level plan serves as the coaching agreement. It’s five components give you and the client clarity into what you are focusing on together, and clear measurements to look at when you check in on progress. A good cadence to have an inspect and adapt session with the leaders on progress is quarterly. It gives enough time to make some progress between check-ins while happening often enough to shift direction without losing much time. During these inspect and adapt sessions, the leaders may say to continue moving forward as previously contracted. They may decide that enough progress has been made on a theme that even though all the measures of success are not fully realized, the return on investment is sufficient to shift focus to another goal. They may also decide that the needs of the organization have shifted, and they want to eliminate some goals and add others.   This same process can be followed on a lighter scale with teams. Identify what they want to focus on over the next quarter (for example: speed of delivery), determine what current and end state are. Determine how this will tie to the larger organizational themes and business 

---

## Page 100

 
100 
outcomes. Determine what will be measured to show progress and achievement of the end state. Discuss a few things you might do over the next quarter to reach the goals outlined in the plan. How or if the coaching plan is documented is a decision between you and the team.   These conversations take time and effort, and they are the backbone of your coaching engagement. Without a good coaching agreement, the coach may end up trying to drive because the client hasn’t been clear about where to go. If you want the client to own making their own changes rather than looking to you to do the work for them, a coaching plan is the first step in making that happen.  Coaching organizations is complex work. Professional coaching skills and competencies combined with systems thinking can play a strong role in your success as an agile coach. During the rest of this chapter, the description “team” should be understood as any system within the organization you happen to be working with. These might be leadership teams, groups of product owners, scrum teams, etc.   Developing trust and safety  The coach must help the team members cultivate trust within the team, so they are safe to speak up, disagree, and surface challenges. It is essential to develop trust with each team member and the team as an entire unit, so the team feels safe working with the coach to change and improve.  Part of the work of cultivating trust and safety with individuals and groups is taking a non-judgmental stance. Teams and organizations are filled with amazing people who are very different and contribute different talents, approaches, and viewpoints. The differences in people do not make one better or worse than others, those differences are unique to each individual and each adds dimension to the system. Your role is to help the team see how these differences add to the strength of the team. Help them integrate the differences into the way the team functions. Helping teams to learn to have healthy conflict, to be okay disagreeing, and free to respectfully contribute ideas builds trust and safety on the team. Showing them that you are non-judgmental and don’t show partiality to any of the people, ideas, or contributions is important to building trust between them as a system and you as their coach.   Although you may be tempted, remember that it is not your role to determine what decisions, responses, actions, verbalizations, or inactions are good or bad. In fact, good or bad are very unclear terms that will mean something different to each person - and it’s a judgment. What’s more important than deciding if an interaction is good or bad is simply knowing if it is helpful or unhelpful for this group of people at this moment. Only the team can decide that. You might have an intuition of whether something happening in the team is putting them in a productive or non-productive state but only they can determine that for sure. Staying non-judgmental helps you to see that the different communications are simply information flowing through the system. This is information that the system needs to operate. Your role is to encourage the exchange of 

---

## Page 101

 
101 
information and reflect to the system what you see surfacing. Let them decide what to do with that information as their awareness of it becomes clearer.  Building resonance can also increase trust with you and the team. Resonance is a felt sense of connection between individuals' experiences and emotions resulting in deeper mutual understanding.  For example, you tell me about an experience you had. I had a similar, yet different experience so I make a mental and likely emotional connection with what you said. It resonates with me. Our stories have very real yet separate meanings for each of us, but they create a shared connection between us. Resonance between people is built by creating bonds through the telling of individual stories and sharing experiences. The choice of how much to reveal must reside with the individuals. Trust increases with each disclosure. As trust builds each small risk-taking action creates stronger links. The greater risk-taking happens as resonance increases. This is true between the coach and the system and with the members of the system. Trust on the team must be cultivated as well as trust between the team and coach.  System coaching presence   Having system coaching presence means that the coach must exercise presence, not with the individuals but with the group. This is another area where systems thinking is important.   Taking a step back to look at teams and help them look at themselves as a coherent system, rather than a collection of individuals and a bunch of cause-effect phenomena can have huge implications for the team dynamics. If they can see themselves as a single functioning system and realize that each team member serves a valuable function, they can begin to understand the role each one plays in the success (or failure) of the team. For example, instead of getting frustrated with that member of the team who seems to have a negative perspective, they can begin to appreciate the function that person serves in helping the team make well thought out decisions. Without this person focusing on risks the team might end up making big mistakes that can be avoided with a bit of critical thinking. They might also realize that the person who seems to be direct and outspoken or even disagreeable is serving a much-needed purpose on the team. They are the person who brings new perspectives and teaches the team to have robust discussions about multiple options before settling on the first idea to come forward. Each person brings something to the team that the team needs to function at its best. When the team embraces system thinking, they can realize that together the system is a much stronger unit than just the sum of its parts.    System thinking also impacts the way we approach coaching. When you take a system perspective, you will recognize that the team’s interactions aren’t just a series of cause-effect mechanisms.
