Systems are way more complicated than a string of single cause-effect actions and responses.   It may be tempting to look at the team and see simple cause-effect feedback loops happening. However, the cause-effect thinking style is narrow by design, “I do something, causing something else to happen/change/occur.” While this approach works in a lot of cases, more 

---

## Page 102

 
102 
often, it does not. Knowledge workers rarely deal with simple or complicated systems (see Cynefin section) these days, they are mostly complex and/or chaotic.    When working with a system, we must realize that at any given moment there are multiple cause-effect loops happening and impacting one another. So, we cannot think of single cause-effect loops in isolation. Thinking of a desired effect in isolation can be a sign of shortsightedness, and in some cases a grave mistake.   By stepping back and taking a system perspective you can get a clearer view that things don’t happen by simple cause and effect; rather, the desired outcome in a complex system can be achieved in numerous ways they occur through pulling a variety of levers. In addition, almost none of those levers are digital (i.e., on/off) but rather analog (i.e., they have a range of impacts, from negligible to extreme). At the same time, it is always a mistake to think that a single lever affects a single desired outcome, and desired, variable in the complex system. The effects are often unpredictable (and overlooked) if observed through the cause-effect prism. This explains how decisions made in one part of an organization can have major consequences in other parts of the organization when there doesn’t seem to be an obvious connection.  The moment we accept the fact that the team is a complex system with a lot of levers can open our awareness to many more opportunities in our work with teams.  These levers can be used to launch, accelerate, slow down, or sustain a variety of feedback loops. By understanding them and even utilizing them, we gain more opportunities to affect team workings and dynamics in ways that were not possible with a cause-effect approach.  There is another huge implication for starting to look at the team workings as a system. Systems are non-human. While a system may be composed of humans, once they come together and form a system that system is no longer a collection of individual humans - it is a non-human entity: the system. What it means is that we are looking at feedback loops, stocks (stored, accumulated material and information), and flows of the system as something naturally occurring where we can push and pull some levers to modify the system’s state. The system is devoid of an actor – a person who pushes and pulls the levers. Of course, there are specific people who affect the state of the system, but the focus isn’t on them as humans, only as actors who affect the system functioning, one way or another. Thus, a person, or a group of people are never in the center of the system, never its focus. In system thinking, we focus on the system's parts and components, such as stock, flow, levers, etc. and not on the person, their actions, or even worse, their character.  And this is the key to understanding an important property of a system, its blamelessness. Yep, you read it right – the system is blameless, and making it non-anthropocentric, i.e., replacing a person in the center of the system with a bunch of system descriptors, helps us to get away from what haunts a lot of teams and human relationships – blame. Blame is unhelpful, unproductive, and toxic. Hence, accepting the system approach to team functioning and coaching we can make huge strides on driving out that toxin.  

---

## Page 103

 
103 
 Systemic active listening   The act of actively listening is not the same as utilizing active listening skills you may have learned in your last business class. It includes those, however that is only a very small portion of what it means to actively listen. Actively listening when coaching a system utilizes the competencies of active listening used in coaching individuals and modifies them to work with a multi-person client. Systemic listening requires that you focus on the system while cultivating all the voices of the system and drawing out marginalized voices whose opinions are not the popular collective opinion or that may hesitate to speak their thoughts out loud.   Teams and organizations are complex systems. Communication happens in the system in many ways and every communication has a meaning for the individuals and the group. Communication in the system may come, among other things, in the form of words, body language, sounds, emotions, behaviors, opinions, divergent ideas, and how the system’s components (generally people but might include other parts of the geography the system operates within) organize themselves. All the ways that a system interacts, or doesn’t interact, reveals information about the system. Your role as the coach is to make the system transparent to itself. This may mean that you are making observations about how the system is interacting. It might mean that you make observations about the way the team is not interacting. It might mean that you notice verbally how the team has organized itself into smaller subsystems. For example, by the way they gather in small groups by role to collaborate to make decisions or possibly by the way they organize their seating arrangements. Remember as you notice these things that there is nothing inherently wrong with any behaviors they display. Those behaviors are simply more information for the team to utilize to understand how it can best operate. The team will need to decide if what they are doing is useful or unuseful and make decisions to change accordingly.  When sharing observations and questions with the system, it is important to focus on the system as one unit. To do this, you will, for the most part, refrain from having individual conversations with members while working with the group. For example, if you are in a session with the team don’t have a conversation with one member while the others watch. Instead take what that member says and reflect to the team. Cherie calls this “speaking to the center.” If you can imagine that the system is a circle with all the members forming the boundary, you want to direct your questions and observations to the neutral center space rather than directing it to an individual. When you work with a system, you are working with the relationship that ties the system together to make it an intact system. So, speaking to the center places your questions and observations in the neutral space where any part of the system can respond, and they are more likely to respond to one another than to you.  Reflecting what one person says back to the center of the system might sound like, “What’s it like for the rest of you to hear that perspective?” or, “What are some other perspectives that haven't been heard yet?” or “How do you respond to this perspective?” or, “I’m noticing that 

---

## Page 104

 
104 
when person 1 and person 2 are discussing this issue, others seem to check out. What’s happening?”  Systems are interconnected and information flows through the system. This means that it is not uncommon for multiple people to be thinking the same thing. And for them to hesitate to say it out loud. Then, when one individual finally says what they are thinking, others in the group reveal that they were thinking or feeling the same. One way to look at this is to say that information that is flowing through the system belongs to the system and is revealing the system. When one person speaks, they make what the system is thinking or feeling transparent to the rest of the system.  The coach can help this process by reflecting to the system what they hear emerging in conversations and by putting non-verbal communications into words. Verbalize what you notice rather than just noticing. Cherie says, “Coaches often tell me that they noticed things happening in the system and just tucked it away in their mind. It is more powerful to say what you see, sense, and notice so the team will have new information to work with. When the system sees itself transparently, it learns more. When it learns more, it can become more effective.”    Evoking system awareness  Instead of getting caught up in problems and solutions, focus on the team interactions which hamper or enable effectiveness and performance. Maintain a system focus by bringing what is happening with one individual back to the group to work with.  When coaching individuals, the client focuses on the problem and the coach focuses on the client. The same is true when working with teams. Instead of the coach getting caught up in the problems and solutions, they are more effective if they focus on the team. Let the team focus on solving the problem. You focus on the interactions and collaboration happening in the team so they can work together more effectively. This enables the team to focus on solving the problem leveraging the improved communication and collaboration achieved through the coaching process.  One way the coach can maintain system focus is by bringing what’s happening with one individual back to the group. Phrases like, “To what extent does that resonate with the rest of you,” or, “It sounds like you are experiencing ‘x’. What are the rest of you experiencing?”   Another way the coach can help evoke awareness in the team is using metaphor. Metaphors are powerful for helping the team condense information. Condensing happens when one person realizes something and expresses it to the team using a metaphor. The metaphor resonates and the team starts to build on that idea. Through the metaphor, the group pools its thinking and new understanding emerges. You can solicit a metaphor from the team by simply asking them, “What does this remind you of?” or, “What’s the image that comes to mind that would describe 

---

## Page 105

 
105 
what’s happening right now?” If you hear a team member utilizing a metaphor you can pull on that metaphor and see if it resonates with the team. Often, by stepping outside of directly speaking about a problem the team can be more innovative and get past blocks by working with a metaphor and then bringing back what they learned to apply to the problem.  Keep in mind that the goal of coaching is always to evoke new awareness in the client. Getting that new awareness is what creates the new mindset. When the client has a new mindset, they can see broader and have new ways of solving problems. The more they learn, the more they grow and the higher performing they can become.   Facilitating system growth   Coaches can often help systems become more transparent to themselves and decide how they want to improve using assessments. The goal is not for the coach to grade the team on a satisfactory/unsatisfactory scale. Instead, it is a way for the team to generate new insights about itself. The team must assess itself. If the team is assessing itself in an area the coach has expertise in, like technical practices, it is helpful for the coach to do an independent assessment based upon their interactions and observations of the team.    Again, the coach’s assessment isn’t to show the team or the team’s manager what they are doing right or wrong or if they are performant or not performant. The goal is to have more data points for a learning conversation. This coach’s perspective should not override the team’s view of itself. The intent is not to prove that the coach is right, and the team is wrong. Instead, it should merely be a way to uncover new information and provide feedback about the team’s performance and interactions from the outside looking in.   It doesn’t do any good for the coach to tell the team that they are doing something wrong that the team isn’t ready to hear. It’s okay if the team rates itself higher than the coach does in a certain area. What’s not important is not who is right or wrong. What’s important is the discussion about the gap. When discussing the gap. They will sometimes explain what they see that caused them to rate themselves higher than you did. If you are humble and open to learn from the team, you’ll see that they are correct. If your position is different, share it with them. If they are ready to see your perspective, they can accept it. If not, that’s okay too. What’s important is that the team gets enough perspective to make decisions about what they will focus on improving until the next time they assess themselves.   It's not up to you to decide what the team should be focusing on or to determine the priority order of improvements. It is more important to have the team decide what they would like to focus on. If they focus on improving things, they are passionate about improving, the changes are more likely to stick. Sustainable change doesn’t happen by force. It happens by opting in.   You might also utilize other group behavioral assessments that focus on how individuals on the team tend to interact with others. Assessments like this that have a group component can be 

---

## Page 106

 
106 
powerful tools in the hands of the team to understand and leverage team dynamics in a stronger way. Alex and Cherie use a few different assessments when working with teams and organizations. They always utilize assessments that have a team profile component because they provide both individual and team-based data to work with. The team profile gives an insight into individual thinking styles and behavioral characteristics. It also asks important questions about how these individual characteristics fit into the team’s complex system with its collective thinking and behavioral profiles.   Team composite reports show aggregated data with no personally identifiable information that the coach can debrief during a team coaching session where everyone has their own profile handy, as well as a collective team profile. As when debriefing any assessment, a good place to start the coaching session is to ask a question, “What stands out for you in this team profile?” and follow the flow as answers from the team emerge. You usually don’t need a huge bank of questions to help the conversation flow. For example, if the team identifies one or more behavioral traits at their extreme, it might be useful to help them think about the implications these extremes might have on them and their desired outcomes.  Helping the team connect several traits into balancing or reinforcing feedback loops can open the conversation up to more complex and useful insights. Team composite reports can be used over time to identify potential improvements rather quickly. Help them, design a few small actions and to build accountability methods around those. From time to time they should inspect the outcomes of the actions, and help them decide what actions to take, and repeat the process.   The team profile becomes useful as an ongoing reference for the events that might be happening in teams’ daily interactions. It gives the team the ability to speak in a much richer way, with non-judgmental language. It helps them to quickly identify and verbalize challenges and conflicts by naming specific actions rather than making personal attacks on individuals.   The team has the data to help them connect actions to traits in the profile. For example, if someone believes that an individual is dominating the meetings, they might point out that the behavior in question is actually a characteristic of the team and inquire if it would be useful to balance the trait by letting others speak in turn.   Taking a system perspective  To work from a systems perspective, the coach must take a step back to identify all the systems represented. This perspective will reveal a slew of systems and subsystems such as the team, the organizations around the team, and other teams. This viewpoint helps the coach identify if occurrences are happening in isolation, if triggered by something in another part of the system, or if it is a systemic issue that is part of the culture and how the larger organization functions. Without this viewpoint, the coach might try to resolve problems inside the team with minuscule results because the root of the problem resides outside the team.  

---

## Page 107

 
107 
A system perspective will also enable you to help a client organization collaborate, communicate, and design processes. It will also enable you to see how other subsystems inside the team and organization interact and learn. Some standard subsystems found in organizations are circles of friends, role-based groupings, full-time employees, contract staff, legacy team members, new team members, etc. These subsystems are part of the larger whole, so those subsystems’ workings will have impacts elsewhere in the organization. For example, two team members are good friends and discuss work outside of the team space. When the team collaborates, the friends have pre-determined their position on the matter and present as a united front, disrupting the team’s ability to diverge and converge ideas. Because they have pre-determined their position, the friends may not be as open to new ideas or building upon new perspectives.   Finally, taking a system perspective can help you to identify when challenges or impediments that surface are isolated to an individual on the team, the team, or the system around the team. Isolated challenges can be approached at the point of the challenge. Systemic challenges need to be approached from a higher systemic level to make progress on resolving them.  Overall, while coaching teams and organizations can be tricky, by incorporating systems thinking and some adjustments to scale core competencies across multi-person systems, you will cross the first hurdles.  
  

---

## Page 108

 
108 
Part 3: Coaching the Agile Enterprise                

---

## Page 109

 
109 
Chapter 13: Engagement Types  The success that many companies have with Agile can be enticing to senior executives who want to make their organizations more competitive. They're encouraged by stories of Agile teams who can deliver products quickly to market. Convinced Agile will affect the change they seek; they excitedly send a team off to Agile training in hopes that come Monday that team will start churning out the results they’ve heard Agile promises. However, what often happens is that the team returns to the office and attempts to implement changes to the way they work only to discover that they are blocked by the existing organizational processes, rules, structure, and culture. This is when they realize that change must happen outside the team level to make the desired impact. Oftentimes, companies find that the culture that has worked for them in the past is now in direct contrast to the Agile operating model of self-organizing teams and transparent workflows. What happens next is all too familiar. Frustration occurs. Unfortunately, because the system is so big it is hard to see that the root cause of the perceived failure is not the individual teams, it’s not the leaders, and it’s not Agile. The root cause is that the company is desiring to see changed results without the ability to make the changes necessary to experience those results. The problem is the existing culture that must change to release the ability for the entire organizational system to change. Installation, Adoption, or Transformation Cherie shares her perspective. “In my experience as an Agile coach, there is a lot of confusion about what companies actually want. The company isn’t sure, and the coach isn’t sure. Most of the frustrations I hear between Agile coaches and their clients are because there is a misunderstanding about the work the client is trying to accomplish.” Companies sometimes only want training and installation Scrum, Kanban, or some other process. Coaches want the clients to “go Agile” and are frustrated when the client isn’t willing to make changes above the team level. Other companies want to adopt Agile and target specific areas of the company like engineering and product development. They don’t have the control or influence beyond these two groups to make a full company transformation effort happen. Coaches get excited and work with the clients, but soon the changes are rubbing up against the barriers of departments outside of engineering and product and the coach gets frustrated because the client isn’t willing or able to make the wider sweeping changes necessary to be a completely Agile company. Although “transformation” is a big buzzword in the Agile community, very few companies are ready to make the investment in a full Agile transformation. The larger the company is, the less likely a full transformation effort will occur. The words Agile transformation are thrown around so loosely in the industry that there isn’t clarity on what the term means, much less the time and investment it takes to get there. In this section we hope to bring a bit of clarity for coaches to understand the real scope of work they 

---

## Page 110

 
110 
are doing with clients, so there can be alignment for the engagement. Understanding what the client really wants is the first step in ensuring that you are focusing on the right scope of work. Without this understanding and alignment, you may end up pushing change that the company isn’t ready to make which will frustrate your teams, the leadership, and you. Process Installation Engagements This is the point in the book where some people will roll their eyes and say, “No! That’s not valuable!” Let’s investigate that viewpoint for a few minutes. First, we acknowledge that what we want for our clients is full transformation because we know that Agile gets great results. We don’t go out recommending that a client simply install Scrum or Kanban and declare, “We’re Agile!” We also acknowledge that since we don’t hold the budget, we don’t dictate what clients decide to do with their company. And often, the person who contacts us for an engagement doesn’t hold the budget the size of what’s needed to do anything more than installation. The question to ask yourself isn’t about if it is wrong for the client to only do an installation. The real question to ask is, is this the kind of work you want to do? If you are willing to see the client as fully competent to make decisions for their business, it will be easier for you to come to terms with the fact that there are many reasons far beyond what you are able to see for why clients make the decisions they make. Trust them. Partner with them. Move at the pace that works best in their world. What are some indications that your client wants an installation only engagement? They are primarily focused on training and/or team startup. They only want a short-term engagement, 3-6 months. They only want you to focus on teams. Managers are not accessible or interested in making changes to the way the organization operates. They are not willing to hire and give authority over the product to the Product Owners. They aren’t willing to assign people 100% allocated to a single cross-functional team and put work on backlogs rather than moving people to work on projects. They are not willing to pass all work through the product backlog with the PO having authority over prioritization. Work is directed to the team by managers who override PO’s authority. Where coaches tend to get frustrated is when they want the client to go further on the journey than they are willing to go. Instead of being frustrated and pushing against the client’s resistance to going further, leverage the momentum you have and focus on making the most sustainable impact you can make during the time you are there. 

---

## Page 111

 
111 
Cherie worked with a small client who had received initial training in Scrum and wanted 160 hours of coaching over a four-month period to help Scrum masters understand their roles better. While she was there, she tested the system to see how much change it was willing to tolerate. Several of the Scrum masters wanted to learn and grow as much as possible, only two managers were interested in engaging, some team members wanted to make improvements, and a group of product owners wanted to learn as much as possible. Everyone was very clear that no major structural changes were going to be made in the organization or its operations. Rather than trying to push the organization into adopting changes they weren’t ready to adopt, Cherie embraced the constraints that were in place. She partnered with the people who did want what she had to offer to come up with a plan that would add the most value. They decided to do a series of workshops covering a backlog of things they wanted to learn and practice implementing. Product Owners focused on learning and implementing product management tools and techniques. Scrum Masters focused on coaching skills, facilitation, and various Agile topics. At the request of the development team members, Cherie scheduled her time in the office on days they held Scrum events, so she could attend them and give feedback on how they were collaborating. The two managers wanted coaching, so they had 1-1 coaching sessions weekly. She spent most of her time in training and mentoring mode in response to what the client wanted and the amount of time she had to add value to the organization. These types of engagements can be successful when you are willing to accept the constraints and focus on what can be done rather than what won’t be accomplished. Stop focusing on what you can’t do, or the client won’t do. Focus on what you can do with the time and momentum you have available to you. Not all process installation engagements are this simple. Sometimes the client is willing to pay you to be there infinitely, but they are only willing to allow changes to happen that don’t disrupt the status quo. These engagements are often a bit harder to recognize, especially when there’s a systemic issue that’s covering the truth about what the client wants to accomplish. One large Fortune 500 company Cherie worked with was roughly divided into multiple enterprises with divisions and departments. Departments had roughly 10 teams each. The division leader was an Agile fan, so he was driving Agile transformation from the top and had required all his departments to transition to Agile. When she interviewed the department leader, she was going to be working with he said all the right things. He was fully invested, had the budget to support the engagement, was willing to promote change from his level, would be available to me and make sure his managers were also. There was also a larger effort at the company level and Agile was being supported to be adopted across all the enterprises and there was even a Board of Directors initiative with a goal that was tied to the performance ratings of some leaders. [see metrics chapter for our thoughts on this strategy] This company gave all the indications that they wanted an Agile adoption. On the ground, it was a different story. Cherie worked with them for about a year and made tons of progress at the team level. Scrum Masters were solid in their roles. They had moved into test automation and built a CI/CD pipeline. Teams experimented and some chose Kanban, others 

---

## Page 112

 
112 
selected Scrum. They were delivering consistently and were influencing other areas with technology. Though scaling wasn’t needed in that organization because the teams all worked on separate products and had very few dependencies, at their request we set up quarterly planning events, so teams could demo the features built in the previous quarter and give visibility into what was coming up in the next quarter. Until this point all the technical progress being made in the department was in alignment with what the managers and department leader wanted. The biggest challenge the managers had to overcome early in the engagement was letting go of separate frontend and backend teams. They resolved their concerns about reporting structure and oversight by keeping matrixed reporting. So, they settled on cross-functional teams with team members reporting to the discipline manager. Not optimal — but it was working for them. The rest was accomplished without managers having to make changes they didn’t agree with. They were at the point where to have an Agile adoption, things would have to change at the next level and culture would be impacted.  It was about eight months into the engagement when they finally hired product owners. This is when it became clear that this engagement would never move beyond team level process installation. To move forward the management team would have to agree to change. The Scrum masters started pushing on managers to stop giving work directly to team members and to work with the product owner to get work prioritized into the product backlog. Managers didn’t want to let go of the control of what their people worked on. They agreed to. Then, when they wanted something different than the product owner, they told the team members to do the work. Since the team members reported to the discipline manager (thus, not optimal) they had to listen to the manager. In essence, the product owners had no ownership of the products. When Scrum masters continued to push on the managers about working through the product owners it became tense. Scrum masters started confronting cultural challenges that violated Agile values and principles and things got even more tense. Finally, the Scrum masters were told that they would be fired if they didn’t stop pushing because there was no intention to make the changes they were promoting.  Cherie followed her normal procedures for handling a client engagement that is stalled or has reached the limits of its tolerance for change. She met with the managers and department leader and discussed next steps. She explained that they were at a place where no further movement forward could be made without change at the management level. They said they still wanted her to stay on as a coach, and they would work on it. Thirty days later they met again to discuss the lack of progress. The department leader admitted that they would not make any further changes, but they were being forced by the division leader to have a coach. They asked Cherie to stay and to just keep working with Scrum masters and product owners. They were willing to pay her to stay another year. Since the client made it clear that they were happy with team level process installation and had no intention to move forward with Agile adoption, Cherie declined. She offered that when they were ready to move to the next level she would come back and work with them. But until then she would move on to another client. 

---

## Page 113

 
113 
Cherie says, “I have learned that trying to force an organization to move forward when they aren’t willing is a waste of my time and their money. It doesn’t matter what the original agreement was. Once an organization has reached its maximum tolerance for change, it’s time to back off and let the changes settle.” Process installation doesn’t move beyond the changes that can happen at the team level. As you can see from these two examples, there are varying degrees of process installation. In the last example the ceiling was at the level of management change. For other companies they don’t get nearly as far as the teams in this example because they have technology challenges that become blockers. It is also common that the client believes they want Agile adoption only to discover they aren’t willing to make the level of change necessary for Agile adoption. Therefore, it’s important to continue listening to the client and being aware that it is not only individuals that have varying degrees of change tolerance. Organizational systems also have varying degrees of change tolerance. Because it’s extremely hard to predict the level of change tolerance in an organization, you must keep the big picture in mind and how the whole system is working together. Every organization has a place where change will no longer be tolerated. When you’ve reached that limit, the work is done.  Prerequisites for Agile Adoption Agile adoption is much different from process installation. Agile adoption is culture transition work.  Agile is a system of values and principles that govern the way a company thinks about how they envision the products they design and deliver. It impacts the practices they use, their operating model, and the way they interact with the individuals who are employed there. It is a way of thinking, believing, reacting, acting, and collaborating. It is a shift in the distribution of power and a transition to what Scrum calls its three pillars: Transparency, Inspection, Adaptation. The Agile Manifesto is where Agile adoption begins. When companies want to adopt Agile, they must first understand what they are adopting and why. Agile is not a process. Starting Scrum is not the same as adopting Agile. Having Kanban teams is not adopting Agile. Using a workflow or lifecycle management tool is not adopting Agile. Implementing SAFe or some other scaling framework is not adopting Agile. It is not unusual to join an organization that says they are an Agile organization and have been “doing” Agile for months or years and yet, they don’t know about the Agile Manifesto values and principles. They are often doing Scrum or Kanban and don’t know about the Scrum Guide or Kanban Guide. These organizations are generally doing some level of process installation and are using the buzzwords “Agile transformation” or “Agile adoption” to describe it. Our position is that a company must know what they are adopting to do so. For a company to adopt Agile, they must first understand the Agile Manifesto. It says: 

---

## Page 114

 
114 
We are uncovering better ways of developing software by doing it and helping others do it. Through this work we have come to value: Individuals and interactions over processes and tools Working software over comprehensive documentation Customer collaboration over contract negotiation Responding to change over following a plan That is, while there is value in the items on the right, we value the items on the left more. As an Agile coach engaging a client who is asking to adopt Agile, you must start here. What is the position of the leadership of the organization (not just a single leader, engineering manager, or hiring person) on these values? To find out, you must first understand systems thinking (see the chapter on systems thinking). Individual conversations cannot tell you how the leadership system thinks. To understand if the thinking of the leadership is in line with these values, you must view the system as one living entity. We recommend that coaches approach working with the leaders of the organization from the perspective that they are a human system. Before any Agile can be done, first the leaders need to determine what set of values and principles they will adopt and work within. The coaching work begins by helping the leaders decide if they will adopt these values into their ways of working, so the culture can be shifted. To adopt these values as an organization, first the leaders must be willing to adopt them. That individual adoption is a mindset shift that takes time, effort, and a true desire to change. Let’s look at each value a bit closer to understand what adopting them means. We value individuals and interactions over process and tools  If the leaders are focused on training, fixing the teams, or adopting a work management tool, they may not yet be ready to move forward with Agile adoption. Agile adoption goes beyond these things and creates a cultural change. To create culture change that reflects agility, first, they must choose to value the people who work in their organization and help those people have constructive interactions with each other and with the leadership. They must be willing to utilize processes and tools to assist individuals to have better interactions — not to determine how people work. Processes should be built to solve specific problems. They shouldn’t be rolled out as simple solutions to complex problems. Leaders must be ready to give up the perceived power that determining solutions, processes, and tools gives them. They must be willing to wait until challenges emerge and apply the right solution for that challenge instead of dictating blanket processes for everyone. This means that simply rolling out “best practices” to everyone doesn’t create value in an Agile culture. They must be flexible on process and allow problems to be solved at the point closest to the work. 

---

## Page 115

 
115 
Depending on how far from this value the leaders are, it will take time for them to change the way they think and interact. The big question that needs an answer before you get started is, “Do you really believe that people interacting and collaborating to design the right solutions is more valuable than using your current processes and tools to run your company?” If the leaders can explain why they agree that this is the best way to work and the kind of leader they want to be, then they are ready. If they can’t or argue against relinquishing power, they are probably more in alignment with a process installation. “Another way to test this value is to see if the company is willing to invest in people and in relationship quality,” says Michael. “Is there time for learning and collaboration? Or is delivery work the only type of work that is valued?”  We value working software over comprehensive documentation and customer collaboration over contract negotiation To adopt this value into the culture of the organization leaders must be ready to experiment, get feedback, and experiment some more. Their viewpoint on failure should be one that expects a feedback loop and embraces failures as ways to grow. Their focus shouldn’t be on the “one neck to choke” but on what they can learn and utilize as they move forward. Adopting this value means that they must be ready to be comfortable not knowing everything upfront. They must be okay with changes and experimentation. They must be willing to listen to what the customers need and adapt to those needs as they change. They must be ready to focus on the outcomes they want to achieve and the problems they want to solve rather than prescribing the solutions. They must be willing to focus on value delivery outcomes instead of day-to-day outputs of individual team members. These changes may move out further than just the department and impact other enterprise-wide processes. Are they ready to tackle collaborating with those organizations to figure out how they are going to operate differently and still serve the needs of both groups? If they can describe why they believe this is the right way to lead, they may be ready for Agile adoption. If not, they may not be ready to let go of the perceived control they have with other ways of working and move towards Agile adoption. We value Responding to change over following a plan If they are going to adopt this value, leaders must be ready to allow teams to determine how much work it will take to solve problems. This means that no one can pre-determine deadlines for delivery without first consulting the people doing the work. It also means that they must be flexible and realize that as new information emerges the scope of the work delivered during a release period may change from the original projection.   Are they ready to deal with not knowing all the answers upfront and making changes in scope and timeline as more is understood and based upon customer feedback to the work being 

---

## Page 116

 
116 
delivered? The leader’s ability to think differently about how the organization approaches work determines the organization's ability to adopt Agile values and principles. Agile Adoption Engagements Adopting Agile is more than just adopting new processes and procedures, it involves changing the way the culture of the company approaches the work. How open to experimentation is leadership? How do they react to perceived failure? Do they have a fixed mindset or a learning mindset? These are all critical questions and shifts that must be made for the organization to successfully adopt Agile. Agile adoption must happen at the organizational level, and it starts with the people who have the power to make change in the organization. They lead the way because they determine the culture of the organization. Adopting Agile will change everything. It’s a different way of thinking about how you run your business and do product development.  Agile adoption also has a ceiling where change stops and that generally falls just outside where the highest leader who wants adoption has control. They develop a beautiful ecosystem with a culture that honors Agile values and principles, and they are getting more of the business results they want. Then, at some point the blockers to progress occur outside the scope of control of that leader. For every company the boundary is different. It might be that the compensation and performance system of the company is forcing team members to be competitors instead of collaborators. They may want to change performance management so that it is team focused with an individual component instead of individual focused with a team component. If the highest leader doesn’t have the power, influence, or permission to make these changes, they are blocked. The company’s funding model is a common blocker. If the highest leader can’t get that to change it becomes a barrier to progress. With enough of these blockers, progress stalls, and it becomes clear that beyond this point, the wider organization does not have the tolerance level needed for the amount of change needed for a full Agile transformation. To do that, the leaders at the top of the enterprise must decide if Agile is the solution for the business problems they want to solve. They must decide if there’s enough return on investment to make changes throughout the entire company. Agreeing to Agile transformation means change on a larger scale and includes areas outside of product and engineering that impact their value delivery. Without that commitment from the very top level, it is not possible to have an Agile transformation.  Agile Transformation Engagements  Agile transformation requires the entire organizational system to change. It goes beyond practices and processes. It goes beyond adopting Agile values and principles and technical practices. True Agile transformation requires that everything about the way you do business changes. Hiring practices, compensation practices, performance management, organizational structure, the way you contract with clients and vendors, the physical set-up of the work 

---

## Page 117

 
117 
environment, sales and marketing, the way work is funded, and ownership and accountability for decisions.  It is moving from project management to product management and many other aspects of the way a company operates. Depending on the size and structure of the company, an enterprise might be a stand-alone part of the company who has power over all these aspects held within. Sometimes an enterprise is a portfolio or division. Other times it's the entire company. Therefore, people who are working as enterprise coaches need such a wide skill set, and they often need other coaches to partner with because no one can know everything. There is an abundance of coaches in the industry who are engaging with clients and calling the work they are doing Agile transformation. Oftentimes it’s not. It is often the case that an Agile coach says they are leading an enterprise Agile transformation, but they can’t get access beyond the first or second layer of management. This is an indication that the expectations and desires of the coach and the expectations of the company are out of alignment. If the top leaders are not involved, the company is not interested in a full Agile transformation. Usually, what’s happening is that the organization which may be only engineering and/or product want to adopt Agile. Many simply want an installation of Scrum or Kanban. Coaching an enterprise Agile transformation requires that the client and the coach are in alignment with the vision and direction they are going. It also requires that the coach understand more than how an enterprise should function in theory. The coach can be a much stronger partner if they have experience understanding the complexity of running enterprises and the complexity of introducing changes into large organizations. Understanding and experience in organizational change management, human change theory, conflict management, and other soft skills are often overlooked but very strong skills for an Agile coach to have. Working at the enterprise level also requires the ability to communicate with executives, appreciating the complexity of what they do and how hard leading a company is. The skills needed to do the transformation work for an entire company are immense. It usually takes a team of coaches. The coaching team helping the company evolve needs more than just Agile knowledge, technical expertise, and experience. To be well-rounded, the team needs skills and experience in areas like executive decision-making, performance management, hiring practices, termination and downsizing practices, making decisions about pay rates and bonus structures, vendor management and contracts, strategic planning, and organizational structure. It requires experience in change management and process improvement. It requires professional coaching skills, collaboration skills, conflict management, and negotiation skills. It requires strong facilitation and teaching skills and enough experience to give a professional opinion about which route might be the best direction to go next. But most of all, enterprise coaching requires humility and endurance. One of Cherie’s experiences with a client who wanted a full enterprise Agile transformation was with a midsize company that was growing through acquisitions. The founder and CEO of the company had a business problem to solve. They were growing at an exponential rate and could not keep up with the demand. They needed the ability to deliver faster, respond to customer needs, and scale their platforms to handle the capacity. Agile held solutions to many of his business problems, so he went all in and committed to a full Agile transformation. He 

---

## Page 118

 
118 
understood that it would likely be a multi-year effort, and they would never fully “arrive.” Continuous improvement would always be in their future. Another enterprise coach who was local to the company’s headquarters led the engagement and partnered with the company to make major changes over the first year before Cherie arrived to partner on the effort.  The level of buy-in and willingness to shift everything was amazing. They focused on the most critical problems and implemented changes to solve those problems. Then, they focused on the next most critical problems. They experimented and embraced failure. Power over product decisions and roadmaps was given to product owners. Organizational and reporting structures were shifted to have cross-functional teams fully dedicated to products all reporting to a single manager. Performance management, merit increases, and bonus structures were changed to be iterative and focused on team effectiveness with a component of individual performance. They experimented and developed creative ways to hire agilists, created job descriptions and pay structures in alignment with Agile roles. The work area for Agile teams was completely redesigned giving each team their own team rooms with everything they needed available. The space was large enough for them to have isolation when they needed to focus and the ability to collaborate with other team members as needed. They changed their contracts to Agile contracts and began to contract with their customers to have their own dedicated teams that focused fully on their product. There were no more projects and project funding. The funding model was changed to fund teams instead of projects and capitalization tracking was shifted to a higher level, so team members didn’t have to track every hour and spend time filling in timecards to bill it to some line item. Teams were given the power to make their own decisions about their work schedules and time off. Work wasn’t monitored by managers to make sure everyone put in 40 hours. Leadership focused on outcomes and the teams were responsible for delivering and trusted to manage their work accordingly. The changes listed here are just a small sample of the total transformation efforts the company committed to. There were many changes to culture and a focus on continuous improvement, a growth mindset, experimentation, and a commitment to living by Agile principles. They held hackathons, had communities of practice, and even set up an internal conference to share ideas across the company. Everyone had a budget for training and development and tuition reimbursement. Everyone in the organization had the ability to stand up and say, “I disagree.” Everything wasn’t perfect and improvements were still being made when the company was acquired by a larger Fortune 500 company. The CEO went on to take a seat at the acquiring company and was able to bring many of the same changes before leaving. Even with all this progress, the work was far from done at the larger company when Cherie left after four years.    
Chapter 14: Coaching Culture Change  

---

## Page 119

 
119 
Why culture matters The effectiveness of Agile's autonomous teams is like a siren song to company leadership, and it's easy to see why. Because you can improve performance by changing the operating model. Take this exercise that Michael uses during presentations to Agile practitioners. He says, “I divide them into two groups. I ask the first group to organize themselves by first name in alphabetical order. With the second group, I designate someone to be the manager to organize the team to accomplish the same task. None of the members of the second group are allowed to talk to each other. What is the outcome? You know the answer. The first group accomplishes the task much more quickly because they're self-organized, able to communicate with each other, and are empowered to make decisions. The second group must work through one person to get things done. The team is more hierarchical and bureaucratic, and hence, progress is slower. This exercise demonstrates some differences and simulates some effectiveness of working in an operating model with centralized decision-making vs an operating model with distributed decision-making. It sounds like a win for Agile, right? Self-organizing teams’ rule. But there's a catch. For all its wonderful practices, an Agile operating model will most likely fail if the company culture is in direct opposition to it. An organization’s culture is one of the leading impediments to adopting and scaling Agile, according to the 13th Annual State of Agile Report by VersionOne. It's one of the reasons it's so natural for Agile coaches to do culture and leadership work with an organization first before working on the operating model. We know that true Agile transformation can take place only when the culture supports it. Still, culture change is an evolutionary process. And company leaders may not be ready to dive into the culture conversation right away. Instead, they're looking for quick wins, such as improving deliverables to customers or running more effective meetings. We have seen plenty of companies optimize their operating models within an existing culture. Ultimately, though, culture is the key ingredient to a continuously successful operating model. Another benefit to changing the culture is you will have a wider range of operating models to choose from, models that can flex and bend as the economy expands and contracts, new competitors arrive at your doorstep, and technology introduces more efficiencies. Those operating models may draw from Agile and Scrum practices, or something else entirely. For example, Spotify began with an operating model based on Scrum and then evolved into an operating model involving squads, guilds, and chapters. What culture model do you have? 

---

## Page 120

 
120 
In his influential management book, Reinventing Organizations, author Frederic Laloux defines different versions of organizational culture and assigns each one a color. This model is one of several well-known and studied cultural models. The point is not to use one model or the other but to use the models that are most useful in a particular engagement. Here we use the Laloux model to illustrate this approach. We encourage you to substitute your preferred cultural model. In the interest of time, we are simplifying Laloux's findings into four categories to help you identify what type of culture model you have now and which one you aspire to have and encourage you to investigate these further in Laloux’s book: Red: A rules-based, bureaucratic culture.  This is the type of cultural model where you must follow the rules. If you break them, you get in trouble. Say you drive 30 mph in a 25-mph zone. It doesn't matter that it's just 5 mph faster than the speed limit. The cop will still give you a ticket for the infraction. Orange: A performance-based culture. In an orange culture, financial success is the goal. This is associated with hierarchical power and decision-making, although this practice is not seen in all orange cultures.  It's the world of more, in which orange companies want to get bigger and bigger. Green: A family-based culture. In this culture, organizations care about their people and treat them like family. It's not the opposite of orange, because companies with a green culture still want to make money, but profit is only one piece of the puzzle. Teal: A self-management, evolutionary-based culture. In a teal culture, everything is in harmony, balanced, and suited for a particular purpose. A good example of teal is the social intelligence of ants. If you step on an anthill, within a few hours, ants will reorganize and rebuild their home. This isn't to say that an organization is only one color and nothing else. Most companies must worry about the bottom line, after all. And they may switch cultures based on the situation. A green company may become red when sales go south. They might also switch cultures when a new leader with a different management style steps into the company. We are not saying that one culture is better than another. They all serve a purpose. For example, take a primarily red organization. When an earthquake happens and the building is shaking, you want leadership to tell you what to do and where the exits are. This isn't a situation where you ask someone how they feel about the earthquake before making a move. But in the world of problem-solving, the type of culture you work in will either restrict or enable your abilities. If you work in a red culture with rules and bureaucracy, your degrees of freedom are limited as is the set of operating models from which to choose. As the culture moves from orange to green to teal, the degrees of freedom expand, enabling more adaptability, diversity of opinions, greater collaboration, and innovation. So, as you look at each culture model, ask yourself three questions: 

---

## Page 121

 
121 
1. What culture am I in now? 2. What culture would I like to be in? 3. Do I want to take responsibility for changing the culture? Questions 1 and 2 are the easy part. If you're swimming against the tide at work, it probably won't surprise you that most of us live in an orange world. When I give presentations about this topic, most audience members identify as working in a red or orange environment. But they want to work in a green or teal environment. Question 3 is by far the harder question. So how do you make a difference? Baby steps to changing the culture Shifting the culture first requires looking inward. To a large degree, culture reflects our internal state of personal growth and awareness. It is how we show we care about other people, and how we show respect to others. If we change ourselves, we can eventually influence change in our sphere of influence.  One way to become familiar with incremental culture changes is to learn about David Marquet's Ladder of Leadership. A former U.S. Navy captain, Marquet introduced this new approach to culture when he was commander of a troubled nuclear submarine. Under his watch, the USS Santa Fe went from the worst-performing submarine in the fleet to the best. He turned the ship around by switching from a command-and-control culture to one that inspired the crew to assume responsibility for their actions. In his book, Turn the Ship Around, Marquet describes The Ladder of Leadership as having seven rungs, and each one pertains to the relationship a boss has with an employee or employees. The format for each level is the boss’s stance/employee’s stance. Either can be the initiator of the conversation. Level 1: Do this... / Tell me what to do... This is a standard, top-down, command-and-control model.  The boss tells you what task to do, you do the task, you tell them that you're done, and the boss gives you another task. On this rung of the ladder, the employee is not empowered to make independent decisions. They ask what to do. Level 2: What do you see?... / I see… In this situation, the boss seeks to expand his visibility into the organization. For example, say the boss has a 10-person team. Through inquiry, he goes from seeing not only what he can see but what 10 other people see. The organization has become more capable because it values the observations of others. On this rung of the ladder, the employee has the freedom to approach the boss with unsolicited information to influence decision-making. Level 3: What do you think?... / I think… Now the boss relies on more than observations and information. He asks his team what they think so that he is receiving input, opinions, and 

---

## Page 122

 
122 
recommendations from others. On this rung of the ladder, the employee is encouraged to approach the boss with opinions and recommendations. Level 4: What would you like to do?... / I would like to do… In this situation, the boss asks his team members what they want to accomplish as individuals. This is in line with the boss making problems transparent and allowing the employee to determine options for how they want to solve them. On this rung of the ladder, the employee is encouraged to notice problems and approach the boss with suggestions for how the employee might solve them. Level 5: What do you intend to do?... / I intend to… Now the boss is looking for solutions that will benefit the whole. There is a presumption here that the employee does not need permission. The decision-making power has been given to the employee and the boss has shifted to a being informed stance. On this rung of the ladder, the employee assesses the problem, determines the best solution, and keeps the boss informed of the actions they will take. Level 6: What have you done?... / I have… In this case, the team has become more autonomous. They are still expected to keep the boss informed, but they are expected to make decisions and take actions without delay and inform the boss later. On this rung of the ladder, the employee is given the power to work more autonomously with the expectation that they regularly check in to keep the boss informed. Level 7: What have you been doing?... / I've been doing…At this level, employees are truly autonomous. They are identifying problems, making decisions, acting, and have ownership of the work they do. They check in with the boss occasionally, so the boss has visibility into progress. On this rung, the employee is fully accountable and responsible for their actions, decisions, and lack of action. The boss becomes an advisor. By using this Leadership Ladder, you can do three things to change the culture of your organization. Here's how: 1.  Observe your leadership style As you study your style of leadership, you'll see how it differs depending on whom you interact with. You may be a Level 1 with one person, a Level 4 with someone else, and still another level if you haven't gotten enough sleep. Over time, you'll notice patterns. 2. Observe other people's leadership styles Over time, the different leadership levels will become second nature to you. The next step, then, is to start noticing other people's leadership styles. Soon you'll be able to walk into a room and within 10 minutes identify everyone else's leadership style. This gives you a highly context-sensitive understanding of leadership styles. 3. Invite shifts in leadership styles 

---

## Page 123

 
123 
The final step is to invite shifts in leadership. By influencing how leaders work with you, you encourage them to let go of control, trust others, and become more comfortable with autonomy. For example, if you have a boss who is a Level 1 and is telling you to do this or that task, you might respond by saying, "I would be happy to do that. Do you mind if I also tell you what I see?" Utilize Management 3.0's Delegation Poker18 as a coaching tool to help teams and leaders see their current level of delegation and where they might make potential shifts As you can imagine, this doesn't happen overnight. It's a process that takes time and commitment. But it's worth it. These types of interactions allow you to incrementally shift the leadership in your organization and by so doing, eventually, evolve the culture.  So, when people are sent off to weekend Scrum training, eventually, they'll return to a culture that is more in alignment with the changes they are being encouraged to make. As Michael Sahota so elegantly states, "Success with Agile requires us to focus on culture. There is no other way." If you approach an Agile evolution this way, you're more likely to pave the way for success. 
Chapter 15 - Coach Client Partnership Consulting vs. Coaching Perhaps the greatest difference between coaching and consulting is where the intellectual authority lies. Coaching is a partnership, with the coach and client collaborating primarily utilizing the client’s intellectual authority and experience to design new experiments, decisions, and ideas. With coaching, the client is the one with the answers. It’s not the coach’s job to provide advice and instruction, but to ask challenging questions, make observations, and open new perspectives, so the client can see options and formulate the best solutions for their environment. Coaches help clients take time to reflect, learn and develop new ways of thinking. With consulting, the intellectual authority is typically in the hands of the consultant. Clients turn to consultants for advice, instructions, and professional opinions because the consultant can provide answers in areas where the client doesn’t have the experience or expertise. Consultants often step in and do work for the clients. Helping clients learn may involve tapping into your own experience and expertise in each area, but you never want to simply hand over the answers and dictate action. You also want to be careful about combining coaching with consulting or mentoring, as switching between stances can be difficult. Unless the client really understands and has experienced professional coaching from a trained and qualified coach, they may not understand what the distinctions are between the two. Many people instinctively believe that a professional coach is like the personas of sports coaches who call the shots and tell players which actions to take and how to improve. Sport coaches sit in a place of authority over players and can dismiss them from the team or bench them at any time. So, players don’t have much leeway in refusing to do what the coach tells them. If you do coach this way, clients can become dependent on you and look forward to you telling them what to do.  18  https://management30.com/practice/delegation-poker/ 

---

## Page 124

 
124 
This places the responsibility for success or failure on you. In such cases, you’re no longer a coach, but a consultant. You surely agree that a coach and a car mechanic have two different jobs. But that doesn’t stop clients from frequently expecting the same type of service from their coach as they get from their mechanic. No, they’re not expecting us to change their vehicle’s spark plugs or work on the brakes. But they often want the same kind of easy drop-off service that mechanics provide. Just bring your organization in, drop it off, and then come pick it up when everything is fixed. That is not how sustainable organizational coaching works. Instead of leaving the scene to let the coach do all the work, the client and coach form a partnership to get the job done. This chapter explains what partnering is all about. The concepts in this chapter will help you to understand how an Agile coach can operate as an enterprise coach working from a coaching stance. We draw on ICF Core Competencies and discuss how these competencies look in practical application. Keep in mind as you read this chapter that the client could be a single person, a group of people, or even an entire organizational system.   Client Anti-Disempowerment Even if the client understands what coaching is and a coaching agreement in place, they may fall into one of the most common traps. That trap is shifting the power to the coach. When this happens, you want to practice client anti-disempowerment. The term can be best understood by breaking it down: Empowerment: Authority or power given to an individual to do something. You don’t need to empower the client. The client is already empowered. They have the authority and power to make decisions and changes in their environment. Disempowerment: The removal of the authority or power of an individual to do something. Clients may attempt to disempower themselves by transferring authority or power over to the coach. This happens when clients want or expect the coach to fix their organization for them. Anti-disempowerment: Counteracting the client's attempt to shift the power and authority from themselves to the coach. Client disempowerment tends to occur when the client doesn’t want to, doesn’t know how to, or doesn’t have time to do the work. They may be passionate about reaching their goals and improving their operations, but they believe that making the changes is something they can delegate to the coach. They see the work as simply determining processes and pushing them out. Another, often subconscious, reason a client disempowers themselves is to remove themselves from the equation if things go wrong. If the plan blows up, they aren’t responsible. At the same time, they can re-insert themselves into the equation if things go right. If the plan creates exceptional results, they can take credit for the success. This doesn’t happen because the client is irresponsible or greedy for promotion. More often, it’s a symptom of the environment. If there is a culture that responds to failure with punitive measures, fear runs rampant. To deflect punitive repercussions for failure, people deflect responsibility. If the culture is a command-and-control type environment where decisions get overridden and people are expected to just follow directions, the client will follow the cultural norms and expect to be told what to do. 

---

## Page 125

 
125 
Sometimes, the client attempts to shift responsibility for the change onto the coach because, while they want change, they don’t have time to focus on changing. If that’s the case, then they need to give power to someone in their organization to own and make changes on their behalf. The executive needs to specify who is authorized to make changes, determine goals, and take responsibility. Cherie says, “I won’t accept an engagement where I do not have an individual or team of people who will be leading the change effort and have authority, decision-making power, and organizational influence to get the job done.”  If potential clients insist, they want the coach to fix the problems for them and take responsibility for the changes, that client may be better off with a consultant instead of a coach. What Partnering Means For our purposes, partnering refers to collaboration where both coach and client are equally invested in the outcome. That doesn’t mean; however, each party holds the same responsibilities or performs the same amount or type of work. The coach helps the client discover options, brainstorm ideas, and gather motivation. They challenge the client’s way of thinking and give useful feedback, but they don’t have authority over the client, their decisions, or actions. The client decides the focal point of the work and makes the decisions. They determine where they focus next and how they will measure success. They partner to think through strategizing and problem-solving. However, the client makes the decisions and owns the solutions, next steps, and experiments. The client must take ownership of their change. If the coach is the forcing mechanism for the client’s change, then long term success is dependent on the coach’s presence. If the coach leaves, the client’s changes are likely to roll back. Michael says, “Let’s say the client and coach are in a car heading to the mall. Both know where the mall is. The client is driving; the coach is navigating. The client gets to choose if they turn left, turn right, go in reverse, or even drive in circles. The coach helps increase awareness of options, but not to make the decisions on the route they’re going to take. The mall is the business goal. The car is Agile. The driver is the client, the decision-maker. The navigator is the coach, open for co-brainstorming and sharing experiences and what they see in the client. Clients are free to drive as far and wide in any direction as they wish if they believe it will get them to the goal. This is much more effective than the coach taking the wheel. The clients are the ones who know what works or doesn’t work within their organization. And the client has the authority to make changes in the organization, unlike the coach.” Every company is different. It’s not the coach’s responsibility to know how the company operates, or to dictate the path they must follow. The coach can, however, work with the client to shine a light on areas of the company that will benefit from change. This concept can be understood more clearly with another metaphor. Cherie says, “Two people are walking side-by-side exploring a cave. One person has a flashlight; the other does not. The coach is the one holding the flashlight, and the first question to the client is: “Where do you want to explore?” The client is choosing the way. They are the decision-maker that selects the direction to move through the cave. The coach is helping the client increase their understanding of the different paths that are available by shining the light on them, so the client can see them more clearly.  

---

## Page 126

 
126 
The client decides which paths they want to explore, and which are not useful. Along the way, the coach shines the light on stumbling blocks and dangerous cliffs, so the client can respond and mitigate the risk of injury. If the client decides to climb down a dangerous cliff, the coach shines the light to make sure they see all the possible pathways and impediments. They discuss the plan for getting down safely and the coach accompanies the client in the endeavor, keeping the light shining as they adjust the plan along the way. The paths increase agility which, in turn, helps achieve the overall business goals. The cave is the organizational and business environment, which includes the company’s culture, operating model, and business model. The flashlight is the coaching conversation, shining awareness and providing options, perspectives, experience, and tools. It can bring issues or options to light that the client never even realized existed. Coaching Competency — Partnership According to the ICF, when a coach is competently partnering with clients, these things will be apparent when setting up and working throughout the engagement, The coach partners with the client and relevant stakeholders to establish an overall coaching plan and goals, Partners with the client to determine client-coach compatibility, Partners with the client to identify or reconfirm what they want to accomplish in the engagement, Partners with the client to define what the client believes they need to address or resolve to achieve what they want to accomplish in the engagement,  Partners with the client to define or reconfirm measures of success for what the client wants to accomplish in the coaching engagement,  Partners with the client to manage the time and focus of the engagement Continues coaching in the direction of the client’s desired outcome unless the client indicates otherwise, Partners with the client to end the coaching relationship in a way that honors the experience. Similarly, ICF expects competent coaches to maintain a relationship of mutual respect and trust with the client. They will also partner with clients to create a safe, supportive environment that allows the client to share freely. Some of the things that will be apparent in the engagement if the coach is competent are, The coach seeks to understand the client and client system within their context, which may include their identity, environment, experiences, values, and beliefs, Demonstrates respect for the client and client system’s identity, perceptions, style, and language and adapts one’s coaching to the client,  Acknowledges and respects the client and client system’s unique talents, insights, and work in the coaching process. 

---

## Page 127

 
127 
These three indicators focus on seeing and embracing the client for who they are and where they are at this moment in time. Whether the client is an individual in the organization or the organizational system, you need to be able to step into their shoes and have some empathy for what it is like for them to live in their world. One reason many coaches pick a niche in a particular field is that it is easier to work with clients when you understand their world. As an Agile coach, you understand Agile and how it works. The question is, “Do you understand what it’s like to be your client working in the midst of this change in their imperfect world?” It is important for the coach to be able to develop the empathy to recognize what challenges the client faces. We will not always have the full context to understand the client’s world and the politics and systemic issues that are holding them back. In these moments, we must draw on our empathy and listen to the client. Look beyond the story. Imagine how it must be to live with the unique challenges of their environment. The coach’s responsibility is to take the steps necessary to gain whatever context is needed to meet the client in their world. You don’t have to understand the details of their technical systems and processes, but you do need to understand their culture and how the system functions. You need to understand what it’s like to be in an organization going through massive change that is often out of your control. You need to understand the stress that executives and managers face and recognize that Agile is not their whole job. Coaches must be careful to not dismiss the client’s perspectives because they see things differently. Everyone has different experiences and will often have a view of the world that is unlike that of the coach/Agile coach. Just because your client comes from a command-and-control or waterfall background does not mean that they don’t know what they are doing. Don’t dismiss their experience or discount it because it doesn’t mirror yours. Technology has enabled companies to search out and employ the best talent around the world. This means that you will work with individual clients inside of organizations who have different cultures and backgrounds. As a coach who works with a large variety of people, it is your responsibility to be educated about the context of how your clients experience everyday life. When working internationally, you also must be aware of how people experience the world in their respective countries. You must be open to understanding their world and respecting their viewpoints. You must hold the context in which they experience life as valid, even when it is different from your own. In short, you must remain curious. Curiosity helps you understand the progress the client is making and the courage they must exercise to make individual decisions and changes. Being genuinely interested enables you to have some understanding and empathy. However, you need to make sure that you do not convert that empathy to sympathy because sympathy assumes client brokenness. If you can’t develop this empathy, you will not be able to develop trust and safety in the relationship. Your client, whether the client is an individual or organization, should feel seen, heard, understood, validated, respected, and accepted by you. When this happens, your client can be sure that expressing their truth will be met with a non-judgmental, validating willingness to understand. To create a safe container for your relationship to thrive, you must move into their space rather than expecting them to move to yours.   

---

## Page 128

 
128 
It helps to apply systems thinking when fostering coach-client partnership. Seeing beyond the client into the world they inhabit can help you see through a wider lens and recognize how the client generated the perceptions and beliefs they hold. It can help you gain context for their concerns and suggestions. Understanding is the first step to acceptance. It is also vital that you refrain from making mental connections about information the client shares in a world you have not experienced. Instead, ask questions that allow the client to explore possible connections. It is also crucial that the coach doesn’t presume to understand what it is like to be the client. Be forthright when you don’t have experience in their domain and stress that you will listen to understand more. Be open in disclosing that you might say something inaccurate or unhelpful and ask the client to freely challenge the assumption and make a correction. When you make observations or share intuitions with the client, always ask for the client’s input. Questions following an observation or intuition might be, “How does that resonate with you?” or “Am I getting that right?” or “What do you think?” or “What’s it like to hear that?” It is also yielding direction to the client. When you find yourself thinking, “I don’t know what to ask,” or “I don't know where to go from here,” or “I’m stuck,” that is usually an indicator that the right next move is to ask the client. It may help to understand that you and the client are a system. When you are not sure where to go, this is an expression of the system. It may be telling you that you are trying to lead and are unsure where to go because the next move belongs to the client. Always listen to what the system is telling you.
