Be vulnerable enough to ask, “What would be the most helpful direction for us to go now?” or “Where shall we go from here?” or “What else would you like to say?” This passing of the power shows the coach’s willingness to be okay with not knowing what to do, and it offers a full partnership with the client. Chapter 16 - Coaching for Sustainability  For an Agile coach, one of the most important things to think about is, “What will happen when I’m no longer here?” From day one, you need to be thinking about what will happen when the job is done. Technically you might be done when the contract is over. But let's assume for a moment that the contract doesn’t determine when you disengage, you do. We don’t believe that an external Agile coach should find a permanent home with an organization. External Agile coaches should focus on getting the client up and running and developing sustainable ways to continue improving without rolling back when the external coach leaves. Sustaining Agile means continually responding to change, and continuously learning, and continuously improving without being dependent on an external coach to keep the effort stable. Agile is Never the Goal What do you mean, Agile is not the goal? Of course, it’s the goal! That's why the client hired an Agile coach. This might be hard to hear, but it’s the most important lesson you need to learn if you are doing Agile coaching. Agile is not the goal - business results are the goal. If your client 

---

## Page 129

 
129 
believes Agile is the goal and you are marching to their orders, you’ve got a lot to learn about Agile coaching. Too many times we have worked with organizations and when we question them about why they are adopting Agile methods, frameworks, and practices, the answer is some version of, “I don’t know.” If the client does not know why they are adopting Agile, they shouldn’t be doing it. Not that Agile won’t work. Sure, it will help them improve. The question is, what outcomes are they looking for with the improvements? What return on investment do they want to get if they successfully implement Agile?  In our other chapters in this book, we offer several methods that you can use to work with your client to identify the business results they want to achieve and believe that Agile is the solution to their problem. Without understanding the business results the client wants to get; you won’t know what to focus your efforts on when partnering with them to get Agile started. You might be thinking, everyone knows how to get Agile started. Do training and put people in cross functional teams and help them figure out how to do the ceremonies well. Make sure they are doing daily Scrums (standups), writing user stories, having planning sessions, doing backlog refinement, doing a sprint review, and most importantly doing retrospectives that are fun and engaging. Okay. Good. How do you know that’s what they need? If they are doing all the Scrum events, what business results will that impact? What outcomes will the company see because of the great team meetings? Agile is not plug and play. It’s not something you install the same way every time.  Let's assume for the sake of argument that when creating your well-formed outcomes with the client, they prioritize the number one business problem they are trying to solve: revenue reduction. Customers are abandoning their product, a web and mobile application, because the quality is horrible. They have already identified that the problem in production isn’t that developers are introducing new features and functionality that has defects. The core problem is that they can’t move as fast as their customers need them to and integration and regression tests take weeks to run, so instead of doing full testing they spot check and push to production. Then when the new code is introduced, it works fine but it breaks several other things. The company’s profits have dropped 35% over the past 6 months and there is a continuing downward trend. If they don’t get this fixed, they’re going to be out of business in 12-15 months. Someone recommended Agile so they figured they would give it a try. How does the plug and play starting point of, teach them Scrum, put them in cross functional teams, and make sure they know how to manage a backlog and perform Scrum events address the number one business problem the company needs to solve? It doesn’t. Creating sustainable change in the company is not about getting them to adopt new processes. It’s about getting them to identify the business problems they need to solve and making the changes that solve those problems. Every company won’t need the same cookie cutter 

---

## Page 130

 
130 
installation plan. If they do, there’s a problem. If they do, the coach is likely leading the engagement and installing the equivalent of vanity metrics. “How many Agile teams can we stand up in three months?” It doesn’t matter how many teams you stand up. If the business problems are not being solved, you are wasting your time and your client’s money. The goal is never Agile. The goal is always business results. The Client Can Solve It Stepping in to take over solving the client’s problems for them is not sustainable. At best, such an approach gets compliance and not true change. At worst, it fails completely. It fails because the solution is not coming from the people who must implement it. As previously mentioned, the client is the expert on their company, not the coach - especially if that coach is external.  Allowing the client to devise their own solutions is vital for several reasons. For starters, you may have seen their problem before, but not in their environment. Only the client knows their internal system well enough to decide what will work and what will not work. If we simply dictate what will work for them, we’re just pushing the solution on them. Not only could our proposed solution totally fail in their environment, but pushing a solution is not coaching. “Just do this” is pushing the solution. It’s also an incredibly oversimplified point of view. For companies with tens of thousands of employees and multiple moving parts, “Just do this” could involve hundreds or even thousands of changes. Some of those changes are business choices, and companies may not be able to change them so easily, or at all. The client is the one who must live with the implemented solutions to their problems. We don’t. Their solutions must work for them. Their solutions also need to be sustainable, so they continue to work going forward. It’s not our company. We don’t have to live with it. That’s an incredibly important point to keep in mind. Michael says, “Partner with the client and let them come up with their own solutions. Not only do they have to live with the results of the solution they design, but they are the ones who must implement the course of action they decide to take. An Agile coach can be a source of information, but not the source of the solution.” Indicators of Sustainability We have listed several conditions that give an indication that the change being adopted by the organization is sustainable. There are others, and each client is different. However, if you partner with the client to find ways to create these conditions, they will be well on their way to having sustainability. Culture of Learning: This means that learning is valued throughout the company, and on multiple levels. This striving for continued learning is built into the company’s core and will become integral, as reflected in everything from job descriptions to the everyday behavior of managers. The organization is moving forward, learning new things, and implementing them without the coach prompting or pointing the way. Experimentation is happening and when new methods are found that solve problems the organization has, they are shared with others. 

---

## Page 131

 
131 
Teams are teaching teams and there is a mentality of ownership and a self-enforcement of continuous improvement.  Continuous Improvement Cycles: When a company can implement inspection and adaptation on a regular cycle, as well as accurately analyze and measure the results of those changes, that is an indication that it is ready to sustain agility. But the key to this, is its being continuous. Every single quarter or week, at multiple levels or functions, potential improvements are considered. If implemented, feedback, quantitative and otherwise, is gathered about the benefits of the change.   Change Based on the Company’s Context; Not Copying the Success of Others: The successful change comes from within. It needs to be developed based on the needs and strengths of an individual company. To simply copy the success of another company ignores that no two companies are alike. A giraffe’s neck would not work for a mouse; likewise, one company’s operating model or culture may not work for another company. To be sustainable a company must be able to innovate and create in ways that are designed for its individual strengths and success. A Method to Measure Experiments: This is more than having experiments that are measurable or a system for measuring. Rather it is a change in mindset. An experiment is not actually experimenting if it is not given the space to fail. A company is not sustainable on its own until it is freely able to implement trials that are allowed to fail. And additionally, systems must be in place to learn from those failures to adapt for future trials. Evidence of Sustainability: Perhaps the most surefire way for a coach to know the company is ready for them to move on is to simply leave and come back. One way to accomplish this is by achieving a measure of sustained agility with one section of a company, and then leaving to work with another part of the company for several months before returning to see how the section did on their own. The End of Coaching May Never Come Remember that these are just possible signs that a company has achieved sustainability. Ideally there is an interplay of several markers. And, over time, every Agile coach will identify other markers that serve as signposts of sustainability. It is important to understand that many organizations will never achieve a sustainable Agile transformation. Some engagements will end with sustainability. Some engagements will be a maintainable one-time improvement. Others will revert to the old way of operating. This isn’t necessarily a failure of the coach, it’s a symptom of the client system. Mental Models Here are four mental models that help coaches support organizations in achieving sustainability. 

---

## Page 132

 
132 
1.  Double Loop Learning and Improvement: Most organizations reflect on execution, but do not reflect on goals, policies, and structure with the same intensity and frequency. Chris Argyris, who first described double loop learning, gives the example of a thermostat set to 68: single loop learning involves raising and lowering the temperature, so it is 68; double loop learning involves reflecting on whether 68 is the right temperature. 2.  Radical Transformation: What are the “untouchables” in the organization that cannot be changed? Apple Computer took the Computer off its company name and went into business selling digital music and phones for more profit. And IBM switched from hardware to services. Not holding on to rigid notions of identity, but instead being open to changing everything about the business, let these companies radically innovate and adapt. To the degree that a company can do this, its Agile transformation is more likely to be sustainable and to lead to success. 3.  Four-Frame Model:  The four frames are structural, political, symbolic and human. The key is helping the client to not only view an organization through these four lenses, but also making sure that they are able to act based on that understanding.  4.  Focus on Leadership: An organization is only ever as Agile as its leaders. How are leaders invested in agility, not only in the company’s but in their personal participation in achieving that agility. 5. Various Organizational Change Management Models: Models such as Kotter, Adkar, Nudge, Kubler-Ross, Bridges, and Satir are various models that can be used with thinking about how to bring about sustainable change in organizations.  Practices to Achieve Sustainable Agility Here are six practices worth mentioning that can support creating a sustainable Agile company 1.  Encourage Communities of Practice (CoPs): These highly organic communities can be encouraged and nurtured by Agile coaches, but from there they are meant to flourish on their own. CoPs are examples of cooperative learning across multiple levels of a company, creating an ideal situation for a culture of learning. 2.  Develop a Coaching System within the Organization: Focus on developing internal coaches and mentors that will stay with the organization and continue to partner with them over time. Scrum Masters are a standard role that involves coaching. Also look for people who can be developed into technical, business, product and innovation coaches and mentors. 3.  Retrospective Practices: An examination of the past to improve the future is a common practice in highly Agile organizations. Understanding a complex system is not easy. If it is easy, then it is not being understood. One specific practice that can help is Toyota’s A3 Report which, when done well, can feel like a root canal.  4.  Amplifying What Is Working and Implementing Successful Communication and Sharing Patterns: If one part of the company finds something that works successfully, what 

---

## Page 133

 
133 
good does that do the organization at large if the message doesn’t get out? Buurtzorg is a nationwide home nursing organization founded in 2006. Though employing 9000, they are broken up into 900 different teams. At Buurtzorg, nurses who find successful practices directly share them with other nursing teams through a company-wide messaging system instead of petitioning management for approval to roll out the improvement. 5.  Innovation Events: Hack-a-thons, Code Jams, Code Fests, call them whatever you want. These types of events ensure that the organization is taking the time to innovate and improve. When employees are saddled with too many deadlines or the production goals are too tight, that means there’s nothing left over for the experimentation and innovation that leads to agility. These events can help. 6.  Creating an Executive Agile Council: This is a team that is authorized to help agility spread throughout the company. It is typically composed of people from multiple levels and from different functional areas. 
Chapter 17 - Coaching Values and Principles Coaching Agile Values and Principles True or False: As an Agile enterprise coach, your goal is to uphold the four values and 12 principles outlined in the Agile Manifesto — while enforcing those values to ensure every single client does the same. Before you answer, be aware that this is a trick question. The first half of the statement is true. You certainly do want to uphold the values and principles in your coaching. But the second part of the statement is false. While you do want to introduce clients to the Agile values and principles, it’s not your job to enforce them to ensure compliance. The coach serves more like the messenger and less like the enforcer. In fact, you’re not an enforcer at all. Coaches need to walk the Agile walk, but your clients don’t necessarily have to. That decision is up to them. We’ll get deeper into this concept later in the chapter. We’ll also look at embedding the values and principles into the culture, coaching a client according to their values and principles, and identifying and coaching anti-patterns. Before we move forward, let’s review the four values and 12 principles found in the Agile Manifesto to ensure we’re all on the same page from the get-go. Four Values: Individuals and interactions over processes and tools Working software over comprehensive documentation Customer collaboration over contract negotiation 

---

## Page 134

 
134 
Responding to change over following a plan   12 Principles: 1. Our highest priority is to satisfy the customer through early and continuous delivery of valuable software. 2. Welcome changing requirements, even late in development. Agile processes harness change for the customer's competitive advantage. 3. Deliver working software frequently, from a couple of weeks to a couple of months, with a preference to the shorter timescale. 4. Business people and developers must work together daily throughout the project. 5. Build projects around motivated individuals. Give them the environment and support they need and trust them to get the job done. 6. The most efficient and effective method of conveying information to and within a development team is face-to-face conversation. 7. Working software is the primary measure of progress. 8. Agile processes promote sustainable development. The sponsors, developers, and users should be able to maintain a constant pace indefinitely. 9. Continuous attention to technical excellence and good design enhances agility. 10. Simplicity—the art of maximizing the amount of work not done—is essential. 11. The best architectures, requirements, and designs emerge from self-organizing teams. 12. At regular intervals, the team reflects on how to become more effective, then tunes and adjusts its behavior accordingly. Sensing Values Every organization you meet is going to have its own set of values. One of your first tasks as an Agile enterprise coach is to sense what those values are. Sure, you could pour over lists, documents, and well-written vision statements to figure that out. But there’s a magic trick here you can use instead: Just ask people. You want to understand how the culture is already in alignment and not in alignment with the organization’s values, and the best way to find out is by asking those within the organization. Surveying management, various teams and different functional areas will give you a lot of good information. You want to help the client see how the organization is honoring (or not honoring) the values and principles. Cherie does a collaborative exercise to bring this transparency. First, facilities a breakout discussion on the full intent of the values and principles. (Note: this might be several events where the group of people look at a few items each time you meet.) Once the participants are clear on what the value or principle in question means, the gather in group to brainstorm three things: How is this value/principle expressed in our organization today? 

---

## Page 135

 
135 
In what ways is our organization suppressing or violating this value/principle today? What will need to change for us to get the best results while honoring this value/principle The output of these sessions is an improvement backlog. The items are categorized into things the teams can handle, things that need to be elevated to managers to handle, and things that are systemic issues that need bigger solutions and leadership involvement to change. One method Michael uses to survey the organization is to list the four values, along with a scale that runs from one to 10. Ask people to rate where they are with each of the four values, and then ask them where they want to be. Let’s say for each of the four values, the organization is currently at two. Ask them where they want to be. If the client finds they are not honoring their values, your next step is to ask them what they want to do. A client may not want to slide it all the way over to 10. Coaches need to respect that. This holds true with the values as well as the principles. Michael says, “One of the big mistakes coaches make is assuming every client wants to be perfect on Agile techniques. The coach assumes the organization wants to take everything to the max, take every category to an 11. This is a surefire way for the coach to end up frustrated. Coaches who don’t respect the client’s desires typically have an agenda all their own. You want to avoid that. Let the client drive the change.” How to Coach According to Values and Principles When the client appears to violate a value or principle, it’s crucial for the coach to refrain from slipping into a place of judgement. Coaches need to start from a place of curiosity, always phrasing things in terms of curiosity rather than judgement. Rather than admonishing a client, be curious about what’s happening for the client and what problem the client is trying to solve with the action they took. Act like a partner to the client because that’s what you are. Explain how the situation appears to you. Then inquire about their perspective. Focus on the outcomes, not the outputs. There may be a dozen different behaviors or activities that achieve the same desired results. Encourage your client to come up with behaviors or activities that align with the values and principles and will get them the outcome they desire. Then, collaborate with the client to design a process that incorporates those behaviors, to get more of what they want while being consistent with their values and principles. Coaching Frustrations Using Values and Principles When people within the organization are confused or frustrated, it’s often an indication that some value or principle is not being honored. People will feel impeded, as if things are not flowing smoothly. Emotions are a great gauge for checking in on values and principles, raising a red flag that it’s time for a check in. These types of issues are great for a 1-1 or group coaching session. At a high level, this is what a coaching session would cover. First, allow them to ventilate a bit about what’s frustrating them. People need to know that their concerns are heard and validated. Find out what specifically is frustrating them whether it is an action, a decision, or 

---

## Page 136

 
136 
something else. Then, ask what problem someone was attempting to solve by doing “x” that is causing the frustration. Let them brainstorm some ideas and get perspective on why “x” is happening. Then, ask them to reflect on which values or principles are being violated with “x.” Once they have identified the values or principles that are being violated, they will get a better perspective of what antipatterns look like. They will also be able to see clearer how violating the Agile values and principles creates frustration even when people don’t realize consciously that there is a violation. This is because the Agile values and principles inherently lead to a better work environment. When violated they lead to frustration. Lastly, ask them how they can get the same results without violating the value or principle. Let them brainstorm ideas and experiments to try. Close by having them develop a plan for their next actions to resolve the frustration and solve the original problem at the same time. Anti-Patterns From an Agile coaching perspective, we could define a pattern as something in our experience that has led to success. Maintaining a pattern doesn’t necessarily mean we follow a precise template to achieve that success; it’s instead a way of encapsulating the results. A pattern takes you to desirable results while remaining in alignment with Agile values and principles. An anti-pattern does just the opposite. It’s a decision, action, or solution that solves the presenting issue but violates the Agile values or principles and ultimately leads to unwanted impacts. Even though the “anti-” prefix and the thought of unwanted results may make you think you must shut out all anti-patterns, that’s not always the case. Working from home is an example of a common anti-pattern. This previously non-ideal behavior has become acceptable for many businesses as stay-at-home orders were put into place during the Covid-19 pandemic. Circumstances may put anti-patterns into play when it comes to business decisions, but you don’t want to make them a habit when it comes to enterprise coaching. Coaching Anti-patterns to look out for include: There aren’t only anti-patterns that violate Agile values and principles. Some anti-patterns are exhibited by coaches, and they violate coaching competencies, ethics, or mindset. The examples listed are “Do no harm” violations. Collusion: This occurs when two people talk about a third person behind the third person’s back. The two people are colluding together, with one enlisting the support of the other against the third. Here you’re not respecting people, not taking them to be complete and whole. You’re conspiring against them. A coach can collude with clients when the client is complaining about others. When the coach jumps in, thinking they are giving empathy, and agrees and allows the conversation to focus on 

---

## Page 137

 
137 
what others are doing that the client can’t change. The coach is colluding with the client to not take responsibility for their own contributions to the problem and to focus on the only thing they can control — their own actions. A coach also colludes with clients when they offer too much support and not enough challenge. If you are watching your client make bad decisions or see them ready to walk off a cliff, and you don’t challenge them, you are colluding with them to continue in their sub-optimal performance. Ninja coaching: This refers to coaching without permission. Coaching is a partnership. You cannot coach someone who has not asked for or agreed to coaching. Every conversation is not intended to be a coaching conversation. Coaching skills are good communication skills so utilizing them in everyday conversations is fine. However, if you find you are trying to move the person you are chatting with forward when they haven’t asked you to do that, you are ninja coaching. If people say things like, “You’re doing that coaching thing to me!” It's time to take a good look at your interactions. Policing: If you’re acting as the enforcer of the Agile way of doing things, you’re not coaching. You’re policing. Here you’re taking control away from the client. The client needs to be the one to make the change; control needs to always be in the hands of the client. Focus on what they want to improve or change, not on making sure Agile by the book is adhered to. Superhero mentality: Coaches aren’t meant to swoop in and rescue an organization. If a client cries, “Come fix this!” and you do, you are falling into this mentality. The goal of Agile coaching is not to fix an organization. We serve as guides, not superheroes. If you need to save your clients, reflect on why. The impulse to save them might be a habit that needs to be broken, or it could be something deeper. Sometimes saving others and protecting them gives people a sense of value or serves some inner need. If that’s the case, then you are focusing on your needs instead of the client’s needs. Sometimes the urge to save others is because you are assuming that the client is broken or not competent to save themselves.  Which brings us to the next point. Not seeing clients as competent: There’s a very simple marker for this anti-pattern: simply review the thoughts that run through your head about the client. If you’re thinking how you can’t believe they’re in an executive position, or how you could do a much better job running the business than they do, you are falling into this trap. When you get frustrated because clients are doing things that are not right, you may be judging their competence. Instead, get curious. Give them the benefit of the doubt that if they are doing something that seems strange to you, there must be a valid reason. Judging: Judging involves assessing if a client is doing good or bad. It’s not for the coach to evaluate if the client is doing a good job. The coach doesn’t decide if decisions or solutions determined by the client are right or wrong. Clients are supposed to evaluate themselves. Coaches can certainly help them get raw data to review, but it’s not the coach’s job to do the actual reviewing. 

---

## Page 138

 
138 
Being sucked into the system: Also known as being “in the grip,” this occurs when a coach becomes emotionally invested in the client or situation. The coach can no longer hold emotional distance from the organization. Warning signs include coming home exhausted or finding yourself hoping and praying everything works out. This is an indicator that you are sympathizing with the client rather than empathizing. When you empathize with a client it’s like seeing them fall overboard and recognizing that they likely need a life preserver and help to get back in the boat. When you sympathize with a client it's more like seeing them fall overboard and jumping in after them when you know you can’t swim. Now both of you are likely to drown. Taking sides: Disagreements inside an organization are not uncommon, and it’s the coach’s job to remain a neutral third party. If you advocate on behalf of one person, group, or viewpoint, you are taking sides. You want to help ensure all voices are heard, even the marginalized ones, without bolstering one side or the other. Fearing for your job: There’s a big difference between doing what the client wants and doing what the client needs. If you’re leaning toward the former just to save your job, you’re falling into the trap. You’re also fueling a fear-based mentality that may be rife throughout the entire organization. Coaches need to be okay with keeping their job or not keeping their job if they’re doing what’s right. This might be a hard way of thinking because the reality is everyone needs income. However, if you constantly make decisions using, “How can I keep my job?” as criteria, you are making fear-based decisions and those aren’t helpful for you or your clients. Learn to separate your needs from the needs of the client. Focus on what the client needs when coaching. Focus on what you need in private or with a coach or coaching supervisor. Using vanity metrics to prove your value: Vanity metrics are typically based on activities, like how many people you coached, how many attendees came to your seminar, or how many seminars you’ve held in the past year. As we’ve said before, the number of Agile teams you’ve started and the number of successful ceremonies you’ve attended only show that you are doing something with your time. Instead, focus on the business outcomes the client wants to achieve, create clear measures of success and progress for those. Use these as the measures that indicate that you are helping to drive business outcomes. [Michael] Recently I had a client focusing on business development. He wanted to reach out to a one-million-person non-profit organization to develop a relationship that might lead to a business deal. Our coaching helped him figure out how to effectively reach out to them. He not only started a relationship but went on to close a partnership deal with the non-profit. This is what matters: business results. Coaching a single person with a powerful outcome is worth much more than coaching 1,000 people with nothing to show for it. 
   

---

## Page 139

 
139 
 
Chapter 18: Experimentation   Running Experiments If Christopher Columbus had used the traditional approach to planning we see today, he would have likely ended up at the bottom of the ocean. The traditional approach involves mapping out a step-by-step game plan that is executed to achieve a promised goal at the end of the road.  One problem is that anything that falls short of reaching the goal is considered a failure. Organizations may try, again and again, sticking with their approach no matter what, even if dismal results appear to be the only thing on the horizon.  Another challenge is that the traditional approach only tends to work in a fairly stable, predictable, and understandable environment. The massive, unknown stretch of the Atlantic Ocean in the late 1400s hardly counts as any of the above.  Columbus never reached his goal of finding a westward water route from Europe to Asia, but he’s not considered a failure. He’s considered a great explorer for what he did find instead. He didn’t stumble across the Americas by adhering to a rigid plan that dictated his every action no matter what. He instead was looking at the environment every single moment, consistently adjusting the sails to best catch the wind, and constantly adapting to changes as quickly as needed.  In short, he was inspecting and adapting to change. Adapting to change is one of the Agile Manifesto values, and this chapter examines the practice and culture of experimentation as a way to be more adaptable.  What Experiments Are Running experiments simply refers to trying things out and learning from what happens. We are not referring to “experiment” in the scientific sense, although we will certainly draw on some aspects of a scientific experiment. Organizational experiments can run the gamut from a small change in how paperwork is filed all the way to a larger restructuring of a company process.  Unlike experiments in the scientific sense, there may be no hypothesis that’s to be supported, refuted, or validated. It’s essential to have various options while we experiment in our coaching approach to see which experiments yield favorable results.   Probes into the Unknown Thinking of experiments as probes can be helpful. They are probes exploring an unknown map, just like Columbus, where you don’t necessarily know where to go next until you gather more information.  

---

## Page 140

 
140 
You’re sending out probes into unchartered land. You need to know where the food is, where the dangers are, where the water supply is, where you can find shelter and other things you need to survive.  Rather than running one experiment at a time, you’re able to run many probes in parallel. Multiple probes can be sent out at the same time, all of them looking for answers that align with what needs to be achieved.  As you get into the groove of running experiments, you’ll be able to run probes at faster rates and in greater quantities. You may also strengthen the ability to quickly shift from a probe that’s not working into a new option that might.  Not an Easy Process Sending out probes is not easy, especially when faced with organizations that are cemented into doing things the traditional way. To get an organization from where it is to where it wants to go is going to require changes. This could include changes in processes, changes in the management system, or changes that require additional training.  Even when a change is for the better, some organizations may become defensive and resistant19 about giving up what they’ve become accustomed to. In fact, most of them do. Resistance against change can come from any combination of factors, such as: ● Company culture: Change runs the risk of disrupting the company’s cultural equilibrium, which may produce unwanted consequences for certain employees ● Company structure: Companies tend to have set structures that have become rigid. Certain people and departments may only interact with other select people or departments. Change can knock people off their well-known perches. ● Sunk costs: Organizations may have already invested in a plan to reach a specific goal, and they may look at changing that plan as losing their investment. They can also fear the potentially high cost of change, and the risk of losing money in yet another venture if the ideas don’t pan out. ● Existing contracts and customers: Changing course may come with a change in existing contractual agreements with vendors, suppliers, and individuals. Companies can also fear losing key customers who may not be happy with a change. Even though organizations may bring on enterprise coaches to support the changes they so desperately need, some level of resistance may still be part of the package. Trying to push change through a single individual in the organization is not going to work. Our aim is to acknowledge and accept the factors that may be birthing resistance, and work within them.  Right Sized Plan It’s likewise crucial to create experiments that are the right size for the company. Not only the right size in terms of the amount and types of changes they may require, but the right size for 
 19   https://www.inc.com/samuel-bacharach/why-companies-even-yours-resist-change.html  

---

## Page 141

 
141 
their operations and budget. You don’t want to propose multi-million-dollar experiments that involve restructuring entire organizations when the budget is in the thousands.  A right sized approach is also one that’s the right fit for what the individuals within the company feel they can afford in terms of time and effort. The probes must fit the organization, with ideas they can actually try and are comfortable with. You never want to make a company feel uncomfortable, or as if they’re biting off far more than they can chew.  You’re not going to enter a situation and say, “We’re going to cross the Atlantic or bust.” You first need to figure out how to cross the river, then the Great Lakes, and then the ocean can be next in line.  You want to start with a situation as it is. Ask how big of an experiment can we do? How much can we change at this phase of our development?  No Plan Set in Stone Experiments differ from the standard mindset when it comes to sticking to a plan. Tasks outlined in traditional plans sometimes appear to take on the power of overriding law for what’s to be done, and when.  It doesn’t matter if the plan is obviously failing, employees and customers are unhappy, or if the business is losing money while the plan is in motion. It needs to stay in motion, regardless, until it’s seen all the way to the end.  Running experiments under the Agile approach to coaching is quite the opposite. There is no plan set months, or even years in advance. You instead devise and run a set of experiments that may or may not move the organization in the desired direction. Based on the results of those experiments, you can then decide what’s next.  Unlike the traditional approach that outlines a set goal at the end, we enterprise coaches cannot tell our clients what the results and benefits of each experiment are going to be. We’ll only know that after the fact. Also, unlike the traditional approach, someone is not simply collecting data on what’s been done. Yes, gathering data is still part of the process, but so is learning. Everyone is learning as the experiments are taking place and adapting or adjusting course as that learning is happening. If one experiment doesn’t work, you need to be willing to switch quickly to the next one – without considering the first experiment a failure.  Michael: I was coaching a 40-person team for about six weeks when I found myself getting stuck. The mistake I made was to not experiment. I had picked a coaching approach in advance and was banging my head against the wall, as the approach was not working. Instead of listening to the environment, listening to the people, and acknowledging the obvious signs that the approach wasn’t working, I dug my heels in further. “I know I’m right,” I told myself. “This has to work.” 

---

## Page 142

 
142 
 Now I know it’s about being humble. Being flexible. My ego insisted I was right, railed against changing course. But I needed to be doing something from a place of curiosity, not ego. Curiosity lets me approach things with wonder, with a true fascination with what may work and what may not. Ego holds onto its insistence that it’s right, even when the ship is clearly sinking.  Redefining 'Failure' Regardless of the outcome of any given experiment, none of them are considered failures. That’s because something valuable is gained along the way. Even if the experiment does not provide an outcome that is particularly beneficial to the client, everyone involved in the experiment has learned something.  They are better off now than they were before the experiment began, as they now have additional knowledge. Even knowledge of what doesn’t work is helpful, as it can serve as a guide moving forward for setting up the next set of experiments.  With this type of approach, lots of things are not going to work. That’s not only perfectly fine, but it’s also actually expected. Things aren’t always going to work when you’re probing into unexplored territory and trying something new.  The only way to prevent so-called failure is to never do anything new. It’s essential to have a culture and mindset that allows for trying new things that may not work. You don’t want to yell and scream at the existing system. You want to take what you’re given and determine how it can be made better.  The harder the problem, the more valuable it is. And the more learning you’re going to have to do to solve it. More challenging problems are going to require much more experimentation than easy problems, providing many more chances for the experimentation not to work.  Coaches need to keep this in mind to ensure you don’t get frustrated and start doubting yourself. Self-management is crucial to keep your own emotions and needs in check. Another key thing a coach needs to do during the experimentations is to not give up.  Don’t throw up your hands in despair when yet another experiment may not deliver the results you want. Simply move on to the next option that might.  -- Michael: I was working with a 300-person startup, and we started off by holding one-hour weekly meetings. At the first few meetings, there was a lot of fanfare and tons of people in attendance. But the latest meeting only had two people in attendance, and one dropped out after 30 minutes.  

---

## Page 143

 
143 
“Wow,” I said to myself. “This is obviously not working.” I needed to better communicate the value proposition of the collaboration we were trying to achieve. But I also needed to be humble and experiment with different approaches.   I started thinking that maybe I needed to cut down the meeting time to 30 minutes, hold meetings every other week, or send emails instead of expecting people to take time out for a meeting. Simply trying to force my way to work was just me thrusting my own agenda on an organization instead of listening to the organization and paying attention to what I must learn. The audience is clearly telling me that the meetings are not valuable enough for them to spend an hour a week attending.   Experiment Template Setting up experiments can be much more effective when you have some type of template to follow. The following template might be helpful: ● What is the current state or situation? ● In the current situation ○ Describe what is going well: ○ Describe what is not going well: ● What is going well or not going well in terms of ○ Specific facts or statistics ○ Emotions ○ Needs ● What is needed to improve the situation? What is the overall direction we need to travel? ● Here are things we can try out to move in the desired direction.  How are people feeling inside and outside the organization? What do they need to have to improve or enhance the situation? Traditional approaches may not always, or ever, take emotions and needs into account.  Once it’s clear what’s lacking in the current state, the client can then establish a north-star goal, or the overall direction in which the client wants the organization to move. All the experiments the company runs can then be designed to proceed in the direction of the goal.     Example of Experiment Using Template Let’s use a dip in customer service satisfaction as an example of the template. It might look something like this: ● What is the current state or situation? ○ Customer satisfaction down 20% ● In the current situation 

---

## Page 144

 
144 
○ Describe what is going well: Customer service reps are very attentive to every detailed need of customers they help ○ Describe what is not going well: Reps spending more time than typical with each customer, leaving many customers waiting for extended periods to talk to a rep or receive an email response ● What is going well or not going well in terms of ○ Specific facts or statistics: Customers who do get help are happy about the extended time the support team spends with them ○ Emotions: ■ Customers who fall between the cracks are frustrated by lack of attention ■ Employees frustrated by customers complaining about lack of attention, fed up with explaining the same solutions over and over to most customers who call for help, fed up with being yelled at by customers who are waiting to be helped, distracted and overwhelmed by customer service requests piling up and needing answers ○ Needs: ■ Customers need support, attention, security; need to feel content, secure and cared for ■ Customer service reps need appreciation, respect, encouragement; need to feel content, helpful, intrigued, relaxed and as if workload is under control ● What is needed to improve the situation? What is the overall direction we need to travel? ○ Increase customer satisfaction rate to be on par or above past performance ○ Communication, clarity, more efficient response process ○ Meet emotional and human needs of customers and employees as outlined above ● Here are things we can try out to move in the desired direction. ○ Automate certain customer service requests that keep coming up again and again ○ Invest in a chat bot to handle routine requests ○ Set up automated response to email requests to ensure customers at least know their emails have been received ○ Assign different customer service reps to work in different teams, with teams each responsible for different segment of customers needing help ○ Hire more customer service reps ○ Provide more information and instructions on website ○ The list goes on….  Benefits of Running Experiments Running experiments comes with a multitude of benefits, with the foremost being that we are modeling what we are teaching. As enterprise coaches, we’re constantly instructing companies to take an Agile approach – and we need to be Agile ourselves. We need to listen to our audience and be willing to learn from so-called failure.  

---

## Page 145

 
145 
The process of collecting and examining data throughout the experiments is another huge benefit. Data serves to drive decision-making. As the company changes, it finds itself in a different place so continuously examining data is critical.  We can’t say we’ll be in the same place we’re currently in in a month from now. If we’ve done a month’s worth of work, we’re going to be in a different place that requires different experiments. The traditional method of creating a one-year plan puts forth the proposition that we’re able to predict where we’ll be 11 months from now. It expects the tactics outlined in the plan will still be relevant and effective. They may very well not be. If we’re disciplined and impeccable in our ability to gather and analyze data throughout the experiments, we’ll have a good idea about where we are. We’ll also know what has worked and what hasn’t worked to get there.  One more remarkable benefit of running experiments is being able to face reality as it is. It shows we both recognize and accept that the world is unknown and highly unknowable. We don’t live in a clockwork universe. We live in a VUCA world.  Experiments Ideal in Our VUCA World The military concept of VUCA describes today’s world quite accurately. The VUCA acronym20 comes from the U.S. Army, which coined it to describe the post-Cold War environment. VUCA stands for: ● Volatile ● Uncertain ● Complex ● Ambiguous  This same environment exists for many organizations in the corporate world. Rapid change makes the environment volatile. The lack of predictability makes it uncertain. The unknown cause and effect of various forces leads to complexity. And there is an enormous amount of ambiguity when plunging into new situations. Experiments are the ideal way to determine what works and what doesn’t in a VUCA world.  One final benefit of experiments is giving us coaches the green light to say the three little words that have instilled fear in many for centuries: “I don’t know.” Not only are these words incredibly freeing, but they allow us to approach each situation with a beginner’s mind, a child’s sense of wonder.  Yes, it’s a far cry from the standard mindset where experts are supposed to know the answer, but it’s also the way some of the most exceptional growth and discoveries are made. We’re not trying to fit preconceived notions of what we “should” be doing according to some extended plan. We’re looking at what works in this situation, for these particular people, for these particular circumstances.  20   https://chiefexecutive.net/understanding-vuca-environment/ 

---

## Page 146

 
146 
 We’re constantly re-examining what we’re doing and recreating ourselves to be the most effective in the current situation. This dynamic way of doing things can often bring about the most amazing results. If anyone out there needs additional proof, simply ask Christopher Columbus.  After mapping out the challenges and possible experiments using the template it’s time to start running experiments. You could just try things and see what works. And it will be more helpful if you define how you will know that the experiment is successful in terms of making the expected impact and adopting the experiment as a new way of working.  Scientific experimentation lends us another process too that can be helpful when running experiments: Testing with hypotheses.   Let's look at the list of experiments defined in the example above.  ● Here are things we can try out to move in the desired direction. ○ Automate certain customer service requests that keep coming up again and again ○ Invest in a chat bot to handle routine requests ○ Set up automated response to email requests to ensure customers at least know their emails have been received ○ Assign different customer service reps to work in different teams, with teams each responsible for different segment of customers needing help ○ Hire more customer service reps ○ Provide more information and instructions on website  The experiment template gets you to a list of things to try and the hypothesis tells you what to look for in the experiments. Keep in mind that you may get a different result than expected. Sometimes that result is great, and you’ll want to adopt it and other times it's not so great and you’ll learn what you don’t want to continue doing.   The goal of the experiments is to impact the list generated in the template. So, the hypothesis should look at the experiments through this lens. The experiments should be contributing to moving towards these outcomes. ○ Increase customer satisfaction rate to be on par or above past performance ○ Communication, clarity, more efficient response process ○ Meet emotional and human needs of customers and employees as outlined above  A quick and easy template for hypothesis is: Experiment: [ High level statement of experiment] We believe that [by doing whatever experiment you are doing] [this will result] Which will: [impact on the outcomes you are trying to achieve] We will know that we have a successful outcome when: [measurement criteria] 

---

## Page 147

 
147 
Timebox for experiment  Let's take this one and develop a hypothesis statement: Experiment: Provide more information and instructions on website We believe that: by providing easily identified and accessible information and instructions on the website we will reduce customer service wait times by ___ %.  ● We expect customers who check the website before calling will utilize the website information to answer most of their questions which will reduce the number of calls coming into the call center and thereby reduce the overall wait time. ● We expect call center personnel to share links with customers directing them to the information which will reduce contact time with each customer and reduce overall customer wait times.  To determine if this experiment successfully brings us closer to our goals of increasing the customer satisfaction rate, we will measure the following things: ● Number of calls to the call center month over month [we expect the number of calls to be reduced] ● Wait time to reach a call center representative [we expect it to go down] ● Number of visits to the information on the site  ● Length of time visitors stayed on the pages with the information ● Interaction levels with the website information [what they are interacting with and what they are not] ● Percentage of searches that do not produce results [indicates missing information we may want to add] ● Number of emails customer service reps are sending with links ● Trend in customer satisfaction  Measurements and responses: ● We will run baseline measurements at the beginning of the experiment. ● We will review measurements monthly and monitor trends over 6 months. ● If trends are moving in the expected direction we will continue to move from experiment to adoption. ● If trends are not changing, we will investigate adjustments and continue for another 3 months ● If trends are moving in a negative direction, we will pull the information off the site and end the experiment.  Utilizing experiments that produce data helps your client to be able to make educated, evidence-based decisions. It also provides a tangible way for you to show your value as a coach to the organization. Which brings us to the next chapter … Metrics.    

---

## Page 148

 
148 
Chapter 19: Developing Metrics that Matter [Alex and Cherie will be adding more to this chapter]  Metrics that Work for Agile Coaching   When it comes to proving the value of Agile coaching, which approach do you think works better: ● Telling the CEO that everything’s great, employees and customers are happy, and even the air feels lighter since you’ve started coaching at the organization.   ● Showing the CEO a color-coded graph that indicates company revenue has increased 20% and market share has increased by 12% since you’ve started coaching at the organization.  The second approach is going to win you more credibility—and clients. And it’s all because of metrics.    When it comes to metrics that work for Agile coaching, however, not just any old measurement will do. Not only do you need to be selective about the metrics you choose, but you must be equally fastidious while putting your measurement methods into practice.   This chapter fills you in on metrics that work for Agile coaching. You’ll get a rundown on measurements that make the most sense, best practices for putting them into action, and crucial elements to keep in mind as you help move a company forward toward success.  Metrics Defined  For our purposes, metrics are defined as the results of measuring something. With a definition that’s so straightforward, you may wonder how things could go wrong.  But they can. And rather quickly. That’s why you need to be clear and decisive about what metrics you’re intending to use from the very start.  When you’re looking for measurements that show progress, you’re not looking for team metrics or organizational metrics. And you’re not looking for metrics that show progress in Agile—you want metrics that show progress is being made about business results.   Agile coaching helps to accelerate the business results. But you don’t want measurements related to Agile. You want the numbers related to the actual business results or goals the company is aiming to hit.   ● What goals are being accelerated? ● How do you measure them?  

---

## Page 149

 
149 
 These questions are best answered during the initial engagement with the organization to ensure you’re all measuring the same thing from the get-go.   Part of the Agile coach’s responsibility is to help companies select the correct metrics. Organizations often have the wrong metrics or are using them incorrectly. For example, a company may say it wants to increase team velocity, which is an admirable goal. But it’s not appropriate as a metric for your purposes.   Michael says, “Many organizations automatically ask us for Agile stuff—metrics related to Agile training or improving the Agile team. CEOs don’t care about metrics related to Agile. They care about metrics that matter to the business, like revenues or market share. Not things like Agile team velocity.”    Since many companies haven’t thought about the different metrics that specifically align with business results, Agile coaches must help them with this. And, as noted, it’s important to provide this help right from the start.   Keeping Things Balanced  Cherie - add content on Balanced Scorecard  Unless they’re aiming to go out of business, organizations want their metrics to improve. They want more revenue. A greater market share. More products to market, and faster.  Any single metric can be easily gamed. But you also must look at what will be lost. If you’re trying to go up with one of the metrics, another metric is destined to go down. You want to have a set of metrics that balances each other out.   A good example here is companies that want to release products to the customer more frequently. One way to increase the frequency of releases would be to release junk. If the frequency increases in this manner, the quality and/or customer retention is going to decrease.   It’s essential to have a balanced perspective—and a counterweight. You need to know what impact the increase of one metric will have on the others.   The Agile coach helps companies establish metrics that will be valuable to them across the board, going to a higher level of thinking than they are accustomed to. Especially when it comes to keeping things balanced.   Product management expert Roman Pichler takes the concept further with his approach to Balanced Product Scorecards.   INSERT MORE INFO FROM CHERIE HERE   

---

## Page 150

 
150 
Metrics Rules of Thumb  Making metrics work for an organization is easier when you keep several guidelines in mind.   Consider at least three counterbalancing metrics. Once you target a metric, make sure you counterbalance it properly. You need to pay attention to the unintended consequences. What behavior will you drive by targeting a specific metric?    We can again use the speed to market example. You’ll increase the number of releases, but you may end up with tons of junky releases. Counterbalance the speed to market metric with metrics like quality, customer complaints, net promoter score (NPS), and escaped defects.    Know what you can measure. You can measure just about anything—if you’re willing to put in the effort. Figuring out how to collect data for the metric requires effort, as does building out the measurement capability itself. Once an organization is willing to invest in measurement capabilities, it can measure things it didn’t think it could.    Move measurements from subjective to objective. When it comes to measurements, you want to move up the scale from subjective to objective as quickly as possible. On one end of the scale, you’ll find subjective measurements involving opinion and qualitative feedback. The other end has objective measurements involving objective input and quantitative feedback. Companies can move up the scale one step at a time.  Here’s an example involving customer satisfaction:    ● Subjective and qualitative: Involves organization’s opinion and feelings ● I feel our customers are unhappy.   ● Subjective and quantitative: Involves organization’s opinion and quantitative measurement, such as a numeric scale ● On a scale of 1 to 10, our customer satisfaction level is probably around one.    ● Objective and qualitative: Introduces objectivity by gathering input directly from the customer, but still involves customer’s opinion ● I had a hideous experience with the company.    ● Objective and quantitative: Objective input from customers put into quantitative measurement, such as NPS.   

---

## Page 151

 
151 
● On a scale of 1 to 10, how likely would you be to recommend this company to a colleague?  Disarm the weapons. The move from subjective to objective measurements makes it less likely metrics will be used as a weapon. When feelings and opinion are the basis for measurements, it’s easy for companies to start thinking measurements and key performance indicators (KPIs) are a bad thing.   Once that sentiment sets in, negative feelings come with it. Organizations start to question why they’re measuring things in the first place if all it does is spur negativity and bad results. So, they stop measuring altogether.   That’s the end of metrics—and measuring progress. Now the company has no way of knowing if it’s improving.   Automate as much as possible. Another way to stop companies from dropping metrics is to automate as much of the process as humanly possible. This helps to ensure the process is fast, easy, and error-free.      Michael adds, “You don’t want people sitting around with spreadsheets trying to manually track and calculate numbers. Manually tracking metrics is laborious and prone to errors. Some will stop doing it because it’s so labor-intensive. Others will stop doing it because they’ll start finding mistakes—the “garbage in, garbage out” concept. Tracking measurements manually is the first step towards stopping to track at all.”      Consider dashboards for good visualization. Once you have the metrics, you need to be able to communicate what they’re saying. Dashboards work better than raw numbers for conveying meaning, as do graphs, charts, and other visual elements.    Trends mean more than absolute values. Don’t get hung up on specific numbers with metrics. Look at the overall trends instead. It doesn’t really matter if the customer satisfaction level is a three or four if it’s moving up from the baseline.    Establish baselines. Set up baselines during your initial sessions. Your goal is to measure the impact of Agile engagement, and to ascribe the gain to it. To show the impact of Agile at, say, day 100, you need to know where the organization started.    Test measurements with hypotheses. Is the metric driving the behavior you expect? While you may think the metric is going to cause a certain reaction, you want to make sure that is the case. Check in on it often. Measurements are only useful if they catalyze change. If you’re not getting the change you expect, then you should change the metric.    

---

## Page 152

 
152 
Gain alignment at the highest level on what the metrics should be. Whatever organization you’re working with, make sure the group of people at the highest echelon is on board with the metrics you’re using.      Michael shares, “It’s not uncommon for those at the VP level to agree on a set of metrics—only to have the SVP say they’re not happy with the way Agile is going. When you show them the metrics that indicate progress, the SVPs will say they don’t agree with metrics. Avoid this scenario by making sure you get the SVPs to align with the specific metrics you intend to use right from the start.”  Measurements at the Team Level  The key to effective metrics at the team level is to understand their purpose. Team measurements are created by the team so the team can better understand itself. Metrics here are a self-awareness and self-improvement tool.  Examples of team metrics are things like lead time and delivery rate. If the team has no way to measure its performance, it has no way of knowing if it’s improving, declining, or staying the same. It also has no way to communicate its effectiveness and value to stakeholders.   Measurements at the Organizational Level  Metrics at the organizational level extend across the entire value stream of the company. These measurements touch everything that touches the customer. Measurements associated with the final product are a good example, as the product may be built by multiple departments or multiple teams.   Product-related metrics can include:  ● Test coverage ● Escaped defects ● How many phone calls the organization is getting about the product  Employee morale is another measurement used by many organizations. It can be measured by looking at things like turnover rate and how often people are calling in sick. The cost of training new people is another useful metric, one that can be particularly helpful for prompting companies to take action to boost morale.  Metrics related to employee morale are leading metrics. They give an advanced indication of how things are going before the business outcome comes to fruition.  Measurements at the Enterprise Level  On the flipside of leading metrics are lagging metrics, which is what you get at the enterprise level. Lagging metrics take a long time to show up, providing insights after 

---

## Page 153

 
153 
the fact. Depending on what’s being measured, companies may need to wait a year or more for certain metrics.   Examples of enterprise-level metrics include:   ● Return on investment, or ROI ● Market share ● Customer satisfaction levels ● Stock price   Enterprise-level metrics are difficult to associate with a single product, although you can glean valuable insights from looking at trends. It’s also imperative to look at performance relative to the industry.   For instance, let’s say company stock is up by 10%. If the overall stock market is up by 20%, the company’s 10% gain is not necessarily something to celebrate.  But if you’re down 10% while the rest of the industry is down by 20%, your 10% loss can be viewed as a win.   Summing It Up  When selected carefully and used meticulously, metrics are a mighty force—and not just for showing the value of Agile coaching. They are the only way to truly measure progress toward any goal, whether it’s increasing revenue or decreasing employee turnover.   They also serve as checkpoints to ensure a company is heading in the right direction, allowing you to adjust and change course if it’s not. Establishing a set of agreed-upon metrics from the start will go a long way toward bringing everyone involved to the best possible finish.  SOURCES: https://www.romanpichler.com/blog/balanced-product-scorecard-template/ https://www.klipfolio.com/resources/articles/what-are-business-metrics https://learn.g2.com/sales-velocity
