## Page 154

 
154 
Chapter 20: Promoting a Learning Organization  Learning Organizations  What’s a good way – if not the only way – for companies to stay competitive in the business environment? While dozens of different strategies and tactics may immediately come to mind, one way is to become a learning organization.   A learning organization is a company that embraces and promotes the concept of continuous learning for all its members. This provides a strong foundation for creating new products, launching new services, solving problems, and updating processes that are no longer serving the whole. It also gives organizations the ability to constantly transform themselves, so they stay current, relevant, and successful.  When we stop learning, we stop growing. Companies that aren’t constantly learning end up repeating the same old processes again and again – no matter what else is going on in the business or worldwide environment. Any changes they do try to implement are typically only surface changes that are short-lived. This is the opposite of agility: instead of adapting to change, these companies are resisting change.  On the flipside, companies that are continuously learning can continuously grow – which lets them continuously innovate, adapt, and ultimately thrive. This chapter gives you the lowdown on learning organizations. You’ll get a look at its history, what’s required to create a culture of learning, and several examples of methods that are helpful for facilitating learning through an organization.   History of the Concept  The learning organization concept was coined by Peter Senge, and he keeps the definition to the point. He says that a learning organization is “a group of people working together collectively to enhance their capacities to create results they really care about.”  The concept started hitting the mainstream in the early 1990s, with the publication of Senge’s book21 “The Fifth Discipline.” Here he outlines the five characteristics, or disciplines, of a learning organization. They are:  ● Shared Vision, with all employees on the same page with where an organization needs to go. ● Mental Models, which are generalizations and presumptions that influence people’s actions. For instance, the hierarchical structure is a typical mental model in most companies. Changing mental models starts by examining your own beliefs and the behavior that results from them.  21  https://www.amazon.com/Fifth-Discipline-Practice-Learning-Organization/dp/0385517254  

---

## Page 155

 
155 
● Personal Mastery, which refers to the strength and endurance required to be proactive, to keep learning to keep achieving desired results. ● System Thinking, the fifth discipline, is the ability to examine patterns within an organization by looking at the organization as a whole – not as small, unrelated, and independently managed parts.   Transformation Challenges  Organizations don’t automatically become learning organizations. In fact, the opposite is generally true. As companies expand, they tend to lose their capacity to learn. Structures and thinking become rigid. Things become stagnant and stale.   Transforming a stagnant organization into a learning organization can be just the remedy the company needs, but it is not without its challenges. Some of these challenges include:  ● No time: Other issues may keep taking priority over a wholesale culture change.  ● No guidance: If the organization doesn’t have efficient and effective help or training to initiate the process, it can be impossible for employees to commit to a time-consuming, vague process. Companies need to know the exact steps they need to take to solve their problems. This is exactly where a skilled Agile enterprise coach can step in. ● No relevance to company’s needs: Organizations may be facing daily issues that may appear as if they can’t be helped by a transformation. The solution here is to build a transformation strategy that remains linked to daily issues and business results, making the relevancy and association between the two clear. ● No focus on personal development: Some companies put the entire focus on professional development rather than personal development. The former is more likely to directly contribute to company performance, while the latter may not seem to have as immediate or visible results.  ● No shared vision: One challenge the leader may face is not being able to recognize that their personal vision may not be the same as the shared vision held by the organization.   Problems Addressed  Learning organizations were designed to address some of the biggest problems that halt company growth and overall success. Three of the most crucial are fragmentation, competition, and reactiveness.  Fragmentation: Fragmentation occurs when organizations break problems into pieces. This is a very common problem, evidenced by all the different departments typically found within a single company: accounting, IT, marketing, human resources, and others.  Competition: Outside competition is a given, but sometimes you’ll find an even fiercer version inside the company. Employees spend great amounts of energy and time trying to outdo their colleagues, rather than pooling their strengths through collaboration.  

---

## Page 156

 
156 
 Reactiveness: Reactiveness happens when companies only make changes as a reaction to outside forces, acting in defensiveness when cornered to do so. Learning organizations proactively initiate change.    Michael: It’s crucial to understand that a learning organization is always moving, but it’s moving in a way that perpetually increases their knowledge.  It’s not the same thing as constant firefighting. When you see firefighters in action, it looks like they’re doing a lot of stuff – but they’re not necessarily learning. They are repeating activities in which they’ve been trained and already know.   Learning is not the same as activity. It can encompass activity, provided the activity is expanding their knowledge or teaching them something new.   Creating a Culture of Learning  Learning organizations are those that value learning, and they make that value a part of their very culture. And making learning an active value in their culture goes far beyond mentioning it in a value statement written by management and pasted on their website.  These organizations will live, breathe, and perpetuate learning at every turn. You’ll see learning highlighted and practiced both implicitly and explicitly throughout the organization. Learning organizations spend massive amounts of time, both individually and in groups, increasing their knowledge and sharing ideas.  Not only will they spend time learning, but they will spend money on it. They have training budgets, putting money in the direction of ongoing learning for everyone within the organization.   You’ll also find leaders who don’t just talk the learning talk – they walk the learning walk. Leaders who fully embrace and exemplify the learning concept, serving as role models for the rest of the employees.   Michael: In learning organizations, leadership will constantly be sharing what they’re learning. They’ll pass along new information as they obtain it. They’ll say things like, “I’ve been reading this book,” or “I saw this Netflix documentary that I learned from.” They will explicitly say they value learning. They will freely pass along information, making it clear that learning is not something they simply talk about, but something they do on an ongoing basis.    A prime example of a learning organization is Miro, an online collaborative whiteboard platform designed for remote and distributed teams. Learning through collaboration is one of the most 

---

## Page 157

 
157 
effective ways to learn, and collaboration is a central theme in both the Miro company culture as well as its product.  One of the goals of its platform is “creating a space for shared understanding,” or a shared vision that’s an integral component of a learning organization. Not only does the company strive to help change the way the world collaborates to learn, but it implements many collaborative learning practices within its own organization.   Team members are consistently learning and evolving, with the company always striving to create a better version of itself. That value is one of the hallmarks of a learning organization.   Another key element in a learning organization is embracing failure. When you’re learning new things that have not yet been mastered or even tried, failure is bound to be part of the landscape. Rather than treating failure as a setback or something for which people need to be punished, it needs to be viewed as something from which people can learn.  If employees are fearful of the repercussions of failure, they will go out of their way to avoid it. They’ll become unwilling to share new ideas or try new approaches. They will, in effect, be shutting down the idea of learning before it can even begin.   Examples  There are many ways to begin incorporating a culture of learning into an organization, and we’re sharing several examples below. The examples start with learning on a one-to-one basis and expand to get more people and even the entire company involved.  As with all the examples we outline in this book, you’re not obligated to use any of them in your practice. They are merely offered up to illustrate tactics and strategies that have worked to help companies develop a culture of learning within their own organizations.   Just because a company is using any of these practices doesn’t mean they are automatically a learning organization. And just because they don’t doesn’t mean they aren’t. The examples are here for your reference and knowledge.   Learning How to Learn: Creativity Traffic Jam  This involves learning about what it takes for you to be creative. The idea is to identify when a creativity traffic jam is occurring, or an environment that is not creative. Non-creative environments may contain lots of criticism, along with a lack of time and space for creativity. Break through the creativity traffic jam, and you can accelerate into the learning zone.  Cherie: I’m not certain where the original concept generated for Rubber Duck Productivity because there are many articles floating around on the topic. However, this is a brilliant technique that a team I was coaching used to break the creativity traffic jam. Rubber Duck 

---

## Page 158

 
158 
Productivity was an experiment where people found that they were more productive when they kept a rubber duck on their desk.  When they encountered a problem, they were having trouble solving they would talk to the duck and tell the duck the problem. In the process of explaining the problem to the duck they would usually discover the solution. Thus, being more productive than just trying to solve problems in the reaches of their own mind.   15-Minute Rapid Feedback on New Work  Feedback can be crucial to learning, providing insights on whether new ideas are working. This can be done by providing employees with a company contact that can take 15 minutes to review and provide feedback on new work.   Cherie: I was able to introduce this technique to a company I was working with as a project manager, and it was very effective. I was managing a project (yep - before my agile experience and the company was not doing agile in any way) and the internal customer group that we were building a website for kept changing the requirements. That wasn’t the problem. The problem was that each time they wanted to change something, all work halted while they spent days revising wire frames and requirements documents. I was seeing how much time was being wasted and asked if we could try something different. I told them that I would take total blame if it didn’t work or if we got reprimanded. I suggested that we just stop all the documentation. I invited them to come sit down next to the development team once a day for a few minutes and look at what they were producing, make change suggestions, and let them implement changes while they were sitting there. I told them once the website was built, we could generate a matching wireframe and requirements documents to satisfy the PMO requirements. It worked beautifully. After that, I ran all my projects this way and the funniest part of the whole experience was that the PMO always praised me as being the best project manager in the company because my documentation was always spot on.   Pairing  Just as it sounds, pairing, which is an eXtreme Programming concept, refers to two people working on the same thing at the same time. It can be applied to coding, design – anything. In the case of coding, one person could talk while the other codes, and the two could switch roles as needed or desired.  Pairing was key to catapulting Google into a household name (and changing the course of the internet). It all started when computer scientists Jeff Dean and Sanjay Ghemawat22 sat down to code together at the same computer.  “I don’t know why more people don’t do it,” Ghemawat said of programming with a partner.  22  https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2018/12/10/the-friendship-that-made-google-huge  

---

## Page 159

 
159 
 Dean offered a tip for making the pairing a monumental success. “You need to find someone that you’re going to pair-program with who’s compatible with your way of thinking, so that the two of you together are a complementary force.”  Developmental Relationships  Development relationships refer to relationships in which two people get together to pair, pushing each other to reach higher and higher heights. Michael de la Maza developed this concept, based upon the concepts in the book Developmental Organizations.23 Developmental Relationships are a highly effective way for two people to work together to support each other’s personal growth.   Characteristics of a developmental relationship include: ● Warmth: The people in a developmental relationship genuinely enjoy spending time with each other. ● Transparency: The ability to communicate and collaborate with genuine candor.  ● Witnessing: The ability to connect without judgement. ● Discomfort: Because both people are constantly focused on edges. ● Rapid personal development: Because the developmental partner allows you to see edges that you do not see.  Some keys for building and maintaining a developmental relationship include:  ● Being yourself: Try introspection, meditation, and other self-reflection tools.  ● Nurturing each other: Care for and encourage the growth and development of each other. ● Lifting each other up: Try listening, awareness, wisdom. ● Crushing it: What’s possible now?  Additional information on developmental relationships can be found in this video24, which goes into more details on how they can result in greater and greater levels of success.  Developing Peer Mentorships  Mentoring has many different structures and can look many ways. Often when people think of having a mentor, they think of a professional arrangement with someone in their chosen field. This arrangement might be a paid contracted relationship, it might be in the confines of a program in the workplace, or some other formal arrangement.  
 23  https://www.amazon.com/An-Everyone-Culture-audiobook/dp/B01N4RFATK 24  https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0AYK6Ch1aIE&t=2s 

---

## Page 160

 
160 
Cherie, for example, has lots of formal mentoring relationships with Agile coaches. She often does mentor groups and accepts a few formal mentoring contracts each year with high potential coaches who are invested in doing whatever it takes to become great Agile coaches. These are formal relationships where the people Cherie mentors are committed to making an investment in their own careers. They make a time and financial commitment and take on the responsibility for their own development. Cherie partners with them for a year to give them guidance, feedback, and helps them to develop their confidence, competence, and skills as Agile coaches.   People can get certified as professional mentors in a domain in which they have expertise, whether it’s software, design, leadership, or another area. Cherie and Alex, for example, are both certified as Mentor Coaches for people who are aspiring to become credentialed with ICF as ACC, PCC, or MCC coaches. They often tag-team and both work with a mentee giving them insightful feedback on their coaching competency and helping them to develop high levels of competence.   A mentoring relationship can also be informal. Agile coaches often mentor scrum masters, product owners, managers while on coaching engagements. Sometimes managers and leaders are mentors in the development of those they lead. You might even have a relative who you view as a mentor or a public figure who you follow and even if they don’t realize it, they are a mentor to you. A great example of someone who has thousands of mentees she has never met is Lyssa Adkins. People watch her videos and read her books and she inspires them. She serves as a role model for many scrum masters and agile coaches she has never met.  One of your greatest superpowers for creating a sustainable environment of growth and change in an organization is to work with them to develop mentoring as a part of the structure and fiber of the way the company continuously learns and improves.  The only qualifications needed to be a mentor to another person is that you are a bit farther down the road than they are and that you are willing to share what you know with them to become successful in that area. People who are lower in the ranks of a company can certainly mentor higher ranking employees if they have more expertise in the area the higher-ranking employee wants to learn about. It’s not about hierarchy. It’s about learning and sharing knowledge. Mentoring helps both people grow and can be done through peer relationships.   Cherie: Almost every person who has been a mentor to me over the years has been a peer whom I worked with. They taught me and I taught them. I was willing to share my knowledge and professional opinions with them and they were willing to do the same. We both learned. We both challenged each other’s thoughts and ideas. And we both grew from the experience. Some of the peers who have mentored me have been much younger with less experience in the professional world. One example is Allison Pollard25 who partnered with me to develop ideas for the work we did in enterprise coaching, developed conference talks, and learned to work the conference circuit, and went on the journey of professional coaching together. I was humble  25  https://www.linkedin.com/in/allisonpollard/  

---

## Page 161

 
161 
enough to learn from her and she was humble enough to learn from me. We grew together, as peers. An example of a time when I had a complementary mentoring relationship with someone above me in the organization was when I worked with Ross Hughes.26 He was my boss at a full-time employee engagement, and he learned as much from me as I learned from him. Rather than worrying about appearances and having to “be the boss,” Ross was open to listening to ideas from my experience and specific training that he didn’t have. He was also willing to share his experience with me. It was a great growth experience for both of us and I believe we both became more confident in our ability to make an impact.  Cherie has partnered with clients to help them develop mentoring programs that fit into their culture. As an example, some programs pull experienced scrum masters who were developing as agile coaches from one division to work developing scrum masters in a different division. A program like this enables both scrum masters to grow and develop the talent they want to cultivate and creates a growth path that is not dependent on the Agile coach. It also enables you as an agile coach to train coaches that are employees of the company to coach other teams and scrum masters so the company will be equipped with the coaching competency they need when your engagement with them is complete.   Creating a Feedback Culture  When managers provide feedback, it’s often during a performance review. It’s also often tied to whether an employee will get a promotion or bonus. Decoupling the feedback from the performance review allows feedback to be given in a way that’s not tied to your overall job status or security. It removes the worry, fear and judgement that are attached to traditional feedback. This provides an environment that’s conducive to learning.  As an Agile coach working with organizations, you are in a great position to influence the way feedback is given and received. Many of the traditional ways of getting and receiving feedback create a culture filled with defensiveness and fear rather than a culture that values growth and vulnerability.   Cherie: Bob Small,27 whom I worked with in 2016-2017, had a wonderful outlook on feedback. He views feedback as a gift. He is one of the few people I’ve known that often came to me after an interaction and asked, “Do you have any feedback you can share about my interactions during that session?” It was refreshing to see someone who wasn’t afraid of the “F word” even though it is often used as a weapon by people who want to share unsolicited opinions. Bob was a model for how feedback can be embraced and following his lead, others in the organization began to be more comfortable asking for feedback. It also gave Bob lots of credibility as a coach because he proved that he was listening to his clients and colleagues and constantly looking to 
 26 https://www.linkedin.com/in/rossphughes/  27  https://www.linkedin.com/in/bob-small/  

---

## Page 162

 
162 
improve. His vulnerability sent a message that he didn’t believe himself superior to anyone and it gave others the freedom to exercise their own vulnerability and humility to grow.  One of the traditional and damaging ways of giving and receiving feedback is through anonymous 360 feedback programs or anonymous surveys. When an anonymous survey is collected at the organizational level and not targeted at individuals it can be a great way to gauge trends. To gauge trends, the feedback has to be collected on a consistent cadence and in the same manner each time. A baseline survey can tell the organization where the starting point is. Each time the survey is repeated, sometimes quarterly or semi-annually, there is another data point on a trend line. After 4-5 collections of data trends become a bit clearer. However, if the organizational leaders are not using the data collected to make changes, the data collection is worthless. Also, if the data is being used as a whipping stick or performance management tool or sliced too thinly to be able to target small groups of people or individual managers it also becomes useless.  Cherie shares, “My opinion is that anonymous 360 feedback creates more harm than good. I hate it and always recommend against it for individuals. Organizations and individuals often use anonymity to create psychological safety to get honest feedback. The first problem with this is that they are treating the symptoms of the real problem. Instead of going around the problem, address the reality that the organization does not have enough psychological safety for people to believe they can give feedback honestly. And if they do, by requesting anonymous feedback you are suggesting that they shouldn’t.  I have never experienced receiving anonymous feedback that was helpful. Either the feedback was fluffy and told me what I already knew, or it told me things I didn’t know and left me frustrated because I didn’t know what to do with it. I had no way to ask questions to understand more so I could understand the changes that would be helpful to make. I just had to guess what the feedback meant and that wasn’t helpful at all. In fact, it created defensiveness because whoever gave the feedback didn’t tell me directly that I was doing something wrong. If you’re honest, you’ve likely had this same reaction. It’s also likely that after receiving this kind of feedback you spent at least a few days trying to figure out who said it. This creates a barrier to psychological safety. In my opinion this is using feedback as a weapon instead of using it as a gift.  I make it my standard practice to refuse anonymous feedback. It isn’t helpful and I can’t do anything with it so it’s not worth being stressed over it. I don’t give feedback to anyone on any survey that I have not already said to them directly. And, if I fill in the form, I make it clear that the feedback is coming from me and if they want to talk about it further, they are invited to speak to me. I believe that it is unfair to expect people to contribute 360 feedback to a peer’s performance review. It’s an enormous waste of time to have twenty 360 requests to fill in. No one in their right mind sends a 360 request for a performance review to people whom they know will rate them poorly. Most of the time the feedback is not valuable because no one wants to say something that might hurt a peers performance review. There’s definitely better ways to get feedback.” 

---

## Page 163

 
163 
 Part of the goal of coaching is to help the client organization develop a culture of collaboration and learning. What better way to do this than to help them learn to give and receive feedback as a standard way of working. Anonymous feedback doesn’t create psychological safety, but openness and respect do. When working with teams of people, establish working agreements that include how they will give and receive feedback. Teach them techniques that are effective for giving and receiving feedback. Here are a few ideas to get you started:  ● Impact Feedback using SBI28 method - This method can be very effective as a conversation starter for feedback. The key to making it effective is to have them practice the method utilizing positive feedback. People tend to think feedback is only for constructive criticism. It’s not. When you practice giving feedback on things you see people doing right it makes it much easier to use that same pattern to give constructive feedback. ● Feedback Circles: This technique developed by Cherie can be used during retrospectives, Community of Practice sessions, or other meetings where people are there to learn and grow. Have people get in groups and give feedback to one another. Each person gives an observation of something good they have experienced with the other person since the last time they met. Then, they give the person an observation of something they can do more effectively with an idea of how it might be improved. The point of the circles isn’t to focus on criticism, but to focus on being more comfortable giving and receiving feedback as a gift. Each person leaves the circle with one thing they were “caught” doing well since the last circle and one thing they can choose to work on until the next circle. These circles can be effective for creating a collaborative learning environment that is open to giving and receiving feedback as a gift rather than as discipline. Doing feedback circles at least once a month gives people time in between to work on improvements. They also have the opportunity in the next feedback circle to ask for feedback on improvements they are focusing on since the last circle.  Performance management in an organization can be a tough battle. Most organizations have performance management and merit rewards that are focused on 1-1 performance even though people are working in teams. This can reduce the effectiveness of the team because everyone needs to protect their own performance rating. When you see this happening in an organization you can work with the leaders to develop different ways to measure performance. There will likely always be some portion of performance that ties back to an individual. However, if the team has shared goals that the whole team is rated on, they have a common reason to perform at their very best rather than either being a superhero or a saboteur.   Cherie worked with one organizational leader in 2014, Dan Afseth,29 who ran multiple experiments in his organization that put performance management into the hands of the agile teams. He sent them on a mission to figure out the best way to do performance management in  28  https://www.the10minuteleader.com/how-to-give-good-feedback/  29  https://www.linkedin.com/in/danafseth/  

---

## Page 164

 
164 
the organization as self-organized teams. There were only two stipulations, first, they had to have a method in place for all team members to get feedback from the rest of the team a minimum of once a month. Second, they had to have team focused goals that all were going to be accountable for delivering on and those goals had to be in support of the organizational goals. He ran the experiment for an initial period of 3 months at the end of which they would come together and describe the processes they developed and what worked or what didn’t work. Then, they would decide if they should continue the experiment for another 3 months before deciding how they would proceed forward. [note: cherie has a bit more to add here after speaking with Dan]   Bite-Sized Learning  Bite-sized learning is gaining widespread popularity as a learning tool. It involves serving up information in bite-sized chunks, with one to three-minute lessons people can access at their convenience. Employees no longer need to sit in a class for hours on end. They can instead learn smaller bits of information on demand. Many learning organizations make these mini-lessons available inside the company, with kiosks, apps or other tools employees can use to get their lesson of the moment. By making these lessons available on the company intranet you can help the company build sustainability and also scale yourself as an agile coach because people can be redirected to learn from you in a more permanent place, they will have access to after you leave.    Internal University  Another element you can leverage in helping clients develop into learning organizations is an internal university. This consists of a university inside a company that develops training for employees. You may find human resource training, onboarding training, software how-to training, or training on how to do sales. The overall goal is to make employees in the company smarter about doing their jobs.   Cherie has worked with a few of her clients to create an Agile University inside their company designed to develop an entire learning path for those who want to embrace and incorporate the Agile principles into their work. It provides a growth path for agilists to further their careers and provides the organization with a stream of agile coaches they can select from internally when positions open up. The more learning and growth the client can complete on their own, the more sustainable the changes they make in agility will become.  Internal Open Space30  Another way to promote learning is with internal open space events. Here attendees collaborate on creating the agenda for the event, and then select the topics they want to attend. Open  30  https://openspaceworld.org/wp2/what-is/  

---

## Page 165

 
165 
space can be a great learning experience for internal organizations to discuss improvements, impediments, or any other focus topic. Open space events can be as short as a couple of hours or full day events. They are great ways to get people from different parts of the organization to share ideas and experiences.  Internal Conferences  Internal conferences involve a standard agenda and a more traditional take on a learning event. But that doesn’t make them any less useful. Many companies hold them on an annual basis. This can be a great way to develop the next set of agile leaders who will be taking the reins of the agile adoption work forward after the coach has disengaged. Internal conferences set the stage for multiple people to be seen as subject matter experts who can be called upon when needed. It also enables teams to share experiences with others who can learn from them.  Cherie: One client I worked with had a group of scrum masters who set up semi-annual internal conferences that had tracks for individuals to present learning topics, a track with teams sharing experiments they did that worked and didn’t work for others to learn. There was also a track for technical talks so teams could share new techniques they adopted and taught others how to use them. One the opposite semi-annual cycle, the company conducted Hackathon events. So, each quarter there was an investment into innovation and growth.  Hackathon  A hackathon is a learning event where employees set aside a day or two to focus on innovation. They can develop anything they want; it doesn’t have to be tied to the company goals or previously discussed ideas. The event can be held for a day or over the course of several days or week, providing an environment open to massive exploration. Usually, at the end of the hackathon there is some sort of show and tell where people share their prototypes, improvements, and experiments with others so the whole organization gets to benefit from that learning. In some companies the product leaders have a “shark tank” where people can pitch their ideas and show their prototypes to the product leaders. If their ideas are adopted, they get funding to work in an incubator lab for a year with a full team developing the product and testing it in the market. If the incubator produces good results, the product is picked up by the company.   Internal Knowledge Creation: Proprietary Assets    Companies can expand their knowledge through the creation of proprietary assets. These assets are generated and owned exclusively by the company, and they’re free to share them with others – or not.   Proprietary assets can give you an edge over the competition as well as a way of looking at and understanding things through the specific mental models created by the company. 

---

## Page 166

 
166 
Organizations can share knowledge through blogs, videos and experience reports accessible through the companywide intranet.   Community of Practice, Mastermind Groups A community of practice refers to a group of people that share a common activity or interest who regularly get together to learn how to improve that activity or deepen their knowledge of that interest. One key to a great CoP is that it is intended to be a place where you can learn new skills and practice them in a safe environment. A great way to build sustainable growth into the company you are working with is to develop communities of practice. Depending on the size of the company and the interest you might build one agile CoP or several that are sorted by role type. Getting a community of practice started is easy - developing it to remain stable when you are no longer involved takes a bit longer. Some common steps for developing sustainability into your CoP are: ● Get it started! At first you may need to gather people, build a backlog of topics they want to learn more about. Depending on the organization you may have to lead several sessions to get it started. This might mean that you are the one in the spotlight for 4-6 months if they have sessions once a month. You might even have to organize the sessions at first. However, it is recommended that you have a person who is an FTE take ownership of that.  ● The next step is to work with people to help them develop sessions. You might need to partner with them as they deliver their first session then set them off on their own. Sometimes you will need to partner with someone to learn the skills or gain the knowledge in an area of interest. Then partner with them to put together a session to present. After several months of working with several individuals to start presenting you can ask those people to partner with new people who are willing to learn and share their learnings with the group. They don’t have to be experts. They just must be willing to open a conversation and talk about what they know and listen to others share their own ideas so the group can learn together. ● If you have not already done so, the next step is to hand over the organization of the CoP to the community. Solicit a team of volunteers who will run the CoP and make sure it’s scheduled and there are people to present every month. ● Once they take over the presenting and the organization, you can back out gracefully and watch the CoP run without your involvement. Check in every once in a while, just to see how they’re doing and ensure that it is self-sufficient, and you’ll be pleased to see sustainability in action.  Mastermind groups are similar, with like-minded individuals meeting on a regular basis to brainstorm, share knowledge and support each on the road to success.   Each person in the group might share about a particular topic for a few minutes, providing useful information they’ve learned or used in their own practice. When people learn what others are doing to achieve success, they tend to learn faster. There is generally an opportunity for someone to sit in the “hot seat” for a few minutes and share a challenge they are having and get coaching or mentoring from the rest of the group. That individual gets more information about 

---

## Page 167

 
167 
how to handle their challenge and others learn ways to deal with similar challenges they may encounter.   Community of practice and mastermind groups can be used at different levels throughout the organization, each focused on targeted areas of common interest.   Cherie: I’ve noticed that managers tend to like the concept of a mastermind group more than CoP group. If they are learning I’m happy to help them get started in any format they wish. I’ve also noticed that some companies prefer to run the CoP as Lean Coffee events so everyone brings topics, and the group gets to touch on multiple topics rather than have a single topic for an hour.  Summing It Up  A learning organization has the capacity to consistently gain and incorporate new knowledge. Yet the transformation into becoming one is not going to happen automatically. It takes a lot of hard work, the ability to overcome obstacles and a willingness to change. All that hard work, however, can pay off by producing a company that continuously evolves into the very best version of itself.
