Chapter 21: Promoting Psychological Safety  Psychological Safety It happens in every profession in every industry. An employee may notice something is about to go wrong with something in the organization – but doesn’t speak up or do anything to stop it. Why not?   There could be a whole host of reasons. The person may be too scared to speak up. Maybe every thought or idea they’ve brought up before got shot down. Perhaps their boss had chided them in the past for “talking about things they know nothing about.” The person could fear feeling like an idiot – or being called one – if they say something and they’re wrong. Or maybe the employee is so fed up and full of contempt for the company that they are secretly elated that something horrible is about to happen to bring it down. All those scenarios, and many others, can stem from the lack of a crucial element in the workplace: psychological safety. This chapter provides a thorough rundown of what psychological safety is and its extreme importance in the team environment. In this chapter, we’ll also give a few techniques you can use to build psychological safety with clients in your enterprise coaching practice and describe some communication patterns well-known for destroying it. What It Is The concept of “team psychological safety” was first introduced by Harvard organization behavioral scientist Amy Edmondson31. She defines it as: “A shared belief held by members of a team that the team is safe for interpersonal risk-taking.” To elaborate on this, psychological safety is the belief that the environment is safe for taking an interpersonal risk without being seen as negative, disruptive, incompetent, or ignorant. Team members feel they can ask questions, offer suggestions, admit mistakes, and otherwise be vulnerable in front of each other without being disparaged, embarrassed, or punished. Michael says: Let’s say you have a team project, yet one team member is unclear on the project’s goal or his exact role in the undertaking. If he’s on a team with high levels of psychological safety, he’ll feel safe enough to ask those questions without fear of being made to feel foolish. 
 31  https://www.hbs.edu/faculty/Pages/profile.aspx?facId=6451&view=publications 

---

## Page 169

 
169 
If he’s on a team that lacks psychological safety, he may decide to remain silent, not asking any questions. He might feel it’s easier to simply play along and hope for the best without a firm understanding just so he won’t be looked at as being ignorant. Team members may be more worried about being perceived as ignorant than they are about messing up the work because they do not fully understand what’s going on. Michael’s example illustrates how lack of psychological safety can create huge problems in the output of the team. When Google researchers32 set out to discover what makes a team effective at Google, they found the most important factor was not who was on the team but how a team worked together. The research, entitled “Project Aristotle,” looked at how team composition and team dynamics impacted the effectiveness of the 180 different teams targeted for study. Researchers conducted hundreds of interviews with leaders, reviewed existing survey data from the annual Google employee engagement survey, and ran more than 35 statistical models on hundreds of variables to discover what made exceptional teams click. They boiled it all down to discover the five key dynamics of effective teams. Psychological safety was the foundational element. It precedes dependability, team structure and clarity, meaningful work, and impactful work. The research also found that employees on teams with higher psychological safety levels: ● Were less likely to leave the company ● Were more likely to be open to diverse ideas from other team members ● Brought in more revenue ● Were rated as effective by executives twice as often as employees on teams with a low-level of psychological safety What Psychological Safety Looks Like If you were to give team members a list of team attributes that comes with psychological safety, it would look something like this: ● Making a mistake is not held against you ● You’re able to bring up tough issues and problems ● No one is rejected for being or thinking differently ● Diverse opinions are embraced ● It’s okay and even encouraged to disagree ● Opinions are respected ● Questions are encouraged ● You feel safe to take a risk ● Failure is seen as helpful for learning  32  https://rework.withgoogle.com/print/guides/5721312655835136/ 

---

## Page 170

 
170 
●  It’s easy to ask other team members for help ● No other team member purposely behaves in a way that undermines your efforts ●  Your unique skills and talents are both used and valued working with other team members  How to Build Psychological Safety It would be great if you could just hand managers and team members the list of what psychological safety looks like and tell them to go for it. But it doesn’t work that way. Building this element with a team is possible through strategic techniques and concerted efforts. Here are several for consideration. 1. Three Techniques from Edmondson Edmondson discusses three techniques for building psychological safety in her TEDx talk33: Frame the work as a learning problem, not an execution problem. Recognize that both uncertainty and interdependence exist. Say something like, “We’ve never been in this territory before, and we need to have everyone’s input.” Acknowledge your fallibility. This applies to all team members at all levels, from subordinates to peers and colleagues. Say something like, “I may miss something, and I need to hear from you to make sure I don’t.” This creates more safety for speaking. Model curiosity and ask lots of questions. When people with positional authority ask questions, team members are more likely to speak up and answer. Two Tips for Leaders   Leaders and managers can contribute to building psychological safety by using these two suggestions that came out of Google research. ● Solicit input and opinions from the group. Like Edmonton’s suggestion to model curiosity and ask lots of questions, directly asking team members for input opens the doors for people to speak up. ●  Share information about personal and work style preferences and encourage others to do the same. Disclosing information about yourself reveals a layer of vulnerability which is a key component of psychological safety. 2. The VAST Cycle 
 33  https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LhoLuui9gX8 

---

## Page 171

 
171 
Another way to build psychological safety within an organization is to show up in a way that invites people to connect, trust, and feel safe. A tool that can help you increase this type of engagement is the VAST cycle34. Developed by Michael Sahota and Olaf Lewitz, the VAST cycle is an acronym that stands for: ● Vulnerability ● Authentic Connection ● Safety ● Trust It’s an incredible way to build awareness and choice for organizations, particularly when it’s contrasted with a fearful and mistrusting way of being. Keeping the cycle top of mind can help guide behavior as well as create ideas for experiments. The VAST cycle can be used for organizations and individuals in both professional and personal contexts. One way to use VAST is as a tool for introspection. Team members can be encouraged to ask themselves: ● How trusting am I? ● How safe do I feel? ● How connected do I feel? ● How vulnerable am I choosing to be? ● How authentically am I acting? Once individuals answer those questions for themselves, they can use the VAST model to adapt their behavior accordingly. For example: ● I can choose to trust the other person and notice how my behavior changes. ● I can choose to be vulnerable and share how I feel. How I am feeling unsafe. How I am not trusting. ●  I can state what I want. “I want to restart this conversation. I want to focus on how we can support each other. I want to focus on the goal.” ● I can ask for help. Michael: In my experience, vulnerability is a powerful way to create psychological safety. Owning our experience. Owning how we feel. And then sharing it with others. It kicks off the entire VAST cycle, opening the door for authentic connections with safety and trust to follow. Cherie: In my experience, I have noticed that vulnerability gives other people hope and makes them feel like they can accomplish anything. Many leaders believe that to be seen as a leader they need to project an image that says, “I’m perfect. I never make mistakes. I know everything.” But this image doesn’t help other people. That belief only serves to make the leader feel more confident that other people see a superhero in them. But when people see how “perfect” we are  34  https://shift314.com/vast-connection-cycle/ 

---

## Page 172

 
172 
it more often causes tension and a feeling that they are not good enough. What makes people feel like you are someone they can look up to as a role model is when you can say, “Here are the mistakes I have made along the way and what I have learned from them.” or “I’m struggling with this thing. I don’t know all the answers and I could use some perspectives from you.” When people who work with us see our vulnerabilities, it makes us more human. When they see the things, we have accomplished and are made aware of our imperfections, they start to believe that they are also capable of accomplishing great things. Trust is one of the components in the VAST cycle; it’s also a key for psychological safety in any relationship. University of Houston research professor Brené Brown35 uses the acronym BRAVING to outline specific elements that need to exist for trust to be present. 3. BRAVING: The Seven Elements of Trust  Boundaries: You respect my boundaries, and when you’re not clear about what’s OK and not OK, you ask. You’re willing to say no. Reliability: You do what you say you’ll do. At work, this means staying aware of your competencies and limitations so you don’t over promise and can deliver on commitments and balance competing priorities. Accountability: You own your mistakes, apologize, and make amends. Vault: You don’t share information or experiences that are not yours to share. I need to know that my confidences are kept and that you’re not sharing with me any information about other people that should be confidential. Integrity: You choose courage over comfort. You choose what is right over what is fun, fast, or easy. And you choose to practice your values rather than simply professing them. Non-Judgment: I can ask for what I need, and you can ask for what you need. We can talk about how we feel without judgment. Generosity: You extend the most generous interpretation possible to the intentions, words, and actions of others. How Psychological Safety Gets Destroyed In a psychologically safe environment, team members communicate with each other openly, honestly, and with a level of vulnerability that encourages authentic connections, safety, and trust. But that environment can be destroyed rather quickly when unhealthy, unproductive communication patterns arise. 
 35  https://daretolead.brenebrown.com/ 

---

## Page 173

 
173 
The Gottman Institute, which brings a research-based approach to relationships, identifies four of these patterns, dubbing them the Four Horsemen36. Just as the Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse depicted the end of times, these Four Horsemen of communication styles can predict the end of relationships: ● Criticism or Blaming ● Contempt ● Defensiveness ● Stonewalling While the Gottman Institute focuses mainly on family relationships, these four elements can likewise spell doom and gloom for relationships within organizations, annihilating any sense of psychological safety. Cherie refers to these four elements as Toxic Communication Strategies. By introducing healthy communication strategies to counteract these toxic strategies we can help team members learn to both intervene in their tendency to turn to a Toxic Communication Strategy and de-escalate a conflict when someone else is utilizing a Toxic Communication Strategy.   It is helpful to remember that just because someone utilizes a Toxic Communication Strategy it doesn’t mean that they are a toxic person or even a bad person. Everyone utilizes these strategies at some point and we all generally have a favorite “go to” strategy we run to when we feel threatened, angry, or some other negative emotion. These are learned strategies that often start in childhood. For example, think of a small child who is trying to play with a toy that isn’t working. They get frustrated, bang the part on the toy, and cry. The parent comes and helps them. Then at age five, he wants a cookie. The parent says, “no,” and the child gets frustrated and throws a tantrum. The parent is busy and tired and just gives in and gives the child a cookie. Then, a few years later as a teen, the child wants to go somewhere with friends and the parent says, “No, we have other plans.” The child throws a tantrum, and the parent gives in. This behavior is repeated throughout childhood and into adulthood and the child has learned that by getting angry and throwing tantrums they can get what they want. In the workplace, when they don’t get what they want they throw similar tantrums and thus… Toxic Communication Strategies show up on the team.  Think about your natural tendency when you don’t get your way or when someone tells you something you don’t agree with. Do you get a bit defensive? Do you blame someone else for the problem or start domineering to bully them into submission? Do you shut down and hold your anger inside but give the silent treatment? Do you walk away and tell someone how horrible the other person is or roll your eyes and say, “whatever”? When we can identify our natural tendencies, we can be vulnerable and help our team members understand that these are normal human behaviors. However, they are hurtful behaviors that destroy psychological safety in our relationships.  36  https://www.gottman.com/blog/the-four-horsemen-recognizing-criticism-contempt-defensiveness-and-stonewalling/ 

---

## Page 174

 
174 
When educating your clients on these toxins, it is often helpful to have them identify the toxin that is their personal “go to” which normalizes the strategies. Then, explore together how the team as an intact system reacts to conflict. Which toxic strategies do they most often fall into? Do they tend to stonewall? Do they tend to blame someone outside the team for the challenges they are having? Once they identify some of the “go to” strategies of the team ask these questions:  ● What situations bring out these toxins in the team? ● How do we behave when we are utilizing these toxins? ● What is the belief behind the toxins? ● How are these toxins trying to be helpful? Once the team members understand how these Toxic Communication Strategies work and can identify behaviors associated with them it is helpful to introduce healthy communication strategies that can counteract the toxins. Criticism and Blame The person giving the criticism may believe on some level they are trying to be helpful by offering a suggestion, but their delivery method turns it into an attack. Cherie views both blame and criticism as offensive actions. Complaining is different from criticism because criticism attacks the person where complaints focus on a specific behavior. Variations of criticism and blame can include: ● Aggressive attack: An obvious verbal assault ● Bullying: Attempting to coerce through intimidation ● Harsh startup: Beginning a conversation with blame, criticism, or attack ● Chronic criticalness: Perpetually adding negative words about a character or personality ● Domination: Attempting to control or influence through intimidation ● Criticism: complaining about a person instead of addressing behavior directly Healthy strategies that can combat criticism and blame 1. It is helpful to understand that while it may be hurtful, criticism is an unskillful attempt at giving useful feedback. When we can remember that the person has some useful information they are trying to relay, we are often able to separate the delivery of the feedback from the feedback itself and receive it without turning to defensiveness to protect ourselves from the perceived attack. 2. It is also helpful to identify your contribution to the problem. We are often so focused on what others are doing (blaming) that we don’t take ownership of how we are partly responsible for what is happening.  ○ Example: I am frustrated because a team member is always late for our meetings ○ My contribution: I insisted that the meeting is first thing in the morning because that was convenient for me. I didn’t take into consideration that they have 

---

## Page 175

 
175 
children to get to school and a long commute that sometimes gets delayed by traffic. 3. When you feel frustrated by the actions of others, stop yourself from criticizing by thinking through your complaint. Focus on specific behavior; don’t attack the person's character ● No: You’re so irresponsible ● Yes: It hinders the team’s productivity when you are late 4. Turn the complaint into a request. Instead of complaining about the behavior, you do not want, ask for the behavior you want. ●  No: You’re always late ● Yes: Can you email us your updates when you are going to be late so we will know how to get started on the work? 5. When someone is complaining to you, you can turn the complaint into a request by asking a question like these: ● Can you tell us what you want to happen instead? ● What would you like to request us to do differently? 6. Rather than focusing on individuals and the actions of individuals or your frustration, focus on “What does the relationship need from us now?” ● No: Everyone else is always on time.  ● Yes: What can we do to promote everyone’s ability to be at meetings on time regularly? Defensiveness Defensiveness escalates conflict and is the most recognized Toxic Communication Strategy. Cherie views defensiveness as reactive blaming. It’s a defense mechanism to deflect blame from oneself to others. Rather than accepting or admitting a mistake, defensiveness turns the tables to imply, “It’s not me. It’s you.” Variations can include: ● Not being open to influence ● Refusal to take responsibility ● Deflection ● “Not my fault, not my job” mindset Healthy strategies that can combat defensiveness Blaming often occurs when we get triggered by the actions or words of others (often through one of the other Toxic Communication Strategies).  1. When you feel triggered by what someone else has said, rather than jumping into defensiveness, breathe. Repeat what you believe you heard them say and ask for clarification ● No: I did NOT press the wrong button to disconnect the call. ● No: So, you’re saying I hit a button that disconnected the call? Which button was that? 

---

## Page 176

 
176 
● Yes: It seems like you are saying I disconnected the call, possibly on purpose. Is this what you intended to say? 2. Similarly, when you sense that someone else is triggered and they are getting defensive in response to what you have said or done, ask them to tell you the message they believe you were sending; clarify ● No: What’s your problem? ● Yes: I’m sorry, it seems like what I said may not have come across as intended. What message do you believe I was trying to convey? (Clarify as needed) 3. Similarly, when you feel triggered, you can give the other person the benefit of doubt by taking the posture that you may have misunderstood their message or motive:  ● Yes: I’m not sure I understand what you’re saying. Can you clarify what you believe went wrong? 4. Another helpful strategy is to listen beyond the words. Instead of looking at the disagreement point, remember that there is an intention of giving some useful feedback. Even if the complaint is not being communicated skillfully, there is probably some truth in it that your defensive reaction is causing you to overlook. Search for the element of truth in what you are hearing: ● No: So, what if the phone system is new; I don’t need classes to figure it out. ● Yes: This is a new phone system.  It is possible that I hit the wrong button in error and hung up.  Stonewalling Stonewalling feeds other toxins. Stonewalling is cutting off communication. This can be done via silent treatments, refusal to engage, withdrawal, or reluctance to directly communicate. Stonewalling can be overt like refusing to speak to someone when they talk to you. Only answering with short words in a sarcastic tone, etc. And it can be covert such as not giving your ideas or not speaking up when you have an idea that could solve a problem. In essence, it’s disengaging as a self-protective measure. Sometimes people go into stonewalling when they are getting angry because they are using it as a shut off valve to control their anger. They figure better to shut down than to blow up. Both reactions can be equally toxic. Variations can include: ● Not being open to influence ● Avoidance ● Uncooperativeness ● Passivity or passive aggressive behaviors ● Disengagement ● Withholding information ● Silent Treatment   

---

## Page 177

 
177 
Healthy strategies that can combat stonewalling It’s often hard to recognize if someone else gets triggered and starts slipping into stonewalling behaviors quietly. But you can recognize when you are getting triggered and develop strategies to respond. First, it is important to understand the things that trigger you to shut down. Anger, fear, frustration, confusion, feeling threatened, being overwhelmed and other similar things may trigger you to start shutting down. When this happens, notice what happens in your mind and body. Do you feel tightness in your throat or chest? Does your mind start racing? Do you start shaking inside? Do you start to clench your teeth or fist? Perhaps you get a hopeless feeling? 1. By recognizing what it feels like to go into this triggered state of mind you can utilize this information to shift your natural tendencies to shut down. Develop a habit that counteracts the shutdown. Some people have found these helpful: ● Take a deep breath and release it slowly ● Take a drink of water or step out to get a drink of water. Walking releases the tension building up. Stepping away from the situation gives you time to compose your thoughts. Changing the perspective using moving to a different space can change your perspective ● Ask to take a break and resume the discussion at a specific time. (15 minutes, tomorrow at 3 pm) But don’t leave this open-ended - by not setting a specific timeframe on the pause you are likely to go deeper into stonewalling by hiding from the interaction. 2. Find a way to shift your perspective of what is happening  ●  No: They don’t like my ideas – fine! I won’t offer any ever again. ● Yes: This isn’t personal. We are all throwing thoughts into the pool so we can develop an idea together. All ideas are helpful. Once anyone releases an idea into the team space the team gets to own them and decide what to do with them. We are all just throwing thoughts in to see what other ideas we can provoke and build on. 3. Recognize that every voice is important and holds critical information for the team. ●  No: They only want to hear the best ideas that are guaranteed to work. ● Yes: We need to hear ALL the ideas. We can make the best decisions when we have input from everyone. Someone needs to shoot holes in all ideas to counteract the risk we don’t see when we think we have a great idea. 4. Design safety conditions that will allow everyone to speak up in meetings. Design working agreements with the team around how conflict is handled and what people need to feel safe enough to contribute.  ● No: Who wants to go first? ● Yes: Let’s go around the table, with each person sharing their ideas. If you don’t want to share, feel free to just say pass and we’ll respect that and keep going. If you change your mind later and want to jump in after you’ve already passed, please do so. 

---

## Page 178

 
178 
5. Evaluate if other toxic behaviors are impacting safe communication. What Toxic Communication Strategies are the team using regularly? Teach the team about these strategies and healthy ways of communicating that can be more productive. ● No: Asking the group “Why are you all so quiet? Don’t you have any brainpower?” ● Yes: Speaking with individual members privately, asking if there is anything you can do to help them feel more comfortable sharing their ideas. Contempt Contempt is fueled by long-simmering negative thoughts. It conveys disgust and condescension. It’s frequently the result of ongoing disdain, whether it’s disdain for an individual, a group, an institution, or the world in general. Variations can include: ● Sarcasm and hostile humor ● Personal attacks, including belittling and name-calling ● Cynicism ● Disrespectful gossip ● Demeaning tone ● Belligerence ● Eye-rolling     Healthy strategies that can combat contempt 1. Contempt in a team can be extremely damaging. One way to combat bringing contempt into a conversation is to use “I feel…. I want” statements to replace your contempt statements. ● No: Geesh, we went over this same material three days in a row. Do you have holes in your brain that you can’t retain the information? ● Yes: I feel frustrated when we review the same information multiple times. I want to feel stimulated and excited. Can we move to the next step? 2. Contempt behaviors are often surprising and aggressive. Team members can often be at a loss for how to respond or may respond with another toxic behavior that escalates the situation. One de-escalating strategy is to address the behavior directly without “You” statements: ● No: Your eye-rolling has got to stop. ● Yes: I feel threatened and demeaned when you roll your eyes during the discussion. Can you help me understand what is happening for you? 3. Another way to de-escalate contempt statements is to address them by asking the person exhibiting the behavior to clarify the intention. This can help the person with the 

---

## Page 179

 
179 
behavior understand that their behavior is not acceptable and can also clarify for others when insult or offensive behavior may be unintentional.  ● Yes: What are you trying to accomplish by doing that? ● Yes: Can you clarify your intention in saying that? 4. Another useful strategy that can be used in a one-on-one situation to address concerns about behavior is to utilize the SBI Feedback Model37 which describes the situation from your point of view, the feeling or impact it has on you, and what you would prefer to be different. ● No: I noticed you were bothered yesterday. Every time Sasha shared an idea you rolled your eyes and sighed. You were making everyone uncomfortable. What was going on? ● Yes: I noticed that in the meeting yesterday you rolled your eyes and sighed when Sasha shared his ideas. I was uncomfortable with these responses, and I am concerned about the impact it may have on the team’s ability to collaborate in safety. Can you give me your perspective on what was happening? It is often most helpful to introduce these Toxic Communication Strategies and the counteracting healthy communicating strategies to teams when things are going well or during team formation activities. By doing this in a fun way, the team can enjoy the vulnerability of exposing their humanness and develop language and agreements on how they will be when these toxic strategies inevitably show up. This will help them grow in their ability to identify these and develop responses that are de-escalating and create better team psychological safety and increase the willingness to collaborate.  Not only that, but they will also have a rich array of tools at their disposal to help guide the team. Keep in mind that most of the de-escalation strategies work on any of the toxic communications so by simply learning one or two that feel comfortable to you, you can have a strong tool to reduce conflict to a healthy level.   
Chapter 22: Knowing When the Engagement is Over Knowing When the Engagement is Over 
You’ve heard the term “beating a dead horse,” right? That’s exactly what you don’t want to do when it comes to ending a coaching engagement. There’s no sense in trying to breathe new life into something that has run its course, and a coaching engagement may have run its course for any number of reasons.  37  https://www.ccl.org/articles/leading-effectively-articles/closing-the-gap-between-intent-vs-impact-sbii/ 

---

## Page 180

 
180 
Part of your job as a professional Agile enterprise coach is to be aware of signs that indicate the end has come, and then exit with poise, professionalism, and grace. This chapter provides information that helps you ascertain how to know when: ● You’re done ● You’re done for now ● It’s time to fire the client ● You’re about to get fired ●  It’s time to give up Let’s start by looking at the definition of “done.”  What It Means to be “Done” In the most general sense, an engagement is “done” when a coach is disengaging with whoever they are working with and moving on. But the definition varies slightly based on the role. It’s different for external consultants than it is for those that are working at the company. When an engagement is over for external coaches, it could mean they no longer work for the company, period. If they were working with a specific department or team within the company, it could also mean that they are finished working with that segment and they are moving on to work with another group within that same company. Internal coaches are employees of the company, and they generally don’t walk out the door when an engagement is over. They typically move on to the next group or department that requires their expertise, remaining within the company while retaining a coaching role. How to Know When You’re Done One of the most common and straightforward ways to know an engagement is over is by acknowledging all the goals have been met. The initial agreement describes what it will look like when the goals are complete. Once the goals are hit, you’re done. Develop a coaching plan that clearly indicates the outcomes the client is looking for and how they will measure success in the beginning of the engagement. This will give you both a way to inspect progress along the way and shift goals or call certain things done - even before all the measures of success are met if the return-on-investment warrants calling it done. If you have a limited engagement contract like a certain number of months, hours, workdays, or sessions it’s a bit easier to see and plan for an exit. However, in many of these engagements, the client is getting enough value that they find the budget to extend the contract. Michael: When you’re crafting the engagement, think of how your time can be best used to benefit the client. Instead of putting four weeks of work into a single month, you can spread it out. I’ve had agreements where I would spread 100 days of work over a year. The client and I 

---

## Page 181

 
181 
mutually agreed on what times I should be working, and that the 100 days should be allocated over a 365-day period. I worked a lot at the start of our engagement and reduced from there. At the beginning, I would work five days a week, then four days a week, then three days a week. Toward the end, I was only working one day a week. Allocating the work in this way gave the client a greater impact at the same cost. It also allowed them to digest their own changes before we moved on to the next item. It was more beneficial than having me there 100 days straight. Your agreement with the client may also consist of a mix of on-site and off-site work. Some coaches charge less for off-site work than for onsite. This, along with any travel expense considerations, needs to be factored into the agreement when establishing the overall amount of work that’s to be done and the cost for that work. Costs are very often time-based as a consultant, and you want to make sure your client is getting the greatest value they can for their budget. You also need to be realistic about how much change is possible: How much can be done in a month? Six months? One year? Another way to set up the engagement is by breaking a one-year period into four quarters of cycles. You can provide four cycles of coaching over the year, with each cycle consisting of a specific set of reasonable and attainable goals. However, the agreement is set up, it’s crucial that it defines the engagement while also outlining a measurable, mutually agreed upon definition of success. In addition to achieving all the goals outlined in the agreement, other indications that your coaching engagement is done include: ● Time or budget ran out: This can apply whether the goals have been met, based on the amount of time or sessions designated in the initial agreement. When the client has a limited budget, you agree to do what you can in the time you have but don’t over-promise. You can only control what you do. You can’t control the rate at which the client adopts change or learns. Communicate throughout and help them to find ways to continue once your engagement ends. ●  The coach/client engagement is not a good match: Certain relationships just don’t click. It’s a waste of your time and your client’s time if you happen to enter one of them. ● A mutual agreement can also end the engagement. Both client and coach may realize they don’t need each other any longer and continuing the engagement wouldn’t make sense. ● The client doesn’t want to make progress: Sometimes clients simply don’t want to change, even if they were gung-ho about the idea when they hired you. Other times they can only tolerate and consume so much change at one time and they need a break to digest what they have and really make it part of the working culture before doing anything more. Michael: Sometimes an engagement will be done but the client wants to continue working together. I’ve had clients sign up for a certain amount of work but wanting more work once our 

---

## Page 182

 
182 
initial engagement is complete. That typically means creating a new engagement that outlines a new set of goals and terms.  How to Know When You’re Done for Now As you saw with the example of spreading the coaching work over an extended period, coaching doesn’t necessarily happen all at once in a short burst of intensity. Some clients may prefer to work with a coach for several months, then pause the coaching while they make progress on their own. After the client’s changes have been implemented, the coach may circle back and work for another set period. The process could be repeated as frequently as needed or desired.    Cherie: One way to craft an agreement is to define the pauses up front. I’ll work with clients over a year-long period, breaking it down into quarters. In the first quarter, I’ll conduct a class and provide two weeks of coaching, for a total of three weeks of coaching for that quarter. In the second quarter, we’ll repeat the process with a different class. It ends up as three weeks of coaching per quarter, or one week of work per month which might be spread throughout the month and not necessarily consecutive days. Michael has entered similar engagements. Michael: With some clients, I’ll facilitate PI (Program Increment38) planning once per quarter. We’ll have a large group planning session where I help them do planning for a quarter, and that’s the extent of the coaching I’ll do for those three months. I’ll come back in the next quarter and help them with the next round of PI planning. It’s all set up in the contract, designating when we’re done for now. I’ll do a little bit of work, pause, and then a little bit more. Another way to set up this type of engagement is to implement an assessment with recommendations. After an initial assessment, the client moves forward and incorporates the recommendations. The client may call the coach back if they need help with some of the recommendations as they execute the plan. The coach then returns to reassess the situation. Again, this setup is outlined up-front in the coaching contract. Cherie has used her own assessment model and Agility Health39 for this purpose. Alex does amazing work with Evidence 
 38  https://www.scaledagileframework.com/pi-planning/ 39  https://agilityhealthradar.com/radars/ 

---

## Page 183

 
183 
Based Management40 assessments and consulting to help leaders of companies learn how to collect and utilize hard data to make evidence-based decisions and measure progress.  Michael: These are some of the ways to get coaching without having a full-time coach, which can be particularly beneficial to the client. Having a full-time coach could hurt the client’s success. It comes with the risk of creating dependency.  How to Know When It’s Time to Fire the Client While many coaching engagements end with a mutual agreement that the time has come, that’s not always the case. There may be situations where firing the client is the best – or only – course of action. Firing the client could mean ending the engagement with a team or specific group you’re working with. Or it could involve terminating work with an entire organization or a company. Clients may need to be fired if they’re not meeting the agreements of the contract. For coaching to work, the coach needs to work with a team of people or a person with authority who owns change. That’s part of the contract. If the client doesn’t provide that, it’s time to move on. The client isn’t ready to make the investment in their own change. You can’t make the change for them - they must be ready. Cherie: I require access to the people with authority who hold the budget and have the power to reinforce change. These people need to be involved with the coaching process for change to take place. If the client says those people are not available, I’ll have a hard conversation with them, explaining the necessity of making those people available. If those people are still not available after the hard conversation, it’s time to let the client go. If they’re not doing their part, I can’t do my part. The coach can’t change the company for the client. The client needs to do it themselves. And if the people with authority who own the change aren’t on board, no change can occur. It doesn’t make sense to continue. Sometimes you focus on the team level, and they have made as much change as possible with the level of authority they have. Then, managers become the blockers to further movement forward. If that level is not willing to change, then they are giving you the signal that the organization has tolerated all the change they can handle at this time. At whatever level the resistance to change becomes an immovable blocker to progress, it becomes the ceiling. No change can occur beyond that point until that level is ready to move forward. In many cases this is a symptom of “I want change, but I don’t want to change,” which frequently happens. Managers will point at the level beneath them or at teams and say, “They need to change,” while refusing to change themselves. This is often also a symptom of them wanting the coach to do the work for them. “You change everything and make my organization better and I’ll just keep 
 40 https://www.scrum.org/resources/evidence-based-management-guide?gclid=CjwKCAjwy7CKBhBMEiwA0Eb7aqREnOxlkzYzCMOb8grPNUFNpX5AWYvZTpJF6T2839ZTuUwxCSHqbRoCkrEQAvD_BwE  

---

## Page 184

 
184 
doing the same things I’ve always done and soak in the benefits.”  In such cases, it’s crucial to have a conversation with management. Cherie: I generally have a straightforward conversation with the management team explaining to them that the layer beneath them has achieved all it can achieve without change and support from their level. “This team is the impediment to more progress in the organization at this point,” I’ll tell management. “What do you want to do about this?” Usually, they say they are invested in change, and they are on board. I’ll then give them 30 days to stop blocking change and to make some progress forward. Sometimes they start making progress after understanding the impact they were having. Other times nothing changes. I have a second conversation with them letting them know that I am giving them a conditional 30-day notice. If their behavior continues to hinder progress, and they show no evidence that they intend to move forward, I disengage and either move on to another area of the company or end the engagement. Toxic environments can be another reason to fire a client. These can include environments rife with elements like extreme conflict, politics, and deception. It can also be environments where people exhibiting toxic behaviors are promoted because the work, they do is good quality. This is often one of the indicators that the management team is not ready to make further changes. Cherie says, “If they enable a toxic environment, they aren’t ready for the kind of work I do.” If the client says they want to make changes that create a less toxic environment, and they exhibit behaviors that indicate they are invested in the change, it may be okay to continue. But that’s not always the case. It’s time to fire the client if a toxic environment continues to persist, or if the toxic nature of the environment is impacting the coach personally. Sometimes you will get into an organization who said all the right things to get you in the door. Then, after getting there you realize that the leader who brought you in did so under duress. Cherie has had clients ask her to stay on as a coach even when they knew that they did not intend to move any further because their boss required them to have a coach on staff and like the atmosphere when she’s around. While that kind of engagement can be lucrative and easy - getting paid to hang around doing nothing, it is not fun and borders on unethical. Michael: I was once coaching a 500-person organization and the person in charge was the impediment. He constantly undermined what we were trying to do. He refused to alter his outlook or behaviors. I fired the client. Two years after I left, he was fired. While he was there blocking changes, there was no progress that could possibly be made. I have all sorts of examples of toxic environments, or environments where Agile coaching just wasn’t going to work. My boss at one organization did not know what Agile was. Another boss was acting in ways that were extremely toxic and dysfunctional. There’s no light at the end of the tunnel in such cases. The only feasible action to take was to fire them. When the client starts to see you as part of their staff and they stop listening to you and expect you to start listening to them, it can be an indicator that it’s time to end the engagement. 

---

## Page 185

 
185 
Cherie: I have always made it my standard practice to not have agile coaching engagements that are contracted for more than a year. I might extend it out a few months at the end of the contract if things are moving at a good pace and there is obvious value I can still add. However, what I’ve noticed happen with Agile coaches who stay on for a few years is that the client becomes used to them being there and starts to see them as staff augmentation. In those cases, it isn’t unusual for the client to stop listening to the sound advice of the coach. Instead, they expect the coach to conform to the client’s bad habits instead of helping them change them. This type of scenario goes against my values as a coach so I avoid it by telling the client from the beginning that I will only be here one year, and they are going to have to learn how to do this without me. That message remains consistent and helps the client to remember that I’m only there for a short time and they need to draw as much value from me as possible before I’m gone. It also forces them to be dependent upon themselves instead of me because I won’t be around long term. I have a specific purpose to fulfill - to get them ready to do this without me.  How to Know When You’re About to Get Fired On the flipside, an engagement may end when the client is prepping to fire the coach. There’s a difference between not having your contract renewed and getting fired. Not having the contract renewed is a normal thing for external consultants. Being fired is generally not. The former involves shaking hands and parting on agreeable terms. The latter involves not renewing with a bad taste left, or the client terminating the agreement early for reasons other than company budget cuts. For a full-time coach, getting fired involves getting marched out of the office and told not to come back. “Turn in your badge, and don’t let the door hit you while you’re leaving.” Telltale signs you’re about to get fired include: Lack of communication: People are no longer communicating with you. You send an email, and you don’t get a reply. From anyone. People avoid you in the hallways. Demotion or losing access: You may go from working with C-level executives to VP-level executives, and then down to teams. You become uninvited to meetings and lose access to the change makers in the organization. Escalation: Instead of discussing problems with you or being straightforward about what’s going on, people may start to go two levels above your head to complain about you. When you are the problem: The coach may be creating friction within the company. This happens when you want something for the client that they don’t want for themselves. You’re trying to push your agenda against their will or desires. In such cases, you’re not managing the situation. The situation is managing you. If these things are happening to you, it’s a good time to stop and evaluate the situation. What can you learn from this that can make you do better in the future? Rather than blaming the client or the environment, take a hard look at how you’ve been interacting with them. Are you the 

---

## Page 186

 
186 
problem? This might be a good time to ask for feedback. If the bridge between you isn’t completely burnt down to the ground, you may be able to get back on stable ground. At the very least, you may be able to learn what you did wrong, so you don’t keep repeating the same mistakes again and again. Sometimes it’s simply not a good fit or what you want and what the client wants is just too far apart. Usually, these things can be avoided with good pre-engagement conversations and agreements. Being willing to say no to engagements that aren’t a good fit can also help you to avoid these situations. Not everyone is at a point in their career where they can turn down jobs that don’t feel like the right fit for them. However, if you can hold out a bit longer and wait for a good fit, you’ll be better off in the end. If you are a coach who works on contracts or subcontracts and doesn't get paid when you’re not working, it’s important to plan for those times when you are not working. Being in a financial position to go without a contract for several months or up to a year can give you freedom to take the right jobs and not just any jobs. It’s worth the discipline to make the investment in your own stability so you can have an enjoyable career. How to Know When It’s Time to Give Up Finally, you’ll know it’s time to give up on the engagement if you are feeling exhausted, depleted or otherwise ready to collapse. In our hard-working, push-yourself society, it’s vital to pay attention to signs that it’s time to give up before you end up pushing yourself over the edge. These signs can include: Taking medication to handle stress or anxiety: If a client situation has you turning to medication for relief, it’s likely in your best interest to terminate the engagement. This also applies if you’re suffering from migraines, sleep disturbances or insomnia that you attribute to the work. Not getting paid enough: If clients can’t afford to pay you at the level you need, or if the pay is decidedly below your skill level and contribution, you may want to pack your bags. Continuing in such a situation has the potential to start breeding resentment and, in turn, actions or behaviors you may later regret. When you see yourself “giving up:” You’ll feel this when it’s happening. If you find yourself at work but you’re not happy to be there you may start cutting corners, or stop being fully engaged or present in your work. You might show up out of obligation and not be motivated to engage. At times like this it’s important to evaluate where you can re-engage and get motivated again. If you can’t, it may be an indication that your time there has come to its natural end. Not maintaining a sustainable pace: Long hours, exhausting tasks, and going full steam ahead for too long with too little rest is a guaranteed recipe for a breakdown. If you are doing this to yourself, take time to evaluate why. Are you in over your head? Are you working extra hours to get the hourly rate? Are you using work to cover up some other emotional issue, so you 

---

## Page 187

 
187 
don’t have to deal with it? If it's the client who is forcing the extra hours, it may be time to move on if it’s impacting your mental or physical health. When you don’t care anymore: This occurs not when you see yourself giving up, but when you’ve given up. You simply don’t care about what’s going on. It’s not fair to the client and not good for you to continue. You’re wasting your time and their money. Do the right thing and disengage. When they see you as a captive employee: Once upon a time you were a respected coach that held their attention. They listened and acted. Now you’ve become just another employee on the scene, and they’ve all stopped listening. When you’re emotionally charged: Being angry is one of the most common scenarios, but you could also be filled with other extremely strong, raw, uninhibited emotions. You’re a ticking time bomb, ready to explode.   If one or more of these signs become part of your daily life, go fill up your gas tank by taking a break.  Summing It Up Entering a new coaching agreement can be exciting and full of promise. In some cases, so can ending the engagement. While certain engagements may not end on the highest note, others can be cause for celebration. The excitement comes from knowing your client can confidently move forward without you, with the promise of sustaining the new concepts and changes they’ve implemented. Both are surefire signs that your Agile coaching made a positive impact – and that you can consider it a job well done.

Part 4: Organizational Complexity By: Dimitar Bakardjiev

Chapter 1: Introduction  Cynefin, pronounced “kuh-nev-in,” is a Welsh word that signifies the multiple factors in our environment and our experience that influence us in ways we could never understand [snowden_leader_2007]. The Cynefin framework is a framework built on sense-making, which is the way humans choose between multiple possible interpretations of various types of input to make sense of the world and decide how to respond to it [snowden_multiontology_2005]. In other words, first, how do we make enough sense of the world so that we can act in it? And second, there is the concept of sufficiency: how do we know enough about the world so that we can act in it meaningfully?   The two key principles for a usable practical decision-making model are: 1. The model must be simple enough to draw on the back of a table napkin -- from memory. If we can’t do that, the concept is of little use in day-to-day decision-making.  And, if that is the case, the concept will surely have no impact at all on our decision-making strategy. Huge models are designed to create dependency on the model creator [see: snowden_table_2015]. 2. For any information or visualization to lead directly to an action, it must be easy to instantly understand without a secondary interpretation. When the decision makers need to have the data interpreted, all sorts of problems come into play. The more interpretive41 layers there are, the more likely things we need to pay attention to will get missed. And the more hidden assumptions we’ll have. To make the best decisions, the interpretive layers between the raw data and the decision maker need to be removed.42  Cynefin is a decision support framework that can be used both strategically and operationally. The model provides a structured thinking process for decision making that considers strategy and routine functional operations and recognizes diverse ways of thinking.  Cynefin was designed as much as a conflict resolution framework as for understanding decision-making[snowden_liminal_2019].  Cynefin allows us to have a differentiating conversation. It helps us see things  41 aka mediation 42 The process of removing the interpretive layers between raw data and the decision maker is called disintermediation. [snowden_disintermediation_2016] 

---

## Page 190

 
190 
from various perspectives.  It creates a shared language that allows people to understand each other at different levels and between different functions in an organization [snowden_fractality_2015] The language we use determines what we do. If the language we use to describe or analyze is different from the language we use to discuss actions, we have introduced a mediation layer.  There is a British phrase — “horses for courses,” meaning that each racehorse runs better on different types of ground. So, if betting on racehorses, we would bet on different horses depending on if they were racing on wet or dry ground. It also means that it is important to choose suitable people for particular activities, because everyone has different skills. The Cynefin idea is, once we understand the nature of the system, we will take a different approach for discovery and intervention/action. Different approaches work in different contexts. To use a philosophical phrase — ontology43 determines epistemology44.  In general, people try to apply one universal approach in a situation where different contexts require different solutions. The problem with things like Six Sigma is that because they come with an ontological assumption, rather than with an ontological discovery, they impose a wrong universal model. All such techniques try to impose universality.  One of the unique things about Cynefin, and one of its key principles, is: we are not saying that traditional techniques are wrong, and we can’t use them. We are actually saying they are right within the proper context and its boundaries. There is nothing wrong with best practices in a rigidly constrained system. There is, however, everything wrong with best practices outside a rigidly constrained system.  There is nothing wrong with doing experimental probes within a complex system. But there is everything wrong with spending money on probes if we’ve got a rigidly constrained system. To say, “Everything is complex,” is as foolish as saying, “Everything is ordered.” Human beings have learned how to create constraints to produce predictability, and that’s part of our success.  Cynefin is about cognitive efficiency and recognition of diversity, without arguments. If we agree on which domain we are in, we immediately move into some type of action. We know where to look when things are shifting.      43 Ontology: a set of concepts and categories in a subject area or domain that shows their properties and the relations between them. [New Oxford American Dictionary] 44 Epistemology: the theory of knowledge, especially with regard to its methods, validity, and scope. Epistemology is the investigation of what distinguishes justified belief from opinion. [New Oxford American Dictionary] 

---

## Page 191

 
191 
Chapter 2: Organization To understand what constitutes a system, we need to understand what is meant by “organization.” It will be easier for us to define what an organization is if we ask ourselves, what is the opposite of "organization"? The opposite of organization is chaos. Chaos is when we have many things that are not constrained by anything. The word “constrained” is important here. To understand what a constraint is, we first need to understand what variety is. Variety (or diversity) means the number of distinct elements in a set. For example, the variety of the set of human sexes {Male; Female} is 2. A constraint is a relation between two sets; it occurs when the variety that exists under one arrangement is less than the variety that exists under another.   For example, if a certain school admits only boys, the variety in the set of sexes within the school {Male} is 1. Since 1 is less than 2, a constraint exists in that school.  Another example is the moon orbiting the Earth. In theory, the moon can occupy any position on the space-time continuum. However, in practice, it occupies a smaller set of positions that describe a particular curve. That is - something restricts the moon from taking some of the other theoretically possible positions. And what limits it? Gravity. Hence, we can say that gravity is the constraint. Further, it follows that every law of nature is a constraint, e.g., the Newtonian law says that, of all possible velocities and positions of the planetary bodies, only a smaller set will actually occur, and then the law specifies what values the elements will have [ashby_variety_2011].  The intensity or the level of a constraint is the reduction it causes in the variety of a given set. Thus, a constraint that reduces the variety from 10 to 1 is more rigid than a constraint that reduces the variety from 10 to 5.  Constraints may be coupling or containing. The first constraint defines the boundaries around something, while the second constraint defines the interactions between entities that form a part of the system [salmon_causal_1990]. Constraints may be physical -- as in a production system. They may also be intangible -- as a policy/practice in an organization. The physical constraints may also be further constrained by the types of policies/practices.  There is also a constraint if the state of one thing depends on the state of another. By state, we understand not only the properties of one thing and its values, but also the positions in the space it can occupy. For humans, state means the values they can share; the methods they can use; the goals they can pursue; the functions they can perform; the rules they must follow. In general, a resource constraint is a situation in which an organization has 

---

## Page 192

 
192 
fewer resources than it perceives to need. The important thing is that the constraint is what people perceive to be true, not what is objectively true. In general, we have the following types of constraints: ● time ● resources: knowledge, information, finance, working conditions, etc.  ● space — volume, area Space constraints do not have to be “physical” constraints. They may be a constrained private space. That is what we call "transparency." Hence, transparency is a constraint.  Why do we focus on transparency? Because later, we will see that transparency is a value in Kanban, and one of the three pillars of Scrum. From this point of view, the world around us is full of constraints. But because we are so accustomed to them, we take most of them for granted, and in fact, often we are not even consciously aware that they exist. To see what the world would look like if a fraction of its usual constraints doesn’t exist, consider fairy tales, or the cinema. A world without constraints would be totally chaotic.  Having described what a constraint is, we will now give our definition of “organization.” An organization is one or more constraints on the properties of a thing: on the positions it may occupy; the choices it can make; the actions it can take; the values it can share; the methods it can use; the goals it can pursue; the functions it can perform; and the rules it must follow.  Generally speaking, an organization is a set of constraints applied to a thing, or a group of things.             

---

## Page 193

 
193 
 Chapter 3: System Organization and Structure A system is a set of things that an organization is applied to. Here are examples of organized systems. The solar system consists of nine planets whose organization is based on constraints — Newton's three laws. Another example is the tax collecting organization, the so-called tax system. System and organization are used interchangeably in everyday language. We say, “Let’s make an organization with a purpose ..."; or "The judicial system is organized as ...". It is important to understand the difference, and the relationship, between the "structure" and the "organization" of a system. An organization includes the connections between the components of a system that must be available for the system to exist. The organization determines the dynamics of interactions in the system, and the permissible transformations of the system[prom_tree_1992]. The organization embodies the identity of a system. If the organization of a system changes, its identity will also change [maturana_autopoiesis_1980]. In a human system, the organization represents the established practice. Accordingly, the established practice determines the identity of the system. The structure of a system, which is the specific implementation of an organization, represents the specific components -- and the specific relationships between them -- that make an organization actually exist [maturana_autopoiesis_1980]. Although the organization is abstract, the structure that implements it is real. Therefore, each organization can be implemented by many different structures. Let's take an example of a mechanical device, like a car. The specific structure of a particular car model may change if we have leather instead of fabric seats. This change in structure will not change the fact that the whole is organized as a car. We should have no problem recognizing such a machine, despite the fact that each car can be made with a different structure and materials. This is, in other words, a duality between the organizational and structural descriptions of a system. It seems that we humans cannot characterize an organization unless there is a way to associate it with some specific structure. At the same time, no particular structure can account for the perception of existence45 it generates unless it is characterized in terms of the organization to which it belongs. We need to simultaneously keep the relationship between the organization  45 its phenomenology  

---

## Page 194

 
194 
and the structure of a system intact, and not confuse the two types of descriptions, which is easy to do [varela_principles_1979].                                       

---

## Page 195

 
195 
Chapter 4: Typology and Taxonomy There are differences between how we perceive things, how we know things and how things actually are. The fact that those three differences exist does not mean that reality does not exist. In philosophy, phenomenology is the way we perceive things, epistemology is the way we know things, and ontology means the fundamental nature of things – how they actually are.  First, we shall see how Cynefin clarifies the types of system based on what they objectively are. There are two basic approaches to classification - typology and taxonomy. The first is typology, which conceptually separates a given set of items multidimensionally. In a typology, the dimensions represent concepts, which do not necessarily exist in physical reality. As such typologies generate heuristics46 which are more adaptive under changing circumstances. On the downside the dimensions (concepts) can be arbitrary, may not be exhaustive and can easily be subject to clashes of interpretation. A taxonomy, on the other hand, which classifies things based on clear empirical47 characteristics, could have rules that allow certain things to be assigned a location. Taxonomies have clear boundaries and allow for rapid decision-making. On the downside, once a taxonomy is established, if something does not fit, it will be forced to fit. Thus, the taxonomy itself creates a filtering mechanism through which we can filter observable characteristics [smith_typologies_2002]. Cynefin uses a hybrid classification. It is a typology, in that its dimensions are conceptual, are based on heuristics, and come from people’s own interpretation of their own unique context. At the same time, the dimensions are based on natural science; hence, Cynefin can also be used as a taxonomy, although with care [snowden_typology_2011]. Based on the concept of “constraint,” the Cynefin typology classifies the world into three types of systems: Ordered, Complex and Chaotic [snowden_strategy_2005].  
 46 loosely defined rules - concepts 47 verifiable 

---

## Page 196

 
196 
 Figure 1: Three types of systems.  If there are no constraints, the system is Chaotic. If the constraints are severe enough for the system to be predictable, it’s Ordered, If the constraints are loose, it’s Complex. That means that changing the constraints can change the nature of the system -- and consequently, the nature of the appropriate situational assessment and decision making. In summary: in Cynefin, Ordered, Complexity and Chaos are ontological states with phase shifts (transitions) between them [snowden_strategy_2005]. There’s also a little fold beneath the Order to Chaotic boundaries, to show how rigid order can fall into chaos.       


---

## Page 197

 
197 
 Chapter 5: Order, Complexity and Chaos Some people see order, complexity, and chaos as being different in the degree of how rigid the constraints are. In Cynefin, we see order, complexity, and chaos as different in the kind of rigidity of constraints. The idea is that, as the constraints get more rigid, a transition called “phase shift” occurs. The result of a phase shift is that the system changes its nature. For example, if we heat up water to 100ºC, it gradually becomes steam.  If, on the other hand, the temperature drops to 0ºC, then the water slowly becomes ice. In a metaphor - Ordered is solid, Complex is liquid, Chaotic is gas [kauffman_origins_1993]. The phase shift is not a spectrum or a gradient, but a clear boundary [snowden_propensities_2016].  Phase shift transitions either produce energy, or require, energy as input48. The nature of humans is that we quickly create connections and constraints intuitively as well as deliberately. To create and sustain a human system in a chaotic state with no effective constraints takes a lot of energy, both to create and to sustain the chaos [snowden_cynefin_2019a].  A shift from Chaotic to Complex does not take any energy and would happen naturally. A shift from Complex to Order, however, takes a big energy input, because we are creating a high level of constraint.   Cynefin has explicit boundary conditions since human beings don’t cope very well with gradients; we need to create boundaries. Having a boundary is essential to human sense-making.  Give us a gradient, and we settle where we are most comfortable; but create a boundary, and then we can behave differently on the other side. We need to be able to say – on this side, things are different. Probably the most difficult thing for people to grasp is that contradictory things work well on different sides of a boundary, e.g., Quantum mechanics49 works very differently from Newtonian mechanics50.  In Cynefin, the boundary conditions are also domains in more sophisticated uses. We will not cover this topic here, but it’s useful to know that the transitions between domains are called liminal states. [snowden_liminal_2017]      48 From a physics perspective, Chaotic is a lower energy state than Complex, and is a proper term in the domain of natural closed systems. But here we are talking about human systems, and those are open, not closed.  49 the branch of mechanics that deals with the mathematical description of the motion and interaction of subatomic particles, incorporating the concepts of quantization of energy, wave-particle duality, the uncertainty principle, and the correspondence principle. 50the system of mechanics which relies on Newton's laws of motion concerning the relations between forces acting and motions occurring 

---

## Page 198

 
198 
Chapter 6: Cynefin Model As we learned, Cynefin classifies objective reality into three types of systems - Ordered, Complex, and Chaotic.  We also saw how to distinguish between the types of systems by using the concept of constraint. Those three types are objective and can be used to both describe and understand the natural world. However, human knowledge is intrinsically a subjective construction, not an objective reflection, of reality. This means that the emphasis should shift from the apparently objective systems around us to the cognitive and social processes by which we construct our subjective models of those systems. This constitutes a major break with traditional systems theory, which implicitly assumes that there is an objective structure or organization in the systems we investigate. This is reinforced by the concepts of autonomy and self-organization, which imply that the structure of a system is not given, but rather developed by the system itself, as a means to survive, and then adapt to a complex and changing environment [heylighen_philosophy_2006]. That’s why Cynefin also has four states (Clear, Complicated, Complex, Chaos) that represent the ways we know things and, accordingly, how we act. Each state is characterized by an appropriate decision model. Most importantly, the Cynefin framework is not about “objective” reality, but about perception and understanding. We understand that different people might be perceiving, and thus hold different perspectives on, the same event or situation. Such an understanding can be used to one’s advantage in many ways. What we care most about is how people perceive and make sense of situations in order to make decisions Accordingly, in Cynefin there are five domains - Clear, Complicated, Complex, Chaos, Confused - that help us understand how humans perceive the current situation they are in [kurtz_new_2003]. Some people see the differences between Order, Complex, Chaotic as only about perception. In Cynefin, we see the differences as being about reality, knowledge, and perception – hence, the three types of systems and five domains.  In order to get better alignment inside an organization, what we actually need is to increase the friction between perception, knowledge, and reality. If we allow reality, knowledge, and perception to separate wildly, everything becomes totally inauthentic. If we increase the interaction (friction) between perception, knowledge, and reality, the three will never be fully aligned, but at the same time, the misalignment never goes too far.    The five Cynefin domains are presented graphically as the Cynefin model. 

---

## Page 199

 
199 
These are the five main situations in which human beings would make decisions. The focus of a domain name is on the nature of the system. The action phrases (e.g., probe-sense-respond) reference how we should gain knowledge and then act. Methods and practices also have characteristics that place them in domains, or in transition between domains. Hence, the designation of Best/Good/Exaptive51/Novel52 practice.    
 Figure 2: Cynefin model.  The right-hand domains (Complicated, Clear) are those of order, and the left-hand domains (Complex, Chaos) are those of un-order. Un-order is not the lack of order, but a different kind of order, and is legitimate in its own way [kurtz_new_2003]. The central area is the domain of Confusion. The shift between order and chaos is shown as a catastrophic fold - a walk over the cliff,  51 a trait, feature, or structure of an organism or taxonomic group that takes on a function when none previously existed or that differs from its original function which had been derived by evolution  52 new and not resembling something formerly known or used: not previously identified 


---

## Page 200

 
200 
without realizing what will happen until it is too late.  The shift shows how easily obvious solutions can cause complacency and lead to a fall into chaos.  Cynefin divides Order into two domains – Clear and Complicated. The separation is a question of perception - some see an issue as obvious, while others see it as hopelessly complicated. Human perception is important for action, and Cynefin is an action-based framework. We may say that Clear is visibly ordered and Complicated is ordered too, but hidden, so that we have to unhide it. The separation is not “objective,” but about human perception and knowledge, so it is shown in a lighter shade of grey. As we saw earlier, the boundaries between the other three domains are about objective reality, knowledge, and perception, all together.  Each domain has its own particular relationship with cause and effect. With Cynefin, we can avoid using a practice established on assumptions of causality in the non-causal domains, and vice versa.  In the ordered domains, there is a known, obvious, and self-evident (Clear) or knowable (Complicated) relationship between cause and effect.     In the complex domain, there probably isn’t a relationship between cause and effect, and even if there is, it is nonlinear, and delayed. Such a cause-and- effect relationship is called “retrospective coherence.” It means that, in hindsight, it's easy to explain why things happened, yet it is impossible to predict them ahead of time; hence, retrospective coherence cannot be used as a basis for acting in the present. Some caution is in order - retrospective coherence may be perceived as “self-evident”! In chaos, there is no relationship between cause and effect at all - the system is, in a sense, random. Cynefin is a fractal framework. Fractality means we may find something to be complicated on one level, but complex on another.  Organization faces situations and issues that can be placed within the various Cynefin domains. The organization as a whole can be in multiple domains, or - and this is critical - in a state of dynamic shift between domains.  All four domains have equal value. We need to really emphasize this: the essence of the Cynefin approach is not to say that complexity is good, and that order is wrong. The key is to understand which domain we are in and just as important, how we plan to move between domains.  There is nothing wrong with order if we can maintain constraints because that produces predictability.    

---

## Page 201

 
201 
Chapter 7: Clear Domain In the Clear domain, the constraints are rigid and fixed. Linear relationship between cause and effect not only exists, but its nature and consequential actions are self-evident to any reasonable person. For example, driving on the same side of the road – the left side in Britain, and the right side everywhere else -- is the reasonable thing to do, because everybody is doing the same in those countries. In the Clear domain, the way we know things and act is sense, categorize, and then respond, based on best practices.  What should organizations do? Clear problems have known solutions. Since constraints are fixed, no deviation is allowed; organizations employ best practices to manage their processes. Best practices incorporate solutions to problems, stored as recurring patterns that allow for high performance as they are detailed and precise steps, sometimes at the edge of automatic responses. They minimize costs, reduce uncertainty, and increase control over processes. In this perspective, deviations from rational decisions are labeled as “biases and errors” primarily caused by human error, e.g., "operator error, organization error, maintenance error, design error, installation error and assembly error.” Applying best practice doesn’t need experts - it is clear to everyone. However, defining appropriate best practices is primarily reserved for the organization's experts.  Organizational structures are bureaucratic; processes are oriented towards formal procedures and controls. Structured techniques are not only desirable but mandatory. When the future is believed to be predictable, and full information is made available, decision makers will stick to single-point forecasting. This is when we assign a single value to an outcome, e.g., to forecasting that the temperature at a given place on a given day will be 23 degrees Celsius.  In contrast, probabilistic forecasts assign a probability to several different outcomes.  In Clear situations, the focus is on efficiency and consistency. [kurtz_new_2003]. We monitor for deviance, for two different purposes: (1) compliance; (2) is it time to change? In the Clear domain, predictability is both a benefit and a danger [snowden_liminal_2019a].  Although KPIs – Key Performance Indicators --work, are valuable and necessary in the Clear domain, they need to be easy-to-understand, few in number, and not require constant reference to manuals or half-remembered training [snowden_liminal_2019a].   

---

## Page 202

 
202 
Chapter 8: Complicated Domain In the Complicated domain, the constraints are governing, but within them is predictability. The difference between rigid constraints (Clear) and governing constraints (Complicated) is important. In the Complicated domain, the right approach is not clear and self-evident, because there are several credible alternative solutions. Hence, we have to use our expertise to select one of the alternatives.  In the Complicated domain, the way we know and then act is to sense, analyze, and then respond.   What should organizations do? Complicated problems, even those with many thousands of components, have a few predictable solutions. It is believed that the environment and the organizations can be fully described, even if, at first glance, that might not be easy or obvious. We can prepare and use models, including systems of differential equations and other high-level math. Since cause-effect relationships are central and are supposed to be given by "nature" or other external authority, experts will, in turn, favor experiments, fact-finding and scenario-planning [kurtz_new_2003]. Within the governing constraints, variation is allowed. If we’ve got the right expertise and the right training that are acknowledged by our peers, then a degree of variation is permitted. A metaphor could be improvisation in jazz - one governing theme and one variation, based on individual musicians. With governing constraints, we have good practice, not best practice; the difference is important. A best practice is obvious and doesn’t need experts. However, if you have to think about it, we can agree on a good practice. Some caution is in order: if we impose best practice in a good practice domain, the people who understand the practice realize that there are variations but are not allowed to perform.  The organizational structures are oriented towards bureaucracy and technical determined processes, managed by formal procedures. Controls are relaxed to allow for variations based on expertise: The right to vary practice is hard won and to a degree bounded by peer review and peer acceptance. Planning and control can be far less explicit because the basics of what are needed are embedded in the professional standards, practices, and training. In this domain alignment on goals and common purpose and goals are useful as they act as a constraint on deviant behavior.
