Goals and targets should not be excessive and should never be created by bureaucrats or consultants who lack experience in the context, are not members in the profession, and do not have peer acceptance.   Decision makers have to rely on expert opinion. Experts here are understood 

---

## Page 203

 
203 
as professional individuals who, through defined training programs, acquire a specialist terminology that is codified in textbooks. The high level of abstraction is teachable, however, given the necessary time, intelligence, and opportunity. As decision makers are regularly not proficient enough in the respective field, relying on expertise shifts authority away from formal hierarchical positions to rather closed communities differentiated from the rest of the organization by a professional identity and a specific language.  The problem in this domain is to stay open for changes despite experts and trivialization, thus, "management of this space requires the cyclical disruption of perceived wisdom53”[snowden_complex_2005].               Chapter 9: Complex, Chaos, and Confused Domains In the Complex domain, there are very loose constraints. This might be perceived as “everything is connected to everything else, but in mysterious ways.”  We call them enabling constraints because they nudge the system to move in a certain direction.   Тhe difference between a governing constraint and an enabling constraint is like the difference between an exoskeleton54 and an endoskeleton55. For an insect with an exoskeleton, everything is contained within the skeleton and doesn’t allow for much variation – that’s a governing constraint.  An endoskeleton has logic and consistency but allows for massive variation – that would be an enabling constraint [snowden_birth_2015].  53 agilists often describe this as "feedback loops" or "inspect and adapt cycles" or "test and learn cycles" 54  a rigid external covering for the body in some invertebrate animals, especially arthropods, providing both support and protection  55 an internal skeleton, such as the bony or cartilaginous skeleton of vertebrates 

---

## Page 204

 
204 
Also in the Complex domain, there are cause-and-effect relationships, although both the number of components (agents) and the number of relationships defy categorization or analysis. In complex environments, recursive56 effects make it impossible to make a rational choice of actions based on cause and effect. Different observers will end up with varying explanations for the same observation. In a complex system, every intervention changes what is being observed. We have inherent uncertainty57. The agents and the system co-evolve. Yet, system behavior is not chaotic or random. Order emerges from interactions of the agents and the system over time. Since the form the order takes is path dependent, it is unique to each emergence. History is important. Different starting conditions give rise to different outcomes. Once the system changes, it is unable to revert back to its exact former state. Emerging patterns can be perceived but not predicted – we call this phenomenon retrospective coherence [snowden_complex_2005]. We can’t rely on any retrospective e.g., lessons learned, process because if the result was positive or not determines how people would describe what happened. We can say with absolute confidence and guaranteed certainty about complex systems:  whatever we do will produce unintended consequences. So, whatever we do, something will happen that we didn’t expect and/or didn’t plan for. Furthermore, we are responsible for any unintended consequences. Even though we couldn’t have predicted the individual outcome, since we knew for certain that there would be unintended consequences, we should have planned for them. So, we need to do small things early in the game so that the inevitable unintended consequences are more manageable.  How do we know we are in a Complex domain? The basic heuristics are: if the evidence supports a competing hypothesis, if there is a dispute, if time pressure is high -- assume it’s complex. The general default is: if in doubt, assume it’s complex. What if for the expert, it is Complicated, and for the rest of us, it’s Complex? That’s okay. If we trust the expert, we can treat it as Complicated, even though it is Complex for the rest of us. If we don’t trust the expert, then we have to treat it as Complex. We can accept that something is Complicated without understanding it – that’s the essence of humanity. We have to be able to trust experts to make decisions for us. The decision model is: probe via experiments, sense the results and respond by either amplifying good, or dampening bad, emerging patterns [snowden_safetofail_2011]. We need to probe the system; when it comes back, we know it’s ready to move, to have a nudge – because we know its  56 relating to or involving a program or routine of which a part requires the application of the whole, so that its explicit interpretation requires in general many successive executions 57 uncertainty that exists as a permanent, essential, or characteristic attribute 

---

## Page 205

 
205 
dispositional58 state.   We can safely claim that human systems are complex by default. Why? Because they are comprised of humans, and humans are definitely not consistent in their behavior. The most important indicator of how we would act is the context. The situation or place we find ourselves in may completely change our perception and judgment. Modeling of human systems is possible. As Murray Gell-Mann, who received the 1969 Nobel Prize in physics, famously said: “The only valid model of a human system is the system itself” [boulton_embracing_2015].  Here is a bit more subtle example of that - the “shadow or informal organization, that complex network of obligations, experiences and mutual commitments grounded in shared experiences, values and beliefs, without which an organization could not survive"[snowden_complex_2005]. To convey knowledge, informal networks prefer stories and proverbs. As such a language is difficult to comprehend and use for outsiders. These networks tend to be closed and cohesive circles, more rigidly restricted than professional ones, and prefer excluding rather than including experts. Membership, even if embedded in formal organizations, is often informally regulated. It is based on emotions, common experience, values, or beliefs, with a long time of apprenticeship, (transition) rituals and enforcing myth-like stories, and long tenure [snowden_chapter_2000].  Different types of problems require different management approaches.  The nature of the system determines the ways we acquire knowledge about it and manage it. Both chaotic and ordered systems can be managed in a structured way. With complex relationships in this domain, "relying on expert opinions based on historically stable patterns of meaning will insufficiently prepare us to recognize and act upon unexpected patterns” [kurtz_new_2003]  For complex problems, the role of management is to create and relax constraints as needed, and then to dynamically allocate resources where patterns of success emerge. We manage what we can manage – the boundary conditions, the safe-to-fail experiments, our determination to rapidly amplify or diminish an activity. By applying this process, we discover value at various points along the way.  Managing complex systems means changing the present without a series of stepping stones, but with a rough sense of direction with the willingness to change [snowden_managing_2014].   58 recurrent behavioral tendency 

---

## Page 206

 
206 
In a complex system, we can only manage the evolutionary potential of the present. We can’t plan long-term, because the cause-and-effect relationships will not be fully known, or knowable, in advance, but understood only retrospectively, in hindsight. Long-term planning is at best a sense of direction rather than a set of goals and targets. A sense of direction can be achieved by visionary goals, but their use has to be done with care. We can plan very short-term, while staying alert and exploiting patterns of possibilities as they start to become visible. However, it is not just about responding to patterns; we can manage the constraints, the catalysts of patterns and the allocation of resources. It is about creating the conditions in which radical re-purposing is easier to stimulate and easy to recognize. Rapid feedback loops, fast failure and learning, and experimentation are all key approaches. The plans and the controls will emerge as we do that. Accordingly, we can only really measure direction and speed of travel from the present.  Exaptation is when -- under conditions of stress -- a trade developed for one function leaps sideways and is re-purposed for a different function/capability altogether [gould_exaptation_1982]. If we look at the evolution – repurposing happens when the system is under stress. If we stress any life form mutation, the rate increases fast. That produces more casualties, but also more variation and more exaptive capacity. Adaptation is a linear evolutionary process - each change happens gradually over time. So, in evolution, we have a linear adaptive process, then we get a stress-based natural leap, and then another linear process. Exaptation accounts for the speed of evolution.  The key on exaptation is to repurpose things in which you are already competent, so that the learning or energy needs are reduced [snowden_cynefin_2019]. Moving to higher levels of abstraction allows us to combine and recombine things in novel ways.  Enabling constraints allows for a huge degree of variety, but there is still governance within the system. If we manage to encapsulate the enabling constraints in the form of governing heuristics or, better still, in the form of habits, then we’ve got a governing structure we can rely on [snowden_purpose_2012].  In human systems, the enabling constraints are called governing heuristics. Generally, decision makers in complex contexts rely, for a substantial proportion of decisions, on heuristics that are adapted to their specific environment. In the Complicated domain we have rules, in the Complex domain we have governing heuristics, one of the ways to embody expert knowledge. Contrary to rules designed to include specific solutions for specific problems, heuristics provide a common structure for a range of similar problems, but also allow for the necessary flexibility in turbulent complex situations and unexpected events. Heuristics handle ambiguity, whereas rules don’t.  

---

## Page 207

 
207 
Examples of governing heuristics are, “Satisfy the customer through early and continuous delivery of valuable software,” “Welcome changing requirements, even late in development,” “Deliver working software frequently”. All twelve principles from the Agile Manifesto are governing heuristics, i.e., enabling constraints. Another example of a governing heuristic comes from the U.S. Marine Corps – if the battlefield breaks down, capture the high ground, stay in touch, and keep moving [snowden_purpose_2012].  Notice that heuristics are measurable for compliance. They are not statements of general principles, which are not heuristics in this sense, because they are too ambiguous. They are measurable – did you capture the high ground, did you stay in touch, did you keep moving? In a governing heuristic, compliance must be measurable -- without ambiguity. Applying broad principles like “Don’t be evil!” won’t work.  Actually, any strategy is an enabling constraint. It gives an organization a direction. Without direction, there is chaos.  We know that if we follow the heuristics, there will be a degree of failure; but that’s acceptable, because it’s considered inevitable. If we try to force a non-failure culture into a complex system, things will get nasty fast.   In the Chaos domain, there are no constraints. Searching for right answers is pointless, because the relationships between cause and effect are impossible to determine.  There is a potential for order, but few can see it; and if they can, they rarely do -- unless they have the courage to act. In this domain, our immediate job is not to discover patterns, but to act.  If we go into chaos deliberately, it takes a huge amount of energy to create.  It is rather like nuclear fusion – the energy required for the magnets to contain the plasma is more than the energy we get out of the nuclear reaction. Making something chaotic is very difficult to do.   The decision model is to act quickly and decisively, then sense and respond.  Chaos is the domain of crises and novel practices.   A crisis is all about control and very little about planning.  There is no time to be nice or consultative, so the imposition of control is likely to be draconian in nature.  The real value comes in working out what to impose and shouldn’t be a final solution; the essence of good crisis management is to create enough constraints that you shift the problem into complexity, which means patterns are starting to emerge [snowden_liminal_2019a].  In the Chaotic domain, novelty arises from stress, from a complete break with the familiar. As a result, radically different approaches would now be accepted, while in normal times they wouldn’t. In order to be effective, the new ways must be accepted by all reasonable people. The case in the chaotic domain is that the 

---

## Page 208

 
208 
context makes unreasonable stubbornness more difficult to sustain [snowden_cynefin_2019b].  Chaos is relative to the context. We can get micro-chaos as well in a specific context, which is, in general, constrained. As far as the context is concerned, there may be no relationships between cause and effect and all of a sudden, somebody makes a novel connection. Take, for example. the invention of microwave ovens. In 1943, an engineer, Percy Spencer, was building a magnetron when he noticed a chocolate bar melt in his pocket.   Realizing the connection, he placed an egg in a kettle and directed the microwaves from the magnetron into a hole cut into the side of the kettle. The egg exploded.  Voila!  The first microwave!  Raytheon filed the patent in 1945 and made a fortune [snowden_granularity_2013].    Before going into the Confused domain, let’s remind ourselves about that catastrophic fall from Order into Chaos. It happens when we make the system very constrained. Sooner or later, tensions build up in the system and there is a disaster. Breaking the laws becomes the norm, corruption follows, and everything else falls through.  The Confused domain is in the middle of the Cynefin diagram. This domain is as significant as the others, in that it is an entry point into the sense-making process. Remember, Cynefin is a decision support framework, so its focus is on understanding the underlying nature (ontology) of the problem being addressed [snowden_cynefin_2020]. This domain is associated with the idea of uncertainty. It is a state of not knowing which domain we are in. There are authentic and inauthentic ways to deal with such confusion.  The authentic way is to acknowledge upfront that we are in a confused state, and then seek to explore options for exiting the situation. It’s authentic, therefore, to use confusion as a transitionary device to eventually understand what domain the present situation is in.  Thus, when we are confused, we are actually at an entry point into the sense-making process. In the Confused domain, we have primary sense-making, namely, creating a sufficient structure of constraints to shift into a domain where the nature of action is known. We can get into authentic confusion when we deliberately create a state of ambiguity or stress in order to achieve a transition. Some people call being in such a state “aporetic.” The Oxford English Dictionary defines aporetia as a state of “perplexity or difficulty.” In philosophy, an aporia is a puzzle or a seemingly insoluble impasse in an inquiry, often arising as a result of equally plausible yet inconsistent premises (i.e., a paradox).  In Plato's Meno dialogue. Socrates describes the purgative effect of reducing someone to aporia: it shows a person who merely thought he knew something 

---

## Page 209

 
209 
that he does not in fact know it, and that instills in him a desire to investigate the subject further.   The inauthentic way is not to acknowledge we are confused, or, to acknowledge it but be unable to come out of it. Staying in confusion is truly a bad place to be.  In inauthentic confusion, we assume we are in the domain in which we feel most comfortable – e.g., we feel certainty, when uncertainty should be the norm. It is a state of false confidence, assuming we know how to act without first making an ontological situational assessment. An example of an inauthentic state is when someone tries to apply Six Sigma in software development projects -- by using Complicated techniques while in a Complex situation. Human beings’ situational assessments are often completed on the basis of an action they have already decided to do. In general, humans often try to apply one universal approach in a situation where different contexts actually require different solutions to be applied. That is what traditional management consultancies usually do. Instead of trying to discover the ontological context of their clients, they come with an ontological assumption that it is ordered. As we know, in the Complicated domain, we need good practices, and in the Clear domain, we need best practices. Traditional management consultancies embody good and best practices in business cases. However, the case-based approach is dependent on understanding the context from which the originating cases were derived. If we try to repeat what worked before, the context may have shifted. Since we probably won’t fully understand the context anyway, we can’t rely on it to the same extent.  Тhe fundamental danger with the case-based approach is the confusion of correlation with causation. The fact that we’ve got a correlation doesn’t mean we’ve got a causal link. Trying to repeat a How without understanding the Why is foolish.  Popular-end business books aren’t concerned about that – their correlation is from three cases, based on what the authors want to hear. It appears that those authors started with a conclusion, and then cherry-picked stories that could be fitted to that conclusion. All of the management books are based on the same blue-chip companies -- which should make us suspicious anyway, because those same companies are used for cases that support competing theories.  The danger of being confused is that we may accidentally slip into chaos because in terms of human perception, the Confused and Chaotic domains have much in common.  Unable to withstand the perception of being in chaos, humans tend to panic and impose too-rigid constraints. A situation that is not clear may 

---

## Page 210

 
210 
lead to a catastrophic fall into chaos. That’s why Cynefin prevents us from jumping into action and instead insists we do a situational assessment first.   Chapter 10: Intervention  The Cynefin framework is first about contextual awareness, followed by contextually appropriate action. So first, we must understand what type of domain we are in. Second, what are the dynamics that shift between domains? Thus, Cynefin has two classification systems: domains and dynamics. As we increase the level of uncertainty the dynamic classification becomes more important than the domain classification.  In an organization, all of the dynamics and all of the domains will be in operation at the same time. For example, at the board level, things may be stable, while at the team level, things may not be stable. Complexity is fractal. Dynamics and domains are value-free! The value lies in the ability to move situations and systems between domains.  
  Figure 3: Four dynamics.    


---

## Page 211

 
211 
  All four dynamics are presented on Figure 3. [snowden_cynefin_2015] ● The stable dynamic is shown in blue: it moves between the complex and the complicated domains, and, if needed, back again.  ● The yellow line shows the dynamic of shallow dive into chaos: breaking up established practice to allow novel practice to emerge or reset the system in order to allow a new stable dynamic to emerge.  ● The green line shows the dynamic of going down into the Clear domain: when we settle on one best practice of doing things and we enforce that in all barring exceptional circumstances.  ● The red line shows the grazing dynamic, in which the overall volatility is such that stability is rarely other than transitionary; we stay in complexity while constantly skimming the surface of chaos.  The most stable dynamic is the movement between the Complex and the Complicated domains [snowden_cynefin_2015]. People who think that complicated has a negative connotation, however, cherish complexity! We argue that Complicated is not negative. Something that can turn Complex into Complicated is great, because doing so - coherently allows for scalability and repeatability. Pretending something is Complicated when it’s actually Complex, however – that’s an error.  For complex problems, we employ numerous coherent59 and safe-to-fail, fine-grained experiments in parallel. These experiments will provide feedback on what works and what doesn’t. We manage the experiments as a portfolio. Some of the experiments must be oblique, some naïve, some contradictory, some high-risk/high-return types. When we run several probes in parallel, they will change the environment anyway, so the logic is that all probes should be roughly the same size and take roughly the same time to complete.  In practice, those experiments generally merge, mutate, or fail; then a solution or solutions emerge. There is concern that there are insufficient coherent hypotheses, or more important, insufficient contradictions. If that is the case, then we should be concerned that we may have an insufficient range of experiments and may be at risk of missing a key weak signal, or signals.  Then we must apply sense-making to the feedback and respond by continuing or intensifying the things that work, correcting or changing those that don’t. As they start to work in a consistent way, we apply constraints. If we continue with the probes, it is then under increased constraints. Now we get onto the key test – if we increase the constraints and that produces  59 to the individual, not to the group as a whole -- although the group must accept that it is a valid viewpoint 

---

## Page 212

 
212 
unexpected consequences, we relax the constraints and keep the process into the complex. When we increase the constraints, if that produces predictable behavior, we’ve made the process complicated -- and that’s good news.   Any linear experimental testing/prototyping process is effectively a Complex to Complicated transition by nature. If we were doing parallel, smaller experiments, they would stay in the Complex domain.   Now we’ve successfully moved into the Complicated domain. And that Complex to Complicated cycle is essential, since Complicated is for exploitation, while Complex is for exploration.  When people manage to shift the project to Complicated, since they assume it will always work, they don’t carry on testing. But in reality, the context may shift; in that case, the constraints put in place may no longer produce predictability. If that happens, then we cycle back into Complex. There is a natural cadence to that movement.  Going too slow or too fast between the domains is a mistake. Getting the cadence right is an experimental process and means there is stability.  Generally, people do the movement in a complicated way, but not the reverse. If we don’t do the reverse, however, what will actually happen is that every now and then, we’ll have to perform a “shallow dive into chaos.” Basically, we have allowed things to become entrained, allowing governing constraints when we should have gotten back to Complex. Then we have to break things up in order to allow them to re-form. In other words, we have to get a bit chaotic. And we have to be careful with that.  In the Chaotic domain, the novelty arises from stress, from a complete break with the familiar [snowden_cynefin_2019].  Hans Selye is frequently claimed to be the father of the stress concept. Since he used the term stress for the response rather than for the stimuli, he invented the word stressor for the stimulus that triggered this response. Given that a particular stressor, or set of stressors, is perceived as threatening or negative, humans report this as “stress.” Stress is a general, unspecific alarm response that occurs whenever something is missing, i.e., when there is a discrepancy between goals and reality. In general, the alarm occurs in all situations where expectancies are not met.  The alarm continues until the discrepancy is eliminated, by changing the expectations -- or the reality, when possible. The alarm is uncomfortable, and “drives” the individual to the proper solutions. Stress is, therefore, an adaptive response [ursin_cognitive_2004].  When people are under stress, they face a coping decision:  either to accept the opportunity to cope with the stress, or not. The term coping covers either the result or the act. We’ll look into both -- starting with coping as the result, i.e., a reduced level of stress.  

---

## Page 213

 
213 
Coping is when the individual establishes an expectancy of being able to cope, i.e., an expectancy that most or all responses to a stressor will lead to a reduced stress level. To emphasize – it is neither  the action, nor the feedback from evaluation of the action, that matters in this case, but rather, the subjective feeling of being able to cope that reduces the stress. Sometimes individuals may achieve the result of coping through helplessness or hopelessness.60 Helplessness is the acquired expectancy that there is no relationship between anything the individual can do and the level of stress. In other words, the individual has no control. However, even when the individual “accepts” that there is no solution, the level of stress may be reduced. It may also be reduced if the helplessness leads to secondary gain and support from society. In such cases, helplessness may function as a coping strategy, and the secondary gain may reinforce and sustain the helplessness condition. Hopelessness is the acquired expectancy that most or all responses lead to a negative result. There is control, and responses have effects, but they are all negative. The negative outcome is seen as personal fault since the individual has control. This conclusion introduces the element of guilt. The second meaning of coping is the coping act – to reduce or eliminate the stress itself by action. When the stressor is a resource constraint, the literature proposes two identifiable coping strategies that humans, who believe they are able to cope with the stressor, are likely to pursue – a simplification-of-focus strategy and a compensation strategy. Simplification-focus coping strategy occurs when humans eliminate unnecessary or less valuable parts of the work process. It can be perceived as analogous to revamping a value-chain, re-engineering a business process or waste elimination in Lean. Simplification is not always a part of the strategy, as people sometimes proceed directly to the focusing action: concentrating on the key components of the work process. A compensation coping strategy involves closing the gap caused by the resource constraint by utilizing coping assets that compensate for the constrained resources. We define coping assets as the skills and capabilities that the organization either possesses or can gain access to and can utilize when dealing with resource constraints [rosenzweig_how_2016].  Unlike the simplification-focus strategy, where people eliminate parts of the work process and focus on the remaining ones, in compensation they add to the work process by utilizing their coping assets. People exploit compensation because the challenge pushes them to optimally utilize the materials, knowledge, people, and other processes that are already available [eisenhardt_making_1989].  Another form of compensation strategy is bricolage. Bricolage refers to  60  [ursin_cognitive_2004] 

---

## Page 214

 
214 
solving problems and taking advantage of opportunities by combining existing resources [baker_creating_2005]. A great example of this strategy is Kaizen in Lean [sugimori_toyota_1977]. A simplification-focus strategy is exploitative in nature. It entails the exclusion of processes or materials, and thus focuses only on components that are already part of the work process. As such, this strategy limits the ability to achieve benefits that only exist outside the boundaries of the system. A compensation strategy is exploratory in nature. Compared to a simplification-focus strategy, a compensation strategy requires people to add to the existing process and knowledge by borrowing knowledge and resources from outside the closed system, outside the scope of the current work process, even outside the organization. Thus, its impact may extend beyond the boundaries of the stress, i.e., the strategy actually over-compensates because it entails new and meaningful learning opportunities.  Our distinction between the two coping strategies and their associations with exploitative and explorative learning processes is particularly valuable in the context of innovation. The strong link between simplification-focus and exploitative learning indicates that a simplification-focus strategy is likely to lead to incremental innovation. Conversely, the strong link between a compensation strategy and explorative learning indicates that a compensation strategy is likely to lead to radical innovation. Importantly, both coping strategies yield positive innovation performance; the type of innovation, however, is a key dimension that may distinguish between the two strategies. In summary, because a compensation strategy provides a richer toolbox for current and future encounters with challenges, it is superior to a simplification-focus strategy [rosenzweig_how_2016]. Still, a single organization may implement both coping strategies in a case in which managers eliminate non-valuable work process actions through simplification-focus and add valuable actions through compensation. Therefore, despite opposing behaviors, it is possible that the implementation of these two approaches overlaps, as manifested in Lean. An example of how novelty arises from stress, as described above, is the way Honda developed new products, as described by an executive: “It’s like putting the team members on the second floor, removing the ladder, and telling them, jump or else! I believe creativity is born by pushing people against the wall and pressuring them almost to the extreme.” [takeuchi_new_1986] This comes from the article that claimed to be the origin of Scrum: “Scrum for software was directly modeled after ‘The New New Product Development Game’ by Hirotaka Takeuchi and Ikujiro Nonaka, published in the Harvard Business Review in 1986.” [sutherland_takeuchi_2011] Another example comes from Ohno: “I strongly believe that necessity is the 

---

## Page 215

 
215 
mother of invention…The key to progress in production improvement, I feel, is letting the plant’s people feel the need.” [ohno_workplace_1988] Let’s take stock and list the necessary -- but not sufficient -- conditions for innovation:  ● Stress caused by a stressor, or a set of stressors, e.g., resource constraints. ● A subjective feeling of being able to cope. ● A perspective shift by borrowing knowledge and resources from outside the closed system, outside the scope of the current work process, even outside the organization itself.  Stress, coping, perspective shift. Remember cognitive activation? Only when the old patterns will no longer survive do we see things differently. So, if we starve people of resources, we actually help them cope --and if we put them close to different sources of knowledge, then they will be wonderfully inventive. A great illustration of this can be found in the movie Apollo 13 [snowden_apollo_2009]. Some things go down into the Clear domain, where, in terms of innovation, things go to die. This isn’t a value statement. Clear may contain 90% of the value of an organization. The point is, we only put things into Clear when we don’t need to change them. If we do need to change them, Clear is a bad place to go [snowden_cynefin_2015].  With human systems, if we impose excessive constraints, sooner or later people will find ways to work around them.  In fact, sometimes the only way to get the job done is to break the rules. That actually is a habitual pattern, an attractor for breaking the rules. The energy consumption of the system actually goes up – it may appear to be efficient on the surface, but the reality is, it’s extremely inefficient. If rules-breaking is an exception, then it works differently. By following a Complexity Thinking-based approach, we would have a few more rigidly enforced rules, and rules about who can break the rules, and when. We also have heuristics, which operate on the other side of the rule boundary.   This is all too common: when faced with complexity, people don’t absorb it but instead try to eliminate it.  The fluidity of complexity is far too scary. Increasingly these days, we find that the level of volatility is such that things never stabilize long enough in any type of regularity, and so we never really get certainty. If that’s the case, we need to stay in the Complex domain while constantly skimming the surface of chaos. That is called a Grazing dynamic [snowden_cynefin_2015]. Different types of problems require different management approaches. For Complex problems, the role of management is to create and relax constraints as 

---

## Page 216

 
216 
needed, and then dynamically allocate resources where patterns of success emerge. We manage what we could manage – the boundary conditions, the safe-to-fail experiments, rapid amplification, and dampening. By applying this approach, we discover value at points along the way.   Learning and adapting to a complex environment is accomplished by running safe-to-fail experiments, i.e., catalytic probes. Does a “probe” mean a scientific type of experiment? Absolutely not! Probes are not experiments, in the strict 19th-century scientific context. Rather, a probe is an experiment in the more general sense of the word – do something, then see if it works. Experimenting means risk reduction, betting after the horse race has started. With probes, we use the present in order to influence the horse race while it’s still on. Here is how we do a probe. We identify any coherent hypothesis about what could potentially improve things. To agree that our hypothesis is coherent is not the same as agreeing that we are right. Every probe gets a small amount of resources to run a short-cycle, safe-to-fail experiment. The rule is very simple – the experiment must be safe-to-fail. That means if it fails, we’ll be able to recover [snowden_safetofail_2011].  Each probe should be: • coherent • safe-to-fail • finely grained, tangible We run all probes in parallel because we want to stay in the Complex domain. Actually, this is a significant conflict resolution tool, because if we do only one probe in sequence, somebody has to be right.  Therefore, everybody has to emphasize the evidence that supports their proposition to get resources. Hence, they get more and more defensive about it and less and less prepared to think it’s wrong, and so attack other people’s ideas more. That’s when things are binary: in order to win, we have to show that the other side is wrong.  On the other hand, if we say, I don’t agree with you, but I think your idea is coherent, that’s a very different proposition. That way, we can reduce strategic conflict. The heuristic on a portfolio of probes is – over the whole portfolio, if some experiments succeed, then others must fail. Because if all of them can succeed, we are not spreading our senses wide enough. We don’t learn by doing something right.  We learn by making mistakes and correcting them. The point is – if we don’t know what’s going to happen, we need broad expansion. We have to run all the probes, not shut some of them down. That’s because the one that works in the present circumstances this time may not work in other circumstances next time – because the experiments will change the space anyway! In a complex system, every intervention changes the thing observed. It’s not about whether this hypothesis is right – if the hypothesis is coherent, then 

---

## Page 217

 
217 
next time around it may work, because next time around, the butterfly will flap its wings in a different sequence. Overall, the portfolio should contain: • some probes that are oblique in nature • “naive” approaches • a few high-risk/high-return options The first portfolio rule is that at least one of the experiments should be oblique, which means trying to resolve a related, but different, problem. Interestingly, the rule basically concludes: When we are stuck trying to solve a problem, if we forget about it and try to work on something else, we often find a solution for the initial problem.  The second rule is that at least one of the experiments must use a naïve asset. That means getting somebody who has never addressed the problem before, but has deep expertise in a related field, to try to come up with a solution. Creative insights come from people who aren’t wedded to the preconceptions and assumptions that characterize the field. What we are doing here is managing the portfolio of experiments in order to force diversity. As experiments are run, the space will change, and sustainable solutions will become more self-evident. Experiments in this regard are generally fusions. When we run several probes in parallel, they will change the environment anyway -- so the logic is that all probes should be roughly the same size and take roughly the same time to work. As this is a very time-consuming effort, traditional management is likely to fall back into command-and-control mode, as this ostensibly promises to provide for quick and efficient answers [snowden_leader_2007].   Scrum, at least its initial application, is a great example of how to stay in the complex domain while constantly skimming the surface of chaos. As Jeff Sutherland said, “In Scrum, we introduce chaos into the development process, and then use an empirical control harness to inspect and adapt a rapidly evolving system.” [sutherland_Scrum_2008] Scrum was formally presented by its co-creators Ken Schwaber and Jeff Sutherland at the 1995 OOPSLA Conference in Austin, Texas. Their presentation, “SCRUM Development Process,” is still available. Here are some quotes: “The system development process is complicated and complex. Therefore, maximum flexibility and appropriate control is required. Evolution favors those that operate with maximum exposure to environmental change and have optimized for flexible adaptation to change. Evolution deselects those who have insulated themselves from environmental change and have minimized chaos and complexity in their environment.” [schwaber_Scrum_1997] We acknowledge that the above assertions are made on purpose. Because per 

---

## Page 218

 
218 
Cynefin, we know what chaos, complex and complicated mean.   “The closer the development team operates to the edge of chaos, while still maintaining order, the more competitive and useful the resulting system will be.” [schwaber_Scrum_1997] “Operating at the edge of chaos requires management controls to avoid falling into chaos.” [schwaber_Scrum_1997] Every Scrum practitioner should be able to map Scrum to what we said above.   What Scrum is supposed to do is:  take a set of complex options, probe them in parallel, and spill, as a complicated solution, whatever is ready to be delivered. This cycle is contained in a sprint.   We can see what we are doing here – essentially this is a language of diversity for decision making -- a language not dependent on the individual qualities and training of the leaders. A very important principle here is that Complexity Thinking focuses on interactions and processes, not on individual qualities. Because if we focus on individual qualities, that never scales fast enough to achieve change and is a good excuse for not achieving change. We can always say that people didn’t have the right mindset. (Even though that shouldn’t be a legitimate excuse.) We need to be able to operate with systems where people may not have the right mindset, but still do the right things. An example is the military – their decision-making is based on the assumption that they will work with average people whom they have to train to fight as a unit. We must have processes appropriate for the Cynefin domain we are in. Governing heuristics are, in fact, rigidly enforced processes. There is nothing wrong with that, provided the processes are appropriate to the domain. The problem is, people associate process with Order, but it doesn’t have to be. With humans, rituals, social constraints, and habits become processes.   Chapter 11: Using Cynefin in Projects  Cynefin is a sense-making framework - not a categorization framework. In order to enable sense-making in every unique context, the Cynefin framework is created anew. That is called contextualization. In categorization, the framework precedes the data. In contextualization, the data precedes the framework. As a result, the boundaries between the domains emerge from the data in a social process. 

---

## Page 219

 
219 
A completed Cynefin framework that is contextualized to the environment and operations of a community, team or organization provides a new shared language with which decision-makers can discuss situations, perspectives, and possible actions. It can be used to talk about interpretations of current conditions based on gathered data, to evaluate strategic interventions, and to constructively manage conflict and bring about consensus, without removing conflict [kurtz_new_2003].  Here, we will use the Cynefin framework to negotiate meaning between Client and Capability contexts when working on a new project [bakardzhiev_adaptable_2016]. The Capability is represented by the delivering organization’s knowledge, skills, and capacity. The value of the contextualized Cynefin framework is that it provides a unique shared language that reflects the context of the project. The Client and the developers can use this language to discuss perspectives and possible actions.  We will create two contextualized Cynefin frameworks – one for the Client, and another for our Capability.   The first thing we need when creating a contextualized Cynefin framework is to write down the user stories to be delivered on hexagon cards. Next, we describe the three ontological Cynefin domains. Then the cards are positioned relative to each corner of the overall Cynefin space – without boundaries. Clusters are allowed to form but are not required. This activity can be done in-person or electronically – but it must be done socially. Discussion is encouraged while placing the cards. This exercise allows us to stop people from saying, “These are the problems; we know about them,” and allows us to say, “These are the problems; frankly, we are not yet clear about them”. Then we let participants decide for themselves what’s in and what’s out of a domain.  The uncertainty about the nature of the Client’s requirements defines the Cynefin domains for the Client’s context. The labels are about “what” options they need to be delivered. Here are three cases: ● The client knows exactly what they need and has already defined it. (Clear) ● The client has a few ideas about what they need. Expert analysis will be necessary to select one alternative and define it. (Complicated) ● The client has a vague idea about what they need and will have to explore many alternatives before arriving at a definition. (Complex)  We draw those labels in the corners on a very large flipchart, as presented on Figure 4, and then talk about the extremes, in the context of the overall purpose 

---

## Page 220

 
220 
of the project. Then we find three cards that fit at the extremes, and place one card in each of the three corners of our flipchart. Then we select one of the remaining cards at random and place it -- as if it has elastic bonds -- on the three corners. Should it be more like this, or that, or closer to the middle? Again…it’s a group discussion.  Next step:  we take another card at random and place it against the three corners, next to the one we just put down. After that, we keep going until all the other cards are placed. After the initial placement, we never allow to move a card intact, but only by breaking it down into new cards and moving them.  
 Figure 4: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 1.  Some caution is in order – when we are doing this, we never give people an example from their own data of what should be in each of the three corners! As complexity thinking facilitators, we realize that people closely watch what we are doing and assume a huge amount of intention on our part. If we want to emphasize an opinion, we choose an example that has nothing to do with their data.   The next step is to define the boundaries, as shown in Figure 5.  When all options are placed, lines are drawn between the user stories that are clearly in one domain, leaving a central area of disorder. Then the lines are “pulled in,” to make the distinctions between domains clearer. If a story is in the central area of 


---

## Page 221

 
221 
disorder, or lies on a line, it is split into two or more user stories that can then be moved to a particular domain. This process involves discussion, as a consensus must be reached.  By defining boundaries, we are not pushing people into categorization.  After the contextualization, then we move into categorization. However, we want to have it without having it prematurely defined.  
 Figure 5: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 2.   Cards can stay at a boundary, but not in the central space. The central space is Confused, and we want to get rid of it over time, while we learn more about the nature of what we put in there. Eventually we want to be able to take action, and what is in Confused is not actionable. Typically, the cards that end up in the middle are there because of their improper granularity. The option on the card is too big – part of it may be complex, and another part may be clear. That’s why we can’t tell what kind of option the card really is.  


---

## Page 222

 
222 
 Figure 6: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 3.  We take those cards in the middle of the flipchart and break them up into multiple cards. The goal is that each new card will fit into one of the three corners. By reducing the granularity of requirements, needs and capabilities, we allow the cards to combine and recombine more readily, thereby creating more sustainable and resilient projects and activities. The result is shown in Figure 6. This is a two-stage emergence, which is a key that we are breaking things down, recombining them in a different way, and then repeating that process. This is designed to break entrenched thinking, detect new patterns, and gain new perspectives and insights.  Next, we contextualize the same set of options but this time, from a capability perspective. The labels will be different this time, focused on “how” to deliver the options. The uncertainty about whether our development capability would match the requirements defines the Cynefin domains for the Capability context. Again, here 


---

## Page 223

 
223 
are three examples:  ● We have all the knowledge and skills required to do the job. We do it all the time, in fact. And there is only one way to do it. (Clear) ● We have enough knowledge and skills required to select from the few alternatives about how to do the job. As experts, we’ll research and analyze the alternatives that someone has done before. (Complicated) ● We have none of the knowledge and skills required to do the job. We haven’t done it before, and we’ll need to explore alternatives and build knowledge. (Complex)  Again, we place the cards according to the three corners, as shown in Figure 7. We may not be able to tell if we have the capability to deliver an option, simply because the option itself is not clear enough, from a client perspective. This makes it Complex from a capability perspective as well. As a general heuristic, we need to be conservative and pessimistic. That means that if an option is between Clear and Complicated, we put it in the Complicated corner. 
 Figure 7: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 4.        


---

## Page 224

 
224 
Next, we draw the boundaries, as shown in Figure 8.  
 Figure 8: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 5.                     


---

## Page 225

 
225 
Then we break down the options, which are too big compared to others, as shown in Figure 9.  
 Figure 9: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 6.                   


---

## Page 226

 
226 
Eventually, as shown in Figure 10, a Cynefin framework is created anew for each of the Capability and Client contexts.  
   Figure 10: Contextualized Cynefin instance: Step 7.    We will use the contextualized Cynefin frameworks for categorization in the unique context of the project. Our goal is to combine the two perspectives into one perspective that we can use operationally.  Here is how we do that.   First, we create a space with horizontal and vertical axes, as presented on Figure 11. On the horizontal axis, we have the level of constraints from the capability perspective. On the vertical axis, we have the level of constraints from the client context. The level of constraints will be qualitative. 


---

## Page 227

 
227 
 Figure 11: Complexity space: Step 1.  Being aware of the Cynefin framework, we can easily put labels on both the horizontal and vertical axes.  They map easily to the three Cynefin domains - Clear, Complicated and Complex - as presented in Figure 12. The Chaos domain is also presented on the diagram. It is the point 0, where there are no constraints, neither from  capability nor from the client perspective.   


---

## Page 228

 
228 
 Figure 12: Complexity space: Step 2.  In order to make the diagram simpler, we will put “Cr” for Clear, “Cd” for Complicated and “Cx” for Complex. The result is presented in Figure 13.  
 Figure 13: Complexity space: Step 3.  The Complexity profile (CP) of our project is presented in Figure 14. It represents the uncertainty about the options we will need to deliver.  


---

## Page 229

 
229 
 
 Figure 14: Complexity profile (CP).  We match the options from each Cynefin framework indexed by their Complexity profile. We do this by taking each of the options and placing them in the respective cell from the Complexity profile. If an option is part of a Complex domain, either from a client or from a capability perspective, it is considered Complex. If an option is part of a Complicated domain, either from a client or from a capability perspective, it is considered Complicated. A Clear domain involves only options that are Clear in both perspectives. Here questions are usually raised:  Why do we need the CP? Why don’t we just place the options in the table as below?   User Story Capability Context Client Context Complexity Profile #1 Complex Clear Complex … … … … #5 Complex Complicated Complex … … … … #14 Clear Clear Clear 


---

## Page 230

 
230 
#15 Clear Complicated Complicated  We don’t use the table above just because we want to show the movement inside the Complexity profile. That would mean making complex options complicated and complicated things clear.  A complexity profile is a maneuverable space. All possible movements among domains are presented below.   
 Figure 15: Three types of systems.  The base movement is to clarify Complex options and make them Complicated. When we have a vague idea of an option, we prepare a concept with a couple of alternatives. In this way, we have made the option Complicated. The movement described represents the accumulation of knowledge about, and also the skills to solve, the problem. This movement is a knowledge discovery process. For example, if we want to move an option from cell CxCx to cell CdCd, we have several alternative routes: ● Linearly CxCx -> CdCx -> CdCd 


---

## Page 231

 
231 
● Linearly CxCx -> CxCd -> CdCd ● Diagonally from CxCx to CdCd, which means we clarify both from capability and from client perspectives  We also see the transition states between the cells presented in stripes. An option is in a transition state when it has left a cell but has not yet gotten into a new cell. That means work is being done to clarify the option.  For example, from a capability perspective, an option is considered complex -- i.e., we don’t know how to implement it and so we decide to develop a Proof of Concept. Then the card will follow CxCx -> CdCx -> CdCd and stay in transition until the PoC provides results.  Another example: if an option is complex from a client perspective, the capability could work with the client by presenting alternatives. This is what we software development experts do -- we provide alternatives, not only about how to do something, but also about what to do. When providing alternatives to the client about what should be done, we have a chance to move the work through the Complexity Profile in a direction that suits best our capabilities as software development experts. One might say that clients who don’t know what they want are the best! We see that the Complexity profile serves as a map to show us how to move rapidly to reach our target position. It helps us act in order to move in two dimensions simultaneously. This dynamic can be presented in a simpler way -- as a movement through a funnel -- as shown in  Figure 16.  

---

## Page 232

 
232 
 Figure 16: Options funnel.  Imagine that we have rotated the complexity profile 45 degrees, so that the lines of movement from Complex to Complicated and then from Complicated to Clear are horizontal. Synthesis is the process of making complex things complicated. Using analysis, we either make the complicated options Clear, or choose one of the excellent alternatives. An example is analyzing how to design a technical system or a user interface.  We see how complex options enter the funnel and come out either as Complicated or Clear following the workflow below. Each option needs to go through synthesis, analysis, and development in order to be ready to be delivered. The discovery process is presented in Figure 17.  


---

## Page 233

 
233 
 Figure 17: Discovery process.  This is how the process works. The first step is to take one option and categorize it. Categorization means placing the option in the proper cell in the Complexity profile. As we saw earlier, the Complexity profile is like a funnel that only Complicated and Clear options can leave.  We can manage the movement of options on an end-to-end Kanban board, as presented in Figure 18. On the right-hand side, we have the upstream Kanban board. Options for new items to be developed enter here. We call them “options” on purpose, to show that it is not mandatory for them to be delivered. 


---

## Page 234

 
234 
 Figure 18: End-to-end Kanban board.  The left-hand Discovery part of the board tracks exploring options. The right-hand Delivery part of the board tracks promises to deliver. Options may be abandoned but promises must be delivered. On the board, an option can leave the upstream part and enter the Delivery part, either from Analysis or from Clear. Cards in black are clear and can directly enter Delivery.  Let’s not forget that in the Delivery part, we are dealing with requests. Requests should not be canceled, because the delivery system has a limited and expensive capacity. In the Discovery part, we are dealing with options. By definition, options can be abandoned. That is presented in the “Discarded options” area of the Kanban board. The cards in deep grey are Complicated. It is not mandatory that all complicated options should become Clear. A complicated option may enter delivery when it is decided that analysis can be done during delivery. That could happen for two reasons. First, when an option is Clear from a client perspective and Complicated from a capability perspective because there are several alternative ways to implement it. The choice of an alternative can be left to the delivery system. Second, when the option is Complicated from a client perspective because small details have to be decided, e.g., text message content, colors.  They can be cleared during delivery.  An option cannot enter the Delivery part from Synthesis.  Also, a complex option should never exit the funnel because it will clutter up the delivery 


---

## Page 235

 
235 
process. The delivery system is not supposed to do the work of the discovery system, because the delivery system has no access to the client.  Both analysis and synthesis of an option may require experiments or probes. A probe may be Proof of Concept (PoC).   How do we show on the board that we are probing [ ]  for an option? We put the option card in a transition cell between CxCx and CdCd on the Complexity profile board. Then, a new PoC card is placed in the Problems column. The PoC card follows the Options - Experiment - Review path. If the review provides enough information for the parent option card, then it is moved into a CdCd cell on the Complexity profile. If not, the card stays in transition. It might even be decided to abandon the option. It is also possible on the Complexity profile cards to move “backwards”—say, from Complicated to Complex. For example, we have a Complicated option with a couple of alternatives about what is to be done. The client is considering which alternative to choose when new information from the market arrives;  suddenly, none of the alternatives is viable any more. The client is back to the point where they don’t know what they want. Once again, the option is Complex.  The discovery part of the board that visualizes the Complexity profile is the Kanban system.  The Work in Process (WIP) limits are set at the top of each column. The numbers show the minimum count of options that need to be present at any given time. For instance, the column Synthesis has to have at least 20 options, and go up to 100. Minimum values get smaller as we move to Analysis and Clear because we are operating a funnel. We need many options to enter the funnel, because only a fraction will leave as delivery requests. We see that the funnel requests start at 20, then becomes 10 and ends in 2-5 delivery requests. Why would we want to have 20 Complex options, and only 2–to-5 clear options? Because Clear options are Clear to everybody else, and so are not valuable. We have a pull system on the Discovery side. That means, if we don’t have at least 20 Complex options, we have to find new ones. But the low limit on Clear will prevent us from picking easy-to-find but non-valuable Clear options. We certainly do not  want non-valuable, Clear options to flood the delivery system with non-valuable delivery requests.  For our products, we strive to have the proper combination of Complex, Complicated, Valuable and Clear options. Finally, we establish a regular feedback loop to continuously revisit and update the categorization of both the user stories in-progress, and the newly added user stories.    We need different people to work on different entries in the Complexity 

---

## Page 236

 
236 
profile. The difference is in the level of complexity they can handle, both from Capability and Client perspectives. For instance – if the problem is clear, then we need a junior person who can simply read a recipe book and follow it. They will need stable requirements, and all the exact tools and technologies from the book. If the problem is Complex, we need a senior person who can cope without a recipe, with changing requirements and whatever is available, in terms of tools and technologies. When we need to adapt, we need more diversity in the system, because then we’ve got more evolutionary capacity. It’s not a coincidence that for living organisms, mutation rates increase under stress – the more diversity, the more adaptability. The requisite diversity is the optimal level of diversity for the context we are in. Diversity means the inefficiency that an organization needs to have. If it is over-focused on efficiency, the organization loses its Adaptive capacity.     

---

## Page 237

 
237 
Chapter 12 - Using Cynefin with COVID-19 Imagine you were climbing a huge mountain that had  never been explored before. From your starting location, it was impossible to tell which slopes can be traversed and which can’t. It was also clear that most of the terrain is hidden behind the slopes and covered in forest. Your climb involved routes that have been tried and abandoned, and there was backtracking and traversing. Many of the successful decisions you made were pure luck! Eventually you managed to climb the mountain and make it to the summit.  When you look down from the summit, you can scan the terrain, but since it is covered in forest it is impossible to see all of the viable routes, the few good ones, or the best route. If you want to check a possible new route, you have to climb the mountain again! The way we remember highlights of an event is different from the way we experience them during the event. When pressed to explain how you managed to get to the top, you pretend that the route you took was indeed the best one. If we learn retrospectively, it always implies causality. With the benefit of hindsight, you declare that you simply chose that route through logical judgment. That is a lie, of course, because it completely misrepresents the process by which you reached the summit.   It’s only human to rewrite the past with a narrative that replaces luck and experimentation with conscious intent. This is called retrospective coherence, and means that, in hindsight, it's easy to explain why things happened, yet it is impossible to predict ahead of time. Therefore, retrospective coherence cannot be used to act in the present          Fine, but we have to act in the present COVID-19 pandemic. Governments should be responsible for protecting their citizens, so they must take action. But what actions?  As of April 5, 2020, the debate about what to do was  limited to two major alternative opinions.  Do nothing, and let the virus run its course, or use unpopular methods to quarantine, and restrict the movement of citizens within and across city and national borders. Let’s look at the alternatives as presented in the media. First opinions insisted on listening to the experts, who wanted to make a rational decision, based on a logical process backed with reliable data. And what did the experts say? “The data we have is unreliable….Human beings fear what they do not understand, and that fear causes them to behave in irrational and destructive ways....When there is talk about how the virus works, there needs to be actual math behind the statements, and peer-reviewed materials….Lockdown 

---

## Page 238

 
238 
means we will watch the entire world commit economic suicide, for no good reason....The government should stand back and let the private sector take care of the problem....We have no problem with a strong  central government response -- as long as it is reasonable and wise….OK, many people will die with coronavirus, but this does not mean that they died of it….The authorities may have distorted the figures….It might appear to be far more of a killer than the flu, simply because of the way deaths are recorded. This, of course, explains why such an overwhelming number of Covid-19 deaths involve so-called ‘underlying conditions,’ which are, in fact, serious, often fatal, diseases…. Since there is no reliable data and the models don’t work, if we don’t know what will happen, we shouldn’t do anything extraordinary and simply wait for “herd immunity” to build instead.”  The second opinion is to take the safest possible position. What do those voices say? ….”We are not against collecting more and better data, but decisions can’t wait….Only historians have good data….Decisions must be iterated as more is learned….Only idiots wait for data in a crisis….By the time you have data, it’s too late….We have neither the means nor the time to accumulate such data right now;  decisions must be made….It is like telling a surgeon with his finger on a massive bleeder that he needs lab tests before deciding what to do….Models are only as good as  assumptions….Let’s test and find out! If we had 125 cases 14 days ago, and 5000 today, then the virus grew 40 times during that period…. Certainly, we have more unknown cases…. The modeling forecasts a huge impact on health services. These levels of demand would be completely unprecedented…. Knowing our government, we would consider one or two orders of magnitude more cases than anything that was reported by them…. The idea, advanced by some doctors and politicians, that social distancing is costing the economy too much is a “fake trade-off... The only way to truly help the economy is by snuffing out the pandemic…. We can't wait! Getting more evidence doesn't help, it hurts… In dealing with unexpected events, the most important move is the first move….Timing is everything….We shouldn't take a certain action without good evidence from data, but not taking that action is also a decision that requires evidence…. In the real world, one must reduce risk in the absence of reliable data, via the most robust to perturbations model….Forgetting models for a moment, if we overreact against catastrophic exponential scenarios, we can take a wait-and-see approach, but if we under-react, it will be too late….In the real world, decisions are normally made with insufficient and flawed data…We cannot wait for evidence when people's lives  are at stake.”   Let’s use Cynefin   as a lens to look at the two alternative opinions.  Our goal 

---

## Page 239

 
239 
is to confirm our perceptions with reality in order to act.  First, what is the Cynefin domain humans perceive we are in? The first group thinks we have to turn to our experts for advice about which good practice to follow. This is a perception of a Complicated situation.  The second group thinks we are in uncharted waters. In order to avoid chaos, we have to act, and our response has to co-evolve with the situation. We have no valid model of how the virus will spread, and how many people will die. Everything is possible! This is a perception of a Chaotic situation.  Second, what is the objective reality? As with any virus, if it is not restrained somehow, the number of COVID-19-infected people will grow exponentially. The virus will mutate, and there will be some attempts to restrain the spread of infection so that the virus and the human population will co-evolve. From the Cynefin perspective, when we hear the term “co-evolve,” we must acknowledge that we are in a Complex system. Waiting for more data is not the way to approach complex, dynamic problems. The Chaos and Complexity domains are not the domains for experts. They flourish in the Complicated domain.  In such a chaotic situation, we have to act, then sense the results and respond accordingly. Speed trumps perfection.  At the same time, we should never forget that the danger in a crisis is premature convergence on a single solution and/or approach. The ability to be decisive while holding many options open is a key leadership ability in a crisis. When taking action, we should differentiate dynamics that we can't affect versus  ones we can affect. We can't keep planets from colliding, but we can keep diseases from exploding -- just by modifying our behavior. Many governments treat the pandemic as if it is an external force on the system that we must respond to proportionally: as it gets worse, we slowly ramp up defensive measures in response to the scale of the force. But it's not an external force, it is an endogenously generated one, and because we grow this force through our behavior, the slow ramping up of mitigation guarantees the expenditure of many more resources across a much longer cycle. Instead, we should have nipped it in the bud.  That’s why the action that could be taken is quite straightforward and has already been done in the history of mankind. It is to isolate, to separate the population in small groups, and then just wait for the virus to die out by itself. After all, the virus doesn’t spread by itself -- it is spread by people!  The extreme version of taking action is to lock down regions, cities, even entire countries. It is clear that, with isolation, the spread of the virus slows down.  Some caution is in order here - if we put rigid constraints that make no sense on people, they will not be obeyed. For instance, we could say that one can go outside  their home at any time, provided they meet no one, compared to the 

---

## Page 240

 
240 
constraint that one can leave their home  only when the government sends them a text message. Quarantines are imposed to reduce the number of those infected, not to prevent infection. That would be a complex technique. Chaotic situations become complex when we start to see patterns. For complex problems, the role of decision makers is to create and relax constraints as needed, and then to dynamically allocate resources where patterns of success emerge. Examples of constraints are the heuristics: for example, “wear a mask” and “don’t touch your face and mouth.” We manage what we can manage – the boundary conditions, the safe-to-fail experiments, our determination to rapidly amplify or diminish an activity. By using this approach, we discover value at various points along the way. Practices that work in containing the infection should be the result of exaptation, which occurs when, under conditions of stress, something developed for one function is re-purposed for a different function. Nobody will ever know if the Stay-At-Home orders worked.  And to what extent. That’s how complexity works. You can’t know how things might have been otherwise.   We know that with complex systems, whatever we do will produce unintended consequences. The bigger the intervention, the bigger the unintended consequences. Let’s look at some possibilities. First, the public is presented with diagrams where the number of infections grows “exponentially.” That particular diagram must have been familiar to every person signing for a loan with compound interest. Maybe one of the unexpected second-order effects from this crisis would be that people start looking back into the usury question of how compound interest really works.  Second, there may be unintended consequences of fear and stress on citizens’ behavior caused by regulations without reassurance as to consequences.  Third, consequences for the decision makers. Obviously, the lockdowns are the road governments would take globally. There are voices who will want the governments to report -- after the pandemic is over --if the measures taken were the right ones. What if the final numbers of the dead are way below the flu? The public will say that it was fooled, and that we have averted something that was never really going to be as severe as we had feared. Obviously, the governments will say that this is because their repressive economy-wrecking measures worked. The public will demand proof, from a thorough independent inquiry, that this is true.  Because if it is not, then we are in danger of the first global political crisis.  Globalization is reason enough to anticipate a “very strange acute virus spreading throughout the planet.” Calling it black swan allows leaders to excuse 

---

## Page 241

 
241 
their not being prepared. This year’s COVID-19 virus, however, was a predictable event. COVID-19 is a wake-up call for the human race. Thank God it is not as severe a disease as Ebola is.  Actually, it is giving us a chance to find new and better ways to live and work together. It will not, however, be the last pandemic.  Once you have internalized that thought, it is impossible to believe that we can ever return to the pre-virus world. Everything we do, all of our investments, and even the way we organize ourselves, will be influenced by new considerations. Will we be protected from COVID-19 and its successors, or made more vulnerable to them? As a first step toward confronting the outbreaks that are certain to come, we must learn to live with uncertainty, rather than allowing ourselves to be held captive by it.  Business and other organizations must switch to a different mode of operating. They will have to abandon their usual annual plans and quarterly targets, and instead, pay full attention to any virus situation as it unfolds. And then adjust their behavior accordingly. It’s a skill and a capacity that we urgently need today. In other words, our entire attention and intention need to quickly align with what is actually happening in the present.
